# What the Wind Keeps

# Chapter 1

By noon the light had gone the color of old brass. Cora Vale stood on the back step with a bucket of mash knocking her shin and looked west where the sky ought to have been empty. It was not empty. It had weight now, a low-bellied wall rolling in over the wheat stubble, brown at the base and black where it shouldered upward. It turned the whole county stovepipe-black.

The warning bell from town started after she had already seen it. The sound came thin on the wind, foolishly late, and got cut to pieces before it reached the yard. She set the bucket down hard enough to slop mash over her shoe and said, to the yard and the hens and God if He was listening, "Damn."

Dell had ridden his bicycle into Morrow after dinner because Ulrich at the garage had said there might be two dollars in a magneto that needed coaxing back to life. Two dollars was lamp oil and coffee and maybe a strip of salt pork if the week turned generous, so she had let him go with a warning to keep one eye on the sky. Thea had gone the other direction on foot, neat as always even on a Sunday, because the north window at the schoolhouse never shut right and she could not stand the thought of readers and maps pasted into mud. She had taken her attendance ledger in a satchel she treated more carefully than most people treated an infant. Cora had watched both of them leave and thought the weather looked mean but not yet murderous. That had been less than an hour ago.

Now murder was coming in plain sight.

She crossed the yard at a run. The hens had already gone stupid with it, running the fence instead of the coop, their wings beating up little gusts of grit around their own feet. Cora flung feed into the dim of the shed to draw them in, then stooped and gathered two by the legs when the rest would not take the hint. Their bodies hit against her skirt, hot and frantic. One pecked her wrist hard enough to raise blood. She shoved them through the coop door, dropped the bar, and turned toward the lean-to where the milk cow had begun to bawl with that raw, offended sound cattle made before bad weather, as if the sky had personally insulted them.

The yard had changed while her back was turned. The west line of the world was gone. Fence posts that had stood black and plain against the fields a minute ago were fading from the bottom up. Dust moved ahead of the storm in low snakes over the ground, hissing through the yard grass, and when she licked her lips she tasted dirt that had not been there a breath before. The clothesline wire gave off a faint high whine. Static lifted the loose ends of her ash-blond hair against her temples.

She got the cow inside, dropped the latch bar, threw a burlap sack over the feed bin, and ran for the house. Every small thing had turned urgent at once: porch chairs, the wash boiler, the pan of peels meant for the chickens, the two front shutters that still hung open because she had wanted light in the parlor while she mended. Since Ezra died there had been nobody to say I'll get that one, and she had learned the ugly arithmetic of storm work alone. One body could not be in three places. One pair of hands never reached the last task in time.

At the kitchen door she snatched the wash boiler up by the handles and shoved it over the threshold with her knee. She hooked the screen, slammed the inner door, and moved room to room latching windows, dropping the wooden bars into place, pulling shades where there were shades to pull. Dust was already threading the seams. In the front room she fastened the west shutters and felt the whole house answer under her palm, a small shudder in the frame as the first hard push of wind struck broadside.

Dell, she thought. Then immediately, angrily, Thea.

She should have sent for them both sooner. She should have kept them close because the day had felt wrong from breakfast on, too warm for April and too still in the mean interval before wind. But that was farm thinking after the fact, the kind that made every mistake look preventable once it had already chosen you.

Cora wrapped a dish towel around her nose and mouth and opened the back door into a world that had gone sepia and loud. Wind took the edge of the door at once and tried to wrench it from her. She leaned her weight into it, got out onto the step, and cupped her hands around her mouth.

"Dell!"

The name blew back in her face. She could not hear herself say it.

She tried again toward the road, squinting through the dirt. Nothing held still long enough to be a person. The yard pump bent and straightened inside the haze like something underwater. A scrap of newspaper spun past so fast she only caught a single printed O before it vanished under the porch. She took two steps off the stoop, then three more, head down, apron snapping around her calves, and that was when she saw a shape at the front gate.

For one clean second relief hit her so hard she nearly laughed. Dell, she thought, and started around the corner of the house.

It was Thea.

She came through the gate sideways, one hand over her eyes, the other knotted in the strap of her satchel. Her hat was gone. Only the hatpin remained, caught crooked in the collar of her Sunday coat like a bit of broken wire. Dust had pasted her dark hair flat on one side of her head and turned the other side wild. Her stockings were laddered at one knee where she must have gone down in the road. Even half blind she still had the satchel cinched to her as if the attendance book mattered to the weather.

"Don't you fall now," Cora shouted, though she could not hear her own voice. She reached Thea just as the younger woman put out a hand and missed the porch post by a foot.

Thea's fingers closed on Cora's forearm with desperate exactness. They were colder than the wind. Dust had packed into the wet corners of her eyes and made gray tracks down her face where tears from the grit had run. Up close she smelled of wool, schoolroom chalk, and the hot mineral stink of storm dust.

"I know," Thea said, or tried to say. The words came raw through the cloth she had tied over her mouth. "You can scold me after."

"Inside."

Cora got an arm around her waist and hauled. The storm caught the front of Thea's coat and bellied it out behind her like a sail. For two stumbling steps it felt absurdly like dancing, the kind done badly at socials by women with no business laughing that hard, and then the porch took their shins and Cora slammed them both through the front door and drove the bolt home with both hands.

The house went dim and howling instead of black and howling. Dust came in under the sill in a fine brown line. Thea bent double coughing, one hand braced on her knee, the satchel still hanging from the other. Cora took it from her and set it on the table before the dust could work deeper into the flap.

"Sit down."

"I don't want to get the chair filthy."

"Everything's filthy."

That, for some reason, made Thea laugh once, sharp and breathless, before she coughed again. Cora took her by the elbow and led her to the kitchen pump. The water in the pail beside it had grit floating on top already. She poured what was cleanest into a basin, found a rag, and tipped Thea's face toward the light.

Her eyelashes were glued with mud. Cora wiped carefully, working from the inner corner out the way Hester had once shown her after Dell got chaff in his eye as a boy. Thea hissed and tried to pull back, then held still by force.

"You went all the way to the schoolhouse in this?" Cora said.

"It wasn't this when I left."

"No, just foolish."

"That too." Thea swallowed, then winced when more dust scratched her throat. "The north window blew in. I thought I could pin the chart cabinet shut and get back before this got any worse. Then it was in my teeth. That was schoolteacher foolishness."

"Did you see Dell?"

Thea's eyes opened at that. Even reddened and watering they stayed peculiarly exact, still trying to force the world back into tidy lines. "No. I saw a bicycle laid over in the ditch by the church, but I couldn't tell whose."

Cora set the rag down. The basin water had gone cloudy brown.

"It may not be his."

"No," Thea said, hearing the lie in it as clearly as Cora did. "It may not."

For a moment neither of them moved. Wind hammered the west side of the house. Something loose in the yard went skidding along the boards with a long scraping shriek. Thea reached up, perhaps meaning only to steady herself, and caught Cora's wrist. Her thumb landed on the inside of the pulse there, in the clean strip of skin above the flour on Cora's hand. It was not a proper touch. It was not even an accidental one, not with the way Thea's thumb stayed where it was for that one spare beat before she let go.

"Tell me what needs doing," she said.

Cora wanted, absurdly and with full knowledge of how mad it was, to bend and put her mouth against the dust-reddened lid of Thea's eye. Instead she shoved the cleaned rag into Thea's hand.

"Wet every towel you can find and jam them at the sills. Then go upstairs and shut your window proper this time."

"Yes, ma'am."

The faint shape of a smile touched Thea's mouth and was gone again. She moved off at once, already wringing the rag out over the basin. Even under pressure she did not waste motion. She set the satchel higher on the table, out of the line of dust, before she went for the towels.

Cora tied the dish towel harder over her face and went back to the front room. By the time she had knelt to stuff damp cloth into the crack beneath the window, the house was dark enough that she had to light the kerosene lamp on the piano. Noon had gone clear through afternoon and come out the far side into something like eclipse. The lamp flame wavered blue before it steadied. Each time the wind struck, the glass chimney clicked in its metal collar.

Upstairs, Thea's tread crossed the boards, paused, then crossed back fast. A moment later she came down with an armload of blankets.

"The window shut, but the top sash won't sit," she said. "I wedged it with the atlas no child has opened since Christmas."

"Good."

"The parlor north sill's leaking dust. Enough that it's gritting in my teeth."

"Then give me that blanket."

They worked without speaking for a few minutes, moving from room to room. The house grew smaller with every door they shut. Dust made its own way in regardless, through the keyhole, around the casing, between the floorboards where years of sweeping had not closed anything. It settled on the lamp chimney, on the spoon Cora had left by the stove, on the framed wedding photograph on the shelf where she had long ago stopped looking directly. When the wind rose harder, the hanging lamp in the kitchen swayed just enough to knock against its chain with a small metallic tick that got under her skin worse than the big sounds did.

The animals were no quieter. The cow hit the lean-to wall with her flank once, twice. Chickens battered at the coop boards, frantic to come through them. Beyond that came the great sustained roar of the storm itself, too broad to call sound exactly. It filled the ears and pressed on the chest. It felt like being buried while still upright.

Cora set her hand on the back latch. "I'm going."

Thea, who had just risen from stuffing a towel under the pantry door, straightened so fast she almost knocked the chair over. "No."

"He's out there."

"And if you open that door now you'll be out there too."

"He's seventeen. He isn't windproof."

"Neither are you."

The answer came too quickly to sound rehearsed. It was not schoolteacher measured now. It was plain and frightened and a little angry. Thea stepped toward her, one hand still wet from the towel, leaving a dark print on her skirt where she had wiped it by habit.

"If you go," Thea said, more quietly, "and he finds his way back, he'll come to a shut house and no lamp in it. He'll think you went after him. Then he'll go back out."

Cora looked at the latch and hated that the sense of it landed. Wind shoved at the door from the other side, proving the point. The whole frame rattled in its casing. There was dirt in the corners of Cora's teeth from breathing inside her own kitchen.

"I should've made him stay in town once the sky turned."

"You didn't have the sky in your hand."

"No. Only the boy."

That stopped Thea. The younger woman took half a breath as if to answer, then did not. Her face changed in a small unguarded way that Cora had only seen in the hour just after waking, before Thea remembered to arrange herself for daylight. The lamp showed the dust on her cheekbones and the nick at the corner of her mouth where she worried the skin in her sleep.

Something slammed outside with a violence that made both of them jump. Then came the scream of torn metal.

"The shed roof," Cora said.

She was through the door before Thea could argue. The storm hit like a thrown load of sand. One instant she had a grip on the knob; the next she had both eyes squeezed shut and her head down against grit hard enough to sting through the towel. She got as far as the edge of the porch and saw almost nothing except the shape of the lean-to shuddering under the force of the wind, one strip of tin peeled up and banging itself to death.

Thea was beside her, though Cora had not heard the second door open. She had wrapped a blanket over her head and shoulders like a nun's veil dragged through hell. Together they leaned into the gusts and fought their way to the lean-to. Cora got the dangling strip of tin under one gloved hand and drove it back against the roof beam while Thea, who had no business being any use at such a job and knew it, jammed the pitchfork handle through the loosened bracket to pin it. The blanket whipped free and vanished at once into the storm. "Christ Almighty," Thea said.

"You do know how to improve a Sunday," Cora shouted.

Thea turned her face enough that Cora could see, even through the dirt, the flash of her grin. "I had other plans."

Then the cow kicked the inside wall so hard the boards boomed, and whatever wildness had broken across Thea's mouth disappeared. They made it back to the porch half blinded, dragging the spare sack of feed Cora had nearly forgotten. When the door shut behind them again, both women stood bent with their hands on their thighs, spitting mud into the sink one after the other like men coming up from a mine.

Dust had found the house in earnest now. The kitchen table held a visible film. So did the stove lid, the breadboard, the blue enamel coffee pot drying upside down by the basin. The air inside had thickened until the lamp light seemed to stop only a few feet from the glass.

Thea stripped off her coat and wrung grit from the hem with both hands. Underneath she wore the pale green dress she saved for Sundays, the one with the tiny self-covered buttons Cora always wanted to undo slowly and never did in daylight. The collar was torn where the hatpin had caught. Her stockings were ripped at the right knee. A bead of blood sat in the scrape and turned brown as Cora watched.

"Sit," Cora said again, this time with less force because what she wanted to do about the torn stocking was not something she could say aloud while the whole town might be blowing away outside.

Thea obeyed. That, too, was part of what made the thing between them feel dangerous. It was never only hunger. It was trust, and trust in Morrow was always the costlier article.

Cora fetched the iodine from the shelf and crouched before her. Thea started to protest, then saw her face and let it go.

"This will sting."

"Everything stings."

"Then you won't notice."

The joke was thin, but Thea gave her a look for the effort. Cora dabbed the scrape clean. Thea's knee was narrow and hard under her hand, the bone close, the skin cold from the storm. She felt the moment the younger woman stopped thinking about the sting and started thinking about the hand touching her. It changed the air between them more surely than the weather had changed the light.

From the road side of the house came a thud. Not the loose slap of debris. Something heavier. Then another, followed by a dragging knock against the front step.

Cora was already on her feet.

"Dell!" she shouted, and the name this time came from somewhere below speech.

She ran through the front room. Dust stirred around her ankles with every stride. Thea was right behind. Cora pulled the bolt, shouldered the door wide enough for a body, and the storm pushed Dell in on top of her.

He hit the floorboards on one knee and both hands, coughing so hard he could not get breath enough to speak. His cap was gone. His dust-light hair stood up in matted points, and his face was stripped raw where he had tied a bandanna too low and the grit had skinned the rest. His left ear bled from the edge like somebody had taken sandpaper to it. He still had his coat over his head, arms tangled in the sleeves, and when Cora hauled it free a spark plug and three cotter pins fell from his pocket and clinked across the floor.

Even now, she thought wildly, even with the whole county trying to scour him off it, he had not dropped machine parts.

Thea slammed the door behind them. Dell got one breath and managed, "Shut the damn draft," before doubling over again.

"It's shut." Cora knelt and got her hands on his shoulders, then his face, then his shoulders again because she could not seem to decide which part of him mattered most. "Where were you? Where in God's name were you?"

"Town." He spat black mud into the crook of his arm. "Where d'you think?"

"Don't get smart with me."

"I'm trying not to choke."

His voice cracked on the last word, and the sound of it cut straight through her temper into the place underneath where fear had been scraping since the bell rang. He was lean and long-limbed and mean-mouthed when embarrassed, all seventeen years of him trying daily to harden into a man, but at that angle on the floor with the grit packed in his lashes he looked about twelve.

Thea brought the basin without being asked. Dell turned his face away from her out of reflexive shame, which made her say, with schoolroom dryness that had been scoured down to its bones, "If you're going to die of pride, do it after you rinse your nose."

"Miss Bell," he managed, appalled.

"Yes, Mr. Vale. Blow."

He did, muttering, "God damn it," into the rag when the pain hit, and the rag came away brown-black. Cora kept one hand between his shoulder blades while he coughed. Through his shirt she could feel each breath catch and jerk. He smelled of cold sweat, bicycle grease, and the bitter hot smell that rose from bodies after panic.

"I left the bicycle by the church ditch," he said when he could talk again. "Chain jumped, then the light went wrong. I thought I could walk it. Then I couldn't see the road. I had to crawl the fence line from Weller's place. Lost my cap. Damn near lost a shoe."

"You should've stayed in town."

"At whose house?"

There it was, the real answer, ugly for being practical. Everybody in Morrow had a house too full of its own trouble. If he had chosen wrong, he might have spent the storm in a shed or under a counter or being resented for the bread he ate afterward. Poverty never stayed private in weather like this.

Cora dragged his coat off completely and threw it toward the stove. Dust burst from it in a cloud. "I was about to come out after you."

Dell looked up then. There was no defiance in him, and not even guilt at first, only surprise. He had plainly not imagined she would step into that blackness for him when she had the house and stock and Thea to hold together.

"You couldn't have found me."

"I would've come anyway."

"You'd have died in the yard."

"Then you'd have had two graves to mind."

The words came out before she could sand them smooth. The room went still around them despite the storm. Even Thea stopped moving.

Dell's face changed. The hard smart look he wore like a new coat slipped, and the boy under it saw her plain. Saw, Cora knew, not just that she had been frightened, but how far the fear had reached. Ezra had died months ago and still the house had not stopped learning the shape of absence. Dell saw then that she had been halfway to imagining the same shape for him.

"Ma," he said, very softly, in the voice he had not used in years unless fever had him or sleep.

She put the back of her wrist against his cheek because her hands were filthy and shaking both. "Get up. Let's get the grit off you before it beds in."

They moved him to the kitchen chair by the stove. Thea heated water. Cora stripped his shirt over his head and found the skin of his shoulders crosshatched red, written over by dust. His ribs worked sharp under the flesh. There were fresh scrapes on both forearms and a deeper gouge in the heel of his hand where he must have hit gravel going down. When she poured warm water over the cloth and wiped him, the basin turned the color of ditch water in spring.

"Ulrich give you anything for the job?" she asked because the ordinary question felt like a rope she could still throw.

Dell gave a short, disbelieving laugh and coughed on the tail of it. "He gave me the spark plug and said if I got the thing running proper next time he'd see."

"Of course he did."

"It's a good plug."

Thea set a tin cup of water in front of him and, because she could not help herself, turned the fallen spark plug with one finger to inspect it. "He paid you in scrap and promises. That's about right for this town."

Dell, raw-faced and exhausted, let out one real laugh at that. It hurt him. He put a hand to his ribs and hissed, "Ah, hell."

"Don't encourage him," Cora said.

"I'm encouraging him to survive long enough to resent me tomorrow."

That sounded more like Thea, and because it sounded more like Thea Cora nearly sat down in relief. Instead she made coffee from the grounds she had been saving one extra day and cut the heel off the bread loaf though she had meant to keep it for supper. Dust gritted between her teeth every time she bit down. Dell drank, winced, drank again. Thea perched on the edge of the other chair with her skirt changed out for an old housedress and her torn stocking rolled down while it dried by the stove rail, one knee bare to the lamplight. Cora kept her eyes off it by force.

Outside, the storm held. Darkness at noon had gone on so long it became its own time of day. The lamp burned and burned. Once the sound dropped enough that Cora thought perhaps the worst was past, and then another wave hit, so dense and furious that the front windows went black as cellar walls. Dell flinched at that despite himself. Thea reached without looking and set two fingers on the table near his wrist, not touching, merely offering the fact of another body in the room. He did not move away.

"You should both sleep while you can," Cora said.

"On what breath?" Dell asked.

"On the one you've got."

But nobody slept. Dell dozed and jerked awake coughing. Thea went once to her room and came back with an extra pillow because the kitchen chair was sawing the back of his neck. Cora checked the stove, checked the back latch, checked the lean-to through the slit of the curtain though there was nothing to see but moving brown. She found herself counting objects to hold her head steady: six spoons in the drawer, three cups not chipped, one good lamp chimney, two quilts fit for another winter if the mice did not get them first. It was the kind of counting she had done after Ezra died, when the only way through a day had been to reduce it to what still remained and what absolutely could not be spared.

At some point Dell's head tipped forward onto his chest and stayed there. Thea rose to ease him into the little settle in the front room where he could stretch his legs. She had to wake him enough to move, and he came up mean with exhaustion.

"I'm not a child."

"No," Thea said. "Children fuss worse than that. Up you go."

He obeyed because she had been his teacher and because he had no strength left to defy anyone. Cora laid one of Ezra's old quilts over him once he was down. The wool still held, in some deep stubborn thread, the ghost of machine oil and the tobacco Ezra had quit twice and never really quit. She tucked the edge under Dell's shoulder and had to look away from the way his face, asleep at last, tipped younger again.

The front room was flour-blasted, only bitter and mean instead of white. Dust lay on the piano keys, on the arms of the horsehair chairs, on the framed scripture from Ezra's mother, on the glass chimney of the lamp, on the photograph of Dell at nine with a fish too small to keep and a grin too large to hide. It had reached even the center of the table under the crocheted doily where Cora had thought nothing could touch it. There were days by then when grief felt less like sorrow than like housekeeping that never ended. Ezra kept arriving in the form of things that needed wiping down.

Thea stood beside her, close enough that the heat from her body reached through the dust-cooled cloth of Cora's sleeve. "He's all right for now."

"For now."

"You can sit down, Cora."

"After I get this room livable."

Thea made the small sound that meant she knew arguing would only waste breath. "Then at least let me sweep."

"You'll only raise it."

"You always say that as if I learned every useless thing except a broom."

"You did learn Latin."

Thea's mouth twitched. "For three years. I would rather have learned how to keep Nebraska out of a windowsill."

"Nobody's learned that."

They looked at each other then, and what passed between them was not comfort exactly. Comfort was too clean a word. It was hunger, yes, and relief, and the animal knowledge that all afternoon they had been one bad gust away from losing something they had not even been allowed to name aloud. Thea lifted her hand as if to touch Cora's face, then checked it and let it fall to the broom handle instead because Dell lay sleeping ten feet away and the windows, even shuttered, still belonged to the town.

"I'll fetch clean water," Thea said.

Cora nodded. She watched the younger woman go through the kitchen doorway, watched the line of her back in the dim and the careful way she favored the scraped knee without turning it theatrical. Then she took up a damp cloth and went to the front window.

She wanted only to clear a space enough to see whether the porch still stood. The dust on the pane had gone thick as sifted meal. When she laid the cloth to it, the surface dragged under her hand with a faint gritty squeal. She wiped a circle, then leaned close and breathed on the part she had missed.

The glass clouded.

At first she thought she was only looking at her own reflection gone strange in the moisture, her face warped by the wavering lamplight behind her. Then the fog on the pane deepened instead of thinning, and another room came forward in it.

It was not this room at all.

August Poeschel's office filled the window: the green-shaded lamp on his desk, the ledger stacks with their cloth backs rubbed pale, the narrow calendar tacked crooked beside the safe. August himself stood at the far side of the desk, heavy in the shoulders even with his coat off, his face shaved smooth as Sunday plateware. He had one palm over the upper corner of a document. The gesture was not dramatic. It was almost casual. That was what made it indecent.

Ezra sat bent over the desk in his work shirt with the cuffs frayed white. Not a remembered Ezra from courtship or harvest dances or the first year of Dell's life when he had still laughed with his whole chest. This was Ezra from the end: rangy and prematurely stooped, beard gone blue by supper, the tendon in his neck standing out when he swallowed. His right thumbnail was split to the quick. She had wrapped that thumb in rag three nights before he died after binder twine cut him open.

Cora did not move. She barely breathed.

August said something first, but the wind of the memory shredded the opening words. Then, clear as ink laid fresh, he said, "Here and here, Mr. Vale."

Ezra did not reach for the pen at once. His mouth moved. The pane gave her only part of it.

"You said till harvest."

August's hand smoothed the page once. Twice. "The bank will consider the extension when the papers are regular."

Ezra looked down. Whatever was in his face then was worse than fear and smaller than rage. Shame fit close. It made him look old. He took the pen. For one second Cora saw the exact stubborn set of his fingers, the way he always gripped too hard and turned writing into a wrestle. Then he bent and signed.

He did not refuse, and he did not make a scene. It was not the clean no he had come home with in his mouth that night when she asked whether August had tried to make him put his name to drainage paper.

He signed.

The moisture on the pane thinned at once. August's office broke apart into streaks. Ezra's hand vanished last, the black line of the pen dragging into nothing. Then there was only the Vale front window again, filmed in dust, giving back a blurred piece of her own face.

Behind her, in the kitchen, Thea set the water pail down. The sound was real enough to make Cora flinch.

She put her fingertips to the glass where Ezra's hand had been. The pane was cool. Grit clung to the damp of her breath and stuck to her skin.

He had sworn on her and on Dell that he had not signed. He had stood in this same front room, not six feet from this window, hat in both hands and the shame of August's visit still on him, and said, I didn't give him that. Not that. She had believed him because she loved him and because belief had been cheaper than opening the full account of what was rotting underneath their life.

Now the account had opened anyway.

"Cora?" Thea said from the doorway.

Cora kept looking at the window until the dust blurred flat and ordinary under her hand. When she finally turned, her face felt wrong on her skull. The storm had shifted more than weather.

"What's wrong?" Thea asked.

Cora looked past her toward the settle where Dell slept under Ezra's quilt, his mouth open a little for want of good air. She thought of August's hand covering the top of the paper. She thought of Ezra signing anyway. She thought of the lie she had lived inside since the barn, a lie so clean it had almost counted as mercy.

"Nothing," she said, because the word arrived first and because she did not yet know what name the other thing would bear.

Thea stood in the doorway another second, searching her face and getting nowhere with it. Then Dell coughed in his sleep from the settle, and that broke the moment cleanly enough for both of them. Thea did not ask again. After a while her tread crossed the upstairs boards and went still.

Cora stayed in the front room with the lamp and the window. She wiped one hard oval in the glass until the rag turned to mud in her hand and the rest of the pane still wore the storm. Each time she breathed close, she expected August's office to come back through the clouding and show her some fresh treachery. It never did. There was only her own face and the dark porch beyond it and the grit settling deeper into the house.

Near dawn she carried the rag out to the pump and worked the handle in the dark, listening to the iron complain. Brown water ran over her wrists and down into the packed yard. It did not wash the sight of Ezra signing from her. Nothing was finished. Nothing was buried. The storm had left dust in the seams of the house and something worse in her hands: proof that Ezra had taken a pen from August Poeschel and put his name where he swore no name had gone. Whatever the weather had not stripped from her that day, this would.


# Chapter 2

By morning the dust had settled into the house the way a bad remark settled into a church social: everywhere, impossible to answer back, finer than any broom deserved.

Thea woke in her narrow room at the Vale place with grit in the hinge of her jaw and a line of it worked into the seam of the pillowcase. She shook out her stockings over the washstand and watched a gray veil drift down the white enamel basin. Her Sunday dress hung from a nail with a dun cast to it, as if the storm had tried it on in the night. When she put it over her head, the collar rasped against her neck.

In the kitchen Cora already had the stove going. She stood in her work dress with the sleeves rolled once, not twice, which meant she had dressed in the dark and not looked down. The kettle lid jittered. A skillet sat cooling on the back of the range, and the bacon grease in it had turned to a pale, ugly paste under the dust. Cora was scraping the top layer off with the side of a spoon, saving what was underneath.

The front-room window had been wiped in one hard oval, no bigger than a dinner plate. The rest of the glass still wore the storm.

Thea paused with one hand on the chair back. She had not asked, in the night, what Cora had seen there. Cora had come to bed late, or not at all; Thea had heard only floorboards and the pump handle outside and then nothing.

"You don't have to go in if the roof came loose," Cora said. Her voice was flat from tiredness, but not unkind. "No use having the schoolhouse fall on you for form's sake."

"If I don't open it, half the town will decide it's closed forever before noon."

"Half the town has decided worse than that."

Thea might have smiled on another morning. She set her satchel on the table and took the rag Cora had left beside the bread board. The bread itself was wrapped in a flour sack, one heel and three uneven slices from yesterday. Cora looked at the loaf the way she looked at kerosene, needles, lamp wicks, every other thing in the house that could run out.

"Did Dell sleep?" Thea asked.

"Some."

"Did you?"

Cora kept scraping. "I laid down."

That was as much as Thea was going to get. Cora's public voice had come into the kitchen before the light had. Every answer cost exactly the least it could.

Dell came in from the yard with the collar of his shirt up around his mouth and his hair standing every direction at once. He was seventeen and thin enough lately that his wrists looked borrowed from a younger boy. He carried the galvanized bucket they used under the pump, and the water inside had a skin of dust moving on it like drowned pollen.

"Pump tastes like the road," he said, setting it down.

"Strain it again," Cora said.

"That'll only make it taste like cloth."

"Then you'll have had both courses."

He snorted, which was close enough to laughter to count. He glanced at Thea, then at the clean oval on the window, and just as quickly away. Whatever had happened in the front room after he got home from the storm, nobody meant to name it at breakfast.

Cora sliced the bread thinner than hunger liked and fried eggs in the grease she had managed to rescue. The yolks filmed over gray at the edges before they reached the plates. Thea ate because refusing would have been its own kind of rudeness. Dust clicked between her teeth when she chewed. Across from her, Dell salted everything before tasting it, an old habit from years when he still believed more salt could make a poor supper feel deliberate.

"If any of them come in coughing blood, send them home," Cora said.

"I'm not likely to mistake that for mischief."

"You'd be surprised what children will do to get out of sums."

It was the nearest thing to a joke Cora had offered. Thea looked up too quickly. Cora had not smiled; she was using the knife to push crumbs together for the hens. But the line had been meant as company, and because it had been meant that way it went straight through Thea.

She wanted to ask what was wrong beyond the obvious. She wanted to ask whether the shut-down look in Cora's face belonged to fear, grief, or the thing on the window. She wanted to cross the kitchen and touch the raw place at the base of Cora's thumb where the skin always split in spring. Dell sat there with his elbows on the oilcloth, older than some men and younger than he knew. The house was too small for want.

"If I get there and it's a wall of dirt and broken desks," Thea said, reaching for her satchel, "I'll come back by dinner."

"Bring the key back if the lock's torn off," Cora said.

"That what worries you? The key?"

Cora gave her a look then, quick and tired and alive under all the tiredness. "No. But I can do something about a missing key."

Thea took that with her out the door.

The road into town had lost its edges. Fence lines came and vanished in drifts banked ankle-high against the posts. At the Olsen place the front step had disappeared entirely, and the milk can by the gate wore a soft cone of dirt like a winter cap. The wheat west of the road had not been cut, only laid over and powdered until each row looked rubbed out. A dead jackrabbit lay against the culvert with its ears full of silt.

Thea walked with her handkerchief tied over her nose until her own breath dampened it. Even through the cloth the air tasted baked and old, as if the storm had dug up some cellar of the earth and thrown it in people's faces.

Morrow itself was quieter than a Monday had any right to be. Usually by this hour she heard at least a wagon axle screaming for grease, the clang from the blacksmith shed, Mrs. Fenske at the post office window telling somebody to stop leaning on the ledge as if federal property were a porch rail. This morning sound carried in scraps. Hester's bakery door stood open, letting out yeast and scorched coffee. Across the street a man she did not know anymore because dust had made everybody the same color was sweeping the boardwalk only to move dirt from one set of cracks to another. Farmers State Bank had a broom propped outside its door and no customers visible through the glass.

The schoolhouse sat where it always had, plain and square and under-loved. The west side had taken the worst of the storm. Dust had pushed in around the windowsills and under the door, then set up residence. When Thea opened the door, one drift inside had curled itself against the far wall like something sleeping.

She stood a minute on the threshold, not praying, exactly, but postponing the labor.

Then she tied her sleeves back and began.

She propped the door open with a geography primer missing its cover and took the broom from behind the stove. The first push raised a cloud so thick she had to turn her head and cough into her shoulder. The room smelled of chalk, old wool, damp plaster, and the sweet mineral smell that followed every bad dusting of the lungs. The map of the United States above the blackboard had gone the color of weak gravy. A tin cup lay on its side under the front row, half full of dirt. Someone's reader had swollen from the damp in the storm and dried crooked.

More desks were empty than filled before she even took attendance. That was what struck her hardest, more than the mess, more than the broken latch on the north window, more than the dead fly plastered into the inkwell tray. The room had already been going thin before Sunday. Now the storm had shoved its thumb into those absences and widened them for everyone to see.

She set the attendance register on her desk and opened it to the week's page. The names had been written in ink that was beginning to brown with age. Some of the children had sat under her eye for four years. Some had been born into this room before she came, growing through the same arithmetic tables and copybook sentences and the same November colds that made every lesson sound nasal. She had scolded them, admired them, sent notes home about them, and lately watched them vanish.

She made two columns on a scrap of paper: here, and gone.

By the time the first child came in, the second column had grown longer than the first.

Ruthie Havel arrived carrying her little brother's tin lunch pail because, as she once told Thea with complete seriousness, its latch had better manners than hers. She had tied one of her braids with a strip torn from a seed sack printed in blue letters, and there was a yellow butterscotch drop bulging her left cheek.

"Morning, Miss Bell," she said around the candy. "Ma says if the stove's no good I oughtn't stay."

"We'll see if it can be reasoned with."

"It never could before."

Vernon Pike came next, long in the knees, one suspender slipping as usual and a packet of beet seed still in his shirt pocket from whatever job he'd done before breakfast. He smelled faintly of axle grease and slept-in overalls.

"Thought for a minute the schoolhouse blew clear to Kansas," he said.

"Kansas would send it back."

"Not if they looked inside."

That got a brief grin out of Ruthie. More children followed in ones and twos: Lila Kopecek with one man's wool sock rolled below each knee because her mother did not waste cloth on matching pairs, Oran Bellamy buttoned crooked and blinking behind spectacles forever sliding down his nose, and little June Worley, who spoke only after she had rubbed two fingers over the rabbit's foot she kept in her coat pocket for luck. There were seven by the time the morning settled, seven children spread through a room built to hold twice that without crowding.

The desks in between stood open-mouthed. One still held a marble in the corner groove. Another had a pencil stub wrapped in chewing-gum foil. At the back sat the Jensen girls' slate, left clean and ready as if their mother meant to send them in late. The Jensens had gone west on Saturday. Thea knew because Mrs. Jensen had come for transfer papers with her jaw set and a baby's sock pinned to her coat to keep from losing it.

Thea did not call every absent name. She could not bear the echo of her own voice landing in so much vacant wood. She marked the register instead, quiet and exact, while the children beat dust from erasers outside and trailed it back in on their hems.

"We're not pretending this is an ordinary day," she said once they were seated. "If any of you hoped for normal copywork, you have my condolences."

Vernon raised his hand halfway. "Does that mean no sums?"

"No. It means you'll do sums in a dirty room, like pioneers."

"Pioneers didn't have to add fractions," he said.

"That's why so many of them died unhappy."

This time even Oran laughed, though laughing made him cough. Thea let the moment stay. The room needed more than order; it needed a reason to keep being a room where children belonged. She had them wipe their desk tops with damp rags, sort the spoiled primers from the usable ones, and make a list of what the storm had ruined so she could send it to a school board that was itself half packed for departure. Ruthie discovered two beans in the stove scuttle and held them up like treasure. Lila shook dust from the globe and said Nebraska had disappeared anyway, which Vernon informed her was an improvement.

There was more honesty in those small jokes than in most town meetings.

By noon the stove had finally drawn properly and the room had warmed enough to loosen the smell out of wet wool. The children ate at their desks. June Worley picked every raisin out of her bread pudding and lined them on the edge of the inkwell tray before eating them one by one, serious as communion. Vernon traded half an onion sandwich for Ruthie's candy and then declared himself cheated by arithmetic and by women in equal measure, which made Ruthie call him a calf and Lila say that calves had better table manners. Thea sat at her desk with a cup of coffee gone to varnish and watched them be children in spite of the dust. It moved her more than it should have.

During the meal she made the second list: families needing transfer papers, families with mail held at the post office, families simply missing from town since before the storm. She knew too much about people leaving. She knew who had cousins in Greeley, who had an uncle outside Fresno, who had sold harness for cash and who had only said they were going, which was a different thing. The county wanted names and destinations because counties always wanted paper to prove they had noticed suffering in the proper column.

Thea wrote them down because if she did not, no one else would do it cleanly.

When the last copybook had been stacked and the children let out early, they scattered home in a pale wind that lifted the road without becoming weather. Ruthie turned at the gate and called, "If it blows again, I'm sleeping under the desk. Tell Ma."

"I doubt your mother will accept school furniture as kin."

"Then don't tell her till after."

Vernon promised to bring a better stove poker in the morning because the current one "had no morals." After they were gone the room grew larger and sadder at once. Their voices had filled what the missing ones had left. Without them the schoolhouse went back to being an emptied crate.

Thea set the chairs straight and took a damp rag to the inside of the west windows so tomorrow's light might have some chance of entering on purpose. She had nearly finished the first pane when she leaned close to blow grit out of the corner where the putty had cracked.

The glass filmed white beneath her breath.

Then the room on the other side of it was not this afternoon's room.

It was colder. Coats stayed on. The stove in the corner was black and dead. Children's breath moved in pale bursts. The desks were fuller. She knew at once it was January, or late February, one of those thin bright days when the wind took the schoolyard and the children watched it through the windows because there was no sense sending them out.

She heard a voice, small and dutiful, reading from the roster not as attendance but as inventory.

"Soderlund."

Another voice answered, "Gone."

"Barr."

"Gone."

"Meyer."

"Colorado."

Then Ruthie, younger by a season and furious at having to take part in it, said, "Pretty soon you'll have more air than pupils, Miss Bell."

A few children laughed because children laughed when they were frightened and because laughter made a thing feel chosen. In the memory Thea saw her own hand resting flat on the desk, weddingless and chalk-smudged, holding the page steady while the names went on. She remembered the shame of it at once. She had told herself she was teaching them geography, record-keeping, the practical business of the world as it was. In truth she had made them speak their losses aloud so she could keep the room orderly for another hour.

The window cleared.

Thea stepped back hard enough to hit the sill with her hip. The rag dropped into the drift on the floor. For a moment she stood with one hand braced against the frame, hearing the ghost of those voices in the boards. The memory had not accused her exactly. It had done worse. It had returned her own little decency in its full shape and shown the mean edge on it.

Outside, somebody called to a team in the road. The ordinary sound came thin through the glass.

Thea picked up the rag and finished the pane because she did not know what else to do.

At the post office Mrs. Fenske had set the mail in three uneven stacks on the counter and was wearing men's overshoes over house slippers, the rubber buckles flapping every time she turned. She kept a blue pencil tucked over one ear and licked her thumb to separate envelopes in open defiance of the dust.

"You're late for your fortune," she said when Thea came in.

"I wasn't aware I had one."

"Long envelope from Lincoln says otherwise."

Mrs. Fenske handed it across with the faint air of someone giving out bad news and hoping to be contradicted. The paper was cream, better stock than anyone in Morrow used for common correspondence. The return address sat in the upper corner neat and city-sure. Lincoln. Public Library. The words were plain enough, but they tightened something low in Thea's body all the same.

"Came Saturday," Mrs. Fenske said. "Couldn't get the bag through yesterday after noon, for obvious reasons."

"Thank you."

"Well, open it. I've had to look at it all morning."

Mrs. Fenske's curiosity was not malicious. It was simply the town's natural weather. Thea slid a finger under the flap anyway, because refusing would have been louder than reading. Inside was a single typed sheet and a smaller card. She read the first lines standing at the counter while Mrs. Fenske pretended to sort circulars she had already sorted.

The library in Lincoln wished to interview Miss Theodora Bell for an assistant position in circulation and children's work. If she remained interested, could she present herself in the first week of June? References requested. Kindly reply by return post.

The card enclosed named a day and an hour.

For a foolish instant the room tipped around one clean thought: it was real.

The leaving people talked about at kitchen tables had always been smoke-talk, California rising off a mean supper like weather or scripture, some cousin's promise going yellow before it ever turned solid. This was a room in a city, shelves, work indoors, paper that stayed where you set it, and a salary from an institution that expected to exist next month. She had written in February, in a fit of prudence so cold it had embarrassed her even as she sealed the envelope. It had been one application, and that was all. She had told herself it was only common sense to ask. Now common sense had written back.

"Well?" Mrs. Fenske said.

Thea folded the letter once before answering. "An interview."

"For what?"

"Library work."

Mrs. Fenske gave a short impressed click with her tongue. "That sounds cleaner than children."

"Children are cleaner on principle if not in fact."

"You going?"

Thea slid the paper back into its envelope. "I don't know."

That, at least, was true enough to bear saying.

Mrs. Fenske leaned both palms on the counter. "If you ask me, anybody offered a chair in Lincoln ought to take it before the chair learns better."

"Did I ask you?"

"No, and I resent the neglect." The older woman softened. "Write back quick if you're of a mind. Mail's slower every week. Half the roads are more ditch than road now."

Thea nodded and tucked the envelope into her satchel between the attendance register and a stack of arithmetic slates. Even there she could feel its presence like a warm thing.

She did not go straight home. She stood for a minute under the post office awning looking down Main Street, where town life was being performed in gestures too tired for conviction. Hester was out front of the bakery knocking dust from a rug with more determination than strength, the front pane behind her gone milky enough that the cake case showed only as a pale block. A wagon stood outside the hardware store with its bed already loaded: chair frames, two trunks, a birdcage without a bird. On the steps of the bank a man in overalls took off his hat and beat it against his knee until a little weather made itself visible. Across from him the church bulletin board still carried Sunday's text under a film of dirt. The words had gone faint enough that "Remember" was the only one you could read from the street.

Thea thought of the library card in her satchel and felt disloyal all the way through. She did not feel guilty. Guilt was for acts already chosen. This feeling was worse. It was the burden of possibility.

At the Vale place the afternoon had flattened into a colorless gold. Dell was on the back step with a carburetor spread over newspaper, his fingers black to the knuckles and his tongue caught at the corner of his mouth in concentration he would have denied if accused of it. He had one screw lined up by size against the grain of the wood and a fifth missing, which meant he would be savage with the machine by supper.

"Did school survive?" he asked without looking up.

"In a legal sense."

"That's about the best one can say for most things."

Inside, Cora had the sleeves rolled properly now and was standing over the sink with her forearms wet, rinsing dust from potatoes no bigger than plums. A pan of onion had already hit the hot grease, and the smell of it filled the kitchen with something that could almost have passed for prosperity if you did not look too closely.

"How many?" she asked.

"Seven."

Cora nodded once, taking the count as if it were grain. "More than I expected."

"Less than last week."

"Everything's less than last week."

Thea set her satchel on the chair by the door. The envelope inside seemed suddenly loud. Cora went on slicing potatoes into the pan, each piece thin enough to persuade the eye there were plenty.

"Ruthie Havel informed me the stove never could be reasoned with," Thea said.

"She's right."

"Vernon Pike says the poker has no morals."

That pulled the corner of Cora's mouth. "He's inherited his father's gift for blaming tools."

Dell came in then with the missing screw triumphant between finger and thumb. "Found the bastard."

"Mind your mouth," Cora said automatically.

"Sorry."

"You aren't."

"No, ma'am."

Thea laughed before she could stop herself. Dell looked startled into laughing too, which made him instantly younger, almost tender-faced. For a few breaths the room stopped arranging itself around fear. There was only the pan hissing, the wind moving at the corners of the house, and three people glad of a joke because the joke had not asked anything from them.

Supper was fried potatoes with onion, eggs again, and the last of Hester's rye bread cut on the bias to make the slices look generous. Cora had stewed a handful of dried apples until they turned back into something like fruit. Dust had worked its way into the salt cellar and made it clump. Nobody mentioned it. You could either talk about dust all day or live in Nebraska; there was not time for both.

Dell ate standing at the counter for the first few minutes, as if sitting down might let tiredness catch him in the shoulders. He wanted badly not to look like a child who had been frightened yesterday. Thea knew the effort when she saw it. She had been teaching boys long enough to recognize the particular stiffness of male pride before it had earned the word male.

"June Worley still keeps that rabbit's foot in her pocket," Thea said.

"That useless old thing?" Dell dropped into his chair at last. "She had that in fifth reader."

"She says it worked. She's still alive."

"So is the rabbit?"

"Only in theory."

Cora set the apple dish down. "If you're going to torment schoolchildren at table, at least pass the bread first."

Dell obeyed with a grin that did not last but counted while it did. He asked after the roof. Thea told him the west window latch had gone and two primers were ruined beyond redemption. He said he could mend the latch tomorrow if Cora didn't need him in the south field. Cora said the south field could wait until morning. There was no money in broken glass, but there was less in leaving it broken.

That was how care went in the house: sidelong, practical, disguised as scheduling.

Thea nearly brought out the letter when Dell said, "School'll be down to three by planting if folks keep running."

Cora's fork stopped against the plate, not enough to clang but enough for Thea to hear the metal hesitate.

"Eat," Cora said.

"I'm eating."

"Then do more of that and less prophecy."

Dell muttered something into his bread that might have been apology and might have been argument starved before speech. The quiet after it was not hostile. It was only full. Thea felt the envelope in her satchel like a live coal.

If she told them now, Dell would go still and watch Cora. Cora would say the right thing first because she always did when anyone could hear. She would say it was a good opportunity, better work, that Thea ought to go if it was what she wanted. Then, later, perhaps in the dark, the truth would come with less grammar and more damage. Or worse, it would not come. Cora would take the news into herself and hold it there like every other hard thing until her body paid for it.

Thea kept her hand off the satchel. She could not bear either version while Cora was measuring out dried apples with the back of a spoon.

After supper Dell took the carburetor pieces to the shed and Cora heated two kettles of water on the stove. Dust in the hair could saw at the scalp if you let it go overnight. By the time the water was warm, the house had gone dim enough for the lamp on the table to matter.

"Sit," Cora said, setting the washbasin down on the chair by the window.

Thea did. Cora stood behind her with the old horn comb, the good one with only one tooth missing. The first pull through Thea's hair caught hard near the nape.

"Sorry," Cora said.

"It isn't your fault the weather's indecent."

"Plenty of things are the weather's fault."

Thea closed her eyes when the comb went through again. Cora had strong hands, not gentle by nature, but careful when care had somewhere to land. Damp fingers divided strands, found knots, worried them loose. Dust lifted from Thea's scalp in a smell like dry cloth and skin. Now and then Cora wiped the comb on the edge of the basin, leaving gray streaks in the water.

"You should've let me do yours first," Thea said.

"Mine can wait."

"Everything of yours can wait. That's the trouble."

For a moment Cora said nothing. Then the comb paused and moved again. "Don't start mending me at dusk, Thea. I haven't got the strength for it."

There it was, the shut door said plain at last. Refusal dropped between them like a bar laid across wood. Cora could talk about stove latches and school windows and the exact degree of rot in a fence post. She could not, this evening, go near whatever had cracked open in the front room after the storm.

"I wasn't trying to mend you," Thea said quietly.

"No." Cora drew the comb through to the ends. "I know."

The lamp made their shadows long on the wall. Through the open doorway Thea could hear Dell in the shed swearing softly at the machine, then laughing once to himself in disbelief at whatever he had done. A hen shuffled under the porch. The whole farm sounded abraded, tired to the grain.

When Cora finished, Thea touched the cleaner heaviness of her own hair and stood. "Your turn."

"There's no use."

"Sit down."

Cora looked as if she might refuse on principle. Then she did the sensible thing and yielded to exhaustion. She sat on the chair facing the table, her back to Thea, and loosened the blunt pins at her nape. Her hair came down ash-blond and straighter than Thea's, with dust lodged close to the scalp where sweat had held it. Kitchen-scissor ends brushed the collar of her dress. Thea saw the bend in her neck, the redness where the sun took her every year before gloves and hats could keep up, the small white scar under one ear from some farm accident Cora had once mentioned and never explained.

The first touch made Cora breathe in, not theatrically and not like surprise, but with the body's blunt admission that it had noticed.

Thea worked more slowly than Cora had, separating the strands with wet fingers before she used the comb. Dust came away in thin gray crescents. Cora's shoulders had been set hard all evening; under Thea's hands they lowered by degrees. The room smelled of lamp oil, onion still in the pan, and the faint clean soap Cora rationed like medicine.

"Ruthie said she'd sleep under the desk next storm if necessary," Thea murmured.

"Might be safer than here."

"Don't say that."

"Why not? It could be true."

Thea bent closer, partly for the work and partly because Dell's shed noises had gone quiet. "Because you don't get to hand me truth only when it cuts."

Cora's head turned a little, not enough to look back. "All right. The window showed Ezra in August's office with a pen in his hand. He signed."

Thea stopped with the comb halfway through Cora's hair.

Cora went on before pity could get in and spoil the truth. "He bent over the desk like he was sick, but he signed. Then he came home and swore on me and Dell that he hadn't. I watched his hand do it in my own front room this morning. That's what I've got."

"You don't know what August put in front of him."

"No. I don't." Cora's voice roughened. "I only know the lie was his mouth and the writing was his hand, and now both of them are in my house."

Thea set the comb on the table. She laid both hands on Cora's shoulders and felt the hard brace of muscle under the dress. "Cora."

Cora caught her wrist before Thea could bend any lower. "Don't kiss me now. It'll feel like mercy, and I'll hate us both by morning."

Thea went still. Cora's fingers were tight and cold despite the hot room.

"I wasn't offering mercy," Thea said.

"No." Cora let go of her wrist and faced the table again. "That's what scares me."

Thea picked up the comb and finished the braid in silence because there was nothing in her throat that could cross that distance cleanly.

When Dell came in smelling of machine oil and cold, Cora had her hair braided again and Thea was rinsing the comb in the basin. He looked from one to the other and then to the murky water.

"You got half the county out of there," he said.

"Only the half that settled on your mother," Thea said.

"Then there's plenty left for me."

"You can scrub your own head," Cora told him.

"Cruel woman."

"Hungry boy."

He took the heel of bread from the board and went out again before either of them could assign him another task. The back door slapped once, then settled.

Thea carried the basin to the porch to throw out the water. It hit the yard in a dark fan, turning dust briefly to mud before the earth took it. In the west the sky had the exhausted yellow of something bruised. Nothing in it suggested rain.

When she came back in, Cora was at the table with both palms flat beside the lamp, looking not at the flame but past it. The shut-down look had returned, though softer now from weariness and the human relief of being touched. Thea wanted her in all the ways that made life difficult: in bed, yes, but also in daylight, at any table she might ever sit at again, in any room that did not require keeping watch on the windows.

"You ought to sleep," Cora said without looking up.

"So ought you."

"I might, if everybody stops advising me."

Thea moved close enough that the hem of her dress brushed Cora's knee. That alone felt dangerous in a house this small, and because it felt dangerous it also felt true. Cora lifted one hand from the table and caught Thea's wrist for only a second. Her palm was rough, still damp from the steam.

"Thank you," Cora said.

It was for the hair, for supper, for not asking, for being there after yesterday. The phrase had to carry all of it because they did not have the room for particulars.

"Of course," Thea said, and hated herself a little because the words were honest and incomplete.

In her room she lit the smaller lamp and took the envelope out of the satchel. Dust had already found the windowsill again. It would find everything again. She unfolded the letter and read it slower this time, making herself look at the typed date on the card, the hour, the request for references, the certainty of a place that wrote as if walls and budgets and tomorrow were settled facts.

If she answered yes, there would be train times to learn, a dress to mend properly, fare to assemble, and a reason to give that would satisfy the town and not betray the house she slept in. If she answered no, she would do it knowing she had refused a door while it was still open.

Down the hall Dell's bed creaked once. In the kitchen Cora moved about with the last small sounds of the day, banking the stove, setting the bread box straight, making sure morning had at least been invited to arrive.

Thea took out a sheet of school stationery, turned it to the clean side, and dipped her pen. The nib scratched in the small room. Dear Madam. Yes, she would present herself in the first week of June. Yes, she was still interested. By the time she had signed her name, her fingertips were black with ink and shaking hard enough to blur the last stroke. She blotted the page with the corner of an old circular, folded it, and slid it into an envelope with a three-cent stamp she had been saving for county forms.

The reply looked too slight for what it had done.

She carried it back to the kitchen before she could lose her nerve. Cora had finished with the stove and was wiping the table where no crumbs remained.

"I need you to wake me before first light," Thea said. "I've got to go into town before school."

Cora's eyes dropped to the envelope in her hand and came back up. "School business?"

The lie came fast enough to burn. "Partly."

Cora stood with the rag bunched in one fist. Whatever she heard wrong in Thea's voice, she did not press it. "All right. I'll wake you."

That was not trust. It was something meaner to receive and kinder to give: room to keep a thing until morning.

Thea went back down the hall with the envelope in her hand. She set it under the lamp, where she would have to see it at dawn, and undressed in the dark so the paper would stay the brightest thing in the room.

For the first time since she had come to Morrow, the room did not feel borrowed. It felt like a room she had already begun to leave.


# Chapter 3

Six washed fruit jars stood on Hester Blum's back table beside a bent teaspoon and a square of muslin she had boiled the night before. If she was going to behave like a fool, she would at least do it cleanly.

The bakery still held its night smells. Yeast had gone slack in the sponge crock. Coffee grounds sat damp in the pot from yesterday. Flour lived in the boards. Wood smoke cooled in the iron throat of the range. Under all of it sat the storm, the same way it sat under everything now, as if Black Sunday had taken a room in every house in Morrow and did not mean to pay. Dust lay on the sill above the dough trough, in the groove of the cake case glass, on the enamel breadbox, and over the little mirror by the washstand in the curtained room where she slept. She had wiped once already since the storm and there was still enough grit left to write a lie in.

Yesterday, leaning across the cake case to see whether the front pane had gone permanently blind, she had breathed on the glass and seen Otto's hand there for one blink: not his face, not enough for comfort, only his hand setting two survey stakes against a wall. The hand had gone before she could even say his name. That could have been grief. She had been living with grief long enough to know it could dress itself in plain clothes and pass for ordinary sight if you let it. After Marta died, there had been mornings when every white apron in town looked like her back from across the street until the woman turned and proved to be someone else.

Cora's story about the Vale front-room window had not helped. Hester had believed her and not believed her in the same breath. A widow could tell the truth and still be misled by wanting. So could an old woman who had buried a husband and a daughter and kept on measuring yeast with the same hands. The dead did not become reliable because you missed them.

She lifted the teaspoon and drew it along the sill above the dough trough. Dust gathered with a dry, soft hiss. It was not like flour. Flour had weight, obedience, and purpose. This stuff skated. It clung to the spoon and then seemed to think better of it, fleeing into the cracks in the grain. Hester scraped it into a jar, covered the mouth with muslin while she worked the next spot, and tamped on the glass lid with the heel of her palm. The old zinc ring caught crooked. She loosened it, tried again, and felt the threads take.

She kept the jars separate because not keeping them separate would have been the act of a person already determined to believe. She took one from the cake case, one from the dough-room sill, one from the breadbox lid where the dust had settled in a gray fur over chipped white enamel, one from the bedroom mirror, and one from the back windowsill above the sink. The last came from the shelf over Otto's old weather notebooks, where nobody had touched anything in three days because she could not bear more cleaning that did not matter.

With the carpenter pencil she used for flour tallies, she marked paper scraps and slid them under each jar: case, trough, breadbox, mirror, sink, shelf. It looked ridiculous. It looked worse than ridiculous. It looked like the sort of private nonsense people forgave in a woman as long as they could tell themselves she had been softened by loss. Hester had worked too long and too publicly for that. Men who had eaten her rye and sweet rolls for twenty years would come in grinning and ask whether she had started bottling ghosts now. Women would lower their voices and say poor Mrs. Blum had not been right since Marta's lungs gave out. There was no appetite in town for an old baker who might be correct about anything larger than salt.

Her chest caught. She turned her head and coughed into her apron. The cough dragged from deep enough to make her eyes water. When she wiped her mouth, the spit on the cloth held a faint gray in it. It was dust again. It was always dust. It sat in the folds of her throat the way old bitterness sat in a family, never leaving cleanly, only thinning and returning.

She set the jars in a row on the sill above the range, where the first easy warmth from the waking stove could reach them without making the glass sweat. The sky outside the back window had barely begun to lift from black to soot-gray. No customer would come yet. No wagon would rattle the alley. This was the one honest hour left in town, the hour before people put on their voices.

Hester picked up the jar marked case and cupped it between both hands. The glass was cool enough to ache. She held it until the chill softened, then breathed across the curved side the way she had breathed across the cake-case pane. Her own fog bloomed on the outside and slid away.

Nothing happened.

She tried the jar from the breadbox. She warmed it longer, rubbed her thumb over the shoulder, and held it near the side of the stove. The dust inside lay still as sifted ash.

There was nothing there either.

The third jar, from the sink window, showed only her own bent face warped by glass and dawn. Hester let it down hard enough that the bottom thudded the table.

"Fine," she said to the room. "Then fine."

She was angry now, which helped. Anger cleared a path through shame. It was better to be cross than hopeful. Hope, at her age, was a thing with too many small teeth.

She reached for the jar marked shelf, more to finish the nonsense than out of faith. Dust lay inside it in a thin drift against one curve. She held it in both palms and let the heat of them build. The glass warmed. She tipped it once, letting the dust slide. Then she breathed across the side.

At first she thought it was the same nothing. The gray inside shifted, no more than that, and she nearly set it down. Then the dust gathered itself in a narrow dark line, not loose now but drawn, as if an invisible finger were dragging through wet ground. Another line crossed it. A tabletop formed around those strokes. The curve of a shoulder followed, then a cuff with flour rubbed into the seam. It was Otto.

He stood bent over a board laid across two chairs, the old way he did when he worked on maps at home because the bakery tables were always needed for dough. The jar gave her his good side first: the left cheek rough with missed whiskers, the little dent near the ear where a horse had kicked dirt at him when he was twenty and handsome enough to think it would never matter. His wedding ring, flattened on one edge from years of survey chain and kneading troughs, clicked against the board as he tapped an inked oval. The nail of his first finger was split to the quick, exactly as it had been all that spring he kept driving stakes into hard ground and refusing gloves.

"There," he said, and even in the jar his voice came to her dulled, as if through a wall. "That black stays wet."

His finger pressed again. The oval darkened. It was not ink now but ground. She saw, with the sick clarity that belonged to dreams and evidence and nothing in between, a patch of meadow gone almost blue with held water while the land around it lay the color of dry bread crust. A fringe of reed grass moved. Then Otto's hand swept over the place, measuring distance in his old practical way, and the image thinned. Dust ran back into dust. The jar showed only gray smears on curved glass.

Hester did not move for a moment. Her hands had gone so tight around the jar that the rings of the lid had printed themselves into the heel of her palm.

She had not thought about the split in Otto's fingernail in years. She had forgotten the exact shape of the dent by his ear. More than that, she had forgotten which corner of the basin map he used to tap when he argued. Grief could give a woman a face. It could give her a sentence if she had heard it often enough. But it could not, not honestly, return a particular fingernail and a forgotten patch of ink she had not held in her mind since before the bank and the men from town called the basin useless water.

She set the jar down carefully this time and went to the drawer under the counter where she kept salves, seed envelopes, and what remained of the papers she had refused to burn. The map lay folded in waxed paper under Otto's weather notebook from 1931 and a bundle of Marta's relief-sewing coupons. Hester unwrapped it with hands that were less steady than she cared for. The paper had gone thin at the creases. She opened it on the table and smoothed the corners flat.

Her breath hitched hard enough to threaten another cough. Otto's cramped hand looked plain and stubborn as ever. Field lines crossed the page. Notes about runoff crowded the margins. A sketched ditch showed where later men had wanted it cut cleaner. South of the Vale acreage he had made a dark, thumb-sized oval and written beside it, "holds wet longest." The mark sat in the same crooked place the jar had shown him touching.

The kitchen pinched inward around her. The stove ticked. Somewhere in the alley a board banged once and settled. Hester looked from the map to the jar and back again.

"All right," she said, very quiet.

She left the map spread open on the table. Widow's vapor did not send a woman digging through waxed paper before dawn. Whatever had ridden glass and enamel after the storm belonged with ledgers and field notes: ugly fact, arriving without manners.

She was still standing there with the map open when the back latch lifted.

Dell Vale came in sideways, as if even the door ought not to know he was using it. He had no hat on. His hair stuck up pale with sleep and dust, and the cracked toes of his boots had been rubbed with stove blacking in an effort to make them look less broken. It worked only in dim light, but seventeen was old enough to try. He shut the door behind him with the care of someone accustomed to old hinges, then saw the jars on the sill and the map on the table and stopped.

"You weren't open," he said.

"And yet you're here."

He shrugged without answering that. The movement made him look younger than his jaw wanted to. His left cuff hung open where the button had gone missing. He had machine grease at the base of one thumbnail and a clean stripe on his trouser leg where he had wiped his hand and immediately regretted it. Hester liked him better for the regret. Boys who still regretted making a mess had not yet turned into the men who left all of it to women.

"I can come back."

"If you were going to come back, you'd have used the front door like a Christian." Hester folded the map once, not enough to hide it entirely. "Shut that draft out proper and sit down."

He obeyed before deciding whether obedience suited him. That too was seventeen. He lowered himself onto the flour bin by the wall, knees up, hands loose between them, trying for a roughness he had not finished growing into. His eyes kept flicking to the jars.

Hester poured the last inch of yesterday's coffee into a cup and set it on the table. She cut the heel from a loaf of molasses bread, spread it with the hard end of apple butter, and slid that over too. Dell took the bread first. Hunger had better manners than pride some mornings.

"Ma said you saw one," he said after a bite. Apple butter had darkened the corner of his mouth; he wiped it away with the back of his wrist before the sentence was done. "On your glass."

"Your mother shouldn't have told you every strange thing in her line of sight."

"She didn't tell me every strange thing. She told me enough to stop me thinking she was worn out and seeing tricks." He chewed once more and looked at the jars instead of at Hester. "Then I saw you carrying those in from the wash yard yesterday."

"So now you've come to save me from myself?"

"No." He swallowed. "I've come because if there's something to it, I need to know what sort."

The cup sat untouched by his hand. That was how she knew it mattered. Dell could drink coffee hot enough to raise blisters when he was pretending he had no nerves.

Hester sat opposite him and put both palms flat on the table. "You need to be more exact than that."

He gave her the wary side of his face, the one he used with men offering advice and girls offering pity. "Do I."

"Yes."

"Fine." He drew breath, let it go, and began again with the irritation of someone forced to show his work. "If that thing on our window was real, then either he signed something or he didn't. Either August has been making up a story for months, or he hasn't. Either my father got trapped or he lied to us. Pick one. I'd like to stand on something that isn't moving."

Hester let the words settle. The boy had come for certainty, but not the kind he could boast about. She heard the shape of another fear under the first one: not only that Ezra had lied, but that Cora had built her next year of life on whatever version of Ezra let her keep breathing. A son could want the truth and still dread being the one who carried it into his mother's kitchen.

"And you thought I'd have one of your choices lined up for you in a jar."

He looked at the map now. "Do you?"

"No. I've got worse than that."

His mouth tightened. "What does that mean?"

Hester reached for the map and unfolded enough to show the dark oval. "It means I saw Otto just now. Not enough to make a religion out of him. Enough." She tapped the mark. "He touched this place."

Dell leaned in despite himself. He had Ezra's quick, practical hands, though not Ezra's way of pretending they were calmer than they were. "What's that."

"Saint's Basin."

"South low ground?"

"Before your time, it was more than low ground." She watched him take that in. "Otto used to say the wet there held the rest of the fields together longer than folks understood. Then men decided standing water was wasted acreage and mosquito trouble and something to tame. They cut and tiled and called it improvement."

Dell followed the sketched lines with his eyes. "That's on our side of the road."

"Quarter mile south of your west field, near enough."

"I thought Saint's Basin was farther." He frowned. "Pa said it used to be over by the Miller place."

"Your father said a number of things in the last year that were easier to live with than they were to prove."

The sentence landed harder than she intended. Dell sat back. The bin creaked under him.

"So it's true, then," he said. "All of it."

"Don't start lying to yourself in the other direction. One map mark and one jar don't make all of it."

He almost smiled, but the expression failed halfway. "You keep saying jar like that helps."

"Would you prefer an omen. A sign. A warning. A miracle."
