"No." He rubbed his thumb over the rim of the coffee cup at last. "I don't need church. I need to know what the hell he did."

Because he had said "he" and not "August" or "Pa," Hester stood and took down another jar.

"You may not get what you came wanting," she said.

"I know."

"Do you."

This time he looked at her square. The softness had gone out of him in a way that made her hate the whole season. "I know enough not to ask Ma first."

That, at least, was plain.

She brought the jar marked mirror to the table. If there was anything decent in the world, it would stay dead and gray. Hester did not like the part of herself that had begun choosing jars by intuition, but there it was, operating beneath reason like yeast beneath dough. She set the jar between them and held it steady while the boy leaned in.

"Touch it," she said.

"Why."

"Because warm hands do as much as breath sometimes."

"Sometimes."

"If I knew more than that, I'd be making money off it."

The corner of his mouth twitched. That helped. She would take any sign he had not turned to rawhide in the last month.

Dell laid both hands around the jar. His fingers were long and still awkwardly large for the rest of him. They were mechanic's fingers, or musician's if he had been born in another county to people with spare time. The gray dust inside did nothing. He looked at Hester as if to ask whether she was mocking him.

"Keep hold," she said.

She bent and breathed across the curved glass. Their fog mingled briefly on the outside. Inside, the dust shifted. Dell's hands tightened so quickly the lids gave a small metallic creak.

The gray did not draw a map this time. It rose in a sheet, then folded itself inward, making depth where there had been none. A kitchen came up, close and narrow, with blue-checked oilcloth on a table and a wall clock whose pendulum stuck at one side before it carried through. It was the Vale kitchen. There was no mistaking it. Hester knew the crack in the plaster by the pantry door and the nick in the table edge from when Cora had dropped the cream separator handle two winters back.

Ezra stood at the table in his shirtsleeves. He was thinner than Hester remembered him from the year before his death, cheeks sharpened, collarbone working under skin when he swallowed. His hair had gone flat from sweat and one side needed cutting. On the back of his right thumb the pale crescent scar from splitting kindling showed when he lifted a folded paper. He smoothed the fold once with the heel of his hand, then again, then a third time, exactly as he used to do with seed bills, school notices, church programs, anything he could not leave imperfectly square.

Outside the window, Cora's shadow crossed the muslin curtain in a blur of skirt and clothespins. Ezra heard it before Hester did. He jerked his head toward the door, snatched open the middle drawer of the table, and shoved the paper under the warped oilcloth lining instead of inside the drawer itself. The drawer stuck on its nail, as it always did. He hit it shut with the heel of his palm, wiped his hand on his trousers, and turned toward the stove with such practiced speed it made Hester's stomach go cold.

By then the shadow had passed the window. The image blurred. Dust fell. The kitchen flattened back into glass.

Dell worried the lid with both thumbs until the tin gave a dry little scream. He put a reflexive test-turn on it, the same motion he'd use on a stubborn bolt, then stopped with the glass biting into the meat of his palms. The color thinned out of him in uneven bands, leaving his freckles and the dust at his hairline too sharp.

"That's ours," he said.

Hester did not answer. There was no useful answer to that.

"That drawer catches on the nail." His voice had thinned. "I fixed it with him. It still caught."

He took one hand off the jar and rubbed it hard down his mouth, as if he could erase the sight before it reached the back of his head. Hester saw the effort it cost him not to break the cup or throw the whole thing against the wall. He had his father's hands and his mother's temper and none of the years required to manage either.

"Could've been anything," he said.

"Yes."

"Could've been seed receipts."

"Yes."

"Could've been nothing that mattered."

"No." Hester kept her voice level. "Don't turn stupid now."

He flinched, which was not the same as offense. It looked more like relief that someone had stopped him before he got farther into the lie.

"I wasn't," he said, and then, because the sentence did not hold, he tried again. "I was trying to think."

"Think better."

He looked at the dead jar again. "He hid it from her."

"He did."

"Why."

"Because paper with figures on it can shame a man more efficiently than a beating." Hester heard Otto in the sentence and did not apologize for that. "Because delay feels like mercy while you're doing it. Because he might have meant to tell her after supper, or after harvest, or after one more decent week, and men are always surprised to find that time is not sitting around waiting on them."

Dell gave a short, ugly laugh with no humor in it. "That sounds like men according to women."

"Usually the reliable source."

That got a breath out of him, not quite a laugh. He let it go and bent forward, forearms on knees, hands loose now between them. The open cuff on his left wrist had folded back, showing a burn scar no bigger than a dime where he had once grabbed a muffler too soon after shutting off the truck. Hester remembered bandaging it while he insisted it was nothing and winced only when she turned away.

"If I tell Ma that," he said, "and it's not the bank paper, then I've just put a hole in him for nothing."

"If you tell her nothing and go on waiting for weather to do your work, that's a choice too."

"I know that."

"Do you."

He looked up, irritated now because irritation was easier than hurt. "Would you stop asking that."

"When you stop being seventeen."

He snorted. Then his face changed again, sudden and miserable. "I don't want August telling it first."

Dell's fingers dug into his knees until the knuckles went chalky. August's version was what scared him: that smooth bank voice getting to Cora first, shaping Ezra before Dell could. Hester understood that fear better than she understood half the prayers she had heard in church.

She reached for the map.

"Then we go where Otto pointed."

Dell's head came up. "What."

"Saint's Basin. Not to fix your father. Not to condemn him either. To see whether the land still carries the mark of what was done to it."

"Today?"

"I've got bread to make and you've got chores unless Cora has died in the last ten minutes."

"She hasn't."

"Then not this minute." Hester spread the paper flat between them. "But soon."

He slid off the flour bin and came to the table. Up close, he smelled of sweat gone cold, machine oil, and the cheap lye soap Cora bought when money pinched hard. He bent over the map the way boys bent over engines, all defiance gone in the face of a thing that might answer if properly handled.

"That's the road here," he said after a moment, finger hovering rather than touching. "And that's the windbreak that used to be thicker before the blight took the middle trees. This bend..." He squinted. "If that's right, then the basin edge would run behind our south wheat, not straight across. It'd cut catty-corner."

"Otto said the ground remembered curves better than men did."

"Ground doesn't have to prove itself to anybody." Dell traced the shape in air again. "There's a place down there where the wheat always comes up meaner on the rise and then goes greener for a strip after a wet spring. Not green exactly now. Nothing's green now. But different. The binder used to bog there when I was nine."

"Your father noticed?"

He took his time answering. "He cussed it enough."

Her fingertip came down on the dark oval. Ezra had cursed that ground often enough to know where it lay. Hester watched the boy stand in the space between what he had just seen and what he still wanted to believe. He was doing it honestly now, which was harder to watch than simple denial would have been.

"If Otto was right," she said, "they didn't just dry out a nuisance patch. They cut the wet out of the whole quarter. That changes the wind, the hold, the yield. It changes everything after. Men in town called it progress because the ground looked flatter and their boots stayed cleaner." She tapped the dark oval. "If Ezra had paper about this, then his shame and your mother's debt and August's piety have all been sleeping in the same bed."

Dell's eyes flicked to hers. "You think Pa knew."

"I think he knew enough to hide a paper from his wife."

"That's not the same."

"No. It isn't. Which is why we look."

He put both hands on the table. The boards complained. "Ma can't know yet."

Hester's first answer rose hot: that Cora was not a child to be managed, that secrecy had already done enough work in this town, that she had no wish to join another line of people keeping a woman outside her own life. But Dell was not asking to keep Cora ignorant forever. He was asking for a day, maybe two, to bring her something firmer than a vision in a jar. The request was wrong in the ordinary way and right in the way seasons sometimes forced on people.

"She'll know if she asks me direct," Hester said.

"All right."

"Don't make me a liar for the sake of your stomach."

"I won't."

"You say that now."

He held her gaze. His face had gone older while they talked, not by much, only by the small amount a bad fact could age a person in one kitchen. "If we find nothing, I shut up and put the drawer back the way it is. If we find something, she gets the truth from us before it comes out of town mouths. That's what I'm asking."

Hester looked at the jars cooling on the sill. They sat gray in glass, nothing and not nothing. Otto's hand was gone from his jar. Ezra had gone from his. The world had already spent those witnesses. Waiting for the next storm meant waiting for chance, and chance had too often favored men with papers and dry collars.

Outside, the eastern light strengthened enough to show the alley ruts through the window. A wagon rolled somewhere on Main Street. In another fifteen minutes the first customer would come wanting coffee, bread, gossip, or credit. The town would put its hands all over the day and smear it.

Hester folded the map along its old creases and slid it back into the waxed paper. She chose a smaller sack for it instead of the drawer, one she could tuck under her apron if need be.

"Tomorrow at first light," she said. "You do the horse and chicken work before your mother wakes enough to count your footsteps. Then you come by the alley. Bring a spade if you can manage it without a speech from Cora."

Dell nodded once.

"And Dell."

"What."

"Eat the rest of that bread. Investigations don't keep a boy upright."

He almost grinned then, and because he did not quite manage it, the expression hurt her more than if he had broken down. He took another bite obediently. Apple butter shone dark on the crust.

"If this turns out to be nothing," he said through the bread, "you'll say so."

"I always do."

"Even if it makes you look foolish."

Hester screwed the lid tighter on the dead jar between them. "Especially then."

He swallowed and set down the last of the heel. "All right."

The second "all right" gave tomorrow a shape: a spade, the south road, the hour before Cora counted footsteps.

When he left by the back door a minute later, he carried his shoulders differently, as if the weight had shifted but not lightened. Hester watched through the flour-fogged pane while he cut across the alley toward the side street, head down against a wind too small to deserve the name and still full of grit enough to sting.

Then she turned back to her table, to the row of jars that had stopped being foolish in the dark and the dough that still had to be punched down before breakfast trade. The practical work remained. That was almost a comfort.

She set Otto's map beside the sponge crock where she could reach it, rolled up her sleeves, and drove both fists into the risen dough. It gave way warm and alive under her knuckles, breathing out sour sweetness and trapped air. Outside, the light climbed over Morrow's boarded storefronts and thin trees and all the ground men had called improved until it began to die. Hester was done waiting for weather to speak first. In the morning she and Dell would go south to Saint's Basin and make the land answer in daylight, before paper had another chance to outrun ground.


# Chapter 4

August let himself into Farmers State Bank before seven, while the light over Main Street was still the color of old muslin and the dust had not yet decided whether it meant to rise again. The key dragged in the lock. Dust did that too, found the cut of the wards and sat there like spite. He shouldered the door, felt packed grit grind under the threshold, and stood a moment in the cool dim smell of paper, coin metal, dried ink, and sweat long worked into the counter rail. Bank air had once steadied him. Numbers did not plead. They either added or refused.

He crossed to the rear office, set down his hat, and opened the safe. The inner hinges gave their small morning complaint. He brought out the till drawer, the wrapped coins, the note packets banded with paper gone soft at the edges, and laid them in order on the desk under the green-shaded lamp. Dust had crept in everywhere after Black Sunday, not in dramatic drifts now but in the meaner way, inside the seams of the cash drawer, along the teeth of the adding rack, inside the blotter where his wrist rested. For three weeks the work of the bank had doubled. First you did the work itself. Then you did the work of scraping weather out of it.

The front room came into shape as the light improved: teller cage with its brass worn pale by hands, two customer stools rubbed slick at the rung, the water cooler with a chipped blue line round the shoulder, the spittoon beside the stove, the rack of deposit slips that schoolchildren used for scratch paper once they were spoiled by a wrong figure. The bank was not a stage set for anyone's wickedness. It was where Mrs. Gansey brought the money from egg sales rolled in old seed envelopes, where burial drafts were cashed, where the school board's thin account sat beside wheat notes already beginning to smell like lies. If the place failed, the town would not gain virtue. It would lose another door.

He finished his count, entered the opening cash in the daybook, and turned toward the narrow mirror hung beside the hat pegs. He meant only to inspect the line of dust at his collar. Instead he breathed, and the glass went milky under that breath.

The silver darkened. Another room moved inside the room.

His father stood at the loan table with Otto Blum's survey paper spread under one big hand. He was younger than August had allowed him to be in memory, shoulders still broad, mouth still willing to harden into certainty before thought had run its course. Otto had come in from the road with a measuring stake under his arm and mud dried white around the welt of one boot. He never could set a tool down while he argued. The old basin lay on the paper in blue wash, south of the Vale tract, a shallow thumbprint of water and grass.

"That wet holds the spring," Otto said. The memory gave only the one line cleanly, but August heard the rest in the shape of the man's jaw. "You cut tile through there and the ground won't keep for you."

His father flattened the blue wash with two fingers, bent on pressing water out of the county by hand. "We're not keeping a meadow for geese."

Then younger August was there too, not speaking, only standing by the safe with a ledger in both hands, watching while the bargain tipped. The indecency lay there. Not in the father who had been dead nine years and could no longer answer. In himself, already old enough to know that a man could be wrong in a room full of paperwork and still have every page come out in his favor.

The mirror cleared at the edges. His own face came back through his father's, heavy now, carefully shaved, furrows at mouth and brow carrying a kind of authority that felt each month more like residue.

He took the ledger ruler from the desk and struck the glass once, hard enough to split it from upper left to lower right. The sound was flat and mean in the empty bank. He struck again. The cheap backing gave way in bright scales. For a second he saw himself repeated in a spray of broken Augusts. Every shard gave the old man inside the frame credit for the damage. Then he wrapped the pieces in yesterday's crop reports, tied the bundle with string, and set it by the stove for disposal.

When Miss Havel came in ten minutes later, she looked at the paper parcel and then at the vacant nail on the wall. She had a habit of touching the split skin beside her right thumbnail whenever she was deciding whether a question was safe. Dust and banknotes opened the crack afresh every day; she kept a blue cold-cream tin in the bottom drawer and fed that thumb like a separate dependent.

"Did something fall?" she asked.

"The mirror gave way."

She glanced once more at the parcel. "Everything is giving way."

"Not everything," he said.

Miss Havel took off her hat, a brown felt one with last year's ribbon turned to hide the fade, and hung it in the back. She moved through the front room setting it to rights with the quick economy of a woman who had been doing two jobs since Mr. Enright went to Colorado and never returned. She opened the cage, shook dust from the rug onto the step, checked the stamp pad, and set the small vase she insisted on keeping by the public pen chain back in the center of the sill. There were no flowers in it, only two stems of fennel gone gray-green from the road edge, but she said the place looked less like a coffin parlor that way.

By eight-thirty the bank had begun its daily performance of normal life. Mrs. Gansey came in with three dollars and change wrapped in wax paper from the chicken yard. She removed her dead husband's spectacles from her purse before she counted the bills, though she had not needed them for months; the gesture was simply part of how she conducted transactions now, and his dead approval still had to be fetched before money crossed a counter. August credited her account, asked after her sister's cough, and did not let his eye travel to the small withdrawal slip already waiting in her passbook for next week.

After her came Rolf Sutter from east of town, hat in both hands, claiming the axle on his binder had set him back worse than expected. Rolf had one ear that reddened whenever he lied. It reddened now. August knew the man had spent part of his last cream check on a train fare to see a girl in McCook and did not begrudge him the impulse. He merely could not finance it.

"Saturday," August said, keeping his voice level enough that Miss Havel at the cage would hear calm and not concession. "I can carry the interest until Saturday, no later."

Rolf's red ear deepened. "I appreciate that."

"Appreciation does not enter the ledger. Saturday does."

Rolf gave a thin smile at that, because people often mistook August's exactness for wit when it was only exactness, signed the renewal slip, and went out into the white street carrying his hat by the brim like something breakable. August watched him through the bank window until he turned the corner. Half the work lay in that too: keeping men from lingering in the doorway with defeated faces, because one defeated face invited a second and a second could turn into a line.

At ten the county examiner arrived from McCook in a dust-whitened Buick. LeRoy Bender came in brushing his sleeves with both hands, though there was no point in it. Dust had breaded him for frying. He was a narrow man with pale lashes and a necktie singed near the knot where cigar ash had eaten it weeks ago. Each time August saw him he was carrying the same round peppermint tin covered in yellow cloth by some careful wife or sister. He set it on the counter before he even removed his gloves.

"Road is filthy," Bender said.

"That is not new."

"No. It's only worsening, which is the distinction that matters."

Miss Havel brought coffee to the back office without being asked. Bender declined sugar, took a peppermint from the tin instead, and let it soften against his cheek while August laid out the current-note register, the delinquent file, and the collateral abstracts. Through the half-open office door the ordinary sounds of the bank went on: cage drawer sliding, pen chain rattling, somebody coughing up dust in the front room and then apologizing for weather, treating it like bad manners.

Bender flipped pages with the backs of his fingers, unwilling to wet them with his tongue in a season when every surface tasted of road. "You have too much paper in extension."

"You would say that in any farm county after a storm like April."

"I said it in Holdrege on Monday and in Indianola on Tuesday. That doesn't make it false here." Bender turned another page, made a small mark in pencil. "State men want to know what is live and what is wishful. By the time your federal land fellow starts sorting farms, anything you've carried out of sentiment will read as concealment."

August sat very still. "Resettlement is not my business."

"No. Paper is. Give me durations and collateral."

August looked at the open register, at figures entered in his own hand across years when weather had seemed severe but still answerable. Drought had first looked like a temporary insult, then like bad luck, then like a test, and only lately had it begun to show itself as a condition capable of outlasting character. He hated the federal language around it because it made collapse sound administrative. Yet he hated more the possibility that some man from outside would come in, read his books, and find not hardship but indulgence.

"These are seasonal accommodations," he said. "Not charity."

Bender sucked the peppermint to one side of his mouth. "Then show me where the season ends."

August did. They moved tract by tract, name by name. Sorensen, carried thirty days. Blakely, doubtful but not yet for sale. Hutter, likely gone west before first payment. The work had the false comfort of dissection. So long as he said the names in order and Bender penciled his marks beside them, the county had edges. It could be classified. It could be borne.

Then Bender tapped the Vale file.

"This one."

August reached for the glass-front cabinet where he kept the active farm notes. Dust filmed the wavering pane. He had meant to have Miss Havel wash it before the examiner came. He had meant many things. He leaned close, turned the key, breathed on the latch without thinking, and the dust woke again.

Ezra Vale stood in the cabinet glass plain enough to have stepped back in from the hall. Sunday coat, elbows gone shiny, hat brim crushed in both hands. He had dirt ground into the seam at one knee and the look so many men had worn in that office in the last years, not yet beaten, merely prepared to bargain away one more necessary thing. Younger August took the deed from him and laid it flat. The memory jumped, because shame preferred the after. Ezra was gone. The office door was closing. August's own hand, ten years younger and steadier than the one on the cabinet key now, drew the file back and uncapped the pen. His wrist hid the lower corner of the page. Where the date line should have looked handled and tired, one figure flashed up too clean. Then the whole image smeared. Even the dust refused to show more.

A fine scratching came off the glass, nib or nail or memory refusing to say which, and lodged inside his teeth.

"Poeschel?"

Bender's voice reached him from two rooms away, though the man sat less than six feet behind. August opened the cabinet, took out the file, and closed the pane before the memory could offer him his own justification.

"Here it is."

He laid the file on the desk. Bender thumbed through the renewal notes, the interest entries, the acreage description, the lien language. His brow did not alter, but he grew slower, and slowness from an examiner was worse than anger.

"This has been carried how long past the first default?"

"The husband died."

"The note did not."

August heard in his own answer the beginning of a protest he should not make. "Mrs. Vale has kept interest from going entirely stale. The farm still has standing wheat, poor as it is. There are hens, some sewing income, a boarder with wages."

Bender looked up over the file. "You're offering me household detail now."

"I am offering you facts."

"Then hear mine. If one file in this room looks indulged past its security, the rest of your books start to look sentimental. If one page reads as amended after execution, the state will not stop at one page."

He said it without relish. That made it land harder. August knew Bender had spent the past month driving from one failing little bank to the next, carrying bad news in figures because figures were the one form of bad news men still pretended to respect. The singed tie, the peppermint, the fine gray dust along the ridges of his ears, none of it belonged to a triumphant man.

"You imagine I enjoy carrying these notes," August said.

"I don't imagine anything of the sort. I imagine you want this place to stay open. So decide whether you're running a bank or preserving your own opinion of yourself."

Miss Havel tapped on the doorframe. "Mr. Knapp is here."

Walter Knapp entered with a hat that had already been brushed three times since he crossed the street and would be brushed three more before he left. He was neat in the way of men whose work required travel but not labor, trim through the middle, collar still unfrayed, shoes expensive enough that Nebraska road dust looked temporary on them. Yet there was a tiredness at the corners of his mouth that kept him from seeming merely ornamental. He apologized to Miss Havel for carrying grit in, then brushed the hat again while saying it.

"Mr. Poeschel. Mr. Bender." He inclined his head toward the file on the desk. "I can start three tracts this week. Clean title, severe arrears, obvious unsuitability for another season. Those should not wait."

August did not invite him to sit. "No land improves under federal adjectives."

Knapp accepted that without offense. "No, but schedules do not improve by waiting."

Bender made a small sound that might have been a swallowed laugh or a cough. August left it unanswered.

Knapp held out his hand for the file. August did not immediately yield it. That hesitation was brief, but everybody in the room felt it. Miss Havel, still in the doorway, lowered her eyes to the coffee tray.

"I'm not here to dispossess anyone personally," Knapp said, more quietly. "I'm here to rank what Washington will touch first. If you do not tell me which notes can be called cleanly, the paper that looks worst will rise by default."

August let go of the file.

Knapp scanned the first page, then the second. He did not linger on Ezra's name. He lingered on acreage, security, carrying costs, and the margin notes August himself had made across the winter. When he spoke again his tone remained maddeningly even.

"The Vale place is west of town."

"Yes."

"Dry quarter, household short of cash, title burdened."

"Still occupied."

"And high on tomorrow's list if this extension cannot be defended by morning."

August felt heat move under his collar despite the cool office. "That is a sentence easier said from Lincoln than from a county seat."

"I came from Lincoln this morning, not heaven." Knapp turned the page back. "By ten tomorrow I need a list of which notes can be called cleanly and which will resist. Not morally. Legally. If you do not rank them, I will."

Scrutiny had tipped into triage: a neat deadline dropped onto years of local postponement. He wanted to tell Knapp that a bank president in a dying town did not traffic in hope. He trafficked in delay, which was a rougher and sometimes more useful commodity. But Bender was in the room, and Miss Havel at the door, and the file with its poisoned page lay open between them, and somewhere beyond the front counter Mrs. Dole was asking whether her money order had cleared because her boy in Denver had not written back.

Knapp closed the file and returned it.

"There is usually a connection between the two," August said.

"In my experience there's usually a lag."

When Knapp had gone, brushing his hat yet again as he crossed the front room, August remained standing. Outside the office Mrs. Dole said, too loudly now, "If the line west can mislay a money order, it can mislay other things." Miss Havel answered at once, calm as starch, but the cage drawer slammed harder than usual. Bender put the peppermint tin back into his pocket.

"Reserve figures after dinner," he said.

He gathered his gloves and left August alone with the Vale file on the desk.

The bank did not soften when the room cleared: the dry slam of the cage drawer, somebody clearing his throat, then coughing until the cough took over the whole place and would not be polite. August opened the file again. Ezra's signature sat there with its old strained force, letters pushed too hard at the downstrokes, pressure trying to pass for honesty. Beneath it the date line looked wrong in a way that would not hold still, too neat in one stroke, too rough in the paper around it, the kind of blemish a tired eye might miss and a suspicious one might never stop seeing.

He rose, took a screwdriver from the supply drawer, went to the cabinet, and loosened the retaining clips that held the wavering glass in its frame. The pane came free warm from the office. For a moment he saw only his own hand through it, large and careful. Then another hand threatened underneath, sleeve cuff and pen hovering where the page blurred out of sight.

He set the pane over the wastebasket and pressed down until it broke. Not loudly. Glass under paperwork gave a soft collapsing music, the sound of brittle things giving way under teeth.

Miss Havel looked in. "Do you need me?"

"No."

"Was that the cabinet?"

"The pane was cracked already."

She absorbed the lie with the blankness of long employment. Then, because she was not merely a witness to male ruin, she said, "If you mean to shorten any of those farm notes, do it before Saturday. Folks talk more on Saturdays."

He looked at her properly then. "You have an opinion."

"I've got an upstairs room over my aunt's store and rent due on the twelfth. Opinions come attached."

That shut him up more effectively than reproach would have. He took out the Vale note, folded a clean sheet into the typewriter, then changed his mind and pulled it free. He did not want Miss Havel retyping the language when the platen misstruck. He wanted the wording in his own hand. He wrote that the present extension would terminate on the first of June, that interest must be brought current by then, that failure would oblige the bank to proceed under lien. He used oblige because required sounded theatrical and force sounded vulgar. He signed it, blotted it, and hated how ordinary the page looked.

At half past four he told Miss Havel he would be out for an hour and took the Ford west toward the Vale place.

Town ended quickly now. One block of feed store and barber pole, another of the bakery and the schoolhouse, then the road loosening into ruts between fields that had once carried promise well enough to invite vanity. The wheat on the Vale quarter stood thin and uneven, a meager green gone gray where the dust had scoured it. Fence posts wore drifts at their windward sides like old men in bad collars. At the farmyard a chicken flapped off the porch rail and landed crooked, one wing missing feathers where some dog or child had once got hold of it and never finished the job.

Cora Vale was at the pump with her sleeves rolled and a dishpan at her feet. She had tied a flour sack over her hair against the grit, but ash-blond strands had worked loose at the temples. Her hands were in the water, red over the knuckles and cracked at the webbing, scrubbing egg baskets with more force than the task required. She looked up when she heard the car, shaded her eyes with a wet wrist, and did not smile.

"Mr. Poeschel."

He stepped out with the folded notice in his pocket and his hat still on. The discourtesy was deliberate; removing it would have felt like soliciting softness. "Mrs. Vale."

She glanced toward the road behind him, checking whether he had brought company. "If this is about the taxes, I told your clerk I'd be in town Tuesday."

"It's about the note."

The pump handle creaked once in the wind and settled. Somewhere behind the house a screen door struck and rebounded. He thought, without looking, that the schoolteacher might be inside. He kept his attention on Cora.

"I've revised the extension."

"Into what."

"The note will stand due on the first of June. Ten days."

She stared at him a second without comprehension, not because the words were difficult but because they were indecently plain. Then she dried one hand on her apron and held it out. He gave her the folded paper. She opened it with care that almost mocked him, eyes moving left to right across the short notice.

"Ten days," she said. Her voice was spare and flat, the kind that made other people fill silence for her. "You told Ezra it'd hold till wheat-cutting. After he died, you told me we'd talk when the stand showed itself."

"Circumstances have altered."

"Whose."

He could have said the county examiner's. He could have said Washington's. He could have said the bank's reserve position, or the way one over-carried farm note invited questions about all the others, or any of the accurate phrases that made men feel respectable while doing ugly work. Instead he heard himself answer with less cover than he preferred.

"Everybody's."

Cora looked past him to the wheat field, then back to the notice. "That field didn't stand any thicker because you drove out in a motorcar and pinned a date on it."

"The bank cannot carry indefinite paper."

"Wasn't indefinite last week."

"Last week no federal review had begun."

"So I catch it because you've got company."

"You are to answer the obligation."

She laughed once, without mirth, and bent to pick up the dishcloth the wind had pushed from the pump curb into the dust. When she straightened, the cloth hung from one hand like something skinned.

"With what money."

He disliked the question because it was the only honest one in the yard. The chicken on the porch rail hopped down and pecked at nothing. From inside the house came the faint clink of crockery, careful, listening crockery. August kept his tone formal because if he let anger into it he would sound personal, and if he sounded personal the whole thing would reveal itself as what it partly was.

"Interest current by June first. Then we may discuss a further arrangement."

"May."

"If there is a basis for it."

"And if there isn't."

"Then the bank proceeds under the lien."

Her face changed very little. People often misread that in Cora Vale. They mistook the economy of her expression for a lack of feeling when in fact it was labor, the same kind she gave to hauling water or mending harness, a practical containment of whatever would otherwise spill and make a greater mess. She folded the notice once, exactly on the crease it had arrived with.

"You should've written that the first time," she said. "Might've spared us the Sunday clothes."

The line struck harder than accusation would have. Ezra's shiny elbows flared in his head, then that maddening scratch at paper after the office door had shut, memory refusing to say whether it accused or merely remembered. August wanted, absurdly, to tell her that he had never meant the thing to stretch this far, that he had tried to hold a structure upright and had spent the years since mistaking the structure for virtue. But explanation would not cleanse anything here. It would only sound like a banker demanding sympathy from a woman at a pump.

"I will expect you Tuesday," he said.

"You'll get what there is."

"That is not always enough."

"No," she said. "I know."

Begging did not enter it. He had not prepared for that. Begging could be classified. Anger could be answered. This plain acknowledgment, spoken with egg water drying on her wrists and the whole starved farm open behind her, left him nothing procedural to stand on except the paper he had brought.

He touched the brim of his hat then, too late to count as courtesy, and turned back to the car. When he looked once through the windshield before starting the engine, Cora was still by the pump, notice in one hand, dishcloth in the other, upright in the yard, endurance in a flour sack and work shoes, and he had just put a date on that too. He drove toward town with the bank's clean language in his pocket and the dirt road shivering under the tires, and by the time Morrow came back into view he knew two things: the books would read better tomorrow, and the past had not been contained at all.


# Chapter 5

By supper, with August's ten days still rasping in her ear, the dust had gone so fine it no longer drifted in visible threads. It lived in the house. It hid in the fold of the flour sack, under the butter crock lid, in the seam of the table oilcloth, and it rasped under Cora's knife as she pared potatoes at the sink. The window over the pump had the weak yellow look dust gave evening now, as if the light itself had been rolled in dirt before being let indoors.

Dell sat at the table with a dead alarm clock spread in a pie tin. He had lined the gears in a careful half-moon by size and kept touching their teeth with a burnt matchstick, not because that did anything Cora knew of, but because he could not bear a broken thing to lie in one helpless heap. He liked failure better when it came apart into named pieces. The clock case stood open by his elbow, nickel worn to a dull pewter, the little glass face clouded at one corner.

"You going to eat that raw or help me?" Cora asked.

He did not look up at once. "I'm looking for where it binds."

"The potato or the clock."

That got the corner of his mouth, not quite a smile. "Clock."

He was seventeen and all elbows until a machine was put in his hands. Then some steadier thing came over him. He bent close, dust-light hair falling toward the tin, and squinted with one eye shut, listening to a spring that had not made a sound in years. Cora watched him a second longer than she meant to. He had Ezra's long fingers and none of Ezra's ease. Even his patience looked strained, as if he had to hold it down with his shoulders.

Thea came in from the back step carrying the wash she had snatched from the line before dusk. Her dress had once been navy and now looked permanently weathered, the cuffs let down and rehemmed so neatly the mending showed only when she turned her wrists. There was chalk at the side of one finger and a line of dust where her collar had rubbed her throat. She set the basket on the chair by the stove and pushed loose dark hair back with the heel of her hand. She was careful, always, even when tired. The care in her had a schoolteacher's training to it in public and something more private in it too, something hungrier and less tidy that Cora knew too well and had been refusing all week.

She had been keeping off Thea for four nights. That was the farm phrase for a horse gone touchy, and it was the true one. Since August had looked over his clean desk and talked about shortened grace as if mercy were a line item he no longer carried, since the front-room window had thrown Ezra back at her with his head bent over paper he had sworn he never signed, Cora had put work between herself and Thea as if labor were a door that latched from the inside. She rose earlier. She stayed out later. She came to bed sore enough to hope sleep might do what will would not. Want only sharpened. It followed Thea's sleeve through a room. It stood behind her when she bent over the stove. It made a fool of Cora's hands whenever a plate passed from one woman to the other.

Thea hung the damp shirts on chair backs and said, "The west room still smells of that vinegar rag. I don't think it ever won't."

"Better vinegar than mouse," Cora said.

"Or schoolhouse paste," Dell put in without lifting his head. "That gets in the back of your nose."

Thea looked at him then, and some of the strain left her face. "You'd know. You used to eat it."

"I was six."

"You were ten and lying about it."

He gave her a look meant to be hard and ruined it by going red at the ears. That, too, he had from no one but himself. Ezra had never blushed. Cora laid the potatoes in the pot and covered them with pump water while Dell nudged a gear with the matchstick as delicately as if it were made of spun sugar instead of stamped metal.

They ate with the lamp burning low to save kerosene. The potatoes were mealy and the salt had taken on grit from the air no matter how tight Cora kept the lid. Outside, what was left of the wheat made a dry paper whisper against itself. There was not enough body in it to call it a rustle. There was not enough life in it to sound like crop. Thea cut bread into small exact pieces before she ate them. Dell salted everything twice. Cora kept hearing August's voice in the pauses, the way he had said ten days as if ten days were a generosity. She could not look at the front-room window after dark now without thinking of Ezra's hand on the pen, the set of his shoulders, the lie she had been living in because the lie had kept the house upright.

Dell said, "Wind backed south."

Cora looked up. "How d'you know?"

"Sound on the shed roof changed." He shrugged at her and then at Thea, embarrassed by his own certainty. "It hits flatter from that side."

Thea said, "Then it'll come through the schoolhouse cracks again. I only just got one row of copybooks fit to touch."

"No children left to dirty them," Dell said.

Thea took that without flinching, which was one of the reasons Cora had gone and kept taking her into bed. Thea did not require prettied-up truth. "There are enough children left for one row," she said.

"For now."

Cora said, "Eat."

He obeyed, though he rolled his eyes at his plate in a way that was pure Dell and almost child enough to hurt her. He finished first, stacked his dishes without being asked, and carried the pie tin into his room as flat and steady as if it were a communion plate. One lost screw would have offended him more than hunger. He came back a moment later and stood in the kitchen door with the empty clock case in his hand.

"Ma."

"What."

"You save any of that thin oil from the binder can?"

"Top shelf by the chimney. Rag's wrapped around it."

He nodded, then hesitated. The question that came out was smaller than the pause before it. "If I get it ticking, can I keep it in the shed?"

Cora blinked at him. "What for?"

He looked away, already sorry he'd asked. "I like the sound."

Thea glanced at Cora, not pitying either of them, which helped. Cora said, "If you get it ticking, it's yours to put wherever you please."

That made him duck his head in a way she had not seen since before Ezra died. He took the oil and went.

The silence he left was worse than if he had stayed. The house was small enough that nobody ever truly left a room. Dell's movements passed through the walls as clearly as weather. His bed ropes complained once. The tin clicked softly against the floorboards. Then came the scratch of his match against the lamp in there, and after that the patient little silences of him fitting one piece to another.

Thea gathered the plates. "Leave those," Cora said, and reached at the same time.

Their fingers met on a cup slick with dishwater. Thea's hand was cooler than the china. Cora felt the jolt of it all the way up her arm and snatched back too fast, as if the cup had burned her. Thea's mouth set.

"I had another letter today," she said.

Cora kept her eyes on the basin of gray washwater. "From who?"

"Lincoln."

The pump handle slipped in Cora's grip and banged the sink. In the next room Dell stopped moving. For a second the whole house listened.

Cora lowered the handle carefully. "What'd they say?"

"The librarian wrote herself this time. If I can come the first Tuesday in June, the interview is still mine. If I need another day, I ought to wire by Monday."

Cora took a plate and wiped it though it was already clean. "Then you'd better go."

Thea held the dishrag bunched in both hands. "That's all."

"What else is there to say?"

"Anything with blood in it might do."

Cora put the plate down harder than she meant to. "Lincoln's cleaner than this. It pays regular. You'd have a room that isn't full of other people's dust and sorrow."

Thea laughed once, too softly for humor, and set the cup in the rack with more care than the cup deserved. "I know what a library is, Cora."

"I'm saying it's sensible."

"You're saying it like you've settled the matter of a stove repair."

"I'm saying you ought to take work that won't grind you up."

Thea folded the dishrag in half, then half again, a schoolroom movement of control that only made the anger in it clearer. "And not like the woman you've been walking around as if my sleeve would scorch you if it brushed yours."

Cora felt heat come up under the dust on her skin. "Not here."

"No," Thea said, glancing toward Dell's room. "Never here. Never where daylight can see."

The quiet on the other side of the wall deepened. Dell was not close enough to hear every word, but he knew tones. Any boy knew tones in a house. Cora said, lower, "We'll talk later."

Thea turned the folded rag in her hands until her knuckles showed white. "You said that three nights ago."

In his room, Dell cursed softly at the clock in a burst of frustration so naked it almost made Cora grateful. It broke the line of the moment. Thea drew one hard breath through her nose, set the rag flat on the sink edge, and said, "I'll finish these."

"Thea."

"No. Leave it. If I say another word with him awake, it'll be the wrong one."

Her anger went into the work. She washed, dried, stacked, and put away every dish with a precision that felt angrier than throwing them would have. Cora stood at the table pretending to wipe crumbs while her whole body attended the sound of crockery touching wood, drawer sliding shut, Thea's skirt brushing the leg of the chair. When Thea finally took the lamp and went down the hall, the kitchen looked abruptly meaner, all the patched corners and dust seams showing plain in the poorer light.

Cora went out to the yard on the excuse of the chickens. The air was not cool. Dust had a way of stealing the night's mercy and hanging on to heat after sunset. The henhouse latch held. The birds shifted and muttered in their sleep. Beyond the shed the wheat lay under a moon blurred behind the suspended dirt, each field row looking rubbed thin, as if a giant palm had gone over the land too hard and worn it down to the threads. Harvest, she thought, and nearly laughed. What would there be to harvest if the wind kept at it. There would be a mean little crop, if any. There might be enough to cut for appearances. There might be enough to give a bank man one more figure to put in a ledger before he took the place anyway.

Stay through harvest.

The words came into her whole, unbidden, and sat there heavy as feed in a sack.

She leaned a forearm on the chicken-yard post and closed her eyes. It was the sort of thing a person said when she had no right to say anything larger. Stay through harvest. Stay through the season's end. Stay until the crop declared itself dead or, by some mean miracle, stood. Small words, enormous ask. Practical enough to pass for a farm request if she dressed it right. That made it uglier. It also made it the only honest thing she had.

Thea's wages helped. So did the board money. That was true. Coffee, sugar, lamp oil, cloth: all of that was true. Cora had counted Thea into the household accounts long before she had counted the deeper thing, because money let a person touch facts without admitting what they cost in other ways. But when she imagined Thea gone to Lincoln, the first blow was the cold of that room down the hall. Then came the table with nobody cutting bread too neatly, the kitchen without that voice saying the potatoes needed using up, the bed with no body slipping into hers after the house went quiet enough to risk it.

Under all of that waited the rougher, less clean fear of being left. Ezra had done that one way, the bank another, the weather another. Cora disliked herself for how quickly the mind shoved every loss into one bin if you let it.

She went back in through the kitchen and avoided the front-room window out of habit. She had been doing that too, moving around glass as if it had turned snitch. The first days after the storm she had breathed on every pane in reach like a madwoman, wanting Ezra again, wanting proof, wanting contradiction. Now she could not bear to see the house offer up another thing she had failed to know. She passed the washstand mirror in the hall with her face turned half aside.

From Dell's room came a faint dry click, then another.

Then, improbably, it began to tick.

It was uneven, tired, but there.

"Ma," he called, not loudly.

She went to the door and leaned against the jamb. His room smelled of dust, boy sweat, oil, and the singed edge of lamp wick. The clock sat on the crate beside his bed with its back still off, ticking with all the determination of a creature too stubborn to know it ought to be dead. Dell sat cross-legged in his undershirt, the pie tin in his lap, grinning at it despite himself.

"Hear it?" he said.

"Hard not to."

"It'll likely stop by morning."

"Then enjoy it tonight."

He nodded. The grin had already faded, as if he distrusted his own pleasure. "You ought to get some sleep."

She almost asked since when he had become the one saying that. Instead she said, "You too."

He lay back without further argument, one hand tucked behind his head, listening to the little tick as if it were music from a farther, saner country. When Cora pulled the door mostly shut, leaving the crack that kept the room from getting airless, she carried the sound of the clock with her down the hall.

Her bedroom still had too much of Ezra in the furniture and not enough of him anywhere that mattered. His bureau squatted against the far wall with one drawer that never closed true. The iron bed they had shared sat with one side nearly against the wall because there was nowhere else to put it and still let the dresser open. In the washstand bowl the dust had settled gray on white enamel no matter how often she wiped it away. The patchwork quilt at the foot of the bed had once belonged to Ezra's mother, and Cora had spent years disliking it for reasons too petty to confess to anyone, chiefly that the yellow squares were ugly and the seams never lay flat. Now she hated herself for the old pettiness more than she hated the quilt.

She set the lamp lower, unbuttoned her dress, and was pulling the last pins from her hair when Thea said from the doorway, "Did you mean it?"

Cora looked at her in the mirror before she turned. Thea had taken off her shoes. Stocking feet on the boards made her seem both softer and more dangerous. She still held the letter, folded twice, creased thin where her thumb had worried the edge.

"Which part?" Cora said.

"The part where you sent me to Lincoln as if you were being kind."

Cora slid the last hairpin onto the washstand. "I wasn't being kind. I was being accurate."

"No. Accurate would have sounded different."

Thea came into the room and shut the door until only the narrowest line of hall dark remained. She did not latch it. She never latched it with Dell in the house. The space between them felt crowded even before she crossed it. Cora could hear the clock through the wall now, a small obstinate tick under the wind's long dry hand on the siding.

"I wanted to tell you sooner," Thea said. "I didn't because there hasn't been one minute in this house this week that wasn't full of paper, dust, or your son. That's true. The other truth is I wanted to make you say something that wasn't sensible."

Cora looked at the letter instead of her face. "How much does it pay?"

Thea shut her eyes briefly. When she opened them, they were bright and hard. "God. There you are again."

"It matters."

"Yes, it matters. Everything here is always counted before it's felt. I know that. Answer me anyway."

Cora stepped closer. Thea did not back up. Dust had settled in the dark curve of her collar seam. Cora wanted to put her mouth there so badly it made her stomach turn. "If it pays enough," she said, "then I don't get to pretend the town might save itself before you need to go."

Thea's face changed, not softened exactly but less armored. "That's closer."

"I'm not good at this part."

"No," Thea said. "You're very good at carrying wood, hauling water, killing chickens, and saying nothing until silence has gone rotten between two people."

That almost made Cora smile. It almost did. "And you're good at saying the thing so exact it leaves no skin on it."

"Somebody ought to."

The tick through the wall kept at them. Cora found herself absurdly grateful for it. It made the room feel less like a held breath. "When would you go?"

"If I write tomorrow, I could catch the train Monday night from Hastings and be there Tuesday."

"That soon."

"Yes."

Cora nodded once. Lincoln was not far on a map and might as well have been another country in cash and trains and what sort of women got to live there by choice. "You want it."

Thea gave a tired, crooked smile with no cheer in it. "Of course I want it. I want shelves that aren't closing. I want windows I don't have to stuff with rags. I want a room where I can breathe without feeling grit move on my teeth. I want wages that are mine and not another fragile local committee's idea of charity. And I want" - she stopped, corrected herself with visible effort - "I want to stop feeling as if every decent thing I want cancels out every indecent thing."

Cora said, "You aren't indecent."

Thea gave a quick laugh and pressed her mouth flat after it. "Not in your room, no. Outside it I'm one rumor and a board meeting away from being put on the road. You know that."

Cora did know it. Widows got one kind of mercy if they kept their heads down and worked. Teachers got another kind of scrutiny entirely. A widow with a boarder could still be pitied. A schoolteacher with a married - no, widowed - woman in her bed could be made into a lesson. Cora looked at the door, then at the wall beyond which Dell's makeshift clock kept time for all of them. "I know."

"Then don't tell me to go as if you're nobly setting me free."

"What would you have me do?"

Thea's chin lifted, that classroom motion she had when she refused sloppiness from a child and from herself. "Tell me whether I've only been where you set down your fear when you can't carry it alone."

The words hit hard because they were close enough to something Cora had feared in herself to sting clean through. "No."

"No what?"

"No, that isn't all you are."

"Then what am I?"

Cora could have lied easier than she could answer plain. The room was thick with soap, dust, lamp smoke, and the faint sweetness of Thea's skin under all of it. She saw the letter in Thea's hand, the crack in the washstand bowl, the crooked drawer in Ezra's bureau, the poor patched hem of Thea's dress. She said the truest piece first because it was the nearest. "You're the only place in this house that doesn't feel claimed by him or the bank."

Thea looked at her for a long time. When she spoke again, her voice had dropped. "That's not the same as saying you want me."

"I know it isn't."

"Then say the other."

Instead Cora reached for the letter. Thea held it a second longer than needed before letting go. The paper was warm from her hand. Cora set it on the washstand and then, because she had been wanting to touch something living for days and was tired of pretending otherwise, she laid her fingers against the side of Thea's throat where the pulse moved under dust and skin.

Thea shut her eyes, just once. "Don't," she whispered, though she leaned into the touch. "Don't use this to get around the rest."

"I'm not."

"You're exactly capable of that."

"Then stop me if I do."

Cora bent and kissed the chalk-smell off Thea's knuckle first, then the corner of her mouth, then her properly. Thea made a soft sound that was anger breaking and wanting coming through it. For a second she held herself stiff. Then her hand caught in Cora's unpinned hair and pulled once, hard enough to hurt, which was as good as an answer.

They kissed standing between the bed and washstand because the room gave them no graceful way to do anything. Cora had forgotten how quickly Thea lost the careful schoolmarm neatness when she was done holding the line. One hand went to Cora's waist, rough with need, while the other kept fumbling between wanting more and wanting to keep her own dress closed until the answer had been earned.

"When did the letter come?" Cora asked against her mouth.

"Three days ago."

That stung. "Three."

"Yes. While you were busy turning into a useful ghost."

Cora kissed her harder for that and felt Thea's breath catch. "You should've told me."

"You should've touched me before now."

The bluntness in it went straight through Cora's anger and into the softer thing underneath. She got the first buttons of Thea's dress open with clumsy fingers. Her hands were better with wire and harness than shell buttons, but she kept at them. Thea helped at last, impatient, and the dress loosened enough for Cora to put her mouth to the hollow at the base of her throat. There was dust there. There was lye soap under it. There was the salt of the day.

Thea said, very low, "If this is all we do, I'll still go."

Cora lifted her head. "Then tell me what counts."

"Something that survives morning."

The clock ticked. Wind rubbed the siding. Dell coughed once in his sleep or near enough to sleep. Cora looked toward the wall and hated it, hated the whole thin house with its cheap partitions and its ability to make every private thing sound near public. The iron bed sat with one leg almost kissing plaster, the frame old enough to speak if breathed on wrong.

Thea followed her glance and huffed out a breath that might have been the start of a laugh. "Your bed's too near the wall."

"The whole damned house is too near everything."

"That isn't an answer either."

"No." Cora took Thea by the waist and turned her to sit on the bed edge where the frame would not bang so easy if they were careful. "But it's the ground we're standing on."

Thea let herself be sat because she wanted it as badly as Cora did, but her eyes stayed on Cora's face. "You can't work your way out of me."

"Watch me fail, then."

Cora knelt on the braided rug. The boards pressed through it at once. Up close, Thea's stockings were shiny with wear at the ankles and expertly darned at one knee. Cora ran a hand from shoe strap to calf and felt Thea shiver. She unhooked the garter tabs one by one. The tiny snaps sounded loud in the room. Her cracked thumb caught in the stocking lace at the top and Thea sucked air through her teeth.

"Sorry," Cora murmured.

"Do it slower," Thea said, which was not forgiveness but something better.

Cora rolled one stocking down, then the other, and kissed the pale skin banded above where sun and dust ever reached. Thea's knees parted for her with visible effort, the kind that admitted want while pretending to maintain pride. Cora put her forehead against Thea's thigh a moment, not to make a show of reverence but because she had been hungry and there she was, finally allowed to say so with her body if not yet her mouth.

She pushed Thea's dress and slip up together. Thea wore plain cotton step-ins, mended too, and Cora had the wild, absurd thought that she loved her for mending underthings no one ought to have seen. Then she put her hand between Thea's legs and felt the heat there, the wet already worked up under anger and waiting, and all thought left her except the coarse gratefulness of being let in.

Thea's hand flew to the washstand to steady herself. "Cora."

"Hush."

"I'm not saying no."

"I know what no sounds like."

She drew the cotton aside and put her mouth on Thea. The first taste of her came mixed with soap and the day's salt and the faint dry grit that had got everywhere, because of course it had. Nothing in this country stayed clean long enough to become symbolic. Thea jerked and then gripped the washstand harder. The basin rattled once. Cora lifted one hand immediately and caught it before it could knock again.

"Easy," Thea whispered, and Cora could not tell whether she meant the furniture or herself.

Cora learned toward care by force. She went slow because the house had ears, then slower because Thea made a sound low in her throat that seemed to ask for both mercy and its opposite. The rug burned Cora's knees through her shift. The iron bed warmed under her forearm. Thea's thighs tightened around her head, then opened again. When Cora slid two fingers into her, Thea gave a breathless curse that ended in a laugh she bit off with the back of her wrist.

"You laugh at the damnedest moments," Cora muttered against her.

"I know." Thea's voice broke on the second word. "I can't help it when I'm scared."

That nearly undid Cora more than anything else said that night. She curled her fingers and felt Thea go taut. Thea's heel scraped the rug. The bed shifted a dangerous inch and thudded lightly at the wall. Both women froze.

In the next room the clock went on ticking. Dell turned over, cotsprings groaning in one long complaint, then stilled.

Thea bent forward, face buried in her own shoulder, breath sawing. Cora stayed absolutely still until the house settled around them again. Then she reached up, tugged the corner of the sheet free, and pressed it into Thea's hand. Thea understood at once. She bit down on the cloth and nodded.

After that, she came hard and quiet enough to keep the secret, though not so quiet Cora mistook what she had done. Thea's whole body drew up around the force of it. Her fingers slipped on the washstand rim. The basin rocked. Cora held it one-handed while she worked Thea through the shaking, more practical than elegant, more intimate for being practical. When it passed, Thea sat sagged and flushed with the sheet corner still between her teeth, hair half down, eyes bright and furious with pleasure.

"That doesn't survive morning," she said when she could speak.

Cora rose, knees complaining, and kissed the damp at the corner of Thea's mouth. "No."

"Then keep going."

The challenge in it made heat go through Cora all over again. Thea pulled her close, found the buttons of Cora's dress and lost patience with them almost at once. She opened what she could, then dragged the cloth and shift up together with a hunger that felt both less practiced and more exact. Thea always touched as if she were learning a text she intended to remember. Nothing lazy in it. Nothing merely grateful. Her palm slid over Cora's hip, her thumb along the waistband of her drawers, and Cora had to brace one hand on the wall to keep from knocking the bed herself.

"Now," Thea said, breath against her jaw. "Tell me."

Cora knew exactly what was being asked and hated how naked it made her feel, far more naked than the open dress or the shift bunched at her waist. "Thea."

"No. You don't get to hide inside my body and call it honesty."

Thea's hand went under the cotton and found Cora already slick and aching. Cora bit down on whatever sound tried to come out. The wall was cool against her shoulder blade. The bed rail pressed into the back of her thigh. Thea's fingers, unlike Cora's, were not rough from pitchfork handles and washboards. They knew how to coax rather than seize. That, too, Cora loved her for and resented her for and wanted without reserve.

"Tell me what I'd be staying for," Thea said.

Cora shook her head once.

Thea pushed her up onto the mattress and came with her, skirts bunched at the hips, one stocking still hanging from a calf because they had not had time for order. Her hair had finally fallen. In the lamp's low light it lay across Cora's chest and shoulder like something spilled. She set her mouth to the place below Cora's ear, kissed once, then slid her hand back between Cora's legs and waited there, inexorable.

"Tell me," she said again.

Cora caught the iron rail and held it to keep the bed from tapping the wall. Even so it gave one small complaint, a swallowed noise. She shut her eyes. The truth came out in a shape meaner and plainer than any vow. "Stay through harvest."

Thea's hand stopped.

The clock kept ticking.

"You ask that in June?" Thea whispered.

"Near enough."

"And after."

Cora opened her eyes. The ceiling above them held a brown water stain from two winters back that no scrubbing had ever shifted. She fixed on it because she could not say this looking anywhere softer. "After, I don't know."

Thea stared at her, anger and hurt rising fresh. "That's a cruel little promise."

"It's the biggest true one I've got."

"You hear yourself?"

"Yes."

The word came out harsher than she intended. She loosened one hand from the bed rail and touched Thea's wrist where it rested on her. "Listen to me. Stay through harvest if there is one. Stay till the wheat's cut or fails standing. Stay till August makes his next move and Dell does whatever wild thing he's fixing to do. Stay till I know whether I've got a farm or only boards and debt and this room. I can't stand in town and hold your hand. I can't tell you I'll make this place safe because I don't know how and I won't lie to get you to stay. But I want your shoes under this bed. I want your comb on that washstand. I want to hear you in the kitchen at dawn and feel you come through that door when the house is finally dark. That's what I know."

Thea's face changed as she listened. The fight in it settled into something graver. "You hide in practical nouns the way other people hide in scripture."

"Maybe."

"And if I say yes to that, what am I saying yes to, exactly. A season. A secret. Your need when it outruns your pride."

"Yes."

The bluntness of it landed harder than defense would have. Thea inhaled sharply. Her hand returned to motion, slower now, not merciful but deliberate, and Cora nearly lost her grip on the bed rail at once.

"That's ugly enough to be true," Thea said. "God help me, it is."

"Thea."

"No, let me think." Her voice trembled and steadied. "Do you know what I wanted? I wanted you to say come with me. I wanted you to be foolish and ruin us both in one clean sentence."

"I know."

"But I wouldn't trust you if you did. That's the worst of it."

She bent and kissed Cora hard enough to split the rest of the thought in two. Then Thea moved her hand with less caution and more claim, and Cora's body answered before the rest of her could. She turned her face into the pillow to swallow the noise. The pillow smelled of dust and old sun and the hair oil she used too sparingly. Thea kept at her until the pressure in Cora wound so tight it felt as if the whole house had narrowed to the space between that hand and her own breath. When release came it was not pretty. It took her in a jerk that thudded the bed once against the wall despite everything. She clamped her own wrist between her teeth and tasted the soap not fully rinsed from her skin.

They went still again, listening.

Dell coughed, muttered something in sleep, and the clock went on ticking with infuriating innocence.

Thea dropped her forehead to Cora's shoulder and started to laugh soundlessly, the laugh shaking against Cora's skin until it turned halfway into crying and then into neither. Cora held her because there was nothing wiser to do. Their breaths slowed together by degrees.

After a while Thea rolled onto her back, one arm over her eyes. Cora lay beside her in disordered clothes and felt sweat cooling in every seam the dust had found first. The room smelled of iron bedframe, lamp smoke, sex, and the wet rag from the dishpan still hanging in the kitchen. Outside, the wind had dropped enough that the wheat could be heard again in its papery whisper.

"I didn't tell them yes," Thea said into the crook of her elbow.

"I know."

"I haven't told them no either."

"I know that too."

Thea lowered her arm and turned her head. In the softened lamplight she looked younger and more worn at once, the hard classroom planes of her face loosened by what had passed but not erased. "I wanted you to ask for me, not for my wages."

Cora let out a breath. "I did think of the wages."

"Of course you did."

"Then I thought of your room empty. That hit first."

Thea studied her as if deciding whether the answer deserved belief. "That's a different thing."

"Yes."

"Don't make me regret hearing it."

"I won't if I can help it."

"There. That's the farmer in you again. Promise wrapped in weather."

Cora reached and pushed a lock of hair off Thea's forehead. "You want me to lie prettier?"

"No." Thea caught Cora's hand and kissed the heel of it, the place callused by shovel handles and washboard edges. "I want the plain thing, only said before I have to drag it out by force."

Cora could not argue. Not honestly. She lay looking at the ceiling stain again, then at the letter on the washstand, small and folded beside the lamp. "Did you want Lincoln bad?"

Thea answered without delay. "Yes."

Cora nodded.

"And I wanted this," Thea went on. "That's the trouble. I'd like one life that is not all renunciation arranged into good manners."

Cora almost said nobody got that, but the sentence died before it reached her mouth. It would have sounded wise and been useless. Instead she said, "I know."

Thea gave her a long look, then sat up and pulled her dress the rest of the way off, not with coyness, not performing at all, simply tired of cloth. Her body was narrower than Cora's, pale where the sun did not find her, with a small scar high on one thigh Cora had once asked about and been told came from falling off a gate at thirteen because she had insisted she could climb it in city shoes. Thea had been laughing when she told that story and furious at the remembered embarrassment in equal parts. She had that odd mixture in many things. It was one reason Cora trusted her more than she trusted people who had only one face for themselves.

Cora sat up too and untangled her shift. Her own body felt used in the best and worst ways, heavy with labor even at rest, the skin of her palms rough enough to snag cloth, the pale lines at her shoulders where Ezra's old apron straps had rubbed over the years before she finally cut them shorter. Thea looked at her without flinching, which was another of her rare gifts. Admiration was too easy a word for it. What she gave Cora was attention.

"We ought to wash," Thea said, practical now because if she stayed in tenderness too long it frightened her.

"Mm."

Cora got up and crossed to the washstand. The floorboards were cool under her bare feet. She poured a little water from the pitcher into the enamel basin. It went cloudy at once from the dust the bowl had gathered since evening. She dipped the cloth and wrung it. The smell of wet metal rose sharp and familiar.

She leaned over to rinse it again and breathed across the basin without thinking.

The gray film on the white enamel silvered.

At first she thought it was only water moving. Then the shape came up in it, small and exact and impossible to mistake: Thea on the bed edge with one stocking hanging from her calf, head thrown back, the sheet twisted in her fist. Cora herself knelt between her knees, one hand braced on the quilt, mouth at Thea's thigh. The image shifted - no, not shifted, gave a second fragment. Cora on her back against the yellow-patched quilt, dress rucked to the waist, Thea bent over her with dark hair falling forward and one hand working under the cotton. The lamp wick burned low in the vision exactly as it burned low now. This time the basin went straight to the present. It held the thing they had done less than ten minutes before, already keeping its own receipt.

The cloth slipped from Cora's hand into the basin with a wet slap. The stand rang softly.

Behind her Thea said, "What?"

Cora could not answer fast enough. The image held a second more, thinning at the edges like breath on glass. The enamel was slick and cold under her fingers when she caught the rim. Her own bare shoulder showed in the bowl as a ghost around the memory of it.

Thea came up beside her and saw.

For once she had no ready sentence. Her breath touched Cora's upper arm. The room seemed to contract around the basin, the door, the wall behind which Dell's salvaged clock kept taking on the stubborn work of time.

"That was just now," Thea said.

"Yes."

"It can do that."

"Looks like."

The memory in the basin thinned further, the shapes going grainy, then vanished into nothing but gray water and the limp cloth. Cora stared at the blank enamel as if it might re-form if she breathed again. She did not dare.

Thea put one hand flat on the washstand. "If he'd come in for water."

Cora saw it at once. She saw Dell half-awake, thirsty, pushing the door with his shoulder. She saw morning light on the bowl before she had wiped it. She saw any pair of eyes in any house where dust settled after a storm and nobody could stop the weather from keeping receipts. The thought was not grand. It was domestic, and that made it worse.

"He didn't," Cora said.

"No." Thea swallowed. Her voice came back with effort. "But if I stay, that's part of what staying is."

Cora turned then. Thea stood naked except for the one stocking still around her ankle, hair down, letter on the washstand behind her, the mark of Cora's mouth still visible at her collarbone. There was no decent lie left in the room. "Yes," Cora said.

Thea looked at her for a long beat, measuring the basin, the wall, the door, the letter, the bed too near the wall and the town beyond it. Then she reached back without looking, took the folded Lincoln letter from beside the lamp, and slid it under Cora's brush instead of into her dress.

"I'll wire them Monday for another day," she said. "I'm not promising you harvest in the dark. But I'm not walking into Lincoln Tuesday as if this room doesn't change anything. If you still mean it when the sun's up, say it then. Until Lincoln answers, I'm here."

It was a stay with terms and a date on it, only a few days bought against Lincoln and daylight, but chosen all the same.

When Thea came back to the bed, she crossed the room stripped of caution by the enamel bowl, by Dell breathing on the far side of the wall, by Lincoln folded under the brush, by Monday already pressing on them. Cora knew the night had turned into something riskier and more binding than comfort, the sort of thing daylight would make them answer for.


# Chapter 6

The half-mended trace lay across Dell's knee while he watched the kitchen door instead of his work. Since breakfast he'd been waiting for Cora to turn her back.

She had set him to mending a harness trace that could have sat another three days, maybe four, and kept finding one more reason to pass through the yard and look at him while he worked. Since the jars at Hester's bakery, she had taken to pinning chores onto him like weights on a fence wire. Check the chicken run. Patch the shed latch. Scrape the grit out of the pump cup. None of it was made-up work. That was what made it harder to fight. Everything on the place needed doing, and Cora knew how to make necessity feel like a hand on the back of his neck.

By noon the kitchen was already hot. Dust had settled in the seams of the tablecloth again, a pale fur in the red checks. Cora stood at the stove with one sleeve rolled past the elbow and a strip of blue sewing thread tied around the crack on her ring finger to keep it from opening any farther. She did not waste words on pain any more than she wasted lard. She only worked around it.

"South fence is dragging," Dell said, making his voice casual and hearing the strain in it anyway. "I ought to look at it before the calves get notions."

Cora did not turn from the skillet. "You ought to finish what's in your hands."

"I will."

"Then say what you mean."

He laid the strap flat on his knee and did not look up. "I mean I can do the fence first and this after."

She set the spoon down hard enough to ring the stove lid. "You going with Hester again?"

Dell hated the quick jump in him when she said it. He hated more that she heard the lie before he spoke it. "I'm going to the south fence."

"That wasn't what I asked."

The room held on the scrape of the spoon against iron. Thea had already gone to the schoolhouse for the day's thin business, and without her there the kitchen seemed built only for work and argument. Dell could smell old coffee, hot grease, and the dust that baked its own smell out of the floorboards by noon. He wished, not for the first time, that Cora would shout when she was angry. A shout could be shoved against. Her quiet kept making him feel small enough to fit in a pocket.

"I said I'm going to the fence."

Now she turned. Her hair, chopped blunt with kitchen scissors weeks before, had started escaping its pins in dry pale strands around her face. "You think I don't know what foolishness looks like when it starts walking out of my yard with a shovel."

"It isn't foolishness."

"It gets to be foolishness if it tangles you in your father's business and leaves you with nothing to show for it."

That made him lift his head. Shame moved in him like something with claws. If he had not heard Ezra crying in the barn that night and lain still under his own quilt anyway, maybe he could have stood there clean and righteous. Instead every step toward the truth felt crooked. He was prying under floorboards after a dead man who had already sunk deep enough to stop answering back.

"Maybe I want something to show for it," he said.

Cora's face tightened, not much, just around the mouth. "Dell."

She said his name when she wanted obedience to remember it was born in love. It almost worked. He looked at the strip of harness leather, at the awl and the spool of waxed thread on the step, at his own hands with the knuckles still dark from axle grease that never washed fully out. If he stayed, he would spend the afternoon stitching a strap and pretending the ground south of the wheat had not started talking.

"I'll be back before supper," he said.

"That isn't permission."

"I know."

He took the shovel from beside the pump house before she could answer. Behind him the screen door rasped once. He thought she might come out and stop him with nothing more dramatic than standing in his way. She did not. That was worse. It left the choice in his hands, where it burned.

The yard gave way to the fields in a few short steps, house shadow to glare. Wheat stood there in mean, uneven strips, ankle-high in some places, gone to bare crust in others, the whole south field looking like a dog's coat after mange. From the porch the ground still passed for flat. Out in it, Dell could see how it had been cut and planed and worried into false straightness over years. Furrows crossed places that wanted to sag.
