A shallow draw had been shaved almost level with repeated plowing, though the wagon tongue still leaned there if he drove over it too fast. Dust lay drifted against the stubble in lines that showed every petty rise and fall more honestly than the crop did.

Hester waited where the field edge broke at the old plum thicket, one hand on the top wire of the fence. She wore a faded man's straw hat with a dark sweat stain around the crown and had Otto's brass survey pencil tucked through her cardigan buttonhole like a brooch she had no intention of making pretty. When she saw Dell she bit a horehound drop clean through, grimaced at the bitterness, and pocketed the rest.

"You made it out," she said.

"For now."

She studied the house behind him, then the shovel over his shoulder. "Your mother knows."

"She suspects."

"Same thing in a kitchen."

He almost laughed. It came out harsher than that. "You walked all the way from town to tell me I was caught already?"

"I walked all the way from town because I don't trust paper to stay paper in this wind." She tapped the pocket of her apron. "And because you read ground better than I do now. Otto had the knees for it. I had the memory."

That shifted something in him, though not toward softness. Hester was not made for that. But she was not using him as a boy sent to fetch a tool, either. She had brought herself two miles in men's work shoes with her cough sitting under her ribs like a closed fist, and she was giving him the piece of the job she could not do alone.

She unfolded Otto's map out of wax paper. The sheet had been copied in a hand more careful than elegant, the lines darkened twice where a turn mattered. Dell recognized the rough square of the Vale house site, the road west from town, the old section line. South of the fields Otto had drawn a basin shape in shallow curves and, beyond it, a straight mark with little cross hatches and the word tile written so small Dell had to lean close.

The map made it less ghostly and more offensive.

"He was sure?" Dell asked.

Hester looked at the paper instead of at him. "Otto wasn't a man for sure until he'd paced it twice and argued with himself a third time. He knew where the wet held before they taught it not to."

Dell's eyes stayed on the hatch marks. "You said there used to be mint down there."

"Mint and sedge and enough mosquitoes to lift a baby." Her mouth twitched. "Marta came home black to the knees one August from cutting cat-tail switches and I whipped her for ruining the hem. Next day I cried over the same hem while I let it down because she'd grown and I had nothing else to give her. That's the kind of memory land keeps. Not only the grand ones."

It was the first time she had spoken Marta's name to him that season without pausing beforehand. Dell looked away to the field. Wind moved across it in a dull skin, not enough to cool anything. Far off, a meadowlark threw two notes from a post and then shut up as if the day had taken offense.

"Come on," Hester said. "If your mother comes after us, I'd like to have earned it."

They started south with the map between them, Dell carrying the shovel and a shorter spade Hester had brought wrapped in sacking. The land lost its house-made proportions as they walked. Up close the field edge was not a line but a ragged negotiation between what still answered to the plow and what had gone over to weeds and stubborn native grass. Needle-and-thread clung in gray-green tufts where the cultivator had missed. Russian thistle seedlings crowded one drifted ditch in a green so unhealthy it looked almost chemical. In low places a white alkali bloom had skinned the soil like dried spit.

Dell kept finding the marks of bad years layered on one another. Fence posts leaned where the ground had blown from around them. A broken harrow tooth stood half-buried at the edge of the section like a black rib. The road ditch south of the fields had silted nearly full, so a hard rain, if one ever came again, would ride the road itself before it found any place to sink. He had lived on this land seventeen years and knew every gate, rut, and gopher mound close to the house. South of the wheat, where men once said Saint's Basin lay, he realized how much of his knowing had stopped where the useful chores ended.

Hester walked without wasting motion, though every few minutes she pressed a fist to her breastbone until a cough passed. She did not apologize for it. When the wind tried for the map she crouched and pinned the corners with her broad flour-rough hands, muttering at the paper as if it were a stupid child. Dell took a side without being asked.

"The house is there," she said, nodding behind them. "The old cottonwood by the basin edge is gone, but the root hump ought to be something yet. Otto marked the overflow cut on the east side. If the years haven't packed it flat, you'll see the fall."

"Water won't forget down," Dell said.

She glanced at him. "No. Men forget. Water's better at its work."

He kept his eyes ahead. He did not want the approval in her face, even mild as it was. It made him think of Ezra in the machine shed, crouched with a stripped gear in his palm, saying nothing for a long while and then, when Dell handed him the right wrench without being told, only, There. You see it.

The memory struck hard enough that Dell missed a step in a prairie-dog hole and cursed.

Hester waited until he had pulled free. "You all right?"

"I'm fine."

"You don't have to answer everything like it's a dare."

"I'm not."

"You are."

He wanted to snap back at her, but the truth of it came too quick. Since Ezra died he had been speaking as if every sentence had to prove he was older than it felt to be left behind. He shifted the shovel on his shoulder and said, "Then quit saying things that make it easy."

Hester snorted. "God save me from boys who want gentleness while they're busy pretending they don't need any."

They came to the root hump of the old cottonwood, no trunk left, only a weathered swell under the soil and a scatter of punky wood that broke in Dell's fingers like stale bread. South and east of it the land opened out in a broad, hard pale. From the house and road it would have looked like nothing more than thin ground where the wheat had given up. Standing in it, Dell saw differences he had missed before. The field skin changed color by degrees, dun to ash to a darker brown peering through wherever the wind had scoured deepest. The surface had a shallow dish to it, subtle enough a man could walk it without noticing, but impossible once seen to mistake for level. Grass held in a ragged crescent at the west edge, coarser and darker than the rest. There were old reeds here once, Dell thought, though he had never seen them. The shape of them still worked in the soil.

Hester unfolded the map again. "Otto put the west lip here. Tile line crossing out there." She pointed eastward, where the ground looked no different to Dell at first than any other worn quarter-acre of the county. "I always thought he was two paces north in this copy, but his notes were damp that day and the pencil ran."

"Let me see."

She gave him the map without hesitation.

Dell crouched and turned slowly, matching paper to land. The section road was a clean enough line behind them. The house sat west-northwest. The basin curve on the map had been flattened by plowing, but not erased. When he narrowed his eyes he could read the dish by the way the light hit it, a faint sag where dust had collected thinner. Eastward, beyond the shallow center, there was a run of volunteer grass a shade greener than anything around it, almost a seam.

"Not north," he said. "South. Look."

Hester bent, peering under the hat brim. "Where?"

He pointed with the survey pencil. "That line there. Not the grass itself. The sink under it. See how it holds straight while the rest wanders?"

She stayed still a moment, then nodded once. "I see it."

The simple words landed cleaner than praise. Dell handed the map back. Together they walked toward the seam. Up close it was less visible and more convincing. The ground had settled in a narrow shallow trough, no wider than a boot length, running straight enough to shame every natural curve around it. Dell put the heel of his boot in the depression and felt the difference, a minute give under the powder crust where the soil below had once been worked and then abandoned to sink as it liked.

"Start here," he said.

Hester passed him the short spade. "Then start."

The first cuts were miserable. The crust broke like crockery, sharp-edged and dry, and the spade jarred up his arms every time he drove it in. Beneath that was packed clay the color of old dishwater, hard enough to spark a thought of giving up and calling the whole hunt another of Otto's dead certainties. Sweat ran into Dell's eyes. Dust pasted itself to the sweat and made mud of a sort that only insulted the word. Hester knelt beside the hole and scraped loose spoil back with her hands, sorting pebbles from roots with the concentration she might have given pie crust.

After ten minutes they had nothing but a hole to the depth of Dell's hand and a worse temper between them.

"Maybe it's farther east," Hester said.

"Maybe there isn't anything left."

"Maybe you dug crooked."

He looked at her. "I did not."

"Then move six inches and prove me wrong."

That, too, was work instead of comfort. Dell shifted six inches and cut again. He hit the same crust and the same clay, and then the spade struck something with a dull thud, not like stone. He paused.

"What?" Hester asked.

"Nothing yet."

He drove the spade edge in alongside the resistance and pried. A curved shard rolled up out of the dirt, orange-brown under the gray, smooth on the inside and rough on the outside, the broken edge thick as his thumb.

Neither of them spoke for a breath.

Hester took the piece from his hand and rubbed it clean on her apron. "Tile."

Dell's mouth had gone dry despite the heat. "Could be from anywhere."

"From where?" She did not say it cruelly. She only held the fragment up between them. "You think a drainpipe came out here to die by itself?"

He wanted to say yes, to say maybe some fool dumped a broken load thirty years ago, maybe it was part of a well ring, maybe anything. Instead he crouched and widened the hole. The shovel bit, scraped, jarred. Another curved section appeared, not broken this time but still in place, laid end to end with the next in a line so deliberate it made Dell feel briefly stupid for every excuse he had reached toward.

Hester lowered herself to both knees with a grunt. Together they cleared enough earth to expose almost two feet of the run. Fired-clay tile, four-inch, set straight through what had once been the low hold of the basin. The joints were packed with silt. Fine roots had crept into one seam and died there pale. Dell put his fingers to the earth beneath the tile and came back with black mud under his nails.

It was mud.

It was not the false mud of sweat and dust, and it was not the thin smudge at a water trough. This was cold and close and smelled when it opened. The odor came up like a cellar unlatched after winter: root rot, mineral, old weed, something shut away and still alive in the shutness. Dell had not smelled ground like that since before the bad years took the slough by the north pasture down to cracked plates.

Hester closed her eyes once, breathing shallow because a deep breath would have cost her. "There you are," she said, not to Dell, not quite to God either. "There you damned are."

Dell scooped another handful from below the tile and watched water sheen for an instant on the black before the dry air started taking it. His stomach turned over. He had come wanting proof and had not understood that proof might have a smell.

"It didn't just dry up," he said.

"No."

"They cut it."

"Yes."

The word sat between them with the weight of the clay in his hand.

Dell looked out over the shallow dish of ground. Once seen through the tile, the whole place changed. The greener seam eastward was no accident. The slight drop beyond it was not ordinary settling. He could almost read the labor of it backward now: men trenching through wet meadow, laying the line, packing it in, maybe scraping a carry ditch where the outflow ran off toward lower land that had no business taking it.

No ghost had done that. No weather had reached down with a careful hand and set clay pipe in a row.

He stood so fast the blood thumped in his head. "Give me the shovel."

Hester passed it up. "Where?"

"East. If it falls there, it has to go somewhere."

They followed the faint trough another twenty yards. Dell moved quicker now, seeing signs he had walked past before: a small cave-in no wider than a dinner plate, a change in weed growth, the straightness of failure. At the basin's east lip the ground rose a little and then broke into a low bank half melted by years of blowing soil. Beyond it ran a ditch so filled and scabbed over that from the road it would have looked like no more than a wash. Dell jumped down into it. The bottom held cracked powder, tumbleweed roots, and a bent tin can from some later year. The sides had been cut by hand once. Even softened, he could see the tool marks in their shape. They were not rain-rounded. They had been sliced.

Hester stayed at the rim until Dell cleared a patch and held a hand up. Then she sat and slid the rest of the way in, muttering at her left knee.

"That one predicts weather worse than the radio ever did," she said, rubbing it. "Dig there."

He did. Two cuts down the spade hit clay. Three more and the edge shaved against more tile, this line angled through the bank and into the ditch. He widened the hole until the direction showed plain.

Hester gave a dry, ugly little laugh. "They didn't even hide the sense of it. Straight out."

"They drained the middle and gave it a throat."

"That's one way to put it."

He looked at her. "What's your way?"

She wiped her mouth with the back of her wrist. There was dust in the web of wrinkles at the corners of her eyes, flour permanently ground into the skin of her knuckles. "My way is that men who were hungry and scared and greedy in different proportions convinced themselves a meadow was wasted ground, because it stood wet part of the year instead of carrying wheat. They laid tile, they cut the overflow, and they called it improvement. Then they plowed what ought not to have been plowed and blamed the Lord when the topsoil started traveling."

Dell struck the shovel into the ditch wall harder than he meant to. Dirt sloughed down over his boots. "You mean my father."

Hester's eyes sharpened. "I mean more than your father, and I mean less. I don't know what part he put his hands to."

"He farmed it."

"Yes."

"He borrowed against it. Signed things over it." The words were coming too fast now, rough in his throat. "Maybe he knew. Maybe he helped. Maybe everybody but my mother knew and she still gets to pay for it."

Hester did not tell him to lower his voice. There was no one to hear but a lark and the dead ground. "That's closer to the size of it."

He hated her for being willing to say it and was grateful in the same instant. The two feelings met and made something hotter than either. He had spent weeks trying to split the story clean down the middle: August on one side, Ezra on the other, one a thief and the other a man cornered past fairness. The tile in the ditch would not allow that. Whatever Ezra had done or failed to do, he had lived in a world where this kind of taking wore a respectable face. Maybe he had worn it too. Maybe he had hated himself for it later. Maybe hating himself had not repaired a damn thing.

Dell thought of the sobbing in the barn the night before the rope. He had heard it through the boards, a sound like a grown man trying not to be one. He had put his head under the pillow and waited for quiet because he had school in the morning and because fathers were supposed to climb out of their own troubles. He had been punishing himself with that memory ever since, polishing Ezra into something too wounded and noble to have made common ruin with other men.

He drove the shovel down again, not from need now but because his hands required an answer. "I wanted it to be August," he said.

"I know."

"Just him."

"That would've been simpler."

"I don't care about simpler."

Hester let that sit. Wind moved over the lip of the ditch and set a dry whisper going in the tumbleweed stems. Somewhere to the north a truck changed gears on the road to town, the sound carrying thin as wire in the open land.

"That's new," she said at last.

Dell leaned on the shovel handle. "What's new?"

"You not caring about simple."

He almost told her to go to hell. Instead he looked at the black mud drying on his fingers, turning from shine to dull skin in the space of a minute. "I cared," he said. "I think I still do. I just don't trust it."

Hester nodded once, like a woman accepting a price quoted too high but real. Then she pushed herself upright and climbed out of the ditch one slow step at a time. Dell followed, taking the spade and the map and, when she let him, her elbow for the last pull. She did not thank him. He was glad of that too.

They sat on the bank to eat because suddenly Dell was shaking a little and hunger was the easiest lie for it. Hester took a cloth bundle from her satchel: a heel of rye bread, two hard-boiled eggs, and a paper twist of salt damp from the air of her kitchen. She cracked the eggs against the shovel blade and peeled them clean with a speed that spoke of decades. Before handing Dell one she rubbed a bit of shell from the white with her thumb and said, almost conversationally, "I hate eggs cold. Otto used to laugh at me for that. Said I'd gripe on Judgment Day if the angels served supper wrong."

Dell snorted despite himself.

"But I hate wasted food more," she went on. "Eat."

They ate looking over the basin. Dell kept his eyes moving while he chewed: root hump, west lip grass, the straight trough where the tile had settled, the ditch cut east. He counted strides between each mark and set them in his head. If he had to walk back here in the dark or bring Cora to it before wind changed the look of everything again, he wanted the route in his legs: twelve strides from the hump to the trough, seven more to the rise, the ditch just beyond.

Hester brushed salt from her palm. "We're not bringing Cora a story. Not yet."

"No."

"We bring her the place. Later, if we have to, we bring her people enough to look at it and know what they're seeing."

"August'll say it's old work and none of anybody's business."

"August says many things."

"You think folks'll care about a meadow now? With fields failing and banks climbing on their backs?"

Hester looked toward town, though it was hidden by distance and the curve of the land. "Folks'll cross Main to stare at a drunk in the gutter and never once look at the ditch eating their topsoil. Let August stand over black mud in those good shoes of his. He'll sweat like any liar."

Dell rolled the tile fragment in his hand. It had warmed there, though the mud under his nails stayed cool. "You kept telling me this was about the land."

"It is."

"It's about my father too."

"Yes."

He waited for the softening after yes. There was none. That was why he believed her.

Cloud shadow did not pass, because there were hardly clouds any more, but the light altered as afternoon started wearing toward evening. It went flatter and meaner, all the shine burned off it. Dell stood and wiped his hands on his trousers, leaving black streaks that would tell on him back at the house. This time he minded. The stains looked like an answer he did not yet know how to speak.

"We should cover these holes some," he said. "Not all the way. Just enough no fool breaks an ankle."

"That fool might be us next week."

He drove the shovel under one more exposed joint. "We could take a whole length."

Hester was already shaking her head. "And let some bastard say we dug up a drain from God knows where and carried it out for show? No. The proof is the line in the ground. Leave it where it can keep accusing people."

That landed harder than if she had snapped. Dell set the shovel aside. Together they worked the dirt back in with their boots and the flat of the blade, leaving the line readable to anyone who knew to look and invisible to anyone who did not.

By the time they turned back north the heat had gone from brutal to lingering. His shoulders ached. Hester coughed twice into her handkerchief and once stopped long enough to spit and bury the spit with her heel. Dell pretended not to notice the care she took with that small privacy. He was learning there were decent ways to witness a person and indecent ones.

At the field edge she took the broken tile from him and wrapped it in the wax paper with the map.

"I want that," he said.

"You'll get it later."

"Why not now?"

"Because your mother empties your pockets when she washes your trousers, and because a shard in your hand proves less than the ditch itself. I haven't come two miles for her to find this before you've decided whether you're bringing her ground or just pain."

He could not argue. He hated that too.

Hester tucked the packet away. "Come by the bakery tomorrow if you can. If not, the day after. We'll pace it again."

"You think we need to?"

"Otto never trusted a thing he'd only paced once. He'd come home with burrs in his socks, drink his coffee cold, then tramp back out and make sure the ground hadn't fooled him. We'll pace it again."

They stopped where she would angle back toward town and he toward the house. The Vale place sat west in the long light, small and tired and still somehow bossy over the ground around it. Smoke from the cookstove went up straight for ten feet before the wind ruined it. Dell could picture Cora inside, setting beans on the table, counting in her head what was left in the jar, what could be sold, what could be mended, what could be hidden from fear until morning. If he walked in talking about Ezra and theft and tile before she had seen an inch of it, she would hear only a son dragging a dead man through the yard.

"You going to tell her?" Hester asked.

"Not all of it."

"That's not the same as lying."

"Feels close."

"Sure it does. So does lancing a boil. It still has to be done."

He looked south once more. The basin itself was invisible from here, folded back into ordinary failure. That was the part that would make telling dangerous. He could come in hot with half the truth and watch Cora brace against him before he ever got her boots on the right patch of ground. Or he could keep his mouth tighter, bring her out when there was enough light, and make the land do its own speaking.

"I thought if I found something," he said, "I'd know whether to hate him or not."

Hester's face did not soften. She looked at him the way she'd looked at the tile in the ditch: steady, unsparing, unwilling to pretty a thing just because it hurt. "That's wanting the rows straight after a blow. Otto used to come in half-choked with dust, cussing because the field still showed every crooked pass. It didn't make the land forget it."

He gave a short laugh with no humor in it. "I know."

"Your father's dead. What he put his foot to isn't. You'd better see the print plain."

Dell looked at the house again, then at the south land beyond it, and felt the shape of his choice settle with the same plainness as the tile line under dirt. He would rather know what ugly thing Ezra had done or helped do or looked away from than spend the rest of his life propping up a saint built out of silence. Saints were useless on a farm anyway. They never lifted, dug, paid, or stayed.

"All right," he said.

Hester touched the brim of her hat, a gesture so slight it might have been to the light instead of to him. Then she turned toward town, broad-backed and stooped and steady, the packet in her apron pocket knocking against her leg with each step. Dell watched until she crossed the road ditch and disappeared in the pale rise of the land.

After that he went home to whatever waited, rehearsing the smaller truth he could stand to say first: the basin was real, Otto had been right, and Cora needed to see it before anybody talked himself into a better lie. He came in after dark with damp earth still on his hands.


# Chapter 7

Cora had washed the oilcloth twice and it still held a gray bloom in the cracks, the table keeping faith with the weather instead of with her. She stood in the kitchen with the dishrag twisted tight between both hands and looked at the room August Poeschel had decided a federal man should see: stove blacked as well as cheap polish allowed, flour-sack curtains beaten and rehung, cups turned so the worst chips faced the wall, the enamel basin set mouth-down to hide the rust along the rim. None of it improved the house. It only showed that she had spent the morning trying.

August's note lay under the sugar jar where she had shoved it after breakfast. Mr. Walter Knapp of the resettlement office would call at eleven. Kindly remain on the premises. Kindly had been the word that raised the heat in her, more even than office. There was nowhere else for her to go at eleven on a Wednesday except the farm she was being asked to watch die in good order.

The wind was up enough to rasp the screen door, not enough yet to make a storm of itself. Dust moved low over the yard in nervous little drifts that gathered at the porch steps and broke apart. Out by the pump Dell had the hood off the truck again, though truck was grander than what sat there: a Ford with one fender tied on by wire and a temperament meaner than a kicked mule. He leaned in with a wrench in his fist, shirt stuck dark between his shoulders, wrists all bone and tendon. He had come in late the night before with damp earth on his hands and some tight silence he had not yet spent. When he straightened he looked older from the back than he ever did from the front.

"You want the hood down before he gets here?" he called.

"Leave it."

He wiped his forearm over his face and left a gray streak across his cheek. "He'll look at it and write down we're half junkyard."

"Then let him write true."

Dell made a face at the engine, not at her. "You want me in the house?"

"I want you civil."

"That so."

"Dell."

He set the wrench on the running board harder than he had to. "I heard you."

That was the trouble with him at seventeen. He heard accusation in everything before the words had even settled. Cora went to the door and stood with one hand on the frame. Beyond the truck the wheat fields lay pale and wrong, heads thin, the color not gold yet but a sickened green with gray worked through it, dust inside the stalk instead of merely lying on top. The south strip had blown bare in places. She could see the lighter scars from the porch. Men in offices called that exhaustion. Men who had not buried a husband in the middle of a planting year called it marginal land. She called it June.

By a quarter to eleven she had changed into the blue dress with the least shine at the seams and pinned her hair back harder than usual. The dress smelled faintly of starch and old sweat and the cedar chest. She had mended the left cuff two nights before by lamplight. Now the stitches showed because she knew where they were, and because she knew, she could not stop seeing them.

Dell came up the steps and stood just inside the kitchen, stalled there for a second before he remembered what he meant to fetch. His hair needed cutting. The dust had bleached it nearly white at the top.

"You don't have to pour him coffee," he said.

"I know that."

"I'm saying you don't."

She looked at him then. There was Ezra in the set of the mouth when he was about to make trouble for the sake of not feeling young. There was Ezra nowhere else. "You think I don't know what a man comes for when he rides out with August?"

Dell's eyes slid off hers to the stove. "Maybe he comes to see what a dead place looks like."

"Then he can use his own damn eyes."

Something like satisfaction crossed the boy's face at the curse. He nodded once and went back out, taking the sound of his boots with him. Cora lifted the kettle anyway. There were rules older than debt, older than August, and one of them said you did not let a stranger cross your threshold into heat and grit without offering coffee if you had coffee to spare. Another said a woman alone with a farm note against her name did not get to choose which rules still protected her.

The car announced itself before she saw it. Tires on dry ruts made a flat grinding complaint, then the horn gave one short official bark at the bend in the road where no horn was needed. August always sounded himself where the house could hear. Cora stepped onto the porch and wiped her palms down her skirt.

The bank car came through its own pale cloud and stopped near the pump. August climbed out first, broad in his town coat despite the heat, collar buttoned, hat brim carrying the same dust as everybody else's but somehow not submitting to it. He paused to brush his sleeves with quick exact strokes before turning to the passenger side.

Walter Knapp unfolded himself from the car with the care of a man used to trains and offices more than rutted county roads, but there was nothing soft in the way he landed. He was trim, narrow in the hips, a year or two shy of forty by the look of him, and his hat was a good one that had lost the argument with Nebraska before noon. He took from his pocket a small clothes brush with a cedar handle and whisked the crown and brim as naturally as another man might wipe his mouth. Then he tucked the brush away and gave the house the courtesy of a full look.

"Mrs. Vale," August said. "Thank you for remaining available."

"I was under the impression I lived here."

August's mouth tightened without changing shape. "This is Mr. Walter Knapp, from the federal office."

Knapp took off his hat. His hair underneath was already damp and flattened by sweat. "Mrs. Vale." His voice carried no town ownership in it. Lincoln maybe, or farther east. "I appreciate you seeing me."

She had expected a government smile, something polished and false. What she got was tiredness under discipline, which made her dislike him faster. "You came all the way out. I'd look poor company if I hid in the chicken shed."

That almost got a smile from him, but he thought better of it. His gaze moved to Dell by the truck, to the pump, to the drift packed against the north side of the porch, and back to her. He missed less than was polite.

August said, "If we can begin with the ground, Mr. Knapp."

"Ground's still there," Cora said. "I haven't mislaid it since breakfast."

Dell coughed into his fist, which could have been laughter. August ignored him with the effort of a churchgoing man ignoring a fly.

They started with the yard and the outbuildings. Knapp walked without hurrying and crouched where he wanted a closer look, then brushed his knees afterward with the same little cedar brush, persistent as a man who thought manners might still win an argument with dirt. He asked what had been planted and what had failed, and Cora answered before August could make the farm his story.

"Wheat on the north and west. Kitchen patch mostly gave up after the May blow. Hens still laying enough to keep themselves."

"Any hogs?"

"Not now."

"Milk cow?"

"Sold her in March."

He wrote that down in pencil rather than fountain pen. A notebook bound in black cloth went in and out of his hand with the ease of practice. August carried a folder instead, thick and official, papers squared so hard the edges could have cut.

At the shed Knapp looked over the harness, the hand tools hung on nails, the seed sacks rolled and tied against mice. He did not poke or smirk. That made it worse. He lifted the broken singletree, saw how Dell had wrapped the split with baling wire, and set it back exactly where it had been. In the truck bed the last of the spare parts sat in coffee tins and a wooden peach crate. Dell stepped closer before anybody asked him to, shoulder high and defensive.

"You keep this running?" Knapp said.

"Some days."

"How old are you?"

"Seventeen."

"Still in school?"

"When they can find enough children to call it that."

"Dell," Cora said.

"It's a fair answer," Knapp said, and wrote again.

The boy's face went closed. He hated being made into a line on paper. Cora hated that she understood, and that there was nothing she could do while standing three feet away.

They walked the field edge next, August speaking acreage and arrears whenever there was space for him to fit them in. Cora heard her own life become columns: past-due interest, tax exposure, yield depression. The phrases arrived clean and fat as banker fingers. Knapp stopped at a place where the topsoil had scudded off and the ground showed lighter beneath, hard and veined.

He stooped, scooped a handful, rubbed it between thumb and forefinger, and let it run out. "How deep has the drift been laying along this fence after the last blows?"

"Knee-high some mornings. Bare by supper if the wind changes."

"Any standing water anywhere on the tract after a rain?"

"Not where it does a body any good."

August said, "The well remains serviceable, but the land is no longer behaving as productive acreage ought."

Cora turned on him. "You want it productive after three years of this?"

"I want clarity."

"No. You want obedience."

Knapp straightened and slipped the notebook into his pocket. "Mrs. Vale, I'm not here to quarrel over names for weather."

"That's useful," she said. "Because weather's been here longer."

He took that too without bowing or bristling. Then he looked past her toward the south, where the field rolled away and the air above it danced with heat already though the day was not old. "Some land should not be asked for one more crop just because paper says it still belongs in a ledger."

There it was, neither threat nor contempt, only the calm sentence of a man who had seen enough ruined places to speak of them as categories. Cora felt the back of her neck go hot under her pinned hair. Beside her Dell shifted his weight. August watched nobody; he watched the process.

"It's not the land asking," she said. "It's us."

Knapp's eyes came back to hers. "Yes, ma'am. I know."

He did know, or enough of it to make the knowing intolerable.

Inside the house the air held the cooked-metal smell of the stove though the fire had gone out an hour before. Cora had opened the back window for movement and now wished she had not; dust had gathered on the sill in a new fine line while they were in the yard. Knapp stopped on the threshold, removed his hat again, and gave it another quick brushing before stepping over. The gesture was almost comical. It also told her something plain: the man could not bear grit, and yet his work led him from one grit-choked family to another all summer long. He must have gone to bed every night tasting other people's losses.

"Kitchen first is fine," he said.

"Kitchen's what there is most of."

He looked at the table, the stove, the shelves. Nothing in the room had escaped being handled thin. The blue willow plate over the door had a crack running from rim to river. Two chair seats had been caned with binder twine after the old rush gave out. August remained standing, one hand on the back of the nearest chair, holding to the form that he would not sit before being invited though he clearly meant to sit once the business began.

Knapp asked, "How many in household at present?"

"Myself and my son."

August added, "Miss Bell boards here while school remains open."

Cora could have struck him for saying her name that way, reducing Thea to a line item he had been waiting to produce.

Knapp nodded. "Teacher."

"Yes."

"May I see the occupied rooms?"

For one mean instant she thought of saying no, just to watch what August's face did. Then she thought of what refusal would be written down. She led them through the front room first, where the sofa springs pushed through the upholstery under the antimacassar and Ezra's old chair sat by the cold stove like a witness nobody had dismissed. Dust had worked into the clock glass again. She hated that they would see that before they saw anything else. The house had sweated fear for them.

Her bedroom came next. Knapp kept his eyes workmanlike, taking in the bed, the trunk, the washstand, the dresser drawer that stuck unless lifted as it pulled. He noted the patched mosquito netting and the water stain in the ceiling corner. He kept his face empty and offered no pity. That lack of performance stripped her harder than pity would have.

Then Thea's room. Cora opened the door and stood aside while something private and frightened scraped its nails down her spine. The room was neat in Thea's particular way. Books were stacked square on the crate by the bed, school copybooks tied with string, a blouse with fresh collar stays hanging from the peg, and on the sill sat a little saucer with three hatpins in it and one button waiting to be sewn back on something worth saving.

Knapp took it in with a glance no longer than the others. "Miss Bell pays monthly?"

"She does."

August said, "Her board has been material to the household's cash balance."

Cora did not look at him because if she had, Dell would have seen too much in her face. Thea's room, their room-not-their-room, was reduced to board, money, utility. Yet that was what kept the door respectable. That was how women survived here, by pretending every need arrived with a receipt.

Knapp wrote a line and closed the notebook. "That will do."

The pantry undid her more than the bedrooms, not because there was less in it than there ought to be; she already knew that. It was the decent tone he used when he asked whether she had enough flour for the month, enough jars, enough soap, enough coal oil to last if the road went bad, and she answered him like a woman applying for charity though she had grown much of what had ever entered that room. There were beans in a crock, two sacks of flour with one already low, salt pork almost gone, coffee enough if she mixed chicory in, and soap shaved thin to save it. Every answer felt like laying her underskirts on the line in front of the bank.

At last he said, "Thank you. We can sit down."

August had been waiting for that sentence from the moment he stepped out of the car.

Cora poured coffee into three cups because Dell had gone back outside and because she would not force him into the room for the signing of his own uprooting. Her hand stayed steady until August opened the folder. Then the cups clicked in their saucers. The sound seemed loud enough to bring the chickens up under the window.

The papers were not a single sheet but a clipped stack of forms, typed pages, a county plat, and something with the bank letterhead at top. August set them on the oilcloth where she had scrubbed until her wrists ached that morning. Knapp laid his hat on his knee and folded his hands.

"Mrs. Vale," August said, "Mr. Knapp's office is prepared to consider this tract under the resettlement and land-retirement program, contingent on cooperation."

"Land-retirement," she repeated. "That's a nice clean way to say bury it."

"It is a way to say it without melodrama."

"You'd know about that."

Knapp broke in before August could answer. "Let me say it plain. Some farms out here are too far gone in credit, water, or soil to carry a family without stripping the family first. If a place can be taken out of production before a sale scatters everything you own, there can sometimes be some room left for the living."

"Room where?"

He did not blink. "That depends on openings, kin, county relief arrangements, and whether a receiving place can be found. I can't promise a better farm. I can sometimes prevent a worse end."

There was the mercy of it, small as a hand on a fevered head and just as helpless against the disease.

August tapped the top sheet. "With your signature, the matter can proceed cooperatively. There would be appraisal, inventory, and a voluntary relinquishment of occupancy when the transfer is arranged."

She leaned forward. The paper smelled of dust and ribbon ink and August's office. VOLUNTARY COOPERATION STATEMENT was typed across the top in hard black letters. Lower down: exhausted acreage. submarginal use. occupant consents to vacate upon notice. Her own house, translated into words fit for a shovel.

"Voluntary," she said.

Knapp said, "I know how that reads."

"Do you."

"Yes."

"Because it reads like a man standing in my kitchen with the bank at his elbow and asking whether I'd care to bless my own funeral."

August said, "Nobody is asking you for blessing. We are asking for recognition of fact."

"Fact is my feet are on this floor."

"Fact is the note stands at twelve hundred and eighty dollars plus interest, the taxes are behind, the crop is failing, and the town cannot maintain fictions indefinitely."

She heard Dell outside at the truck, not moving now but listening. The shame of that hit low and dirty. She lowered her voice anyway, which made the room feel even smaller.

"And if I sign, what do I get besides better wording for losing it?"

Knapp answered before August could. "A chance at managed removal instead of a forced sale. Time to sort what can be moved. Possibly transportation help. Possibly placement if something opens. Some priority with relief, depending on county cooperation."

"Possibly," she said.

"I won't lie to you."

"No. You'll tell it neat."

He let that stand. August did not.

"The alternative is not dignity, Mrs. Vale. The alternative is deterioration followed by action under the note."

"You mean auction."

"If necessary."

She looked again at the form. August had placed the pen beside it already uncapped, a little black thing with a gold nib, ready as a tooth. What snagged her was not the threat. August had threatened before. It was the eagerness under the smoothness. He wanted her name on this paper too badly. He wanted it with the hungry patience of a man who needs a door opened from the inside because he does not trust the lock from his side.

"Why now?" she asked.

August's gaze did not shift. "Because conditions are now plain."

"They've been plain all spring."

"Not to the federal office."

"Then why do you need me to say it for them?"

Silence. Knapp's head turned, not much, but enough. Under the cooperation form another sheet had worked loose in the clip, showing a purple county stamp and a date line struck darker than the rest from a second hard hit of the typewriter. August placed two fingers flat over that corner, almost caressing it, and slid the stack square.

"Because that is how orderly transitions are made," he said.

She nearly laughed. The word orderly in this kitchen, with dust in the sugar and a dead man's chair cooling the front room. It would have been funny if it had not been so ugly.

Knapp said, "Mrs. Vale, I've been in places where they waited too long because pride and grief were doing the talking, and then there was no furniture left worth moving and no stock left worth selling and the children went into town in the same clothes they had slept in for a week. I don't think you're a fool. I don't think this is easy. I think this may be the last moment in which somebody can keep a little shape around what is happening to you."

That should have softened her. It should have. Instead it brought a hard bright anger up through her chest because the man was offering shape, treating shape as all a life amounted to once the wheat failed.

"What if I'm not interested in a little shape around it?" she said. "What if I'm interested in not calling my farm dead before you've even dug the hole?"

Knapp rubbed his thumb once over the edge of his hat brim. "Then I would tell you the hole has been under your feet a while."

August seized the moment. "You have delayed enough, Cora. Sign the form."

He used her given name only when he meant to show he believed ownership and intimacy were cousins. She hated that too.

"No."

The word came out flat, with no speech and no higher ground in it, only the fact of her mouth making a wall.

August's expression changed at last, not into anger but into a colder precision. "I advise you to reconsider."

"Do your advising elsewhere."

"Mrs. Vale," Knapp said quietly.

She turned on him. "If you want me off this place, put your names under it. I won't help either of you pretend I chose the spade."

The room held still. Outside, something loose on the truck tapped once against metal in the wind.

August capped the pen with great care. "Very well."

Knapp said, "Mr. Poeschel."

August did not even turn toward him. "In the absence of cooperation, I will move the note forward. You have ten days before I post formal foreclosure notice and begin publication toward sale."

Dell made a sound outside, not a word, just air hitting anger.

Cora stared at August. The ten days did not mean a deputy on the porch by next week. She knew enough to know that. It meant notices, posting, county steps, men coming to price a life by the piece. But ten days meant he had brought the threat sharpened and waiting in the folder before she ever refused. He had come wanting either her surrender or his excuse.

"You had that ready already," she said.

"I had several eventualities ready."

"Christ."

Knapp looked tired suddenly clear through, the heat finally reaching the place in him he kept separate for work. "That's not what I came for."

"But you'll ride away with it," Cora said.

He did not answer. That was answer enough.

August gathered the papers into their stack. The county-stamped sheet vanished again under the plat, leaving her land reduced to lines and fractions, to home place and north strip, with no room anywhere for Ezra's rope in the barn or Dell's bare feet in summer or the place on the porch rail where Thea set her hand when she laughed and forgot to guard herself.

August stood. "I will expect either payment sufficient to cure the arrearage or a change in your disposition before the tenth day."

"You'll expect till hell freezes."

"Hell," August said, settling his hat on his head, "has already shown an unfortunate gift for weather."

That was the nearest thing to profanity or poetry she had ever heard from him. It left a bad taste in the room.

Knapp rose more slowly. He took his hat, then seemed to think better of something he had nearly said. What came instead was, "Mrs. Vale, if you do change your mind, send word through the bank before the notice goes up. A posted paper brings out a certain kind of man."

"I know the kind."

He looked at the oilcloth, at the coffee he had barely touched, at the place on the shelf where the plates did not match. Then he nodded once, a gesture too respectful to be pity and too late to help, and followed August to the porch.

Cora stood where she was until the screen door slapped shut behind them. Only then did she realize her palms were wet. She had pressed them so hard into the edge of the table that the oilcloth's cracked seam had stamped itself in a red line across the heel of each hand.

Dell came into the doorway from outside and stopped there. His face had gone bloodless under the dust. "Ten days."

"To notice."

"Same difference."

"No. It's not."

"It's if you've got no money."

That one landed because it was plain, with no rhetoric in it, only the truth spoken by her son in front of the place where those men had sat.

"Go water the hens," she said.

"I'm not eight."

"Then act older."

He held himself there another second, jaw working, then shoved back through the screen and went around the house hard enough to startle the chickens into noise.

Cora moved to the porch. August and Knapp were by the car, thinking themselves out of earshot because men in town shoes always guessed wrong about country distances. The wind carried words in pieces.

"...cannot run every file by sentiment," August was saying.

Knapp answered lower. "I said nothing about sentiment. I said pressure this blunt may spoil cooperation."

"There is no cooperation to spoil."

"Then what you want is consent after the fact."

August brushed dust from the car door with his handkerchief. "What I want is a clean line through an irregular situation."

Irregular. The word came thin and bright into her like a fishhook.

Knapp said, "Out here there are no clean lines."

"There have to be on paper."

The car doors shut. Gravel ground under the tires. They drove off with August at the wheel and Knapp turned half around in the seat, looking back once at the house with the restless air of a man who had forgotten something he could not name. Then the road bent, and they were gone.

The kitchen after them smelled of coffee and men's wool and the hot metal of August's pen where it had lain a minute on the oilcloth. Cora went inside, lifted the cup Knapp had used, and saw a neat ring of dust settled in the bottom where the coffee had cooled untouched. She threw it into the dishpan hard enough to chip the rim another sliver.

By the time Thea came home the light had thickened toward evening, not storm-dark, just the long yellow flattening that made every object in the house look tired before its time. Cora had scrubbed the table again. August's papers had left no mark she could see, but she kept wiping the same square, trying to work the typed words back up through the cloth by sheer friction.

Thea opened the front door with her satchel against her hip and stopped at the sight of her. School dust lay fine on her dark hair and had dulled the blue of her collar. One glove was tucked into her belt because she had not had patience to button it back on. She looked over Cora's shoulder at the table, the untouched supper things, the cup in the dishpan with the fresh chip in it.

"He came."

"He did."

Thea set the satchel down quietly. "August too."

"Like a preacher with a coffin catalog."

That wrong-time laugh flashed out of Thea before she could stop it, brief and shocked. Then she pressed her lips together. "I'm sorry. I know that's not funny."

"It was a little."

Thea crossed the room. She did not reach for Cora at once. Dell might be at the window, in the yard, on the porch. The house had thin walls and a boy's ears. Instead she took off her hat and set it on the sideboard, buying them both a second. "What did they want exactly?"

Cora pulled August's note from under the sugar jar, then thought better of that and put it back. "A form. Voluntary cooperation, they called it. Voluntary land retirement. Voluntary vacating my own house when told."

Thea's face altered by degrees, not dramatic, just every careful line in it drawing tighter. "And if you signed?"

"I might get to lose everything in a cleaner order."

"That sounds like Knapp."

"He said it plainer than August. Which made him worse."

Thea leaned one hand on the table. Her fingers were ink-smudged at the side from school papers. "Did he offer actual placement or only the promise of being considered for one?"

"Considered. Possibly. If kin could be found, if county relief, if some office somewhere had room on a shelf for me and Dell."

"God."

"That from you?"

"I teach fractions to six children in a room built for twenty-eight. I'm allowed one God when the federal government turns human beings into a filing problem."

The line should have comforted her. Instead Cora felt the humiliation rise again, hotter because Thea could name the machinery of it. "August wanted the signature more than Knapp did."

Thea looked up sharply. "What do you mean?"

Cora gave her the quick ugly pieces only: the walk through the rooms, the pantry counted out loud, August setting down the pen before she had even answered, the second paper under the cooperation form half-hidden in the clip with a purple county stamp on it and the date line mashed darker than the rest. His fingers had gone over that corner the minute she asked why he needed her. Then, out by the car, the word irregular. She kept her voice low and fast, trying to get through the facts before they changed shape on her tongue and ruined whatever use they still had.

Thea listened with the stillness that always made Cora feel both steadied and seen too thoroughly. Twice she started to interrupt and did not. When Cora finished, the only sounds were the screen door ticking in the breeze and Dell somewhere out by the shed knocking metal against metal for no good reason except that he was young and angry.

"He said irregular," Cora said. "Could've meant the farm. Could've meant that paper. I don't know."

Thea's brow furrowed. "A county stamp?"

"Purple. I only saw the corner."

Thea drew in breath through her nose and let it out slowly. Under pressure she always got more precise for a moment; grammar became a handhold. "That might be nothing more than bad office work. It might be a filing copy he didn't want read beside the form. What I know is he didn't want Knapp and you looking at the same page at the same time."

Cora pressed the wet rag flat against the table. Water seeped between her fingers and darkened the oilcloth. "So I'm not crazy."

"I didn't say that either," Thea said, and the edge of a wrong-time smile touched her mouth before it went. "I said suspicion is earned. Certainty isn't. Not yet."

There was the teacher in her and the hungry private woman beneath the teacher both at once, the one who could parse a sentence until it bled and the one who knew exactly where power hid when it dressed itself as manners.

Cora laid the rag aside. Her hands ached from gripping it. "He wanted my name on the top page so nobody had to turn the rest."

"Maybe," Thea said. "Or maybe he only wanted the quickest road open to him. Either way, don't bless it."

For a moment neither moved. Cora could feel the whole day sitting on her skin: Knapp's notebook, August's pen, Dell outside listening to men price the walls. Thea was close enough that Cora could smell chalk and the clean stale scent of schoolhouse dust in her clothes, different from farm dust, finer somehow, with chalk, paper, and old bindings all ground into it. She wanted to put her head against Thea's shoulder like a tired child. She wanted to tear the kitchen apart with her hands. Wanting both at once made her feel mean.

Thea touched the back of the chair between them instead of touching her. "Come upstairs a minute."

"Dell's outside."

"I know."

"He heard enough already."

"Then sit down at least before you fall down and give him another thing to remember."

That was not tender. It was better than tender. Cora sat because her knees had begun to feel hollow without her admitting it. The chair creaked under her. Thea went to the stove, poured what was left of the morning's water into the kettle, and set it on to warm though neither of them wanted more coffee. She needed something for her hands.

"What did Dell say?" she asked.

"Asked if ten days was the same as done."

"And what did you tell him?"

"That it was notice, not done."

"Was that for him or for you?"

Cora gave her a look.

Thea held it. "I'm not trying to be cruel."

"You can stop being careful with me for one hour."

"I do stop," Thea said, and there was the private edge in her now, the one Cora knew from the dark. "You simply prefer me brave and useful over honest and frightened."

Cora turned that over and hated how much of it was true. The kettle ticked as it heated. Outside Dell swore at something mechanical, the profanity thin in the evening air.

"I'm frightened," Cora said at last. Saying it plain cost more than any curse she had spent all day. "Not of leaving. Of letting him write the leaving. Of putting my own hand under his story."

Thea's face changed then. The hard schoolhouse control in it eased and the other self came closer, the one who could look at Cora and make the room alter around her. "I know."

She reached out finally and laid two fingers against Cora's wrist where it rested on the table. Light pressure only. Anyone opening the door would see nothing that required naming. Cora felt the touch all the way up to her shoulder.

"We could still go," Thea said softly. "Before he posts anything. Before the town gets its mouth on it. Lincoln or even North Platte first. We could take the train from the spur and let him sell dust and nails."

There it was: not the interview itself, not the whole life folded inside that letter, but the road out of Morrow set on the table between them like a second form waiting for signature.

Cora closed her hand over Thea's fingers for one heartbeat, maybe two, then let go. "Not yet."

Thea looked down. "Because of Dell."

"Because of August."

"Cora."

"He wants a clean line through something dirty. He can have the dirt first."

Thea laughed once again, without humor this time. "That's the most alarming thing you've said to me all month."

"You knew I was alarming when you moved in."

"I knew you were stubborn. That's not always the same."

The kettle began to murmur. Thea rose to take it off before it boiled. She poured the hot water into a chipped bowl, found the cleanest cloth from the line by the stove, and wrung it out. Then she came behind Cora and laid the heat at the back of her neck where the pins had held her hair too tight all day.

Cora shut her eyes, not from relief exactly but from the shock of being touched where those men had looked but not seen. The cloth smelled of soap and sun and the last dusting of wind from the line.

"He inspected your room," she said.

Thea's hand paused. "Mine."

"Yours. Asked if you paid monthly."

After a second Thea said, "Did you tell him yes?"

"What else."

"Nothing else?"

Cora opened her eyes and turned her head just enough to catch Thea's face in profile. "Nothing else."

Thea nodded. The cloth moved again, slower now. "Then he saw a room. Let him die knowing only that much."

Something in Cora almost broke on that sentence, not because it was grand but because it was small and practical and exactly the size of the mercy available to them.

The screen door banged and Dell came in without knocking, carrying a chicken-water bucket and a mood like weather. He stopped when he saw them, not because anything was visible in the room beyond a schoolteacher standing behind his mother with a cloth in her hand, but because boys his age could smell currents they did not understand and hated not understanding.

"You making supper or holding meeting?" he asked.

Thea moved away first. "Supper, if you're willing to eat what there is."

"I'm willing."

"Then shell peas," Cora said.

"There aren't enough peas to shell."

"Then snap beans and quit talking."

Dell set down the bucket and took the bowl from the shelf with exaggerated martyrdom. Yet the compliance in it eased something by half an inch. Thea went to the pantry for the beans. Cora watched them both, the boy long and angry and still growing out of his father in pieces, the woman narrow-faced and dusty and careful in every public gesture. This was what August's paper had called household occupancy: this, and not the hands, not the labor, not the dead, not the nights.

Dell said, not looking up, "You signing anything?"

"No."

"Good."

The bean snapped in his fingers with a wet crisp sound. "Then what?"

Cora looked at the sugar jar, the note under it, the table scrubbed raw enough for company and still not clean. August's word came back to her, irregular; maybe the land, maybe the paper under his hand. Either way, it had not sounded like weather.

"Then I go see him," she said.

Dell raised his head. "At the bank?"

"Yes."

Thea set the bean bowl down too carefully. "Soon."

"Soon enough."

"Don't go in there only angry."

"I won't."

Thea's eyes narrowed. She knew the distinction she heard in that answer and did not like it. "What then?"

Cora folded the dishrag into a square, once, twice, until it sat in her palm like a thing made for striking. "I'm done letting him talk over paper I haven't seen."

Dell stared from one woman to the other. "Paper what?"

Cora met his eyes. He was not a child, and he was not yet ready either. That was the misery of sons. "Bank paper. Your father's paper. Mine now."

He looked ready to demand more and did not know which question to choose first. That was good. Let him feel confusion for one evening. She had carried enough of it alone.

Thea said, very measured now, "Then don't go in there naming sins. Ask to see every page he laid under that form. Make him spread the stack out in the open."

"He'll know why I'm asking."

"Good. Let him." Thea glanced toward Dell, then back. "Ask for the note. The plat. The county-stamped copy. Ask why he needed your name before Knapp had seen the whole pile."

Cora thought of August's fingers flattening on the form, of the car by the pump, of Walter Knapp, decent enough to be unbearable, telling her there might still be shape left for the living. She thought of the word voluntary and felt her stomach turn.

"And if he won't?"

"Then he can refuse in words," Thea said. "That's worth more than another guess at this table."

"He can lie to my face then," Cora said. "I'll stand there and listen close."

Thea watched her for a long second. Then, to Cora's surprise, the corner of her mouth lifted, not because anything was amusing but because she recognized the choice and could not stop herself from respecting it.

"All right," she said. "Then when you go, go with the exact words. Don't go in there with rage. Rage is what he expects from a debtor woman. Questions are harder on men like August."

"I can ask questions."

"You can ask them like nails."

"That too."

Dell, still baffled, said, "Somebody want to tell me what in hell either of you is talking about?"

Neither answered at once. Beans snapped. The kettle settled. Outside, the light went thinner over the yard and the wheat beyond it looked almost silver with dust.

Cora reached under the sugar jar and took August's note out from beneath it. She folded it once and slid it into the pocket of her apron. The paper warmed fast against her belly. It was barely any warmth, but it was enough.

"Eat first," she said. "Then we'll see what gets told."

Thea's satchel still leaned against the sideboard where she had dropped it, buckled over school papers she had not yet sorted. Cora left it there. One paper at a time was all the evening could stand.


# Chapter 8

Seven desks still stood in rows beneath the schoolhouse windows. Three more had been shoved to the back wall after the McClearys and both Rausch boys went west in the night without waiting to tell anybody goodbye. Thea fought the swollen lock long enough for the key to stick twice before the schoolhouse breathed stale chalk and old dust at her when the door gave. The Nebraska map hung crooked above the stove. A thumbprint of gray dirt had blurred the panhandle. Somebody, months ago, had carved a careful D into the lid of the back desk and then worried a groove through the tail of it with a pocketknife.

Inside her satchel, under the attendance ledger, lay the school board's note telling her to prepare a final inventory of books, slates, hinges, stove pipe, and salvageable furniture. The handwriting belonged to Mrs. Byers, who had once brought lemon cakes for Christmas programs and now wrote as if the room had already become lumber. Thea kept the note folded. If she opened it first thing, the day would belong to that hand. She set her satchel on the desk, unhooked the windows, and swept narrow paths between the benches with the broom kept behind the cloak hooks. Dust lifted, glittered in the flattened yellow light, and settled back onto the floorboards as if it had nowhere else left to go.

She wrote the date on the blackboard. Beneath it she wrote, in a firmer hand than she felt: Spelling. Arithmetic. Dinner. Song. The word Dinner looked faintly ridiculous among the rest, which was part of the point. Yesterday she had sent the remaining children home with instructions to bring whatever food could be spared and no apologies tied to it. It had been a bold thing to write in a town where every egg had a cash value and every loaf already belonged partly to somebody else. Still, if the school was going to die, she was not going to let it do so by inches and account books.

Irene Dvorak came first, eleven years old and already argumentative in the manner of a woman twice her age, with her dead brother's suspenders clipped over a flour-sack dress because she did not trust buttons to hold. She stamped her shoes on the threshold, sent up a cuff of powder, and said, "Are we truly having lessons, or are we only clearing out?"

"We're having lessons," Thea said. "You may save disappointment for later."

Irene considered that and nodded as if she had granted permission. She went to her desk and took out a pencil sharpened with such fury it looked bitten at the tip.

Opal Jensen followed with her lunch tied in a blue-checked rag and the same chipped front tooth she worried with her tongue whenever she was thinking hard. After her came Paulie Reed, who smelled faintly of axle grease even at eight because his uncle had him under a truck at dawn more often than in church, his cowlick standing up through the dust like wire. Mabel Kruse arrived last among the children, long-legged and thirteen and mortified by nearly everything, with a paring knife in her pocket for pencil work and a hem let down twice in the same month.

They looked at the board, at Dinner, at the room with its back-row ghost desks, and then at Thea. She felt the whole morning balance there. If she faltered, the day would collapse into the dry official business waiting inside her satchel.

"Copy your spelling words," she said. "And if anybody asks, tell them I haven't gone soft. I'm merely lowering standards in a historically memorable fashion."

That earned her what she had wanted: Opal's startled bark of laughter, Mabel pretending not to smile, Paulie climbing into his seat too fast and nearly tipping it over.

Dell came in without knocking, which had always been Dell's method with buildings that had once belonged to him. He had grown too tall for the room years ago, but he still entered it as if he expected his younger body to be waiting somewhere inside. His hair had gone almost white at the ends from dust and sun, and he had a burlap sack slung over one shoulder that clinked when he dropped it by the stove. Nails, hinges, and a bent latch spilled half out of the mouth.

"Found these in the shed out back," he said. "Thought maybe the board would want to count every damned screw before it lets the place rot."

Thea did not look at the carved D in the back desk. "Language."

Paulie grinned. Mabel rolled her eyes in the superior manner of the newly old.

Dell leaned one shoulder against the wall and looked everywhere except at the open desks. "I ain't staying. Mrs. Blum said she'd be along."

"Then you may not stay from the back desk," Thea said. "If you're going to haunt the place, do it properly."

The children's faces turned toward him. The room waited to see if he would bolt. Dell's mouth twitched with unwilling amusement. He crossed to the back row, folded himself into the desk with his knees almost against his chest, and muttered, "Seat got smaller."

"You got larger."

"Wasn't my doing."

That did it. Even Mabel laughed outright. Something in the room eased. The pressure did not disappear. It merely shifted a half inch off their throats.

Thea gave them words that belonged to no test and would help none of them with county advancement: weather, thimble, machinery, meadow, necessary. Irene objected to necessary on principle. Paulie inserted an extra a into meadow and defended it stoutly. Mabel misspelled machinery the first time and went red enough to show through the dust on her cheeks. Dell, from the back desk, kept his eyes on the scarred wood and still supplied answers under his breath before anybody else could reach them.

"If you're going to cheat," Thea said, "you might at least do it in a way that flatters the teacher."

"I wasn't cheating."

"You were vibrating the answer into the room like a telegraph wire."

"I was talking to myself."

"Then say something less useful."

He ducked his head, but she saw the corner of his mouth give. Dell in company always carried himself like a boy walking with a nail in his boot. If he slowed down enough to take it out, somebody might notice where he hurt. Yet when she set Paulie to the board with a column of subtraction and he froze halfway through the borrowing, Dell could not help himself. He got out of the desk, took the chalk, and worked the problem in three quick strokes, all angles and impatience.

"You don't carry the one there," he said to Paulie, not looking at him. "You'll gum the whole business."

"Mr. Vale," Thea said.

"I ain't a mister."

"No. But you're also not a substitute teacher."

Paulie peered at the figures. "You write mean."

"So do you."

"Enough," Thea said, though she was smiling when she turned back to the class. "If Mr. Not-a-Mister Vale would like to return to his illegally occupied seat, the lawful business of education may continue."

Dell snorted once through his nose and sat down again. He left the solution on the board. The chalk dust on his fingers made his hands look older than the rest of him.

By ten the room had warmed. Flies worried the window corners. Thea set the arithmetic slates aside and took out the dog-eared reader with the loose spine, but the lesson kept slipping free of her. Paulie asked whether buffalo were truly as numerous as the book claimed or whether writers lied from habit. Irene wanted to know why Nebraska had a legislature if August Poeschel decided everything worth deciding. Mabel, who had been quiet all morning, said the train west of Holdrege carried whole families on the roof now according to her cousin's postcard, and Opal asked whether California smelled different from here or only looked it. Ordinary questions, absurd questions, frightened questions wearing their work clothes. Thea answered what she could and deflected what she could not. She heard herself doing it, the careful classroom balancing act, and hated how much practice she had at making uncertainty sound civilized.

At noon she pushed the front table against the wall beneath the blackboard and laid out the food as it arrived. A loaf from Hester's bakery appeared first, still warm enough to dampen the towel around it, dark crust cracked along the top where the oven had caught it hard. Then came a stoneware jar of pickled cucumbers from Mrs. Jensen, who did not come in, only handed them through the door with beet-red knuckles and a muttered, "Use them up before they go soft." Opal untied her rag bundle to reveal two cold boiled potatoes and half an onion. Paulie had brought three radishes with the greens still on and an apple the size of a ball bearing. Irene, affronted by scarcity, produced a jar of gooseberry preserves wrapped in yesterday's newspaper as if unveiling silver.

Hester Blum arrived carrying a tin of butterless biscuits against her stomach, her apron striped with flour and her dust cough tucked away somewhere behind her ribs by what looked like force of character alone. She set the tin down and took in the table with one sweep of her sharp old eyes.

"That'll pass for a feast if nobody gets proud," she said. "Miss Bell, if this is school policy from now on, I should've hired on years ago."

"The pay is worse than you think," Thea said.

"So is mine."

Paulie reached for a biscuit before anybody had sat down. Hester slapped the back of his hand with two dry fingers, not hard enough to sting.

"You'll keep all five of those if you wait thirty seconds," she said. "A man who steals hot bread makes a fool of himself in public. I've seen it happen."

"Who?"

"Otto, twice. One time with raspberry jam down his shirt."

That gave them another laugh, and because the laughter belonged to bread and not to survival it rang differently in the room. Thea felt it in the boards under her shoes.

The second adult to arrive was Cora.

She came in with a lard tin wrapped in a clean dish towel and the look she wore in town when she intended to give nobody a loose thread to pull. Her hair had been pinned up in a hurry and one ash-blond strand had already worked free by her ear. Fine dust lay in the seams of her cuffs. Thea noticed that before she noticed anything else. She always noticed what the weather had managed to do to Cora.

"Dell forgot his dinner on the table," Cora said. "So either you feed him or he starts gnawing the furniture."

"I don't," Dell said.

"You would if the day ran long enough."

Her voice was spare and flat enough for anybody listening, just Mrs. Vale being practical in Miss Bell's schoolhouse. That was the trouble with loving her in daylight. Every safe sentence came wrapped around the one Thea could not answer.

Thea took the tin from her. The handle bit into her palm through the towel. Inside were hard-boiled eggs already shelled and halved, the yolks mashed with a little mustard and more care than the town had any business permitting itself. Six eggs. Thea knew exactly what six eggs meant at the Vale house now. She said only, "Thank you, Mrs. Vale."

Cora's fingers brushed hers once at the handoff. The touch was brief and dry as china, the sort a woman might make passing a teacup, and it still flashed through Thea hard enough to sharpen the whole room. Secrecy had trained her nerves to appoint witnesses everywhere. Hester had turned to cut the loaf and was talking to Irene about whether gooseberries deserved sugar. Dell was prying open the biscuit tin with an expression of concentrated innocence. Even so Thea could not stop the stupid leap in her chest, or the sharper feeling under it: resentment that gratitude had to sound like schoolroom propriety when last week Cora's mouth had been against the inside of her wrist.

Cora glanced once at the table. "Looks respectable."

"That's more than can be said for the singing to come," Hester said.

"Who's singing?"

"All of them. It's the price of biscuits."

"Then God help the biscuits," Cora said.

There it was, brief as the touch had been: the line of dry humor Thea knew from lamplight and bed-edge and not from classrooms. She wanted to look up too fast, which was exactly why she did not.

They ate at desks because the wind had risen outside and sent dust scratching along the south wall in low steady passes. The children traded food in solemn violation of every ordinary rule: a potato for half a radish, a biscuit for a spoon of gooseberry, an egg half for a pickle. Hester spread preserves with the back of a knife and passed pieces down the row. Cora, after a token refusal, sat on the windowsill with her hat in her lap and peeled the shell fragments from the towel one by one, a small practical occupation for hands that had no safe place to rest. Dell pretended not to watch whether she ate enough.

For ten minutes the talk was not about departure, debt, or weather. Paulie wanted to know whether coyotes would eat soap if given the chance. Irene claimed her uncle in Broken Bow had once sung so badly a mule kicked through a fence to escape him. Mabel, after prolonged resistance, admitted she had read half a novel hidden inside her civics book and did not regret it. Hester said novels were the only respectable place left for foolishness. Cora said foolishness had always found more economical rooms than that. Thea listened to them and felt, with a kind of ache she had not earned, how quickly a table could begin to resemble a town if enough people sat down without an agenda.

The bad song began because Paulie asked for it with his mouth still full and because Hester, who enjoyed disorder as long as somebody else had to sweep it up, insisted the room could not call itself a schoolhouse on its last decent day without music. Thea chose "Red River Valley" not because it was wise but because everyone knew at least part of it and because she had no strength for pretending the word leaving had not set up house in Morrow already.

"If you sing it like a funeral," she said, "I will mark you all absent in spirit."

"Can you do that?" Opal asked.

"With a sufficiently vindictive pen."

They started too low. Mabel went flat on purpose until Irene elbowed her. Paulie rushed the words, then slowed so much on the next line he nearly toppled the whole thing into a hymn. Dell tried to keep out of it and failed after the second verse when Hester fixed him with a look older than the state and said, "You've got a sound box same as anybody. Use it."

"I'd rather dig fence posts."

"That can be arranged after you sing."

So he sang, grudgingly, with that adolescent roughness still undecided whether to become a man's voice or break apart trying. It made the others laugh, then steadied them. By the time they reached the final chorus they were not good, but they were loud, and there was a difference. Cora had bent her head, smiling into the edge of her hat. When she looked up once, just once, Thea saw open amusement there instead of the defended set she carried through bank offices and public roads. It was almost enough to make the room dangerous.

The song ended in a clatter of wrong notes and laughter. Hester gave a single dry clap. "There," she said. "If the bank closes the school after that, it's acting out of mercy."

Even Dell laughed at that, though he bit it off quickly and dragged his thumb along the carved tail of the D until the desk gave a faint dry squeak. Then he lowered his face over the lid as if the old seat might still pass for cover. Thea saw the motion. She said nothing. He had scratched that letter years ago with all the secret seriousness boys gave to wood they expected would keep them forever. Now his hand dwarfed it.

After dinner the school day thinned in the ordinary way. Thea read aloud while Paulie swung one leg and lost the thread every third sentence. Mabel copied a page of penmanship without complaint, which was practically a public miracle. Irene and Opal quarreled over whether Nebraska rivers counted if they had gone to mud. Dell fixed the loose latch on the north window with one of the screws from his sack and refused to admit he was doing maintenance for a building slated for death.

Then the hour arrived when children begin to lean toward the door no matter what a teacher says. Thea dismissed them one by one with leftover biscuit wrapped in paper and instructions to bring back the borrowed readers only if the readers survived summer. Irene marched out with her suspenders crosswise and her preserves jar empty. Opal tucked the onion skin from dinner into her pocket because she hated waste even in its smallest form. Paulie left still singing the wrong line of the chorus. Mabel paused on the threshold long enough to say, without looking directly at Thea, "It wasn't bad, today," and then was gone before the reply could embarrass either of them.

Cora had left earlier, after gathering the towel and the empty tin. She had said she needed lamp wicks from the mercantile and had not looked back from the doorway. Thea had watched the dust mark her shoulders through the window until she turned the corner. Wanting more than that from a public leave-taking felt childish. She wanted it anyway.

Dell stayed.

He remained in the back desk after the others had gone, one boot braced against the floor rung, scraping chalk dust into pale crescents with his thumbnail.
