The room around his long body looked built for a version of him already missing. Thea stacked copybooks and waited him out. She had taught enough boys on the verge of manhood to know that if she asked the real question first, she would get only the lie.

"You may go," she said at last. "School isn't a prison unless the county improves its funding considerably."

"I know."

"Then why are you still here?"

He shrugged without lifting his eyes. "Seat's cooler than outside."

"Liar."

"Maybe."

He ran the pad of his thumb over the carved D again. "Forgot I'd cut that."

"You were very proud of your initial."

"Didn't have much else to brag on at ten."

"You had long division."

"Hell of a courtship."

She laughed before she meant to. Dell glanced up, surprised into almost-smiling. Beneath the wariness and the adolescent meanness he put on like another shirt, there was still the boy who used to sit scowling through recitation and then hand in copywork neat enough to shame half the room. He hated being seen in that continuity.

"You miss it," Thea said, softer now.

He straightened too fast, the way he did whenever tenderness got near him with its hands out. "I didn't say that."

"No."

"It's just a room."

"Yes."

He looked around at the maps, the scarred desktops, the cracked glass over the alphabet strip, all of it. "Thought it'd feel better when it shut," he said. "Thought I'd be glad to see the end of one more thing telling me where to sit." He rubbed his palm down his thigh as if trying to wipe the words off after he'd spoken them. "It doesn't. That's all."

Thea set the last copybook down. "No," she said. "It doesn't."

He absorbed that in silence. Outside, a wagon rattled past on the road toward the depot, the iron tire catching stones. Somewhere farther off a screen door slapped twice and kept slapping in the wind.

"Mrs. Blum's got men coming tonight," Dell said finally. "She's making bread and plans at the same time, which ought to be illegal."

"Plans for what?"

He gave her a sideways look, measuring what he wanted to tell and what belonged elsewhere. "South of our west field. By the old basin. She says if we wait till July the ground will set like crockery and stay shut. Couple fellows from north road might come with spades if their wives let them. August sure as hell won't like it."

So there it was, not rumor anymore but shape. Hester and Dell had found something with enough reality to gather bodies around it. Thea thought of Cora sitting on the windowsill with the egg towel in her hands, saying almost nothing because public rooms made speech expensive. August had given them ten days to auction. Dell had given them a place south of the fields and a night when bodies with spades might gather. Every road in town presently led either outward or toward a fight.

"Does your mother know?" Thea asked.

"Knows enough." Dell stood up, the desk groaning under the release. "I told you because you'll hear it by supper and think folks have gone touched. Maybe they have. Still."

"Still."

He picked up the sack of screws and latch pieces. At the door he paused without turning. "You ought to keep the books a while longer," he said. "Not for lessons. Just because."

"That's not a category the county recognizes."

"To hell with categories."

Then he was out, long strides down the path, hatless in the glare, heading toward the bakery and whatever Hester meant to begin.

The room changed when he left. Schoolrooms always did once children were truly gone; they became a species of stage after the actors had stripped the place of noise. Thea moved through the desks, righting the ones Paulie had skewed, collecting onion skin and pencil shavings, stacking the slates. She should have opened Mrs. Byers's note then. She should have begun the inventory of state property, counted the readers, tested the hinges Dell had just repaired, and prepared to hand back a key and a ledger like any respectable woman facing the end of her employment.

Instead she sat at the desk and drew the folded Lincoln timetable from between the pages of the grammar book where she had hidden it. The paper had softened along the creases from being handled in secret. Thursday morning train east, connection onward. If she left before dawn, if the roads held, if the depot agent still honored the fare as quoted, if she arrived in Lincoln looking more like a librarian than a dusted-out schoolteacher with too many private entanglements, then there was still a life on the other side of the paper.

She had tried to keep that possibility clean. That was the only way she could bear it. On paper, Lincoln was oak tables and catalog drawers, steady salary, a room of her own, a hat she could brush once and have it stay brushed. On paper, leaving had nothing to do with Cora's body in the dark or Dell's unfinished face in the back desk or Hester coughing over a table of men who meant to go dig up an old civic sin before August could lawyer it dead. On paper, departure was professional advancement, prudence, a woman taking the chance she would be a fool to refuse.

The blackboard had gone gray again while she sat there. The children had smudged the lower edge with cuffs and shoulders; Dell's subtraction problem remained ghosted beneath the afternoon light. Thea rose, took the rag, and wiped a broad wet stroke across the slate where Song still stood in chalk.

The memory came up under her hand.

The memory did not arrive all at once. First the black brightened as if lamplight had been lit behind it. Then sound came up, thin and scratchy, a fiddle sawing at a dance tune fast enough to trip a person into joy or injury. The schoolroom filled itself backward. Benches stood shoved to the walls. Paper chains drooped from the rafters. Women in good dresses had gone shiny at the elbows. Men stood with collars open and faces red from coffee and heat. A pie table waited against the far window.

Ezra Vale stood in the middle of it in shirtsleeves and stocking feet, younger than the photographs on Cora's dresser and broader through the chest than grief had allowed Thea to imagine. His suspenders hung loose at the hips because someone had dared him to dance without the boots. He slapped one palm against his thigh, missed the beat, caught it badly, and launched into a clumsy clog that had all the grace of a gate blown half off its hinges. Laughter cracked around him, warm and unruly, the kind a room gives a man who has made himself ridiculous on purpose because other people need a reason to feel good in their own skins.

Cora was there against the wall, a handsome woman in her full strength and wholly unguarded. She had a pie plate braced to her skirt with one hand and the other over her mouth. Her laughter bent her at the waist. Dell, smaller by years and all elbows, beat time with both palms on a desktop until somebody shooed him back. Ezra kicked too hard, nearly slid, windmilled for balance, and grinned straight at Cora.

"I paid fifty cents for that pie," he said, breathless and pleased with himself. "I'm earning every bite."

Then the rag passed once more over the slate and the schoolroom went back to dust, chalk, and late light.

Thea stood with the damp cloth in her hand until the water ran down her wrist and spotted the floor. She had never wanted Ezra sanctified. The town already did enough of that work for the dead, especially dead men, sanding them smooth until the living could not get a grip on what had actually been suffered near them. What she had wanted was a smaller man: bowed by shame, cramped by bargains and lies, easy to resent beside the woman he left behind. Instead the memory gave her his capable body making a fool of itself for a laugh and Cora's face split open with delight from a season before widowhood and debt and secret bedclothes. Easier to love beside a ghost if the ghost could be reduced to a caution.

He would not stay reduced. That, she understood as the rag cooled in her fist, was part of what the dust kept doing to them. It refused clean enemies and clean martyrs. Ezra had signed something he should not have signed. Ezra had probably hidden things. Ezra had also once danced like a fool in his stockings so a roomful of tired people could laugh and so Cora would laugh with them. Cora had loved a man who could do that. Thea had not been wrong to love her afterward. She had only been wrong whenever she pretended the dead man could be made simple enough to step around.

Outside, the wind hit the south wall in a stronger sheet, then slackened. The timetable crackled in her other hand where she had carried it up from the desk without knowing she had done so. It named a Thursday morning train east and a connection onward. It promised Lincoln by afternoon if the trains behaved and the dust did not.

South of the Vale fields, Hester was gathering men and spades. In the Vale kitchen, Cora would be rinsing the egg tin and counting the remaining money in her head because counting it on paper made the shortage look official. Dell would be pretending he had not sat through spelling and song like a boy with nowhere better to put his grief. Mrs. Byers wanted an inventory. August wanted obedience. Lincoln wanted a woman who could arrive on time and leave the rest of herself behind.

Thea looked at the blackboard again, at the place where Ezra had vanished back into chalk haze. Then she folded the timetable once along its old crease, once across it, and tore it clean down the middle. The sound was small. It still felt decisive enough to alter the air. She tore the halves again, then the quarters, until Lincoln was only a few black letters stranded on separate scraps in her hand.

She dropped the pieces into the empty stove and laid one match to them from the desk box. The paper curled fast. A corner flared blue, then orange. For a second the printed departure times shone brighter than the ash around them. Then they blackened, collapsed inward, and were gone.

Tomorrow could carry forms and keys and the questions Cora meant to put to August. Tonight there was bread at Hester's bakery and a fight gathering over Saint's Basin. The road west to the Vale house would still be there after dark, along with Cora and whatever expression she wore when Thea told her the basin had claimed her too. None of it promised anything clean. It promised argument, lamplight, bodies in motion, wet earth if Hester proved right, and consequences that would arrive by morning. By the standards of anybody who read employment letters with common sense, it was reckless. It was also a choice that other people could answer back to.

Thea closed the damp rag inside the cupboard, took up her satchel, and finally opened Mrs. Byers's note only long enough to see the list of items to be counted. The list named desks, maps, window glass, and stove pipe. She folded it shut again and tucked it under the ledger. Outside, the sky had gone the color of rubbed brass over town. She locked the schoolhouse, tested the knob once, and headed for Main Street with dust gathering at the hem of her skirt and the taste of smoke from the burned timetable still faint on the back of her tongue.

Hester opened the bakery screen before Thea could knock. Flour striped one forearm to the elbow, and men's voices drifted from the back room with the scrape of chair legs.

"If you're counting bodies for the basin tonight," Thea said, "count mine."

Hester looked at her the way old women looked at weather when it finally made up its mind. Then she stepped back from the door. "All right," she said. "Come feed them first. Men with spades get foolish if you leave them hungry."


# Chapter 9

The next day, the bank sat in the yellow noon like a loaf gone stale on the sill, its brick front filmed with dust where yesterday's wind had struck and dried. Cora tied the mare to the rail, flattened her palm against the animal's damp neck, and waited until the leather smell and the heat coming off the horse quit pushing at her face. If she went in angry, August would let her spend it and call that a victory. She needed to go in hard instead. There was a difference.

She had carried the image for days now. Ezra sat at August's desk with the green blotter under his wrists. August's sleeve had been turned back once because of the heat, and the small white knife on the desk kept opening and shutting in August's fingers while Ezra bent his head and signed. Cora had tried, the first night after seeing it, to tell herself a dust-memory might lie in the way grief lied, by showing the worst shape first. Then Hester's jars had proved the opposite. The dust did not invent. It only refused mercy.

She crossed the sidewalk and went in.

Inside, the bank was cooler by three degrees and meaner for it. It smelled of paper, metal, old sweat, and dust trapped in cloth bindings. The wire of the teller cage cut everybody into squares. A ceiling fan turned with all the conviction of a tired spoon. Behind the wicket, Miss Lenz looked up from a stack of bills, rubbed her thumb and forefinger in the saucer of cornstarch she kept by the cash drawer so the notes would stop clinging in the heat, and gave Cora the cautious smile reserved for women whose names lived in ledgers.

"Mrs. Vale."

"I need Mr. Poeschel."

Miss Lenz glanced toward the back office before she could help herself. She was wearing a pale cardigan with one pearl button missing and a safety pin pulled through the threadbare hole in its place. Her hair, which she usually kept pinned flat, had begun to lift in little damp curls at the nape. "He's speaking with Mr. Rittger."

"Then I'll wait."

Cora moved to the bench under the calendar from the implement dealer in Kearney and sat without removing her hat. The bench rocked once on an uneven leg. Somewhere behind the partition a drawer shut, then another. Miss Lenz resumed counting. A farmer Cora knew by sight, one of the Jensens from east of town, stood at the far counter with his hat turning in his hands until the brim looked like soaked felt. Nobody spoke above the level required by money.

Cora kept her eyes on August's office door. The frosted glass had gone gray under dust and fingerprints. She could see shape but not action: a shoulder crossing, a hand lifting, the brief dark movement of a head bowed over papers. The memory from the Vale front window had shown more than the real glass would give her now, and the knowledge bit hard: the uncanny might be cleaner than ordinary sight.

She had not told Dell where she was going. She had told him she needed thread and paraffin and to ask Hester whether there was any coffee substitute left worth buying. He had looked at her too long, the way he did now when he thought there was machinery hidden under a tarpaulin and he was expected not to go looking. If he had guessed bank instead of store, he had been right.

Through the front window she caught him a minute later coming out of Hester's bakery with the red deposit book in one hand. He checked when he saw the mare at the rail, looked toward the bank door, and came on anyway. So Hester had sent him over with receipts, or suspicion had borrowed an errand and made it useful. Before Cora could decide whether to stop him, a chair scraped in August's office and the room took hold of her again.

The office door opened. Mr. Rittger came out with his jaw set in the careful shape of a man who had not gotten what he came for and had decided to call that dignity. August followed him to the threshold, one hand on the knob, heavy in his dark vest despite the heat. He was carefully shaved as always. That, more than the collar and tie, was what made him look official in a town coming apart. A man who still expected the day to justify soap and a razor was a man claiming authority over it.

His eyes found Cora. He did not start. He did not give her the satisfaction. He simply took her in, from the mended glove in her lap to the dust on the hem of her skirt, and said, "Mrs. Vale."

"I need to speak with you."

"You always do."

Miss Lenz dropped one bill and bent to retrieve it. The sound of the cash drawer closing seemed suddenly very near.

August came no farther into the lobby. "If this concerns the extension, I have already stated the bank's position."

"It concerns a paper Ezra signed in your office."

Something passed through his face then, not fear exactly but recognition, the knowledge that a door he had been keeping his shoulder against had shifted in its frame.

He let none of it reach his mouth. "My office handles many papers."

Cora rose. The bench gave a dry scrape over the floorboards. "Green blotter on the desk. Your little knife in your hand. His hat on the chair beside him because he couldn't manage both at once."

August's fingers touched the watch chain across his vest and left it again. Miss Lenz was not counting anymore; the bill she had dropped stayed pinched between two dusty fingers. At the far counter Jensen's hat had quit turning, the brim bent under both thumbs.

Cora took one step forward. "Do you want me to go on out here?"

August held her eyes a moment longer. Then he opened the office door and stood aside. "Come in, Mrs. Vale."

His office smelled more strongly of paper than the lobby did, and of the iron tang from the little safe in the wall. The desk was broad and scarred under a glass top. The same green blotter lay at its center. A brass letter opener and two sharpened pencils sat beside a church envelope used for scratch figures, and beside that lay the small bone-handled knife from the memory. Cora felt something low in her body tighten, not in panic but in recognition so complete it made her want to strike him.

August closed the door. The latch caught softly. Through the glass, Miss Lenz turned back to her station with exaggerated care.

"You have chosen an unfortunate place for theatrics," August said.

"This isn't theater."

"No? You walk into my bank and recite the contents of my desk as if the Lord placed you there invisible."

"Would that trouble you?"

His mouth pinched. He moved behind the desk and remained standing. That forced her to stand too, which was meant to put him above her. Cora kept her hands at her sides and let him have the furniture if he wanted it.

"Say what you came to say," he said.

"Bring out the paper."

"There is no reason to indulge this."

"Your reason is that I know there was one."

"Knowing there was a paper and understanding it are not the same matter."

"Then help me understand." She nodded toward the desk. "Bring it out."

August exhaled through his nose. "Your husband came to me in a difficult season."

"He came to you in every difficult season."

"Yes." He accepted the correction without warmth. "On one such occasion he requested further time against the Vale note."

"Requested. You make it sound like he asked to borrow a hymnbook."

"He was already in arrears, Mrs. Vale. Language does not improve or worsen arithmetic."

"No. Men do that."

For the first time, a little heat touched his eyes. "Your husband was not dragged here. He came of his own judgment. He understood the position of his land, the bank, and his debt."

When he said land, the muscles under Cora's ribs cinched hard enough to steal half a breath. She had expected it. Expectation did not soften a blow. It only taught the body where to hurt.

"What paper?" she asked. "Say it plain."

August looked toward the glass door as if the caution in him still hoped for a witness even here. "It was a deed of security on the basin strip and the associated water claim."

Neat terms made it worse: basin strip and associated water claim. They belonged in a file drawer, not on ground that had carried grass and spring frogs and wet hoofprints once, before men with survey stakes and notes called it waste.

"The low ground south of our fields," Cora said. "You mean that."

"I mean the tract commonly referred to as Saint's Basin, yes."

"And the water."

"And the water right attached to it."

She could see Ezra now in a way the window-memory had not given her. She saw not the shape of his shoulders at August's desk but the hour before he left the house, perhaps, looking at nothing while he pulled on his coat and saying he had to ride into town for binder parts or salt or some other thing so common she had let it pass. She tried to remember any day from his last year when he had come home carrying bank air on his clothes and not looked her full in the face. There were too many to sort.

"What did he get for that?" she asked.

"An extension."

"How much?"

"Enough to proceed through harvest if conditions improved."

"If."

"That is how weather works."

"Don't hand me weather from behind that desk."

August's fingertips moved over the glass top, smoothing nothing. "I am not asking you to like the arrangement. I am telling you there was one. Ezra knew that. He proposed part of it himself."

The claim hit harder than she had prepared for. She had come in ready to find August dirty. She had not come ready for Ezra to have put his own hand down first. The dead had a filthy advantage. They could leave you to perform both halves of the argument.

"He never told me."

"No," August said, and there was a trace of weariness in it now, something older than annoyance. "He did not."

"Because he knew I'd stop him?"

"Because he knew you would not consent."

The room narrowed. Cora looked at the bone-handled knife on the desk and pictured taking it up, not to use, only to have something in her hand that had also been there that day. The urge was so clean it steadied her.

"So he lied to me."

"He withheld a matter he considered temporary."

"Don't improve that either."

August's jaw worked once. "Men under debt do not always make admirable domestic choices."

"And bankers do?"

He did not answer. Silence told her she had moved him, though the gain was small.

"Bring the deed," she said again.

"You have no right to handle bank originals."

"I've got a right to know what was done on my land with my husband's hand."

"The land is not solely yours in the chain under discussion."

"Then I can ask louder."

She saw him judge the distance to the door, to Miss Lenz outside it, to the town beyond the door and the way gossip traveled quicker in drought because there was less else to carry. August was not afraid of sin. He was afraid of disorder. A raised female voice in his bank counted as a species of it.

At last he bent, unlocked the lower right drawer, and took out a file tied with faded tape. He set it on the desk but kept one broad hand over it.

"You will look," he said, "and you will keep your voice at a level fit for a public room."

"Open it."

He untied the tape.

There were the notes first. Renewal slips, receipts, interest notations in a hand Cora recognized from every monthly humiliation. August turned pages with the care of a priest lifting linen, showing her just enough to remind her that paper had ruled her house almost as long as grief had. Then he drew out a folded legal sheet and laid it flat.

"This is the instrument."

Cora stepped close enough to smell his soap under the dust. Ezra's name at the bottom hit her before anything else. The signature was his. No magic trick there, no counterfeit flourish. The hard downward slash of the z, the cramped last letter where his hand had always outrun the room left to it. She had seen him sign egg receipts, seed orders, the school forms Dell brought home, and once the certificate for a stillborn calf because some county fool wanted it in writing. The name belonged to Ezra. She hated him in that instant more than she had hated August walking into the room.

"He signed it," she said.

"Yes."

"Willingly."

August hesitated, perhaps hearing the edge at last. "He signed with knowledge that the bank required collateral beyond promises."

"That wasn't my question."

"No man signs gladly when he is cornered."

"But he signed."

"Yes."

The answer hit with no give in it, and Cora had to brace harder against the desk.

Cora bent over the page. The deed was typed in the bank's purplish ribbon except for the date, which sat darker and blacker at the top line, slightly out of line with the rest. Under the glassy smoothness of the paper there was a patch no bigger than her thumbnail where the fibers had been thinned. She laid one finger over it. The page felt skinned there, scraped and forced flat again. She knew that texture. It felt like a razor under scorch on a cookstove, like sandpaper over a splintered table edge. It was work done to make damage look like finish.

"What is this?" she asked softly.

August said nothing.

She kept her finger on the thin place. "What happened here?"

"The date line was corrected."

"Corrected."

"To reflect the actual encumbrance."

She looked up. He had gone formal enough now that every word came out buttoned. That meant she was near it.

"Did Ezra sign it with that date on it?"

"He signed with full understanding of the obligation."

"Did he sign it with that date on it?"

August's eyes flicked once to the door. Cora heard, through the glass, the faint clack of Miss Lenz's adding machine. They were the ordinary sounds of business. A woman could have her life split open four feet from a column of figures and the figures would still add.

"The timing of the transaction," August said, "was not entered in final form until later review."

"That's not an answer."

"It is the accurate one."

"No. The accurate one is yes or no."

He put his hand near the paper, not over it this time, as if he wanted to take it back and knew it would look too much like guilt. "Your husband and I had an understanding before the paper was completed."

"Did he sign it blank?"

"Not blank."

"Then different."

The silence went hot and slow and thick as dishwater in August.

Cora leaned in farther. "You sold him time. Then you shaved the page and changed the date so on your books he was already beat when he walked out of here with your promise in his pocket."

August's nostrils flared once. "That is not what happened."

"Then tell it."

"The county examiner was reviewing irregular notes. He had already made plain that indulgences without corresponding security would be read as weakness, perhaps concealment. The Vale file could not carry an extension on one side and an unrecorded deed on the other as if they were unrelated acts. The dates had to be brought into conformity."

"You changed it after he signed."

"I placed the effective date where it belonged."

"After he signed."

"Yes," he said at last, and the word came out flat with irritation, as if what offended him most was being made to say a coarse version of his own action. "After he signed."

Cora did not move. If the date had been cut in later, then the rot ran in two directions at once. Ezra had signed away the basin. August had skinned the bargain after the fact. There was no use sorting innocence before she knew the numbers. She let the first blow pass through her and asked for arithmetic instead.

She straightened slowly. The room had gone close. Dust showed in the strip of light at the door, moving as if the bank itself breathed through cloth.

"How much time?" she asked.

August seemed thrown by the question. "I have said. Through harvest if conditions justified it."

"No. How much did he think he bought?"

August looked at the paper. "Ninety days."

Ninety days opened in her head as labor instead of arithmetic: binding twine, scalded jars, Dell's shirts turned and patched, eggs sold a dozen at a time, her own hands raw from washing Ezra's sweat out of collars so he could go to church looking like a man not yet broken. That stretch of days was enough to keep a wife obedient to hope, enough to keep a son from bolting if the wheat might still come in, enough to get a man through one more season of lying at his own table.

"And what did you write afterward?"

"The date on the deed was entered to correspond with the antecedent obligation."

"Speak so a human being can hear you."

His face darkened. "I dated it earlier."

"How much earlier?"

"Several weeks."

"Weeks he was home acting like the farm had a chance."

"Weeks in which the farm did have a chance if rain had come."

"Because he thought you had given it one."

August's answer came quick this time, a little too quick. "I had given him one. I did not alter the weather."

"No. You altered the paper."

He turned away then, one pace only, but it was enough to show defeat of a sort. His hand went to the window shade and touched the cord without pulling it. "Mrs. Vale, you want villains because villains are cleaner than weak men making thin choices. Ezra was not clean. I was not clean. The bank could not afford cleanliness. It could barely afford survival."

"Don't preach scarcity to me."

"I am not preaching. I am telling you the condition of things."

"The condition of things is that you took a razor to a deed."

"The condition of things is that if I had presented that file as your husband preferred it, the examiner would have called the extension what it was: a postponement purchased on security already surrendered. He would have frozen every doubtful note in town and perhaps this bank with them. Men would have lost seed credit by the week. Payroll would have stopped. You think only of your house because it is your house. I had all of them on my desk."

Cora heard in that not apology but creed. If she kept pushing on sin, he would answer with town survival until dark. The only place left to press was the injury he had kept private.

"You keep saying my husband preferred. Did he know?"

"About the later correction?" August asked.

"Don't dress it up again."

He looked at her directly. "No."

So Ezra had carried the false date home without ever seeing it. The wound shifted. Knowing August had lied on paper no longer sufficed; she had to know what Ezra died believing he had bought.

"So he left here believing what?"

"That he had ninety days and that the deed would remain in file unless the note failed."

"And the note was already failed on your books."

"On the chain as regularized, yes."

"You bastard."

August flinched as if she had spat across the desk. It wasn't the obscenity that moved him; it was the commonness. Plain speech stripped him fastest.

"If you intend to insult me," he said, voice gone very quiet, "you may do it in less vulgar terms."

"Why? So it will match your paperwork?"

She put both hands flat on the desk and leaned toward him. The glass was warm from the room and slick under her palms. "Listen to me now. I don't care what fancy name you put on your cheating. I don't care if the county man had his nose in your collar. You sold my husband one story and wrote another after his hand was off the page. That's what you did."

August's own hands stayed off the desk. That suited her. Let him be the one avoiding touch. "Your husband came to me with a property already overburdened, a son nearly grown and restive, a wife who would have called any collateral a betrayal, and a crop not worth the seed in it. Do not speak as if I invented his desperation."

"No. He brought it to you, and you used it."

"I administered it."

"That's the filthiest thing you have said yet."

For a second even the fan in the lobby seemed to chop the air more slowly. August's face had gone the color of old flour. Cora could see his beard beginning under the afternoon shave, that faint blue shadow authority never confessed to.

When he spoke again, the script had worn thin. "What would you have had him do? Tell you? Bring you here to stand over him while you said no to every possible salvage? Keep the basin and lose the whole place that autumn instead of later? Men do not always come to their wives with the truth when the truth would require witness to their failing."

He was trying to move the trial off the desk and into her kitchen, where his forgery could pass for married life. She felt the hook of it for one ugly second and refused him.

She lifted one hand from the desk and pointed at the paper. "You don't get to climb inside my marriage because you held the note."

"I held more than the note."

"I know that now."

He heard what lay under it. Some satisfaction flickered and died in him, ashamed almost before it lived. "Then at least the afternoon has not been wasted."

Cora stared at him. "You think this helps you."

"I think it removes illusion."

"From me?"

"From the file."

"The file can go to hell."

His mouth twitched as if the profanity had touched him physically. Cora did not care. She had spent too many mornings shaking flour weevils out of sacks and too many nights listening to Dell breathe in his sleep like a boy pretending not to be frightened. If a man wanted gentility after that, he could buy it in a town with rain.

She tapped the thin scraped place in the date line. "Who did it?"

"I have answered enough."

"Was it your hand?"

He gave no answer.

"Was it this knife?"

His eyes went to the bone-handled blade before he could stop them, and her stomach turned over.

"Jesus." It came out not pious but sick. "You sat with him. You watched him sign. Then later you took that same little knife and skinned the page."

"I altered a date, not a signature."

"You altered the bargain."

"The bargain was already untenable."

"Then you should have told him that to his face."

August's shoulders lifted and fell once. The movement was not a shrug. He was too weary for contempt and too proud for surrender. "I told him what he could bear hearing and what the bank could bear granting. That was the room available."

Cora nearly laughed at the way he made honesty sound like a cot in a full boardinghouse, as if there had simply been no bed left for it.

She did laugh then, one hard breath through her nose, because if she did not something in her might split. "You speak like a man measuring coffin boards."

Outside the office, a floorboard gave under a careful shift of weight. Cora's head turned toward the door before she could stop herself. Through the cloudy glass she could make out no more than a long shape near the frame. Miss Lenz at her station, maybe. A customer. Or Dell where Miss Lenz had sat him down with Hester's deposit book and told him to wait.

August heard it too. His face closed again. "We are finished."

"We're not."

"You have what you came for."

She looked down at the paper and thought not yet. The deed would stay in his drawer, and anything she carried out of this room besides plain speech would die in his version before supper. She wanted to snatch the page anyway and run it to Hester, make August chase her into the street. But his hand was already moving toward the file, and words were the only proof she could still drag out of him.

"Don't you touch it yet," she said.

He stopped, barely.

"I want you to say it once without your church words."

"Mrs. Vale."

"Say what you did."

His eyes had gone dead and careful. "I changed the date after Ezra signed."

The plainness of it took the room by the throat. There was no apparatus around it, no examiner, no bank, no chain, no conformity. It was just the thing itself at last, stripped and common and ugly as a dead rat behind the flour bin.

Cora nodded once. She did not trust herself with a second movement. "There."

August retied the file. His fingers were steady again now that the confession existed and could be filed like anything else. "You will gain nothing by making noise with this. There is no court in Red Willow County waiting to rescue a bankrupt place on moral grounds."

"Maybe not."

"There is only the auction clock."

"I know what time is."

"Do you?" he asked, and there was bitterness in him now, a true one, no longer hid. "Because your husband did not. He thought ninety days was a country mile. In 1935 it is a blink."

She almost told him then about the nights after Black Sunday, the way dust lay in the washbasin seams no matter how hard she scrubbed, the way Dell's shirts had begun to hang from him at the shoulders, the way Thea's pay envelope sat on the table like something both useful and shameful. She almost told him what a blink could hold when every hour had to be traded three ways. But that would have been gift, and she was done handing him those.

"Open the door," she said.

August set the file back in the drawer and locked it. The click was tidy as a lie. Then he crossed the room and turned the knob.

The long shape beyond the glass resolved before the door was fully wide. Dell was on the bench by the calendar, elbows on knees, cap crushed in both hands. He had gone pale under the dust on his face. One boot heel beat once against the floor and stopped. He was looking not at Cora but at August, with the strained blankness of a boy trying to decide whether to swing or run or ask a question that would take the roof off his life.

Cora felt the blood leave her own face. She had seen him crossing from Hester's with the deposit book and then let the office swallow him. She had not understood he was close enough to hear. He had always moved lightly when he wanted to, all quick hands and angles, as if the air gave him less resistance than it gave other people. At seventeen that grace could still look like innocence until you saw what it let him witness.

"Dell," she said.

Miss Lenz had gone rigid behind the cage, her eyes fixed on her adding machine as if figures might blind her. Jensen was no longer at the far counter. The lobby had emptied itself of everybody who could manage not to be present. In Morrow, that counted as courtesy.

Dell stood. He had gotten too tall for the motion to look easy. "Hester sent me over with the deposit book." His voice sounded scraped. "Miss Lenz said to wait."

Miss Lenz made a small movement that might have been apology. Cora would not look at her.

Dell kept his eyes on August. "Changed what date?"

The question came with no gradual entry and no chosen hour at the kitchen table after supper when the lamp was low and she could decide how much to say first. Truth had reached him through bank glass and adult carelessness, cut into pieces sharp enough to hold.

"We'll talk outside," Cora said.

"No." Dell swallowed. The refusal came quick, almost childish in its nakedness, which was how she knew he was frightened. "No, I heard enough to know outside isn't going to mend it. Did he sign that paper or didn't he?"

August opened his mouth. Cora turned on him so fast the room tilted.

"You don't answer my son."

Something in August's face yielded then, not kindness. Calculation surrendering to exhaustion. He looked at Dell, really looked, and Cora saw with a fresh flash of disgust that the man had known for years exactly what damage sat waiting in the Vale house and had chosen when to collect interest on it.

"Your father signed a deed," August said. Formal again, because formal was how he hid. "He did so believing it would secure additional time on the note."

Dell's jaw flexed. "And then you changed it."

August's silence was answer enough this time too.

Cora stepped between them. She took Dell by the elbow, felt the hard young bone under his sleeve, and hated that even now she met his body through utility first, as if grip could stand in for truth. "Come on."

Dell let her move him two steps toward the door, then pulled back just enough to look over her shoulder. His face had gone unmistakably young. The sudden youth in it hurt worst. He could act older than the weather some days, older than the men breaking crust out on the county road, and then grief would reach up and strip him back to the boy who had once come in from the yard with a sparrow cupped in both hands because he had not known yet that gentle things still died.

"Did he know?" Dell asked.

August's answer took a beat too long. "Not about the alteration."

Cora closed her eyes once. There was no use stopping the rest now.

Dell gave a short laugh that held no humor in it. "Christ."

Miss Lenz's pencil snapped in the lobby. Nobody commented.

"Outside," Cora said again, and this time Dell went.

The heat hit them hard when the street door opened. Afternoon had baked the dust into a thin crust over everything, then cracked it. The hitch rail, the boardwalk, the cuffs of Dell's trousers, the mare's flank, all furred in the same yellow-gray. Cora kept hold of his elbow until they were off the walk and into the sliver of shade cast by the bank's west wall.

Dell jerked free then, not violently, but with enough force to tell her what touch felt like to him just now. He put his cap on and took it off again. He would not look straight at her.

"You lied," he said.

"I didn't know."

"You knew something."

"Yes." She tasted grit on the word. No point wasting time with innocence she did not have. "I knew there was more to it than he ever said."

"More to the note, or more to him?"

She looked at the street because she could not look at his face and answer fast. A wagon moved past at the far corner, slow as if the horses had given up on destinations. From the bakery block down, someone dumped a pan and the dust rang softly against tin.

"Both," she said.

Dell rubbed the heel of one hand over his mouth. His fingers were black in the creases with grease and soil. He had probably come in straight from some repair or chore, drawn by suspicion sharper than any instruction she had given. "He signed away the basin?"

"Part of it. The strip. The water right with it."

"For ninety days."

She turned back to him. "You heard that too."

"Enough." He laughed again, harsher. "Hell. He sold mud for time and didn't even get the time."

The sentence was so like Ezra in its cruel efficiency that it almost staggered her. Father and son did the same ugly thing with pain, cutting it down until it sounded mechanical.

"Don't do that," she said.

"Do what?"

"Turn him into one clean joke because it's easier."

Dell's head came up then. Anger gave him back his eyes. "You don't get to tell me easy right now."

"No." She let that stand. He had earned it. "I don't."

They stayed there in the shade while the town moved around them with the embarrassed discretion of a place that could smell trouble and had learned not to stand close enough for it to stain clothes. Dell kicked at the wall with the side of his boot, not hard, just enough to send a skin of dried dust sliding down the brick.

"Hester knows?" he asked.

"Not all of it. Not this part."

"The date part."

"No."

"Thea?"

Cora's head turned before she could govern it. Dell saw. She knew he saw. His mouth tightened, not from the subject itself perhaps, but from the quickness with which her body had answered another name.

"No," she said.

He nodded once. He was filing that too somewhere. Everything had become file and secret and what somebody knew first. August's world was spreading.

"So what now?" Dell asked.

There ought to have been a plan in answer. A proper mother would have had one ready the minute she stepped out of the bank. Go home. Say nothing till supper. Tell Hester after dark. Keep Dell from town talk. Count money. Protect Thea. Protect the farm if it still counted as protection. But the chapter of her life in which effort produced sequence had been ending for longer than she cared to admit. All she had now was the hard fact of the afternoon and the boy beside her who was not a boy enough anymore to be sent away from it.

"Now I tell Hester what he admitted," she said. "And now you don't go back in there alone. Not today."

Dell stared at the street. "I wasn't planning to."

"Good."

"And then?"

Cora looked up at the bank window. From outside it gave back only light and dust, no Ezra, no August, no proof. It gave back only her own shape and Dell's standing beside it, warped a little by the old glass until they looked like family reduced to rumor.

"And then," she said, "we quit calling that debt honorable."

Dell turned toward her finally. Something in his face had sharpened and hollowed at once. He was still trying to decide what hurt him most: that August had cheated Ezra, or that Ezra had needed cheating because he had already begun by cheating his own house. Cora could not help him sort it. She was standing in the same wreck.

"You still sound like he's worth defending," he said.

The honest answer came before the useful one could. "I don't know what he's worth today."

Dell flinched, though not outward much. He only shut his eyes quickly against the sun. But she saw it. She had finally said of his father what grief had spent a year refusing to let her say. He was not martyr or monster or clean, not even fixed in value.

She had given her son something cruel in daylight against a bank wall, and truth did not blunt it.

He nodded once more, because that was how men in this town accepted blows when they would rather have thrown them. "All right."

They went to the mare. Dell put his hand on the bridle before Cora could, as if he needed some task plain enough to survive, and turned her toward Main Street. His fingers shook once against the worn leather, then steadied. Cora watched that and understood with a cold, tired certainty that whatever she said next, whatever she held back until evening, whatever version she tried to build out of the splinters August had handed them, Dell was already inside the break now. The adult room was gone. The bank had seen to that.


# Chapter 10

Hester woke before the ovens had taken the room because her chest would not let her lie flat another minute. She sat up in the narrow bed over the bakery, feet on the cool boards, and waited for the cough to finish deciding what it meant to do with her. It came hard and dry at first, then deeper. She bent over the washstand and spat into the basin. The spit was gray from dust and striped faint pink. She looked at it a moment longer than she should have.

She had watched Marta drown by inches in her own lungs. That had taught her what a bad cough could be and what it could turn into if a woman kept pretending she only needed sleep. Hester tipped the basin into the slop pail, rinsed it with yesterday's water, and did not grant the matter another prayer. There was no use beginning a day by telling the truth about her own body if the town meant to keep lying about the ground.

The bakery below still held last night's heat. Yeast, coffee, flour, chamomile, dust. Her whole life had settled into those smells so thoroughly that she could have named the hour with her eyes shut. She set the fire, hauled the dough trough into better light, and put both fists into the first batch. The dough gave under her knuckles, cool and damp as a decent thought. Outside, the town stayed quiet in the yellow half-light, which never meant peace anymore. It meant people saving breath.

By the time she had the first loaves rounded, Dell came through the back door without knocking. He ducked his head against the lintel though he had cleared it cleanly for three years now. Dust had gone pale in his hair again. He held a shovel handle in one hand and would not look at her straight on.

"You're up," Hester said.

"You're up too."

"That's how bakeries work."

He set the handle against the wall, then picked it up again as if standing still made him itch. "Ma said you'd want to know. About yesterday."

Hester kept shaping bread. "I already know she went in there wanting blood."

"She got part of it." He swallowed. "He changed the date. August did. After Pa signed. Ma made him say it plain."

The dough puckered under Hester's thumbs. She had expected the shape of the betrayal, but hearing it put into words made it no prettier. It was not grand or biblical or touched with any devil's flourish. It was a date changed in ink after a tired man had signed where he was told. The change was small enough to fit under a fingernail and big enough to take a farm.

"Sit down," she said.

"I don't want coffee."

"I didn't ask what you wanted."

He sat. He looked seventeen only when he forgot to harden his face in time, and the forgetting had gotten more frequent since the storm. Hester cut him a heel from yesterday's rye, put butter on it while it was still hard from the crock, and shoved the plate over.

"You planning to tell me we wait now?" he asked.

"No. I'm planning to tell you we don't walk out there with two shovels and a temper and call it work."

"We found the place."

"We found the place weeks ago. What we haven't found is enough hands to cut that ground before it bakes harder or before August puts paper over it." She wiped flour on her apron. "You'll get me every sound shovel your place can spare. Spades too, not only grain scoops. Rope if you've got it. Water barrel. If Cora still has that wagon with one decent wheel, I want it in my yard by noon."

Dell chewed once, twice, angry at the bread because it slowed him down. "He'll come after us."

"That's why we don't do it small."

He looked up then. "You think folks'll stand with us?"

Hester thought of the town as it was now: porches with chairs but no people in them until evening, windows stuffed with rags, men who had worn themselves so thin they could no longer tell caution from surrender. Then she thought of the other part of it, the part that still flared when somebody named a task out loud. Threshing, a barn roof after hail, a burial when the ground went mean in January. People liked usefulness better than hope. Usefulness asked less of them.

"Some will," she said. "Some will come because they believe me. Some because they hate August. Some because if there's wet soil under there they want first claim on what it might mean. Some will come because they're tired of watching dust eat a town while they hold their hats and call it weather. I'm not particular about their motives as long as they bring iron."

The back door opened again. Cora came in carrying the morning with her: sweat at the hairline already, shirt sleeves rolled, face set so tight it seemed carved from fence post. She had not slept much. Hester could tell from the grain of her temper.

"I milked and fed before light," Cora said. "Dell says you mean to do this tomorrow."

"At first light."

"That's fast."

"Good. That's the point."

Cora took off her hat and slapped it against her thigh once, a habit she only had when she was fighting not to throw something sharper. "Half the town thinks we've lost our senses. The other half thinks any basin worth having was worth having ten years ago."

"Then half the town is tired and the other half is stupid. Neither condition is fatal."

Dell made a noise that was almost a laugh. Cora cut a look at him, then at Hester. "How many are you thinking?"

"Eight would do it if they know how to work. Ten would do it cleaner. Two boys for hauling spoil. Somebody to mind water and keep fools from crowding the trench edge. We start south side where the old line cut through. We don't go straight down. We peel it back and see if the dark holds."

"You're talking like you've got a crew already."

"I've got a bakery," Hester said. "It amounts to the same thing if a woman uses it right."

Cora's mouth shifted despite itself. Hester counted that as a victory. She went on before resistance could stiffen again.

"You bring the wagon. Dell gets tools. I'll spend the day asking the town whether it would like to be useful or only obedient."

Cora leaned a hip against the table and folded her arms. "If August hears before morning, he'll try to stop it."

"He'll hear by dinner," Hester said. "Good. Let him talk in daylight where people can decide whether they're afraid of his note hand or ashamed of it."

"And if nobody comes?"

Hester turned the loaf, slid it into the pan, and met Cora's eyes. "Then you and Dell and I cut till we can't raise our arms. But I don't think it'll be nobody."

She sent them out with bread still warm enough to steam through the cloth. Dell took two slices in one hand and the shovel handle in the other, already moving as if he could drag the whole day by the collar. Cora paused at the door.

"You don't have to spend yourself on this for us," she said quietly.

Hester snorted. "I'm not doing it for your feelings, Cora. I'm doing it because Otto came home with black mud to his knees from that basin and because I baked pies for the men who drained it and listened to them brag over my counter like God had signed the ditch order himself. I know what was there. It isn't sentiment. It's memory with chores attached."

Cora took the blow as Hester meant her to. She nodded once and went.

By eight the first bread was cooling and the street had begun to produce its little daily signs of life. A truck coughed itself to death two blocks over. Someone dragged a coal scuttle across a porch. The screen on the bakery door rasped open and shut with people who had enough coins for bread and people who only had barter and shame.

Hester asked everybody, and the answers came back in different shapes.

She started with Clem Reddin because he still walked like a man measuring grades even after the county stopped paying him to. Clem's hearing had gone soft in one ear years back, and he kept a nickel alarm clock in the breast pocket of his overalls because he no longer trusted church bells or his own head to tell him the hour. He stood at the counter with his bread ticket and his chin rough with white whiskers.

"You still have that mattock from the bridge washout?" Hester said.

"Got it. Handle's split."

"Bring it anyway. First light tomorrow."

He scratched his jaw. "For the basin."

"For the basin."

"Heard tell you all found tile out there."

"Dell found it with his hands in the dirt, not by telling tales." Hester wrapped his loaf. "You coming or only asking?"

Clem shifted his weight. "Edna says if I so much as lean on a shovel the bank'll remember our truck note. Men at Rook's feed are already saying August means to keep himself a list."

"Then we'd better give him a long one."

"I can come till noon. My left knee's bad if I twist it, and Edna will skin me if I come home unable to climb the porch."

"Then don't twist it."

That made him smile. "I miss being told what to do by somebody who knows the ground."

"There you are, then."

Alma Jirak came in next. She always wore her hat indoors, even in Hester's kitchen, because the hat had been bought in North Platte when her husband took her there one anniversary and she had refused to let widowhood take another thing off her head. Today the hat brim was pinned up on one side with a pheasant feather gone ragged from dust. She set down a sack of late peas and asked for bread on credit.

"You can have the bread," Hester said, "and I want your two wash buckets and whatever burlap your girls haven't cut into rugs."

Alma blinked. "Joe told Lena she's staying home tomorrow. He says we owe enough already without parading our name in the street."

"Joe can keep his caution. I asked for buckets."

Alma's lined mouth tightened, not in disbelief but in hunger. "My boys skated there once. Lord, before Joe got rheumatism so bad he couldn't lace his own boots." She touched the feather without seeming to know she had. "I can't dig. You know that."

"I asked for buckets, not heroics."

"You'll get them," Alma said. She lowered her voice. "And if Lena forgets her father's orders tomorrow, I won't go blind from looking the other direction."

People laughed more easily when Hester was dry. It made them feel they had not agreed to something wild, only to something sensible that happened to have teeth. She used that where she could.

By midmorning she had also asked Virgil Dunn, who had axle grease worked permanent into the moons of his fingernails and a weakness for schemes. Virgil did not believe in visions, saints, government mercy, or any other thing he could not hitch to a team and weigh. He did, however, believe in advantage.

"If there's damp under there," he said, standing with one boot up on the bakery step, "that could mean pasture next year."

"If there's damp under there," Hester said, "it means this town was more foolish than even I allowed."

"Could mean county interest too. Relief crews."

"You want your name on a list for some man in spectacles to admire later, is that it?"

Virgil grinned without shame. "Wouldn't hurt."

"Then bring your scraper and stop talking."

He promised one mule and a drag board if his mare came up sound in the morning. Hester accepted him whole, greed and all. Opportunists moved dirt just as well as patriots if they showed up on time.

The skeptics announced themselves without needing to be asked. They gave the dead-eyed little smile people used when they wanted to be kind to madness without catching it.

Mrs. Sayer from the school board came in near eleven for coffee grounds and stood at the window too long, watching Dell and Cora stack tools in the alley. She had a tape measure around her neck because she was forever altering dresses for women who could no longer afford new ones, and she had the narrow-boned look of a person who had turned judgment into a form of nutrition.

"You're encouraging disorder," she said at last.

"I'm encouraging digging."

"People are overexcited by these dust stories."

Hester cut cheese with more force than she needed. "No. People are excited by finding out weather had helpers."

Mrs. Sayer's nostrils tightened. "Miss Bell would do well to stay clear of it. The board does not pay her to line up against the bank in an alley."

Hester set the knife down. "Miss Bell can decide where to put her own shoes. The board can catch up when it likes."

That told Hester something useful. Thea had not even stepped into the alley yet, and already people were imagining where she ought not to stand. Towns like Morrow liked women best when they could be arranged into proper distances from one another and from trouble.

The exhausted ones hurt worst to ask because Hester knew they were not cowards. They were spent.

Bess Runyon came in with the baby on one hip and a diaper pail in the other hand, moving carefully the way women did when every part of the day had already been promised elsewhere. Her oldest boy Tommy trailed behind, holding a spoon like a toy pistol. Bess had once played piano for church sings and still carried the habit of tapping out a measure with her thumb when she was waiting, though there had not been a piano in her house since January.

"I heard," Bess said. "Folks are saying you're set on cutting up the Vale south field."

"Folks are lazy with their tongues."

Bess gave a thin smile. "I can't come. Fred's in McCook looking for work and Tommy's bowels have been water since Tuesday." She set the pail down and shifted the baby higher. "But you can take the two short shovels behind our shed. Tommy uses one for mud pies. Don't mind the red paint on the handle."

Tommy lifted the spoon and said, solemn as scripture, "It's from my wagon."

"Then we will treat it with respect," Hester said.

Bess looked out toward the alley where Cora was tightening a rope around a stack of tools. "If you find water, tell me before wash day. I'd like to stop hauling from the hand pump till my shoulder falls off."

"Now that's the first sensible thing I've heard all morning."

Bess laughed once and then looked ashamed of laughing, as if it wasted calories. She took her bread and went.

By noon the alley behind the bakery looked like the beginning of either a work crew or a crime. Three sound shovels. Two spades. Clem's split-handled mattock. The red-painted child's shovel from the Runyon place. Virgil's promised drag board leaning like a coffin lid against the fence. A pry bar Hester had bullied out of the feed shed on the argument that rats did not need it today. Two wash buckets, three coils of rope, an old handcart from the bakery cellar, and Cora's wagon with the cracked singletree patched by wire and prayer.

Dell kept moving among it all, sorting by size, testing heads for wobble, shaving a new wedge for the mattock handle with quick vicious strokes of his knife. He had said hardly ten words since morning. The less he spoke, the worse he was. Hester knew that in him by now.

She went out with a cup of coffee and stood near enough that he had to acknowledge her.

"You need food if you're going to act like a machine," she said.

"I'm fine."

"Machines still take oil."

He took the cup. His left shoulder was already streaked dark from sweat. "Virgil says if water shows he'll claim a patch for pasture."

"Virgil would claim the moon if it hung low enough."

"You trust him?"

"No."

Dell stared into the coffee. "Do you trust anybody?"

Hester considered that. Across the yard Cora was arguing with Clem over the best angle to come in from the south side, though she was doing it in the low hard voice she used for nearly everything until anger tipped all the way into fear. Clem had his alarm clock out, absurd in his huge square hand, timing the boil of the coffee on the back stove because he timed everything now that the county no longer hired him to.

"I trust labor," Hester said. "I trust what a person reaches for when there's a roof to brace or a child to pull from under something. Motives can stay muddy. Shovel strokes are plain."

Dell took that in. "Ma still thinks this could all go bad."

"Your mother has a gift for naming facts."

"You ever think Pa knew the whole of it?"

The question came rough, as if he hated himself for letting it out. Hester did not answer fast. Dust had made everybody greedy for certainty, and certainty was still scarce.

"I think your father knew enough to be trapped by it," she said. "I think August knew more. I think men tell themselves they're buying one more season when they're really selling three. The rest can wait till after we open the ground."

Dell nodded, dissatisfied in the honest way of the young. He drank the coffee too hot and winced without complaint.

The street thickened a little toward afternoon, not with crowds exactly but with watchers. Men on the bench by the post office. Two girls carrying library books they would soon have nowhere to return. Mrs. Sayer back at her window. People slowed in front of the bakery because slowness let them listen without admitting they were listening.

That was when Hester understood the day had already outrun privacy. If they quit now, they would still have declared themselves. Cora, Dell, and she had become visible in the one way Morrow still knew how to notice anything: by giving the town a side to stand on.

She wiped her hands, went inside, and came back with one of Otto's old survey stakes. It was only a cedar strip, red paint long gone to a brown blush, but the point still held. Otto had used that set, folding rule and all, the spring the county men first talked drainage. Hester had kept the whole set in the loft because wood that had known wet ground felt worth saving.

She planted the stake upright beside the alley wall where everybody could see it.

"Tomorrow," she said to nobody and everybody, "we start from where the basin was cut."

The words carried farther than she expected. A window slid up somewhere. A screen banged. Dell looked at the stake, then at her, and something in his face settled into direction, which was better than peace and likelier to hold.

Thea came near four o'clock with dust on her hem and two schoolhouse ledgers under one arm. She had been at the school closing cupboards, she said, and her voice carried that careful evenness she used when she was tired enough to risk sharpness. She had also brought the school's coal shovel, a spud bar from the furnace shed, and the square wooden first-aid box with the red cross painted crooked on top years ago by some child who had not known better.

"I thought you might want bandages and carbolic," she said.

Hester looked at the box, then at her. Thea's collar was still fastened despite the heat, but she had rolled her sleeves to the elbow and there was chalk on the side of her hand. She had made herself neat all summer as if neatness could keep Morrow from touching her too deeply. Today the neatness looked like something she had put on in haste and forgotten to maintain.

"I might," Hester said.

Thea set the ledgers on a flour barrel by the door. "And if people are lending tools, somebody ought to write down whose is whose before this turns into a resurrection of small-town grievance."

"That's why God made labels."

"God hasn't been reliable on paperwork."

Hester's laugh snagged halfway into a cough. She turned away before it got hold. The cough ripped through her harder than the morning one had. It bent her over the barrel with one hand braced against the ledger and the other clamped over her mouth. By the time it released her, her eyes had watered and the yard had gone silent.

She looked into her palm. The blood was slight, only a narrow thread of bright red through the gray spit, but it was enough.

Thea saw. Of course she did. She had teacher's eyes, the kind that missed whispering only when whispering did not matter.

"Sit down," Thea said quietly.

"Later."

"Now would be better."

"Don't start in on me like I'm one of your third graders."

Thea's mouth tightened, but she let it go. Hester wiped her hand on a rag, folded the rag inward, and stood straight again before the yard could decide she was finished.

That was the exact moment August Poeschel stepped out of the bank and crossed the street.

He had his jacket on despite the heat, because formality was the last decent cloth he owned and he wore it even when it stank. Dust had got into the crease of his trousers and the edges of his hat brim, but he still moved like a man who expected a path to exist before him. People still shifted as he came, even now, with the bank turning meaner and the town poorer. Habit was a hard harness to slip.

He stopped at the mouth of the alley and took in the tools, the wagon, the stake, Dell with the mattock handle in his hands, Cora beside the wheel, Hester by the barrel, Thea standing close enough to count. His face did not change much. August had the kind of control that showed itself mostly in how little he permitted his mouth.

"Mrs. Blum," he said, "I understand you are organizing an excavation on mortgaged land."

"You understand more today than you did yesterday?" Cora asked.

August did not look at her first. That was one of his favorite small cruelties. "I am speaking to Mrs. Blum."

"Then speak faster," Hester said. "Bread does not sell itself."

His gaze came to her. "I am advising you plainly. Any disturbance of drainage works south of the Vale fields may be treated as trespass and as damage to secured property. If someone is injured, the bank will not assume liability."

Virgil, who had drifted back to see what had become of his own idea, shifted on his boots at the word liability. Hester heard it in the leather.

"There it is," Cora said. "The holy word."

"This is not theater, Mrs. Vale."

"No," Cora said. "It never is with you."

Hester stepped in before Cora's anger could outrun sense. "Saint's Basin held water before your father and mine were old enough to shave. Otto walked that ground in spring and sank to the welt. We found the tile. Your bank did not invent soil, August."

"Memory and hearsay are not title."

"Neither is fraud."

That one landed. A murmur went along the street. August's jaw shifted once.

"You are making a public accusation," he said.

"You made a public habit of calling weather fate. I'm correcting the record."

He smoothed the front of his jacket, a small flat pass of the hand over cloth. Hester had seen him do it at funerals, at note signings, at the edge of a lie. "If Mr. Vale's family wishes to impair the value of their pledged acreage before auction, that is foolish enough. If you mean to recruit others into it, then those others should hear me clearly. Injuries sustained there will be theirs. Damages will be theirs. Any person already delinquent with this bank should consider whether open participation in such an act is prudent."

That was the practical retaliation, then: shame and credit before any sheriff ever came into it. A man did not need a jail if he could be starved in orderly language.

Clem spat into the dust and put his alarm clock back in his pocket. Virgil looked at his boots again. At the bakery window Mrs. Sayer held still as stitched muslin.

Hester felt the yard lean.

"Say it cleaner," Dell snapped. He had gone red high on his cheekbones. "Tell them you mean you'll remember names."

"Dell," Cora said.

"No, let him," August said, still formal, still maddening. "I remember all kinds of things, some of them usefully."

Hester took a breath to answer and got dust instead. The cough struck so fast she had no room to hide it. It tore up from her belly, hard enough to fold her over against the barrel. She caught herself on the edge with both hands while the whole alley watched her body betray her in public. Spit hit the dirt by her shoe, pink this time and opening fast into a small ugly bloom.

Someone made a sound. Alma Jirak, maybe. Cora stepped once toward her. August took half a step too and then stopped, which somehow made it worse.

"Hester," Thea said.

There was no soft way to answer. Hester could not spare breath for one. She held up a hand until the coughing eased. Her lungs felt scoured with a wire brush. When she straightened, the street had gone more silent than prayer.

August's voice changed by one shade only. "You should be indoors."

That did it.

Hester wiped her mouth with the back of her wrist and tasted salt, dust, blood, old coffee. "I've been indoors for sixty-seven years, August. I've kneaded through droughts, births, funerals, influenza, and your father's lies. Don't talk to me as if a doorway is medicine."

The words cost her. She felt that at once. The edge of black at the corners of her sight, the weak swim of the yard. She hated her body then, not because it hurt but because it had chosen such a public time to show its terms.

Thea moved before anybody else did. She came to Hester's side, set the first-aid box down on the barrel, and put one hand on the ledger as if claiming the whole makeshift desk for their side of the street.

"Mr. Poeschel," she said, and the schoolroom entered her voice: measured, exact, too controlled by half. "Mrs. Vale consents to work on her own ground. The people here are volunteers. If you mean to threaten them, then let's have it plain enough to write down."

August turned to her fully for the first time. "Miss Bell, this does not concern the school."

"Everything in this town concerns the school. Or did, while there were children enough to fill it." Her fingers opened the ledger. "If your warning's legal, I'll record it. If it's only intimidation, I'll record that too."

Mrs. Sayer made a scandalized little movement at the window. Hester almost wished she had better breath so she could enjoy it properly.

August's face stayed heavy and careful. "Your position does not require public entanglement in a land dispute."

Thea gave a short laugh that had no ease in it. "No. It only requires that I watch every family in town be thinned down to one child, or none, and call that orderly." She dipped the pen into the inkwell she had brought from school. "Who is bringing what tomorrow?"

She did not ask permission. That was the turn of it. She simply took the right to make the record and let the alley answer her.

Cora looked at her then, really looked, and Hester saw how dangerous plain gratitude could be in public. Cora's face did not soften; it sharpened, the way it did when fear and relief pulled together. In a town this small, even that much could be chewed into talk by supper.

"Thea," Cora said low.

Thea kept her eyes on the page. "Put me down for the handcart, the coal shovel, bandages, and two pails from the school pump." Then, after a beat, more bluntly: "Put me down for myself."

Virgil barked out a laugh. "Well. If the teacher's coming, maybe I better not let my mule be the only respectable thing on site."

"You bringing him or only his reputation?" Hester asked.

"Both."

Clem cleared his throat. "Mattock, shovel, and me till noon."

Alma Jirak stepped closer from the street, feather trembling over one eye. "Buckets, burlap, and my Lena if the girl can sneak out from under mending."

Bess Runyon, who had come back for the red-handled shovel and now stood with the baby asleep against her neck, said, "Tommy can carry water if somebody keeps him from falling in." Tommy grinned around the same spoon. His mother smacked the back of his head lightly. "No, he can't. But you can have my wash pole for marking depth."

The ledger began to fill with names, tools, and promises, enough to turn a rumor into a faction.

The skeptics stayed skeptics. Two men on the post-office bench shook their heads and did not rise. Mrs. Sayer lowered her window with the look of a woman storing gossip for winter. One of August's depositor farmers turned away before Hester could catch his eye, all but cringing from the chance to choose. The exhausted ones did what they could: a shovel without a body, a bucket without a back, a boy sent in place of a man who had already sold too many hours this week.

And the believers, if that was the right word, were not believers in magic so much as in work joined to memory. They were people who had seen too much weather to mistake it for innocence anymore.

August let the scratching of Thea's pen continue longer than Hester expected. Then he said, very precise, "You are making yourselves conspicuous."

"That was bound to happen," Thea said.

He looked at the ledger, then at Cora, then at Hester again. His gaze touched Dell last, and Dell met it with such naked hatred that Hester almost intervened. But she let the look stand. Some truths had to scorch before they cooled into usefulness.

"If someone is hurt," August said, "I will not have it said the bank encouraged this."

"Nobody's likely to mistake you for encouragement," Cora said.

His eyes flicked to her, then to Thea still writing beside her, and Hester saw what he was counting. A schoolteacher had stepped out of the civic neutrality he preferred for her and planted herself with a delinquent family in front of half the town. That could be punished as disorder soon enough without anybody naming a private reason for it.

Thea must have counted the cost as well. Her hand paused on the page once. Then she wrote her name more darkly, pressing through two sheets.

August put his hat on. "Very well. I have warned you."

"You have," Hester said. "Now folks can decide what that warning's worth."

He left without another word. The street made room for him, then closed after him in the old obedient way. But not quite as neatly as before.

For a few seconds nobody moved. The whole town seemed to be listening to the bank door shut.

Then Virgil hitched the drag board higher on his shoulder and said, "First light, then."

Clem took out the alarm clock again, squinted at it, and said, "Make it five-thirty. Ground'll still have some mercy then."

"Five," Cora said. "Mercy's gone by five-thirty."

"Five," Thea wrote.

Hester sat because if she did not, one of them would make a spectacle of helping her and she was not ready to permit that. The flour barrel was close enough to count as taking charge of a seat instead of needing it. From there she directed the rest.

"Virgil, south road with the mule. Clem, you and Dell start on the first cut where the old grass changes. Don't crowd the edge and don't let boys underfoot. Alma's buckets by the hand pump. Cora brings the wagon as close as the ground allows. Thea keeps the list, minds water, and if anybody starts acting like an idiot she uses schoolteacher voice till they quit."

That won a few laughs, enough to loosen the yard. Even Hester's own breath came easier when work replaced argument. People understood tasks better than causes. Once given something exact to carry, they could pretend they had never hesitated.

Thea blotted the page and tore out a copy to pin beside the bakery door. Her fingers were steady now. Only the set of her shoulders gave her away. Cora stood close while she pinned it, not touching, both of them more careful than the act deserved. Hester saw Mrs. Sayer's curtain twitch across the street and thought how gossip fattened in a hungry place. Sometimes that alone could ruin people.

"You don't have to come," Cora said under her breath, though not so far under that Hester missed it.

Thea kept pinning. "I know."

"It'll make talk."

"That started before I crossed the street."

There was no softness in it. No private murmur. That was what made it brave. Thea was not naming what lay between them in the open; Morrow was not a town where such things could be named and survived. She was doing the next harder thing. She was letting the town see where her loyalty fell.

By sundown the list on the bakery door had six adults certain, two more likely, three boys useless for much except carrying water, and half a main street newly aware of itself. The believers had faces now. So did the opportunists, the skeptics, the worn-out ones who would not come but would watch from porches and decide whether to admire or mock depending on what the morning gave them.

Hester sent people home with bread heels wrapped in paper and instructions repeated till they stuck. Dell loaded the last of the tools into Cora's wagon and slid Otto's folding rule under the seat where it wouldn't bounce out in the ruts. Metal knocked metal in the bed with a clean unforgiving sound. Thea carried the first-aid box to the wagon seat, tucked a square of faded muslin under its latch with the bandages, and set it there like a pledge. Cora tied down the rope. None of it looked dramatic. That was one of the few mercies left in the world. Important things often looked shabby while they were being made.

When the yard had mostly emptied, Hester went inside and washed her hands at the back sink. The rag came away from her mouth pink again when she checked. More than morning. Less than enough to stop her. She folded the rag tight and tucked it under the flour bin where nobody would find it tonight.

Outside, she could hear Dell testing the wagon hitch one last time and Thea reading names back off the ledger so nothing would be forgotten by dawn. Cora answered in that spare practical voice of hers, all verbs and no sentiment. It sounded almost like any other evening's work if a person did not listen for the strain under it.

Hester dried her hands and stood a moment in the kitchen doorway. The alley lay in long dust-colored shadow. Beyond the roofs, south, the land flattened toward Saint's Basin. You could not see the place from here. Only the idea of where it waited. Ground holding its own counsel under a skin of baked dirt and lies.

She thought of Otto's boots by the stove, black with basin mud. She thought of Marta's lungs and the thin red line in the basin this morning. She thought of August smoothing his jacket while he threatened hunger with polite nouns. And she thought of Thea, neat as a school copybook until she stepped into the alley and put her name down where everybody could read it.

That was the thing about a coalition in a town this small: it began with visibility, not numbers. A shovel lent, a name written, a woman crossing one stretch of street at the wrong time of day and not going back when warned.

By dark, every house in Morrow would know whose tools were in Cora Vale's wagon. By dawn, the town would know which mattered more to it: August's caution, or the chance to put iron into the old wound and see whether it still bled water.


# Chapter 11

Even in daylight, Saint's Basin still looked like a lie more than a place.
