South of the Vale fields the wheat stubble thinned, the ground sagged, and the color changed from dead gold to a meaner gray-green that could have passed for any bad patch in a county full of them. If Dell had not gone out there with Hester and Otto Blum's map copy and cut down through the powder to black damp weeks ago, he might have walked over it the rest of his life and called it poor ground. This morning there were stakes driven in a rough arc, two shovels already standing in the dirt like grave markers, a pick, a spud bar borrowed from the school furnace shed, and a handful of townspeople trying not to admit they had come to see whether the basin would give up water or just one more public embarrassment.

The heat had started early. By nine the sun had a hard brass look through the dust haze and sweat was already making mud under Dell's hatband. He had his shirt stuck to his spine and the skin across his palms hot and thin from yesterday's handle work before they had even cut the first proper line. Hester stood at the edge of the marked trench with Otto's folding rule in one apron pocket and her cough tied down behind a square of faded muslin at her throat. She had hammered the stakes herself, though Dell had watched her have to stop twice to get her breath. Cora was in men's work gloves too big through the fingers, sleeves rolled high on her forearms. Thea had pinned her skirt up awkwardly for walking in ruts and tied a handkerchief over her dark hair. She looked wrong for a ditch and stubborn enough to stay in it all day.

Nine people, if he counted the boy.

Not all of them were there for the same thing. Mrs. Agnes Koehler, whose dead husband's threshing note August still held at the bank, had brought two empty Mason jars and kept pretending she had only brought them to carry water from the well if anybody needed a drink. Clem Havel, with his boot toe wired shut, had come because any rumor of water reached men like a bell. Another man, all collarbone and tobacco cough, had come because he hated August Poeschel worse than he feared looking foolish. Two others had come with the look people wore when they were too tired to choose between belief and ridicule and had decided to call both of them work. And little Rudy Eckert had come because boys came where tools were. His left suspender was mended with blue yarn instead of elastic, and he kept worrying the gap at the side of his mouth with his tongue where a tooth had come in crooked and stayed that way.

Dell saw the adults look at him before they looked at the line in the ground. Seventeen, almost a man by labor and still a boy whenever anybody wanted him out of danger. He hated the switch in their faces more than he hated the heat. He was the one who had walked the grade. He was the one who had found where the old fill lay lighter over the drainage run. He knew how the slope wanted to carry off anything that turned loose. If the basin was there under all this and if the tile line had really cut it open and bled it dry, he could read the ground well enough to find the wound again. He trusted that more than he trusted memory, and he trusted memory more than he trusted what August said.

"Narrow at the top first," Hester said. Her voice had that dry bakery scrape in it before dawn, only now it was under a summer sky. "Don't give the wall more reason than it already has."

"I know how to cut a trench," Dell said.

Hester looked at him over the muslin. "Then prove it by not talking."

That got a short laugh out of Clem. Dell took the shovel and went down where Hester had marked the first line. The dirt at the surface was loose enough to fool you, all powder and grit and old roots that gave with a dry hiss. Under that lay a packed layer that took the shovel with a shock up both arms. He liked that part best, if liking had anything to do with it. There was an answer in resistance. The handle told him what the eye could not. A stone knocked hard and clean. Old fill came up crumbly and mixed with trash root. Ground that had once held water and then been cut wrong had a different drag to it, a gummy reluctance that started below the wrists.

Cora came down beside him with the narrower spade. She did not look at him while she worked. She never did when she was afraid for him. She put her attention into the dirt itself. With the right angle of steel in her hands, she tried to bully the world into decent behavior.

"Keep your feet under you," she said.

"I've got feet."

"You know what I mean."

He did. He hated that he did. The trench was shallow yet, barely to his shin, but the lip had already started to crumble where the upper dust slipped back into what they cleared. The field south wind came and went in rough breaths, enough to sting the eyes with loose grit and then die long enough to let the heat sit directly on the neck. Thea and Mrs. Koehler carried away spoil in buckets and on feed sacks. Rudy darted in for broken clods whenever nobody told him not to. Every few minutes Hester had them stop so she could sight down the stakes and adjust by inches.

"Not east," she said once. "It bent south there. Otto marked the bend for a reason."

"I know where the bend is," Dell said, more sharply than he meant.

Hester's eyes held him. "That's your wanting to be right talking. I'm talking about the dirt."

He drove the shovel harder after that, angry because she was right and angrier because she could hear the difference. Wanting to be right had gotten mixed up with wanting something else entirely, and he could not have separated the two if somebody had put a knife to him and told him to name it plain. Water, yes. Proof, yes. Something August could not argue into paper. Something about Ezra that belonged to the ground instead of to whispers in the bank hallway or the sound of his mother not saying his name for a whole evening after she came back from town white with rage. If Ezra had helped ruin this place, Dell wanted the dirt to say it. If he had only begged and been skinned anyway, Dell wanted that too. He was sick of adults standing over him with half a truth in each hand and acting like the split was mercy.

By ten the trench was knee-deep along the marked arc and narrow enough that only one person could work cleanly at a time. The man with the tobacco cough found the first tile by striking the spade edge against it and sending a dull clay note through the ground. Everybody stilled. Dell dropped to one knee and scraped with his fingers until the curve of fired tile showed through, orange-brown under the gray dirt, laid there by somebody who had meant water to go somewhere else.

Hester came down into the cut before Cora could stop her. She squatted slowly, old not from years alone but from labor, and touched the exposed tile as gently as if she were checking a sleeping child's forehead.

"There you are," she said, and for a second there was no irony in her at all.

Mrs. Koehler crossed herself. Clem spat to the side and said, "Well, I'll be damned."

"Don't be yet," Hester said. "We've only found what stole it."

They widened along the tile and found where the line ran shallow, then deeper. Dell could feel the grade now. Whoever had laid the drain had been practical and mean, two habits that often traveled together in men like that. Take the low place, give the water an easier path, tell yourself the meadow would turn useful once it held wheat instead of frogs and reeds. He could almost admire the decision if he did not live in what it had made.

He was clearing a choke of roots where the tile joint had shifted when a motor sounded over the field path. August never drove fast over rough ground. To him haste was vulgar. The dark Ford rolled into view with dust whitening its fenders and stopped crooked near the wagon Hester had brought for tools. August got out in a coat he had no business wearing in that heat, though he had taken the hat off and was rubbing a crease from the brim with his thumb, lining his words up before he used them.

Dell knew before the man opened his mouth why he had come. Somebody in town had seen the shovels go out before breakfast. Somebody always did.

August took in the trench, the people, the exposed tile. His face changed only a little. It was still enough to satisfy Dell.

"Mrs. Vale," he said. He did not raise his voice. He never did if he could help it. "This needs to stop."

Cora straightened without climbing out of the cut. Dirt streaked one side of her neck where she had wiped sweat with the back of her wrist.

"You can keep walking, then."

"I am not speaking as a passerby. This ground is unstable, the bank has an interest in the property adjoining it, and there are children standing at the edge of a trench."

"Then get the children back from the edge," Hester said.

August looked at her, then at the muslin darkening at her throat where her cough had dampened it through. "Mrs. Blum, I mean this plainly. If this wall gives way, you will have a burial on your hands."

"We have had burials on our hands for years," Hester said. "You have only just taken notice of the dirt."

That landed in the air with the kind of silence that made people decide which side they were already on. Clem shifted his weight closer to Hester. Mrs. Koehler moved the jars farther from August with both feet without seeming to do it on purpose. Rudy stared openly at August's car as if the shiny hood might yet prove more interesting than the trench.

August turned his eyes to Dell. Dell hated that most. When adults chose him it was either because they wanted him blamed or because they wanted him managed.

"Del," August said, using the smaller name like a claim. "Get out of there."

"No."

"You are not old enough to understand the liability here."

"I understand dirt fine."

"That is not the same thing."

"It sure is if you're the one in it."

Cora said, "Dell."

He knew what was in her voice. Come out now. Do not make me drag you by the shirt in front of half the town. Do not make me be only your mother when I need to be more than that for another ten minutes. He kept scraping at the tile seam and pretended he had not heard her.

August stepped closer to the lip. "Your father signed papers he did not understand because he believed labor could outtalk arithmetic. There is no honor in repeating him."

Dell stood so fast he cracked the back of his head on the dirt wall. Pain flashed white. He did not feel it properly.

"Don't you talk about him like you knew a damn thing."

Cora's spade bit too deep and hung in the wall quivering. Her glove cinched hard on the handle, leather giving a dry little squeal, and she turned toward Dell with one shoulder already set to bodily lever him out before speech caught up. Thea set down the bucket she was carrying and came closer, her face sharper than Dell had seen it at school or supper either one.

August's jaw shifted once. "I knew enough."

"Then say it straight," Dell said. His tongue stumbled over the start and made him hate himself more. "Say what he signed for. Say what you changed."

The crowd heard that. Dell felt them hear it. Some had likely known pieces already. Some had guessed. Nobody in Morrow ever carried a secret alone for long. But there was a difference between guesswork and a boy saying it into the open where the bank president had to answer or refuse.

August did not answer. That was answer enough. He let his gaze move from Dell to Cora and back again, looking for the place where family shame might do his work for him.

"This excavation ends now," he said. "If it does not, I will have the county look at it under safety authority before dusk."

"Then you'd better look quick," Clem Havel said. "We're not putting the dirt back for your convenience."

Mrs. Koehler said, "If there's wet under there, folks have a right to know."

August turned, startled enough by her to show it. She was maybe fifty, with a hat the color of old cream and a red seam ripper tucked through the ribbon because she never kept her hands out of mending long. Dell had seen Mrs. Koehler twice in Hester's bakery buying yesterday bread, counting exact coins onto the counter because a widow with a bank note did not get to be sloppy. He liked her immediately for making August have to spend authority on somebody he considered beneath the price of it.

"A right to know what?" August asked.

"What was done," she said. "If you don't care for the wording, fetch another one."

Hester coughed then, a bad one. She bent at the waist and pressed the muslin hard to her mouth until it passed. Nobody moved to help because Hester hated being helped in the middle of work. When she straightened again her voice came out rougher and lower.

"Dell. Keep to the seam. Mrs. Vale, cut the north side back two inches. Miss Bell, get that boy out of the lip if he means to stare himself into the hole."

Thea put a hand lightly between Rudy's shoulder blades and steered him back. "You can carry the bucket and keep your legs," she said.

Rudy, blushing under dust, grabbed the bucket handle as if he had been given command of the whole operation.

August did not leave. He stood with one hand on the car door and watched while Dell went back to work because stopping under that gaze would have felt like laying down flat in front of him. Every shovelful came out heavier now. Damp had started somewhere below the tile, not enough to show yet but enough to change the smell. It smelled nothing like rain or the hot-cow stink of a stock pond. Something greener than the rest of Morrow lay buried there, sour from being shut up.

Dell heard Cora breathing through her nose, the warning rhythm she had when she was angry enough to cut her own tongue if she opened her mouth too soon. She worked beside him in quick, exact strokes, shaving the north wall back and tossing spoil clear. Thea and the others widened the upper lip. Hester kept sighting the stakes. August stayed where he was, a black note at the edge of everything.

When Dell's shovel bit under the shifted tile and gave in a soft, sudden way, he knew before the dirt changed color.

"Hold," he said.

Nobody listened at first. The rhythm was in them by then. Dig, lift, throw, cough, wipe, dig. He straightened and said it louder.

"Hold up."

They did. Dell crouched, jammed his fingers into the spot under the tile, and pulled up a handful of cold black mud. Water did not run. It gathered. It shone inside the mud like something looking back.

For one absurd second nobody said a word.

Then the whole edge of the trench seemed to breathe in.

Cora came down beside him. Her glove darkened at the fingertips when she touched the mud. "It's wet," she said, and the words were so small they hit harder than shouting.

"Of course it's wet," August said from above, too quickly. "A low place after a season of storms can hold damp below the crust. That proves nothing except poor judgment."

Dell would have answered him, but the mud in Cora's glove had gone brighter than mud had any right to go. The brightness did not come from light. It came from charge. Wetness slicked the leather and suddenly Dell was not looking at his mother's hand anymore. He was looking at Otto Blum waist-deep in summer grass that reached almost to his elbows when the wind passed through it. Otto was younger than any of them had a right to remember him, hat shoved back, survey stake in one hand and anger in the other. He jabbed the stake down and turned toward men off to the side of the image.

"You cut this basin open," he said, the words wavering in the wet shine, "and the topsoil will go traveling. You won't stop it after."

The picture shook and vanished. Cora jerked her hand back hard enough to spatter Dell's shirt with black dots.

Hester made a sound in her throat that was not surprise. More like recognition arriving late.

"You saw him," she said.

"We all did," said the man with the tobacco cough.

He had. On the steel of his own shovel blade now, where a smear of mud ran thin, there were children on ice. There were five, maybe six of them, thick stockings under skirts, caps pulled down, coats flying open because movement itself kept them warm. The basin held winter water in the image, skimmed over silver and hard, and the children were cutting circles across it on skates with their arms out for balance. One girl threw back her head laughing and Dell knew, by the sharp nose and the scarf flung loose behind her, that Mrs. Koehler had not come for water alone. Mrs. Koehler's mouth opened with no sound in it.

"My sister," she said a moment later. "That was Lucy. Lord."

Rudy forgot his bucket and tried to lean in. Thea caught the back of his shirt before he could slide to his knees at the edge.

"Stay where your feet are," she said, but she was staring too.

The wet seemed to go looking for surfaces. Hands. Shovel backs. The curved lip of the exposed tile. A patch of water shining in the palm print Dell had left on the wall. Memory sprang and vanished too fast to settle, but not too fast to hurt. A boy in overalls driving geese from the reeds. A line of women lifting skirts to wade after a wash tub that had drifted free in spring flood. Otto again, older this time, kneeling to press black soil between finger and thumb while somebody in church clothes stood above him impatient and dry. The basin gave back not one thing but a crowd of things. The mud had held every witness in suspension, waiting for touch.

People forgot caution first and then forgot sides. Mrs. Koehler was crying without trying not to. Clem said, "Look there, look there," in a cracked voice he would probably deny later. Even August leaned in despite himself when a glaze of water spread over the tile joint and showed a row of men laying the drain line, sleeves rolled, boots sinking. One of them raised his head and Dell knew the set of the jaw before he let himself know it: Ezra, younger and not yet bowed, passing down tile like labor could pay for whatever bargain had begun outside the frame.

The tobacco-cough man crossed himself and said, "That isn't natural."

"Neither's draining a basin dry and calling it progress," Clem said.

Dell dropped the shovel.

The memory shifted as if the wet had taken his shock for instruction. The trench wall beside his bare forearm darkened and threw up another image, closer, meaner. Ezra in August's office with his hat in both hands, not the last-year stoop but the beginnings of it, his face thinner than Dell liked to think it had ever been.

"One season," Ezra said. "Give me one honest season and I'll make it hold."

August, younger too, only half in the frame, said something Dell could not catch. The wet shivered. Ezra's mouth pulled tight like a man swallowing whatever was left of pride because swallowing still cost less than losing land in front of his wife. Then the wall was only dirt again.

Dell felt the whole trench tip under him though the ground had not moved.

He had wanted proof. Now proof was on his skin and it was worse than a lie because it showed want plain. Ezra wanting a season. Otto wanting the basin left alone. The town wanting yield out of something that had once only held water and birds and children in winter. Nobody clean in it. Nobody clean and everybody still hurting over what got taken.

August found his voice before anyone else found sense.

"Back from the edge," he said. "All of you. This is exactly the problem. Emotion is not engineering. The wall is sweating. Get back."

He was right about the wall. Dell could see the change now that he had stopped seeing only what he wanted. Damp had begun to stripe the cut, dark veins spreading through the pale face of the dirt. Grains loosened and trickled in quiet little runs. One clod let go from the south lip and dropped by his boot.

Hester saw it too. "Planks," she said. "We should have had more damned planks. Dell, out. Mrs. Vale, get him out while I think."

That should have been the moment. He knew it even then. The moment a sensible man took his proof and climbed clear. But there, under the tile, he could see a packed plug of root and silt where the line had collapsed in on itself. He understood it in an instant the same way he understood a jammed mower blade or a fan belt riding wrong. Break that choke and the seep would show stronger. Water would come plain enough even August could not sermon it away. He could make the ground answer now, not later after the county, not after the bank, not after the story got turned into paperwork again.

"Just one more cut," he said.

"No," Cora said.

"Dell," Thea said from above, and there was more alarm in the shape of his name than he wanted from her or anyone.

August said, "If you stay in there, you are choosing foolishness over witness."

That did it. Dell looked up at him and all he could hear beneath the words was the barn that night months ago, Ezra on the other side of the wall making the kind of sound a person was not supposed to hear from a father, and Dell staying in his bed because morning always came and because he had not wanted to be the child who told on despair. He had listened and done nothing, and in the morning there had been rope and afterward there had been years of adults using hush as if it were kindness.

Not this time.

He snatched up the spud bar, drove the point into the packed choke below the tile, and leaned his weight on it.

Cora cursed. Hester said his name like a warning dropped down a well. Somebody on the edge shouted for him to get out. He heard all of it and put his shoulder harder into the iron anyway. The choke held for one second, two. Then it gave with a wet sucking break.

Cold shot up around the bar. No gush followed, no miracle either. A harder, cleaner seep pushed black water and mud into the trench bottom and made the whole cut smell suddenly alive, rank with roots and shut-up summer.

The crowd cried out in one ragged voice.

Rudy tore free of Thea's hand at exactly the wrong moment.

Maybe he meant only to see better. Maybe he meant to hand Dell the bucket because being useful mattered more to boys than fear did. Dell never knew. He only saw the blue-yarn suspender flash at the lip and then disappear as the boy's foot sheared the softened edge away. Rudy slid in sideways, arms pinwheeling, heels throwing mud.

Dell moved before thought caught up. The body knew some debts too well.

He dropped the bar, lunged through the seep, and caught Rudy under the armpits just as the boy hit the trench bottom. The impact jarred Dell to the teeth. Rudy was all bone and panic and sudden child-stink, not much weight except that fear made him grab. Dirt started ticking down from the south wall in a dry, quick pattern Dell understood with perfect clarity and too late.

"Take him," Dell shouted.

Cora was already flat on her stomach at the lip, one gloved arm reaching. Thea dropped beside her without hesitation, skirt dragging in mud, both women stretched over the edge so far Dell thought the wall would claim them next. Their shoulders slammed together. Neither noticed.

Dell got a hand in the seat of Rudy's overalls and heaved. The boy kicked once against Dell's bad leverage and then Cora had his wrist and Thea had the back of his shirt and they hauled him upward in one jerking motion that showed every person standing there exactly how practiced they were at moving toward the same danger together.

August saw it. Dell saw August see it. There was no time to do anything with that.

The south wall came in.

It did not roar at first. It sighed. A whole slice of wet dirt simply let go and folded toward him, taking the upper lip, a board, and half the space where he had been standing. Dell tried to turn with it, to throw his left arm up over his head the way you did when a cultivator blade kicked or a chain snapped. The collapsing wall hit his shoulder and chest and spun him sideways into the exposed tile. Something in his left forearm knocked once inside his skin, hard and private as a hammer on green wood. Then the dirt put its weight on him and the breath went out.

For a second there was no pain because there was no room for it. Only weight. Wet dirt over his hip, across his ribs, pinning the broken arm under a pressure so exact he could feel each pulse beat around it. His mouth filled with black grit and cold water. He spat and sucked in muddy air that smelled of roots, clay, and somebody's boot sole inches from his face.

Voices came from far off and right near both at once.

"Dell."

"Get back from the lip."

"Jesus Christ."

"Don't pull that side, you'll bring more."

His mother was saying his name. She was not shouting. That was worse. He tried to answer and coughed dirt onto his own shirtfront. When he moved the left arm, white tore through him so fast it seemed clean, cleaner than the basin water, cleaner than thought. He made a sound he did not recognize until it was already out in the open for everybody.

"Don't," Hester said, close now, command riding over breathlessness. "Dell, don't fight the wall. We've got you. We've got you if you hold still."

Hold still. Stillness had never saved anybody much in this town, but he did. He could not have done otherwise. The weight on his ribs had turned breathing into a mean little trade, each swallow of air won shallow and paid back with pain.

He saw boots above him, edges of skirts, the dark belly of August's coat as the man came nearer despite everything. Hester was on her knees at the cut, muslin gone from her throat now, the cough forgotten. Her hands were already working the soil away from Dell's shoulder with the care of somebody turning loaves from tins. Cora dug barehanded because she had stripped off the gloves and lost them. Her wind-cracked fingers were black to the knuckles. Thea was beside her, not speaking much, only asking once, very calm, "Can you breathe?" and making the question sound almost possible.

He nodded because that was easier than saying mostly. Mud slid into his ear.

August said, "You need the plank under that section before you lift him."

Nobody answered. Then, to Dell's surprise, August crouched and reached for the board that had fallen half into the collapse. Clem helped him. Between them they braced it against the slumping south face while Hester and Cora cleared Dell's chest enough for a fuller breath. The extra air hurt so much he nearly blacked out from it.

"Arm?" Thea asked.

He looked because he could not stop himself. The left forearm lay at a wrong angle under the muck, not grotesque, worse than that, familiar enough still to be his and wrong enough that the sight climbed into his stomach. Mud was plastered over the skin. The wrist had already begun to swell above his hand. He shut his eyes.

"Don't touch it yet," Hester said.

"I wasn't going to," Cora snapped, and the snap broke on the next word. "Dell, look at me."

He opened his eyes because she had asked in the voice from fever nights and bad weather, the one she used only when command had gone past anger into fear.

Her face above him was streaked with dirt except where sweat had made clean runnels. Thea had one hand flat between Cora's shoulder blades, steadying her. It was not a schoolteacher's touch. It was not a boarder's either. It was the touch of somebody who knew where Cora would break if she broke. Dell saw August's gaze flick there and away. Even half buried and half sick with pain, he knew that mattered.

"On three," Hester said. "We lift his right side first. Not the arm. August, if you mean to be useful, hold that plank and keep your mouth shut."

The man actually obeyed.

Rudy was crying somewhere behind them, not loud anymore. Shame had gotten into it. Dell wanted to tell him to shut up, not because the boy deserved it but because listening to a child cry after being the reason the child had something to cry for made the whole thing worse. He could not spare the breath.

"One," Hester said.

Hands dug under Dell's right shoulder, his good arm, his hip.

"Two."

Thea said, very near his ear, "Bite your sleeve if you need to."

"Three."

They rolled him just enough to free the crushed side. Pain came all at once then, complete and indecent. It was in the arm first, a blinding bright line from wrist to elbow, then in the ribs where the wall had pinned him, a deeper spread that made the world narrow to a torn strip. He bit cloth and tasted mud and himself. Somebody said his name again and he thought it was Cora, but it might have been Thea. When they dragged him clear of the trench bottom and onto the stable ground, the sky above looked too large and yellow to belong to the same day.

He lay on a feed sack while Hester knelt by the broken arm and considered it with the terrible respect of a competent person. Thea brought the water bucket. Cora wiped mud from Dell's mouth with the corner of her own sleeve, then seemed to realize what she was doing only after the tenderness was already visible.

"Ribs?" Hester asked.

He tried a bigger breath and saw stars. "Hurts."

"That tells me very little."

"Then it hurts plenty."

The corner of Hester's mouth moved despite everything. "There he is."

She palpated his side with flour-broad fingers, gentle and merciless both. Dell jerked when she found the worst place.

"Bruised," she said. "Maybe cracked, but I don't think broken through. Arm is the ugly part."

"Can you set it?" Cora asked.

"Not here proper. I can make it travel."

Rudy appeared at the edge of Dell's vision, muddy to the thighs, face gone blotchy under dust. The blue yarn suspender had snapped loose completely and hung against his side. He had one of Dell's gloves clutched in both hands.

"I didn't mean-" the boy started.

Dell wanted to be cruel because pain made cruelty easy and because the whole town was looking. Instead he looked at Rudy's shaking chin and thought of a barn wall and the sound of somebody not being saved in time.

"I know," he said.

Rudy swallowed. "You shoved me up."

"Looks like I did."

The boy nodded once, hard, driving the fact into himself. Then he held out the muddy glove without stepping any closer.

Thea took it from him. "Go sit by the wagon wheel and stay there till your legs remember what they're for."

He obeyed at once. Thea's school voice had never entirely left her, even with mud to her calves.

Hester sent Clem to the wagon for slats and sent Mrs. Koehler for binder twine. August said, "He should be moved before the swelling worsens," which was sensible and infuriating in equal measure. Cora did not look at him.

"Dell," she said. "I'm going to hold your elbow. Miss Bell will hold your wrist. Hester's going to straighten it enough for the sling. You hear me?"

He heard her. He wished he had not. He also heard her call Thea Miss Bell with half the town standing there, and saw her hand find Thea's forearm before letting go again, a quick press. The two names fought in Cora's mouth, and only one could come out safe.

"Do it," he said, because if he waited any longer he might start pleading and never stop.

Hester did not count down. That was kindness. She took the wrist from Thea, found the line the bones ought to have made, and pulled while Cora held Dell steady with both hands and a knee against his hip. The correction itself was brief. The body afterward was not. Pain ripped clear through him, so hard he could not hear the sound he made, only feel it scraping the inside of his throat. Thea's hand came over his mouth without thought, not to silence him exactly but to give him something to press against, and Cora bent so low above him their faces nearly touched.

"That's it," Thea said, too fast, then corrected herself into something steadier. "That's it. It's done."

Cora was crying and furious enough at the fact to make the tears look like another form of labor. Thea wiped one off her cheek with a muddy thumb before either of them remembered who was watching. Cora did not flinch from the touch. That was the part Dell saw August register with that banker stillness of his, the kind that made a man look calm only because he was storing what he meant to spend later.

The splint was ugly and workable: two wagon slats padded with folded feed sack, tied at wrist and below elbow, then hung in a sling ripped from one of Thea's petticoat ties and part of the woman's jar rag. Hester tied the knot herself.

"Don't faint before we get you to the house," she said.

"Wasn't planning on it."

"You don't plan most things that happen to you."

That would have been worth a better answer if Dell had had one. He was shaking now with the aftermath, not cold exactly, though the seep had put cold into his clothes. Every jolt of breath found the bruised ribs and worried them. He could smell the basin on himself, a dark wet smell under sweat and dust and fear. He hated how badly he wanted to look back at the trench.

He managed it anyway when they lifted him.

Saint's Basin had not turned beautiful. The cut was a wound in the earth, sloppy-sided and dangerous, with trampled spoil heaps and tools dropped wherever shock had broken hold of hands. But there, in the bottom where the choke had given, black water still pushed up in a narrow steady ooze and spread through the trench floor until it caught the light in patches. People stood around it differently now. They were not spectators, and they were not owners either. They looked like mourners around a body that had coughed once after being declared dead.

Clem had taken off his hat. Mrs. Koehler was crouched near the lip, not trying to fill the jars now, only watching the seep as if it might show Lucy skating again. Rudy sat by the wagon wheel with both elbows on his knees, staring at the mud on his shins as if he had never worn that much of the earth before. Hester, already swaying a little from the effort she would deny, pointed with two fingers and said, "Nobody goes back in that cut till I say. Shore it from both sides. Cover the lip if you've got boards. It's water, not a blessing."

August brushed mud from his cuff and failed. Dell almost laughed and turned it into a grimace when his ribs objected.

"This site will be closed under safety authority," August said. His voice had recovered its formal shell, but only mostly. There was a crack in it now, anger or fear or both. "I will not have another injury here."

"You'll have trouble stopping every person who saw what they saw," Mrs. Koehler said. Her shoe-ribbon hat had slipped sideways. She did not fix it.

August ignored her. He looked at Cora instead, then at Thea, then back to the splinted arm in Cora's hold. Dell could not tell whether the man was angrier at the seep, the crowd, or what he had just learned without being told.

"Mrs. Vale," August said, "your son needs a doctor."

"Then move your car out of the wagon track," Cora said.

It was not clever. It was better than clever. August stepped back.

They got Dell into Hester's wagon because it rode softer than the Ford over broken field. Cora climbed up first and settled with her back braced against the sideboard. Thea passed Dell's good arm over Cora's shoulders and then came up after him without asking where she was meant to ride. There was barely room. She made room anyway, one hand under the sling to keep the broken arm from swinging, the other pressed flat over the feed sack at Dell's ribs every time the wagon shifted, trying to hold the pain in place by force.

From that close Dell could smell dust in her hair and starch gone damp in her blouse. Cora's thigh was hard against his. Neither woman seemed aware anymore of how their bodies had arranged themselves around him, only of the fact that they had. On the ground below, August stood with his hat in one hand, looking up at them with his ledger face on, the one he wore when a number had gone missing and he could not yet prove theft. Hester saw him looking. The corner of her mouth sharpened.

"You'll be late to the bank," she said.

August put the hat back on. "This is not finished."

"No," Hester said. "It isn't."

The wagon lurched toward home. Pain ran bright from Dell's left arm to the base of his neck and settled behind his eyes. He bit it down and watched the basin slide away in pieces between the jolts: the black seam of seep at the trench bottom, the tools leaned careless in the spoil, the little crowd still held there by water and memory and the knowledge that the ground under all of Morrow might remember more than any of them could afford. He had wanted the place opened. He had wanted it enough to mistake urgency for skill. Saint's Basin had opened all right. It had only taken its payment in bone.


# Chapter 12

Dell woke her by swallowing the cry instead of letting it out.

Cora had slept crooked in the cane chair with her forearm across the quilt, feeling for him even in sleep the way she once used to feel for a calf in a hard birth, counting on touch more than sight. Before dawn the room was the color of old tin. The lamp had burned low enough to crust the chimney with soot. Dell lay in her bed on top of the sheet because he could not bear weight over his ribs, his left arm splinted between two shaved laths from a peach crate, wrapped in torn pillowcase strips already going gray from sweat and dust. He had managed to turn half an inch onto his right side, and that half inch had turned his face white under the fever.

He kept his teeth shut. That was how she knew it was bad.

She was up before he asked. The enamel basin waited under the chair with a rag folded in the bottom to keep it from clanging. She got it under his chin just as his breath hitched loose. He retched thin yellow bitter into it and shook afterward as if the bed were laid over a thresher.

"Easy," she said. "Get it up."

"I'm not a horse."

"No. Horses are better tempered."

The answer cost him a look. His right eye opened on her, mean and glazed together. Sweat had pasted his dust-light hair to his forehead in little hooks. There was a cut in the scalp above his ear, not deep but ugly with dried blood, and the bruising along his right side had gone from dirt-brown to the color of smashed plums. Hester had bound the arm straight the evening before, jaw set, cough trapped in her chest like she would rather crack herself than let the boys from town see it hurt. Cora had thought the splint looked too rough then. In the night it had become precious, the only thing holding bone from shifting against bone.

Dell spat once more and tried to take the basin with his good hand. His fingers missed the rim.

"Leave it," Cora said.

"I can hold a basin."

"You can't hit one."

He looked away at that, which was how shame came over him now. Not in apology. In refusal. He fixed his eyes on the window, on the flour sack tacked over the lower pane against the light, and let her wipe his mouth like he was eight with summer croup again. The rag came back streaked with bile and the dust they had never fully gotten off him after hauling him in from Saint's Basin. The trench dirt had been black and damp where the seep rose. It still clung in the shell of his ear, under the edge of the bandage, at the roots of his hair. The ground had marked him and followed him home.

Behind the bedroom wall the house was already asking for things. Hens fretted at the run. The pump chain knocked once in the breeze. Somewhere on the shelf in the kitchen a fly worried itself against a jelly glass. The chores had not gone away because her son had come out of the basin half crushed. They had only acquired an audience.

Dell let his head fall back. The movement made him suck air between his teeth. He tried to hide that too.

"How hot?" he muttered.

"Hot enough to be tiresome."

"That isn't a number."

"You think I keep a thermometer tucked in my garter?"

His mouth twitched, almost a smile, and then the pain took it. He closed his eyes. Cora laid the back of her hand along his throat, then his cheek, then under the rough fall of his hair. Fever had a grain to it. Not just heat. A kind of dry, rising insistence, as if the body were trying to fire itself clean from the inside. She had felt it in Ezra once with the winter grippe, and in Dell when he was a small boy with whooping cough, and in calves that lived and calves that did not. Dell's skin had that same kiln heat now, bright and wrong under the sweat.

The door eased open with care. Thea came in carrying the kettle with a dish towel around the handle and a chipped cup balanced in the crook of her fingers. She had not bothered remaking herself after the night. Her dark hair was twisted up anyhow with one proper pin and one straightened paper clip, and the collar of yesterday's blouse had wilted flat from having been slept in. One stocking had been darned at the ankle with blue thread instead of black because blue was what she had. She set the cup on the washstand and did not look at Cora first. She looked at Dell, quick and professional and unwilling to let pity show where he could see it.

"Hester sent willow bark," she said. "And there were two aspirin left in the tin. I crushed one."

"You used both?" Cora asked.

"One. The other is there if he keeps climbing."

Dell opened his eyes again. "Don't feed me bark like a mule."

Thea drew a breath through her nose. "Then don't burn like one."

He would have snapped at that on any other day. This morning he only shut his mouth hard enough to show the tendon in his jaw. Thea held the cup out. Cora slid an arm behind Dell's shoulders and lifted him. The motion jerked an "nnh-God" out of him before he could bite it back, and Cora felt it in her own back as if his body had thrown a hook into hers.

"Drink," she said.

"Smells like fence."

"Drink."

He drank because he had run out of ways not to. A little of it leaked at the corner of his mouth. "God damn it," he said, voice scraped raw. Thea wiped it before he could turn the words into a refusal. He went red at that, a flush visible even through the fever.

"I can do it," he said.

"You would've," Thea said, "if you'd been quicker than the tea."

It was dry enough to pass for schoolroom wit, and that nearly undid Cora. Thea sounded too much like herself. The room was too small for all the parts of life trying to stay separate inside it: Dell in Ezra's old undershirt because his own had been cut off in the yard; Thea at the washstand where she had stood in her slip on gentler nights, pouring water down her own wrists before getting into Cora's bed; the field beyond the curtained glass wearing its failing wheat like a pelt already coming loose. Cora had spent two years keeping those claims from touching one another. Now they knocked together like crockery in a sink.

Thea set the cup down and reached for the edge of the bandage. "I need to look at the hand."

"You saw it yesterday."

"Yesterday you were still deciding whether to faint for effect."

"I didn't faint."

"Dell," Cora said.

He took that tone for what it was. Thea unwrapped the strip nearest the wrist. His fingers were swollen thicker than their shape, the nails lined in black from the basin dirt. The hand had gone shiny with it, puffed and alien, and the sight of it made Cora's stomach cinch harder than the vomit had. Dell saw her seeing.

"Don't start," he said.

"I'm not starting anything."

"Your face is."

Thea's voice stayed level. "Can you feel this?"

She touched each finger in turn. Dell answered without looking, impatient until she brushed the little finger and he hesitated a beat too long.

"That one," he said. "Not right away."

Thea glanced up at Cora, already counting what still worked and what didn't.

"Move them."

He did, slow and ugly, but he did. Thea let out a breath she had been holding and began to bind the wrist again, firmer this time.

"There," she said.

"Feels worse."

"That means it's tighter."

"That wasn't the bargain."

"You don't get bargains this week," Cora said.

His eyes flicked to her and off again. Bargain was a word with too much blood on it in this house. She had not meant to choose it. Or maybe she had, because anger was easier to spend than fear and she was running low on easier things. She softened her voice by hand, not by instinct, and pulled the sheet up over his thighs.

"You need more sleep."

"I need to piss."

The sentence sat there, plain and hateful. Thea looked toward the chamber pot under the washstand. Dell followed the look and went scarlet clear to the tips of his ears.

"Not in here."

"You aren't walking to the privy by yourself," Cora said.

"Then I won't go."

"That isn't a real plan."

He pushed at the mattress with his good hand. The effort twisted his ribs and he went gray again before he got fully upright. Cora caught him under the shoulders. Thea took his right elbow from the other side without asking permission because there was no time left for permission.

"I said no," Dell snapped, not clear whether he meant the chamber pot, Thea's hand, or the fact of being seen weak at all.

"And I said stand up if you can," Cora answered. "Come on."

They got him as far as the porch because that was the compromise he could bear. Morning had come up white and merciless, already hot under the eaves. The yard smelled of chicken droppings, pump iron, and the damp mineral stink the basin mud had left on his clothes in the pile by the steps. Dell leaned one shoulder to the porch post, panting. He had his head down and his hair fell in his eyes, making him look younger for the half second before shame remembered itself and set his mouth hard again.

"Turn around," Cora said.

"I know how."

"Then prove it."

His good hand fumbled at the fly buttons. He could not manage the second one. The left arm hung splinted against his belly, useless as a stick. Thea turned her face toward the yard with a kind of deliberate courtesy that made the moment more naked, not less. Dell got one button free, missed the next, and said, "Come on. Come on, damn you." His fingers slipped again. "Jesus Christ." At last Cora stepped in and worked the cloth open.

He pissed on the toe of his own shoe from shaking. The stream hit the porch dust and made dark stars. His neck went red all the way down under the collarbone.

"Go to hell," he said hoarsely, though there was no one left to address it to except the day.

Cora did not answer. She wiped the leather with the hem of her apron before the urine could turn the dust into caked paste and then hated him seeing that almost as much as he hated needing it. When he sagged, she got her hip under him on instinct, the same way she had done when he was a sleepy child hauled in from the wagon. He was long now, all knees and shoulder blades and the loose weight of a boy nearly grown, but pain returned him to body alone. There was no pride in body alone. There was only what it could and could not hold.

Back in bed he shook with the effort until the mattress rope creaked under him. Thea wet a cloth in the basin and laid it along his throat. Dell flinched and then pressed into it in spite of himself. He watched the ceiling for a while, breathing shallow. At length he said, "Did the water stay?"

Thea looked at Cora. Cora answered because if she did not the silence would tell him too much. "There was still seep when we left."

"Good."

"Good," Cora said, harsher than she meant. "You near took your fool neck off to see it."

He swallowed. His voice went smaller for the first time all morning, which was worse than the meanness had been. "Didn't get my neck."

No, she thought. Only the arm. Only the ribs. Only the fever that had come up in the night and made the bed smell of wet wool and sick iron. Only enough to move the whole weight of the house off its blocks.

He drifted after that, not into clean sleep but into a muttering half-depth where he kept saying things to the air that belonged to the basin. Grade. Hold the wall. Watch the boy. His right hand twitched once as if closing on a shovel handle. Cora sat with the cloth and changed it when it warmed. Thea went out to throw the vomit into the weeds and came back with the hens fed, which ought to have relieved Cora and instead made her feel displaced in her own morning. She took the kettle to the kitchen before that feeling could turn ugly. The stove still wore last night's cinders. Bread ends had gone hard on the board. A fly had gotten into the bacon grease crock. She swatted it away and stood with both hands on the table because stillness was harder work than hauling sacks had ever been.

The south window above the sink looked over the fields. From there she could usually judge the day by wheat and wind alone. Today the view was interrupted by the shape of the basin road in her mind, by the image of men shoveling, boys leaning too near the cut, wet black soil lifting after years of white powder. She had wanted the truth up and breathing. Yesterday it had come up breathing and nearly taken her son down with it.

Thea came in behind her, carrying the aspirin tin and the second cup. "You need something."

"I need ten hands."

"I can be one of them."

Cora took the cup because refusing kindness was a luxury and she had no room for luxuries before noon. The coffee was thin enough to show the bottom of the cup through it, but it was hot. Thea had put in the last spoon of sugar without asking. Cora knew because the sweetness startled her mouth.

"If he's worse by afternoon, he needs a doctor," Thea said.

"From where."

"Wilton. Even if we have to send to the highway and flag somebody."

"And jolt him six miles over ruts with busted ribs."

"I know."

Thea leaned her shoulder to the doorframe, arms folded tight enough to keep from reaching. Public composure sat on her oddly in a kitchen. It belonged on the schoolhouse floorboards, not here with a kettle hissing and Dell half-delirious in the next room. When she grew frightened, she became careful first and blunt after, as though language had to take off its gloves one finger at a time.

"I know," she said again. "I'm only saying stubbornness isn't a treatment."

"And panic isn't either."

"This isn't panic."

"Then what is it."

Thea's mouth thinned. "Arithmetic."

Cora almost laughed at that and could not. Arithmetic had lived too long in the bank and on note paper and in men's mouths. Yet there it was in her own kitchen now, measuring breath against miles, pain against money. She set the cup down harder than she needed to and went back to the bedroom before arithmetic could settle in.

Dell had kicked the sheet off. The cloth on his throat was hot as wash water. She changed it, slid the pillow straighter, watched him fret at sleep with the same furious little movements he used to make when tangles in fishing line beat him. Every few minutes the house asked again for hens, for dishes, for pump water, for the field, for the basin, for the farm itself. Every few minutes Dell dragged a breath and answered for her.

By late morning the light had gone hard enough to show every fly in the room. Cora had managed the chicken mash, drawn two buckets, and stripped the basin-dirt clothes to soak in lye water without once going farther from the house than the pump. She came back from pinning a sheet over the line to the sound of an engine on the road.

The motor died outside her gate with the stuttering cough of August Poeschel's Ford.

* * *

He did not come to the door at once. Cora saw him first through the veil of the hanging sheet: a broad dark shape on the other side of white cloth, standing still as if arranging himself before entry. Then he stepped around it into the yard, hat on, jacket buttoned though the heat already had sweat darkening the band under his arms. Dust had chalked the hems of his trousers. He carried two folded papers and one narrow board under his arm, painted in quick black capitals. He had shaved clean that morning. That was somehow the ugliest part of him.

Thea, at the line with the clothespin basket hooked over her wrist, went still enough to make the pins stop clicking. One of them dropped into the dirt and neither woman stooped for it.

August lifted a hand, not quite greeting. "Mrs. Vale."

Cora stayed on the porch. "You lost your way to town?"

His eyes moved past her shoulder before coming back. That was all. A flick. Yet she felt it land in the open bedroom door where Dell lay in her bed and in the damp sleeve of Thea's blouse rolled to the elbow. August's face did not change. He smoothed one of the folded papers against the board with the flat of his palm, the motion precise enough to count as a tell.

"I have posted the basin closed," he said. "At the road and at the cut itself. No entry until the county can inspect the ground."

Cora looked at the board. *KEEP OFF. CAVE-IN DANGER.* The words were blunt enough for a fence line, but the paper under his hand was bank language, dense and official.

"The county," she said. "Or you."

"The distinction does not alter the risk."

"It alters who gets to call it."

He took that and set it in the ledger place behind his face where he kept things from becoming personal. "A boy was nearly killed yesterday. The walls are unstable. More people will come to look if it is left open. If there is another collapse, the liability will spread farther than excitement seems to understand."

"Liability."

"Yes."

"That what Dell is now."

August's jaw shifted once. "He is a wounded boy. Which is why I am here before rumor outruns fact."

"Fact outran you yesterday."

Thea spoke before either of them could sharpen further. Her voice came measured, schoolhouse-flat, though the basket handle had gone white in her grip. "Did you post guards."

August turned a little toward her. "No. I posted warning and notice. I am not staffing a battlefield, Miss Bell."

"Then the sign is for appearances."

"The sign is because language is what a town still has before it has force."

That almost sounded tired enough to be true. It angered Cora more. He was always worst when he spoke as if the injury had burdened him with philosophy.

"You want it shut till the water sinks back out of sight," she said. "Then you'll say folks imagined the rest."

"I want no more broken bones hauled out of mud."

"You wanted no mud."

"Mrs. Vale." His voice changed only by tightening, not volume. "For once I am speaking only of safety."

The bedroom floorboards creaked inside. "Shit," Dell said into the mattress, and then something thumped the washstand. Cora had been poised to come down the steps. Instead she turned her head toward the room before she could stop herself.

August saw it. Of course he saw it.

"I will leave the notice here," he said. "You can tell Hester Blum if she comes riding at you with righteous purpose. If the boy worsens, send word. That is not charity. I would rather have a live witness than a dead one."

There it was, stripped clean: accountancy spoken plain enough to almost pass for honesty, too barefaced to call itself mercy.

Cora took one step down until she was eye level with him. "Get your paper off my porch."

For the first time something like temper leaked through the banker's careful shave. "You may call it mine if that helps you. The earth did not collapse because I put ink on a page."

"No," Cora said. "Just most of the rest of it."

He looked at her a moment longer than was safe. Then he set the folded notice on the porch rail instead of handing it to her and tucked the board back under his arm. As he turned, his gaze caught once more on Thea by the sheet line, on her rolled sleeves and the clothespins hanging from her fingers like a row of little jaws. He touched the brim of his hat to no one and went back to the car.

The engine took two tries. By the time it rattled away, Dell was calling from the bedroom, voice ragged with pain and temper both. Cora left the paper where August had laid it and went inside.

He had tried to sit and failed halfway. Sweat had darkened the pillow. The washstand basin had tipped, slopping brownish water down the leg to the floor.

"What did he want."

"Nothing worth your breath."

"What did he want."

Cora shoved the basin upright with her foot and got an arm behind him. "He posted the cut. That's all."

"Son of a bitch."

"Save it."

"He can't post water."

"He can post dirt. Lie back."

Dell tried to resist, because resistance was the only property he had full title to anymore, but the effort stripped him fast. He hissed when the mattress took his ribs. His eyes had gone bright and foolish with fever. That frightened Cora more than moaning would have. Dell usually kept his feelings under his tongue like a plug of bad tobacco. Fever loosened them.

"I should've stayed there," he said. "If they'd shored it from the west side—"

"You should've kept your ass out of a collapsing trench."

"Kid would've gone under."

"Then the kid's mother can thank you by letting you keep your arm."

His mouth worked. The word mother moved across his face before he decided against it. "Water was there," he said instead, stubborn as the heat. "You saw it."

Yes. She had seen the black shine gathering in the broken basin floor, the impossible wet under a country that had forgotten wet. She had seen men and boys and women all crowding close to it with the same hungry expression, as if water itself had become testimony. She had seen Dell drop his shovel and lunge because a smaller body slid where the bank gave way. She had seen the trench wall take shape in air before it hit him. There were truths she would not surrender. There were truths she would not pay twice for either.

"I saw it," she said. "Now shut your eyes."

He did not obey, but fever made the refusal weak. He turned his face to the wall and bit down on whatever came next.

Hester arrived an hour later with a coffee can of broth wrapped in a towel and a handkerchief full of bread ends. She came up the steps slower than usual because one heel of her shoe had been re-pegged with a sliver of tin and kept catching in the boards. She swore at the heel under her breath, then at her own breath when it turned into coughing. By the time Thea opened the door, Hester had one hand braced on the jamb and the other pressed to the breastbone under her black dress.

"Don't fuss," she said before either of them could. "I only lent my lungs to a foolish century. They are collecting."

She smelled of yeast, cloves, and field dust. Her broad fingers were flour-caught at the creases as if no amount of washing got a working life fully out. She set the can down on the kitchen table, untied the towel, and peered toward the bedroom with eyes that had gone sharper the more the rest of her wore out.

"How bad."

"Hot," Cora said. "Arm still warm in the fingers. Ribs are making him ugly."

"He was ugly before."

"Thank you kindly."

Hester snorted, then coughed again, smaller this time. "August has hung his little commandments at the basin."

"So he said."

"He had Ezra Ott's boy nail one to the cottonwood by the road. Ezra Ott kept taking his hat off to talk, poor fool, like bad news ought to hear itself respectful. The paper says 'closed pending inspection' and 'public hazard' and some other Sunday-school sins. There is still water in the cut, and already half the men on Main are talking as if stepping past that sign would bring their own notes due by supper."

Thea set out bowls. "Then it'll still be there tomorrow."

Hester turned. "That is a hope, not a fact."

"The wall almost killed Dell."

"And the wall already did what it came to do for August if everybody stays home today and calls it prudence."

Cora took the broth to the stove. Grease had clouded on top in pale eyes. She broke it with the spoon and watched it drift apart. Thea must have brought August's folded notice in off the porch rail; it lay beside the breadboard with the black letters ghosting through the paper. The argument was arriving ahead of itself. She could hear it in the shape of Hester's silence, in the way Thea laid the bread on the board one piece at a time instead of all at once.

"What do you want done," Cora asked.

Hester tapped the folded notice with one floury finger. "I want two men who can tell bad ground from solid and some planks before dark. Shore the west lip where Dell went in. Rope the south side. Put somebody grown at the road so every fool boy in town doesn't go skidding down there to stare at the seep. If August gets to leave one sign and call that safety, he buys the basin back for the price of a board."

Thea's laugh came short and wrong. When she got afraid, sometimes the sound slipped out before she could school it. "Free. Dell's in there burning through the sheet. He pissed on his own shoe because he could barely stand. We've got one aspirin left, no doctor in the yard, and you want more people on that lip."

Hester's gaze went to her, dry as flour. "I want planks under boots instead of boys under dirt."

"And when the planks tip?"

"Then the man on them backs off."

"Men don't back off when half the town is watching. You saw yesterday."

Hester set her knuckles on the table as the cough nudged at her chest. "I saw August ride in after the seep showed and start saying hazard like the mud had learned a new word for him. Leave it till morning and the lip slumps, the water clouds over, and by dinner tomorrow folks will say it was only a wet patch." She coughed once, hard enough to bow her a little. "And Dell's bad judgment."

There it was, plain at last. Thea folded the dish towel once, twice, too carefully. "Dell's bad judgment still broke his arm. If he climbs hotter by sundown, I need a wagon that won't shake him to pieces on the Wilton road. I need cash in hand. I need, no, Cora needs a road out if his breathing goes bad. And if Wilton shrugs, we need rail after that."

Hester's mouth thinned. "You count wages and miles. I'm counting mud. The west side is already undercut."

"Fifty-two dollars when the board bothers to send it," Thea said. "That is what my wages are. That and a room here. It won't buy another boy if the basin takes one. Don't ask me to nod along while more men crawl down there because August nailed a warning to a tree."

Hester grew quieter, not softer. "I'm not asking boys. I'm asking for borrowed planks, a rope, and two men old enough to hear dirt shifting. If it sits, August gets the county behind his paper by morning, and then what Dell got broke for is a board and a rumor."

Thea swung round then, controlled no longer. "And if his fever jumps while you're guarding a rumor? Honest doesn't set a bone. Honest doesn't hire the wagon to Wilton. Honest doesn't keep August from standing on the rail and counting who was in this house when he came."

"For us," Hester said. "For Dell when the heat breaks and he asks what flattened him. For Cora when the auction paper comes. For me because I already buried one child in this dust and I'm done letting weather take the blame for men."

That shut Thea for a beat. Not because Hester had won, only because everybody in town knew which child she meant, and Marta's absence was one of the few names nobody handled lightly. Hester put a hand to the table when the coughing threatened again, rode it down, and went on in a lower voice.

"I know what waiting costs. Don't mistake me."

Thea's answer came softer and somehow harsher for it. "I know what staying costs. That is what I'm saying."

Cora had let them run because each was speaking a truth she needed and could not bear. Now Dell knocked the bed ropes against the wall and said, "Shit. Ma," with the second word dragging thin behind it, and the choice she'd been pretending was not yet on the table put its elbows there.

"Enough," she said.

Neither woman stopped at once. She hit the table with the broth spoon hard enough to ring the bowls.

"Enough."

Silence came then, ragged but real.

Cora looked from one to the other. Hester with her flour-lined hands and the handkerchief dark at the middle where she had coughed into it. Thea with the dish towel twisted so tight in her fingers it might tear, dust on her shoes from a house she kept helping survive even while she argued it should be left. Beyond the bedroom door Dell breathed too fast. Beyond the yard the field stood in the noon glare, thin and stubborn and worth less each week it remained upright.

"That hole got my boy once already," Cora said. "It doesn't get him again today."

Hester's mouth set. "Today becomes tomorrow quick."

"Then let tomorrow come."

"Cora."

"No." Her own voice surprised her by its steadiness. "You can call me a coward tomorrow if you like. You can call me August's accomplice. I don't care. Dell comes first."

Thea closed her eyes at that, not in disagreement. In relief, almost. Which stung.

Hester saw the relief too. Something in her face folded inward, not broken but put away like a letter. "I didn't come to call names," she said. "I came because once folks sit beside a sickbed they start believing the world can wait polite outside the door. It can't. That's all."

She reached for the coffee can, then remembered why she had brought it and left it where it sat. The small mistake hurt more than anger would have. Cora stepped toward her.

"Hester."

The older woman shook her head. "Feed him. Cool him. I know the work." She drew the handkerchief from her sleeve and pressed it to her mouth while the cough took hold in earnest this time, shoulders jerking under the black dress. When she could speak again, her voice had roughened. "I buried Marta because we kept saying tomorrow till there wasn't one. Forgive me if I don't like the word."

Thea's grip loosened on the towel. "Hester, I wasn't—"

"I heard what you were."

It was not cruel. It was worse. Accurate and tired.

Hester picked up the bread ends, reconsidered, and set those back too. "There's a boy down the road with a mouth full of loose talk who owes Dell his life. I can likely borrow his uncle and a pair of planks without either of you. Maybe not. We'll see." She moved toward the door, heel scraping. On the threshold she looked over her shoulder at Cora, not Thea. "If he breaks hotter after sundown, put onion to his feet and vinegar under the arms. And keep him drinking, however cross it makes him."

"I know," Cora said.

"Yes," Hester answered. "You do."

When she had gone, the kitchen felt worse than if there had been shouting. The air had thickened with all the things left unsaid because the said things were already enough to carry.

Thea bent and picked up the dropped clothespin from earlier, though it had not been in the kitchen at all. Only after she straightened did she seem to notice she had come back in empty-handed except for that absurd little peg. She set it on the table beside the bowls and laughed once at herself, sharp and tired.

"That was well managed," she said.

"Don't."

"I wasn't being clever."

"Don't do anything right this minute but breathe."

Thea did, once. Twice. Then she braced both palms on the table and looked at Cora full for the first time since Hester had gone. "I'm trying to tell you the farm won't stop asking. Not because I don't understand Hester. I do. God help me, I do. But August put his paper on your rail and stood there counting who was in this house. He can close the basin with a sign, and in a town this size he can make a look do the rest. Ezra, then you, piece by piece, and now Dell. I don't know what amount would finally satisfy you."

The words should have felt like accusation. They felt like a hand forced against a bruise to see how deep it went.

Cora leaned back against the stove so the hot iron pressed through her dress. "You think I don't know what it's taken."

"I think you know it and keep calling the knowing duty."

"What would you call it."

Thea opened her mouth, revised, started again. "Sometimes I'd call it love. Sometimes habit. Sometimes fear dressed up respectable. I don't know. I only know that if he needs to be gotten out of here, you can't let acreage answer before he does."

Acreage. Not farm, not home, not Ezra's place or Dell's inheritance. Acreage, like Walter Knapp had said it, like August wrote it. Thea knew exactly what she was doing by choosing the cold word. It made Cora hate her for a second and trust her the next.

"You make leaving sound clean," Cora said.

"No." Thea's voice dropped. "I make it sound filthy and possible. Those are different things."

From the bedroom Dell called, slurred with heat, "Ma."

Cora was moving before the second syllable finished. Thea followed but stopped at the door while Cora sat on the edge of the mattress and got the cup to Dell's mouth. He drank three swallows obediently, which frightened her more than refusal would have. A bead of water ran into the hollow above his collarbone. His lashes were wet with sweat, darker than his hair. He blinked at her as if waking from a far bad place.

"Did Hester come."

"She came."

"She mad."

"Everybody's mad. Drink."

He took another swallow. "Should've braced the west side."

"Dell."

"I know."

But he did not know, not yet. He knew the shape of a trench and the speed of a boy slipping in dirt and the sound a body made when the earth struck it. He did not know what it did to a woman to stand between a fever bed and a field and feel both call her by rightful names. He might. He would, if she kept him alive long enough.

When he slept again, it came deeper. The aspirin had found him or exhaustion had. Cora changed the cloth at his throat once more and eased the sheet down to where it would not drag the bruises. His splinted arm lay on the pillow like a thing returned from some other household, not yet claimed by his own flesh. She stared at it until the shape stopped making sense.

Thea remained in the doorway, one hand on the frame. Outside, a little wind got up and set the pinned sheet knocking on the line. The sound was domestic, harmless, almost cheerful. It made Cora want to put her fist through the window.

"If he's worse by dawn," Thea said quietly, "we go. Somewhere with a doctor. Somewhere with less of this in the walls. We can decide the name later."

Cora sat very still. She had spent so long answering danger with staying put that motion itself had begun to feel like faithlessness. When Ezra died, she stayed. When August shortened the note, she stayed. When Knapp measured her house with his eyes and called departure mercy, she stayed harder. Staying had become the grammar of her grief. It was the only tense she trusted.

But Dell lay in the bed she had once defended as the center of everything worth holding, and the bed now felt less like an altar than a raft. The room held his heat, the sour wool smell of fever, the bitter steam of willow bark, the faint clean trace of Thea's soap from where she had washed the vomit basin. The farm beyond the wall did not enter except as glare and demand. For the first time since Ezra's rope had cut the barn beam, Cora could imagine the land continuing without her and feel not betrayal first but relief behind it, small and monstrous and real.

She rose, went to the kitchen, and took down the blue-speckled flour tin from the top shelf. The money inside was the egg money, the sewing money, the thin little stack she had kept partitioned in her mind for feed, for note interest, for a bolt of muslin if things somehow improved enough to deserve muslin. She did not count it for the note. She counted it into her palm and back again for miles, for medicine, for whatever it cost to move a fevered boy where the next wall waiting for him was not one she had spent years patching by hand.

When she set the tin down, Thea was watching from the bedroom door.

"Cora."

The name held caution in it, and hope, and the fear of hoping out loud.

Cora slid the money back into the tin and put the lid on hard. "How long is it by rail to Lincoln?"

Thea did not answer at once. Her face changed with the effort of not reaching for this too fast. "With a change and if freight doesn't foul the schedule," she said, "most of a day. More if we miss the first connection."

Cora nodded once. She wasn't agreeing yet; she was measuring miles against money and Dell's heat.

Then she took the tin back into the bedroom and set it in the drawer beside the bed instead of on the kitchen shelf where money for the farm belonged. She sat with her hand over Dell's hot good hand and listened to him breathe. Outside, the field kept asking. For the first time, she let it ask without answering.


# Chapter 13

The county inventory sheet rasped against Thea's palm as she stepped into the schoolhouse. The room had already begun to look less like a place children used than a room somebody meant to empty. The blackboard still held a pale fan of chalk where she had scrubbed out sums three days ago, but the desks stood in uneven ranks now, two of them already gone home with families who had asked whether the district would mind if they took what had once belonged to their own children. The map of the United States had been rolled up and tied with string. The flag lay on her desk like a folded shirt waiting to be claimed by a body larger than the one it had fit.

The county sheet called the closure a suspension pending consolidation. It made the thing sound temporary, almost courteous. Thea had read the line three times the evening it arrived and still wanted to take a penknife to the word suspension. Nothing in Morrow had been suspended. It had been starved. The school had not closed on one day. It had thinned one desk at a time, one set of overshoes left too long under a bench, one child absent for planting and then absent for good, one family westbound under quilts and rope and dust.

She set her satchel by the stove, unpinned her hat, and opened the east windows as far as the swollen frames allowed. Heat came in anyway. It had a baked, dirty smell, as if the road outside were being toasted and ground at once. Dust lay in the window tracks, in the grooves of the floorboards, in the leather strap around the globe's equator. When she drew her finger along the ledge it made a clean line and then clouded over again almost at once.

On the desk sat the district ledger, the key ring, three official forms, and the stack of copybooks she was meant to box for whichever larger town ended up swallowing Morrow's pupils. She was to count primers, readers, slates, chalk, composition tablets, maps, stove tools, and ink bottles. She was to note what was salvageable and what was not. She was not, apparently, to note the reason half the third readers still smelled faintly of camphor because their owners had kept them near sickbeds. She was not to note that little Elsie Brand's blue hair ribbon was still wound through the hinge of her desk lid because Elsie had gone to Colorado so abruptly in May that her mother had forgotten to come back for it. She was not to note that Owen Reece, who had once hidden dried grasshoppers in the coal scuttle, had quit school not because he had become dull or lazy but because his father coughed pink into a handkerchief and needed him in the field.

She made herself begin with the books.

There were twenty-one first readers, though only sixteen could honestly be called usable. The cardboard corners had furred open in the damp spring years before the drought, and later dust had worked into the cloth bindings until each cover felt skinned. The second readers were fewer. Two McGuffeys had their backs gone entirely, pages sewn together long ago with white thread by hands that had not been hers. The state geography books lived on the highest shelf because the younger children did not need them and the older ones had dwindled to almost none.

Thea climbed onto the chair by the back wall and began taking them down one at a time.

The room was quiet in a way it had never been quiet when school was only between days. A living schoolhouse kept little sounds in it even when empty: the ping of cooling stove iron, a marble lost in a crack, laughter seeming to sit up in the rafters and wait for morning. This quiet was flatter. It felt official. She could hear a fly worrying itself against a pane and the faint scrape of her own skirt against the chair rung. Outside, somewhere down the block, somebody hammered crate boards together. The rhythm carried the whole length of town now and seemed to say the same thing at every door: pack, nail, go.

She set the first three geography books on the desk and dusted the spines with her handkerchief. *Our Nation and Its Lands.* *The World and Commerce.* *Nebraska and the Great Plains.* Old state texts, thick and earnest, full of rivers that still seemed dependable because the pages had been printed before the weather turned mean for good.

The fourth one did not come free.

She tugged and felt paste tear with a dry little rip. The book slid forward all at once, heavier than it ought to have been, and struck the desktop hard enough to startle her off the chair. It bounced once and fell open on its face.

At first she thought some boy had cut the middle out years ago to hide marbles or licorice, because the pages had spread around a dark square cavity the size of a man's hand. Then she saw the care of it.

The outer leaves had been pasted solid along all three edges, trimmed true with a blade that knew how to keep a line. The hollow itself had not been hacked. It had been worked down through the paper in patient layers, each cut exact enough that the corners met clean. A folded packet of oilcloth lay inside. Beneath it rested another fold of paper gone soft at the creases.

Thea stood very still, one palm on the desk.

There were plenty of men in Morrow who could cut straight, but not many who would take that much time with paper. Ezra Vale would have. He had carried impatience in his body, but when something frightened him badly enough he turned meticulous. She had seen it once at the Christmas program three winters earlier when one of the boys snapped a ruler before the recitations began. The child had gone red and near tears, certain he would be whipped at home for breaking school property. Ezra, who had come to bring in stove wood and stay for Dell's reading, sat down on the back bench with his pocketknife, shaved the splintered end clean, and whittled the length true again while the children sang. He had sanded it with the side of a matchbox until the ruler looked merely old instead of ruined. When the boy thanked him afterward, Ezra had shrugged like a man embarrassed by being watched doing something gentle.

The oilcloth packet had been tied with plain thread. The paper underneath showed pencil marks through the fold. Thea knew before she touched either of them that she had not found a prank.

She looked toward the door, though no one had entered and no wagon had stopped outside. Her own breath came back at her off the hot room. She set the official inventory sheet aside so carefully it was almost reverent, then drew the book nearer and lifted the contents out.

The thread broke at once. The cloth opened on two sheets of lined paper, folded small, the blue rule faded with age and handling. Ezra's hand ran across the first page. She knew it from notes he had sent with Dell about chores, from the one apology he had written when Dell missed a week in lambing season, from labels on seed jars in the Vale pantry. The letters were plain and serviceable, a little right-leaning, as if even in writing he wanted to get the whole thing finished before courage ran out.

Under the letter lay the map.

It was not a school map. It was drawn on thin paper, copied from something older and more exact. A rough oval marked the basin south of the Vale fields. Narrow lines showed the tile run and diversion ditch. In one corner, in smaller pencil, were figures that looked like measurements and a hand different from Ezra's, steadier and finer.
