Otto Blum's, Thea thought. Or Ezra copying Otto so carefully that his own hand had almost disappeared trying to behave like another man's.

On the margin someone had written: *holds spring water here until June if left alone.*

Lower down: *If cut, north field dries faster but wind takes what roots no longer hold.*

Thea lowered herself into the nearest pupil desk because her knees had gone uncertain. The seat bit the backs of her thighs. It had been built for a child and punished adults for forgetting it.

The first line of the letter read:

Cora,

If you are seeing this, then I did not say it living. Miss Bell may have found it first. That is no insult to you. It is only that she handles books, and you would have no call to come hunting in a school shelf. I put it here because men can search a house top to bottom and never think to weigh a geography in the hand.

Thea let the page lower an inch.

He had not known about her and Cora; she did not believe that for an instant. He had known, though, that the school would likely outlast everybody except the woman paid to tend it, and he had used that knowledge the way desperate people used any dependable thing left within reach. The thought made her skin prickle under her dress. Even dead, he had put paper in her hands and made her part of the household's accounting.

She read on.

I kept waiting for a day when there was only one wrong thing to say, and there never was. There was the note and the seed bill and the taxes and Dell's boots and the kitchen roof. Then there was my pride sitting on top of all of it.

You know Otto warned against touching Saint's Basin. Hester warned too, in her fashion. I heard them. I signed anyway.

Thea's thumb stuck to the edge of the page. Dust had worked into the paper fibers. She eased it free and continued.

August laid the note and the tax paper in front of me. He said the south meadow sat wet and useless and that if the basin was cut and tiled there would be one more strip to sow and maybe one more season to breathe. He said if the acreage looked cleaner he could carry me a little longer. I let him say it. Then I said yes.

I told myself a wet place was not a moral thing. I told myself wheat fed people and standing water did not. The plain truth is I was scared and sick of coming in from the field to see you making supper out of almost nothing. I wanted one more season and put my name where it should not have gone.

Thea looked up from the letter and found the schoolhouse gone strange around it. She saw the cracked varnish on the desktop, the chalk rubbed into the grain, and the row of blank hooks by the door where no coats would hang in autumn. She had known, in the broad sense, that debt made cowards and liars. But hearing the bargain in Ezra's own hand was different. He had traded a meadow for time at the bank, and he had told himself it was necessity. August was not clean in it, but neither was this accident.

She read the next paragraph once, then again because the first pass had gone by in a rush of heat.

I went down there when they opened the line. I did not tell you that part. The men were joking because men will joke over almost any damage so they do not have to hear it while it is happening. They had the shovel bites already in and the tile stacked. When the first water found the cut, it ran quick and ugly, brown as dishwater and alive with things that had been still a moment before. A frog came out in the wash and slapped itself against the bank. One of the men kicked it back with his boot because the ditch was where the work was, not the bank. I knew Otto had been right before the last spade went in, and I let it stand because I had already signed and did not know how to be a fool in front of other men after I had been one alone.

The page trembled very slightly where she held it. The movement was too small to call shaking, but it kept the words from settling in one place.

She could smell the basin now though the schoolhouse sat blocks away from it: wet clay broken open in the sun, old roots, the bitter mineral smell that had come off Dell's clothes after the dig. She saw again, because she could not help it, the way his face had gone gray under the dust when they pulled him out, the left arm wrong, the boy he had saved crying into Hester's apron. It would have been simpler if Ezra's letter had delivered a name for the villain and left the rest alone. Instead it put his hand on the first wrong and August's on the next and left the land itself carrying both.

Thea read further.

Afterward I told you less than half. That was mine. I said the basin was men's talk and not for the kitchen. That was a lie. It was our ground and your labor and Dell's breath the same as mine. I kept it from you because I could not bear to hear the whole sound of it in the room.

August's crookedness came after. He said he could make the paper hold for another season if I signed what he put down and let him tend the rest. I believed what kept me moving. When I later saw the date was not what he told me, I was sick behind the shed. He cheated me, but he did not put my hand to the first wrong.

Don't let them say I knew nothing. I knew enough to be ashamed, and then I knew more and kept quiet.

Thea pressed her lips together until the taste of chalk found its way onto her tongue.

It was no absolution for August, no rescue for Cora, and no plea for mercy. Ezra had written like a man finally sick of the tidier lie.

She turned to the second page.

I copied Otto's basin marks because I wanted paper against paper. The line looked ugly there too. Keep them together.

I won't ask forgiveness. I had chances to speak and let them rot.

Do what you think you must with this. Burn it if that is kinder. Show it if the bank starts calling me a fool who never knew. I won't ask you to lay me away better than I was. I loved you poorly in this. That is the plainest sentence I have.

Ezra

Thea sat with the paper open across her lap and heard, from somewhere out on Main Street, a truck backfiring twice before it caught. A dog answered, and then the quiet took the room again.

The room remained hot and official. A dead fly lay in the trough of the window frame. The county's form still waited under her elbow with its lines for usable maps and damaged readers. Yet August had lost the comfort of standing alone in the story. Ezra stood in it beside him now, not as prey only but as a man who had signed, watched, understood, and then gone home without telling his wife what he had done.

What came next in her mind was Cora. Cora would have to read *I signed anyway* and fit it against every small mercy Ezra had left behind: the careful seed labels, the mended harness, the coat peg that still hung empty by the door. Dell would have to hear that his father had not simply been cornered by a banker but had helped start the damage that later rose back up through the basin and into his own broken body. The truth in her lap did not free either of them. It only gave their hurt another edge.

She read the last line again. *I loved you poorly in this.* That sentence hurt Thea in a place she was ashamed to name. There were nights in the Vale house when Cora came apart only after the lamp was turned low and Dell had gone quiet in the next room, nights when desire and grief lay so close together Thea could not have said which one she had touched first. Ezra had always been there anyway: in the money tin, in Dell's face, in the peg where his coat had hung. Carrying this letter home would wound Cora, but it would also drag the dead man out of the gentler place grief had made for him between them. Thea could not pretend she was free of wanting that.

She went back to the map. Otto's notes, or Ezra's careful copy of them, were small enough she had to lean close. The old wet meadow lay there without romance. The page listed measurements, depths, and the direction of runoff. One note by the basin had been smudged and redrawn. Another, near the north edge, read: *buffalo grass held here before plow.* The paper refused myth. The basin had held water until June, the roots had held the ground, and men had cut it open because they thought need gave them leave.

Burning the packet would still be easier. She could strike a match from the box in her desk drawer and feed each page to the stove's black mouth. August would remain the whole visible sin. Ezra would remain the trapped husband Cora had been surviving on. Dell would be spared one more fracture in a summer already crowded with them. And Thea would become one more careful person in Morrow deciding what somebody else could bear.

The idea sickened her not because silence was always wrong. Silence had saved her often enough. Silence was how she boarded in a widow's house without becoming a story men could use against both of them. Silence was how a hand found another hand in the dark and lived to do it again the next night. But there was a difference between refusing a town's hunger and helping a lie stay neat inside a family because love preferred the neat version.

She laid the letter on the desk and stood too quickly. The little chair screeched back. The sound seemed rude in the empty room.

At the washbasin in the corner she poured a thin stream from the pitcher and sluiced her wrists. The water came off the enamel already warm. Dust turned to paste on her skin and ran into the bowl. She watched it collect there, gray-brown, the color of every road in town, and thought of how often people spoke of being soiled as if dirt itself were moral. The basin water cared nothing for guilt. It took what came to it. Paper was harder.

She folded the map with greater care than she felt and slipped it back under the letter. The oilcloth had gone soft at the corners. She wrapped both pages and paper together and held the bundle in one hand, testing the weight. It was absurd how little physical heft a ruined story required.

The geography book still lay open on the desk, its carved-out middle naked to the room. Thea touched the inner wall of the cavity with one finger. The paper edge rasped her skin. Ezra had not cut this in a panic. He had done it over time, probably at a table by lamplight, pages pressed under a board so the knife would not drag. She pictured him stopping every few minutes to listen for Cora's step or Dell's. She pictured him pasting the edges and setting the thing near the stove to dry, then carrying it back to the schoolhouse under his arm on some errand respectable enough not to draw notice. Shame could make craftsmen out of people who had never before wanted such work.

She ought to finish the inventory.

The thought arrived and nearly made her laugh. The county could wait for its numbers. Still, she did not want to leave the room looking as if a storm had passed through her and nothing had been put right after. There was some stubbornness in her that refused to let revelation scatter things without at least one civilized answer.

So she wrote.

Under *state geographies*, she entered the count. Under *damaged beyond use*, she marked one. Her hand remained steady, which seemed to her either admirable or shameful. She tied the map roll more tightly. She boxed the copybooks, slid the globe into its crate, stacked the readers by grade. When she came to the attendance register, she paused.

The year's pages bore attrition in three inks. September names were full and neat. Then the first margin notes began: withdrawn to help at home. Moved to Denver. Out sick. Returned? The question mark had been hers on a day she still believed people came back. By June the page looked like a field after grasshoppers, with empty rows and names that had no final explanation. A school died by ledger before anyone admitted it in speech.

Thea closed the register and tied it shut.

At last there was nothing left uncovered except the hollowed book. She considered putting the packet back just until evening, just until she could think in a room that was not full of children gone. But that, too, would be a choice in favor of delay, and delay had its own appetite. It grew quickly once fed.

She lifted the front board from the geography and let it fall shut.

The cavity made the book close wrong. She could feel the altered weight in it now instantly, and the fact that she had not felt it before annoyed her almost as much as it impressed her. Ezra had counted on habit. People touched schoolbooks as units, not as singular things. To them it had been one more volume to stack, one more spine to dust, one more lesson nobody finished. He had hidden his confession inside the town's thinning future because nobody respected that future enough to inspect it closely.

Thea slid the packet into her satchel, then took it out again.

The leather bag sat by the stove where anyone looking might search it out of ordinary curiosity. She did not trust ordinary curiosity in Morrow. She tucked the bundle instead inside the front of her dress, between chemise and corset cover, where the paper lay warm against her breastbone and scratched faintly when she breathed. Hiding it there felt too close to claiming it, and the thought made her flush. It was not a lover's letter. It was worse. It was evidence.

She put on her hat, gathered the keys and forms, and stood for a moment in the center of the schoolhouse.

This, then, was the end of her wage in Morrow. Fifty-two dollars a month had once seemed hardly worth the trouble of a boarding room and county oversight and the small daily humiliations of being observed by every woman who thought a single teacher ought to know her place. Now the money gone missing from the house ahead of time seemed to echo in the room. There would be no children in September, no salary envelope, and no excuse after this for why she might still be seen walking the road west to the Vale place except the true one, which could not be said aloud in town and would not become any safer for being true.

She took one last look at the desks.

Children had sat there and picked scabs and whispered and copied spelling words badly and grown. They had coughed dust into handkerchiefs and traced rivers they would never see. They had stood for recitation with patched elbows and dirty collars and the stubborn dignity of the poor. Some of them would never finish school anywhere. Some would spend the next years in labor camps or on relatives' floors or married too soon because hunger made decisions faster than age did. The county form wanted numbers. The room gave her bodies instead.

She blew out a breath she had not known she was holding, though there had been no lamp lit to answer it, and locked the door behind her.

The late afternoon light was white and pitiless. Across the street, Mrs. Linder was shaking a rug over a line, though there was no point in it; the rug would collect another skin of road within the hour. She did it anyway with the furious thrift of a woman refusing surrender one beating at a time. Thea nodded to her, received a distracted nod back, and started toward the road west before she could talk herself into stopping at the bakery first.

Hester would have seen too much in her face. Hester saw through flour and weather and politeness with equal efficiency. She would ask what had happened in the schoolhouse, and Thea would either lie badly or tell the truth in the wrong order. Neither appealed.

The walk out of town felt shorter than usual, perhaps because her body had already chosen the destination and left the rest of her to catch up. The letter shifted against her skin with each step, a small dry reminder that she was carrying something capable of breaking a house open.

At the edge of Morrow she slowed.

Beyond the last clustered buildings the Vale road ran pale between fields that had once promised more than they meant to give. Heat shimmered over the stubble. To the south, beyond sight but not beyond thought, Saint's Basin lay with its opened seep and its warning boards and its history newly wet enough for people to fear. Ahead, at the farm, Cora would be moving through the late chores with Dell laid up inside, August's deadline crouched just beyond the day, and no reason yet to suspect that the dead had found another way to interfere.

Thea put her hand, flat and instinctive, over the packet hidden in her dress.

Before the Vale yard came into view, she would have to decide whether the packet went into Cora's hand in private or onto the kitchen table where Dell, laid up and listening, could hear the dead speak too. The choice rode against her breastbone with every step.

She kept walking.


# Chapter 14

Cora had boiled the same strip of muslin twice before Thea came in from town and still could not get the smell of camphor and old sweat out of it. The kettle sat crooked on the stove because the back leg had gone soft in the heat years ago. Every few breaths it gave a little tin complaint. Outside, the evening wind pushed dust along the yard in low, mean skirls that never rose high enough to be called a storm and never stopped long enough to let anybody forget what season it was.

Dell was in the front room on the narrow iron bed they had dragged down from the attic so he would not have to climb stairs with his ribs strapped. He ought to have been sleeping. Instead he lay half turned toward the window, his left arm thick in plaster and sling, his good hand busy with the broken alarm clock he had taken apart three days earlier. He had the little brass works spread on a flour sack beside his hip and was trying, one-handed, to settle a gear back on its post with the point of a nail. He would rather hurt himself than let a machine sit there beaten.

Cora stood in the doorway a moment with the wet muslin hanging from her fingers. His face, in the yellowed light, looked both older and younger than seventeen. The bruise along his ribs had gone from black to a rotten green at the edges. His hair needed cutting. He kept blowing it out of his eyes the way Ezra used to, sharp and impatient, as if hair itself were an insult.

"That thing won't ring if you bully it," she said.

He did not look up. "It won't ring if I leave it broke, either."

"Doctor said rest."

"Doctor says that to folks that can afford it."

He found the gear, missed the post, and swore at it under his breath. Cora crossed in, set the muslin on the chair, and lifted the flour sack by its corners so the screws would not roll into the bedclothes.

"You'll lose pieces."

"I know where the pieces are."

"Do you now?"

He gave her a quick look then, the look that meant he knew she was fussing because she was frightened and meant to punish her a little for it. Under the meanness there was a plea he would never say plain. He had been asking her, in ten thousand ugly small ways since the cave-in, not to leave him alone with his own helplessness.

The screen door rasped. Thea stepped in with her satchel on one shoulder and the old school geography wrapped in a dish towel. She held it tight against her ribs. Dust had worked its way into the hem of her skirt and left a brown tide mark around both ankles. One shoe had rubbed the back of her heel raw enough to show a bright crescent where the stocking had slipped. She smelled of chalk, road dust, and the peppermint lozenges she kept in a tin and rationed as if they were medicine.

"You brought home the whole county?" Cora said.

Thea set the satchel on the table with more care than the books deserved. "Only the bits the board didn't think worth burning."

Dell tipped his head back against the pillow. "They take the globe?"

"Mr. Jurgensen did. Said his youngest wanted to see where California sat."

That got the smallest ghost of a smile out of Dell. "Could've told him. Left."

"West," Thea said, deadpan.

"That too."

She crossed to him, not touching him yet, only looking at the cast with the frank professional concern that always made Dell act as if he minded her more than he did. "How's the arm?"

"Still there."

"And how're the ribs?"

"Also still there."

"A triumph all around."

He tried not to smile again and failed by a fraction. Cora saw it. So did Thea. It hurt in the stupid way small mercies hurt now, because once they had been common and now they arrived like contraband.

Thea took off her hat. Her hair at the temples had curled damp where the heat had beaten the pins loose. She laid the wrapped geography book apart from the others, near the lamp and away from Dell's reach. The extra inches of table between Dell and the book showed first; once Cora understood them, something pulled tight under her ribs.

"Did you eat in town?" Cora asked.

"No."

"There's beans left and half a heel of yesterday's loaf."

"That sounds opulent."

Cora turned back to the stove. The kettle had begun its muttering boil. She lifted it off and poured the water into the washbasin where the muslin waited. The camphor smell rose stronger. Dell made a face.

"You could've spared the liniment," he said. "It crawls up my nose all night."

"It crawls into the bruise too. That's the point."

"Smells like Hester's cupboard."

"Hester's cupboard keeps folks alive."

"Keeps them weird," Dell said.

Thea, taking plates down from the shelf, said, "Those are not opposing conditions."

Dell snorted. His nail slipped. A tiny gear bounced once on the iron bedstead and landed in the blanket fold.

"There," Cora said.

"I heard it."

"You're a wonder."

He found it, then winced when the twist of his body pulled at the strapped ribs. He hid the wince badly. Cora looked away so he could keep his pride a second longer. Thea looked away too, though not before Cora caught the tightening around her mouth.

They ate in the front room because Dell did not want carrying back and forth made of him. The beans had skinned over. The bread was dry enough to shave a man's gums. Dell still cleaned his plate and used the crust to chase the last grease around the edge. Thea drank coffee gone thin from being used twice. Cora ate fast, standing. The geography book sat on the table by the lamp all through the meal like another person waiting to speak.

When Dell was done, he wiped his mouth with the back of his hand and nodded at the wrapped book. "What's that one for?"

"Nothing you need tonight," Thea said.

"That means it's either school business or trouble."

"The school is finished," Cora said. "So guess."

He let his head sink back. "Trouble, then."

Thea stood and gathered the plates. "I'll wash."

"Sit," Cora said. "You've been on your feet."

"So have you."

"Sit anyway."

Thea did not sit. She looked at Dell instead. "Did you take the evening powders?"

"Took one."

"You were told two."

"Two make me stupid."

"One makes you rude, which is a poorer bargain."

"I'm rude for free."

"Dell." Cora said it flat.

He looked from one woman to the other, hearing something in the room he could not yet name. His gaze snagged on the wrapped book again. Cora saw the thought move across his face: paper, secrecy, adult voices gone too careful. Since the bank he had started listening through walls. Since the trench, through his own pain too.

"I can wash the plate one-handed," he said.

"You can sleep two-handed if you shut your eyes," Cora said.

He muttered, but he let Thea take the dish. When she bent to pick up the flour sack with the clock parts, he caught her sleeve with his good hand.

"Miss Bell."

"What?"

"Did they nail the school shut?"

The question came out rougher than he meant it to. His ears had gone pink. Cora felt a small shame at hearing it; he had loved the place more than he wanted anybody to know.

Thea softened in a way she almost never let Dell see. "No. Just emptied it. Mr. Jurgensen took the maps. Mrs. Keeler took the stove lids. The blackboard's still there, though I don't know for what."

"Might leave it," Dell said. "Town could use somewhere to write down who's gone."

He let go of her sleeve and shut his eyes as if he had not said anything worth hearing. Thea stood over him a beat longer, then took the dishes into the kitchen.

Cora came behind with the basin and muslin. Dell had not slept. His lashes lay still, but his right thumb kept moving against the sheet, counting some private measure. Cora knew that motion. Ezra used to do it when seed prices were bad.

In the kitchen, Thea had unwrapped the geography book and set the lamp closer. The old blue cloth cover was cut square through the middle. A packet of folded paper lay in the hollow where pages ought to have been.

Cora put the basin down too hard. Water slapped her apron. "What's this?"

"I found it today." Thea's voice had gone formal with strain, each word laid careful as china on a shelf. "Inside the book. Otto's map was in there too."

"And you opened it?"

"Yes."

Cora stared at the packet. She knew Ezra's hand before she touched it. Even after all this time she knew the pitch of his capitals, the way his e's pinched narrow when he was ashamed or hurrying. The paper had been folded small enough to hide in a coat pocket. Grease from someone's fingers had gone dark at one corner. Dust sat in the crease like soot in a cut.

"Dell didn't see it?" she asked.

"No."

"Good."

Thea drew breath to say something. Cora had already taken the letter.

It had no envelope. Ezra had written Cora's name once at the top and then crossed it out so hard the nib had nearly gone through. Under that, in the cramped slant he used when he was trying to make a page carry more than it ought, he had written:

_If this comes to your hand I did not find the way I kept naming to myself. August said the south flat would hold another wheat year if the basin was cut and the tile run proper. Otto told us plain it was bad country for it and I knew he was right enough to fear him. I said yes anyway._

Cora read the lines twice because the first time they would not hold still.

Thea said quietly, "Read on."

Cora did.

_I thought one more season would put us right with the bank and let Dell stay in school and keep feed through winter. I thought I could bear the wrong if it kept the rest from you. August put papers under my hand and talked extension. Later he changed the dating on the deed and told me if I cried fraud I would lose the place at once and drag all of you under with me._

The room narrowed. Not with dizziness. With accuracy. The dish towel hanging from the oven handle. The chip in the blue bowl. The lamp wick needing trim. The scrape on the table where Dell had whittled a toy truck at nine years old and Ezra had laughed instead of scolding. Every object stood out clean and hard as if the lie had been the only blur on them and somebody had wiped it away.

The last lines on the page were blotted where the pen had stopped and started:

_I told myself I was keeping bread in your mouth. I was keeping shame out of my own. There is a difference I knew too late._

Cora put the paper down. Then she picked it up again as if perhaps her hand had misread it the first time.

"Where's the rest?"

"There are two more pages."

"How much did you read?"

"All of it."

Cora looked up then. What caught in her throat was not Ezra's confession itself but this: Thea had read her husband's hand alone in the schoolhouse, with no one there to see her face at it, no one there to tell her when to stop. Thea had carried Ezra's last decent thing or last rotten thing, whichever it was, pressed to her ribs all the way home before Cora ever knew it existed.

"You read my husband's hand before I did."

"I know."

"You know."

"I didn't know what it was when I opened it."

"You knew soon enough."

Thea did not answer that. She did not need to. The truth sat between them, plain as the lamp.

Cora folded the pages once, twice, not neatly. "We'll burn it."

Thea's head came up. "No."

"Before Dell hears one word."

"That's why not."

Cora turned toward the stove. The lid eye nearest the firebox stood orange around the edges. It would take only a second. Paper curled fast. Ink went black, then silver, then gone. Whole lives vanished easier than a rag.

Thea moved faster than Cora had expected after a day in town. She caught Cora's wrist before the letter reached the heat. Her fingers were cold and dust-dry.

"Let go."

"Not for that."

"You don't get to tell me what to do with my own dead."

"Not when burning him makes him useful again."

Cora jerked free and set the pages on the table hard enough to make the lamp jump. "He's Dell's father."

"Yes."

"He's the name that still gets me mash on credit. He's the reason the feed man says next week instead of no. He's what Dell's built the last two years on. If this gets out before the sale, August won't have to do a damn thing. Town will spit Ezra's name, call the auction justice, and buy our chairs cheaper for it."

Thea's face did not soften. "Then say that. Don't call it mercy."

"It's survival."

"It's a lie that still pays."

Cora laughed once, sharp and ugly. "Of course it pays. Dead men keep earning if they die clean enough."

From the front room the bed springs shrieked. Before either woman could move, Dell said from the doorway, "What letter is that?"

Cora turned so hard her hip hit the table. Dell stood barefoot in the kitchen opening, the sling hanging loose at his side because he had been too impatient to settle it back over his neck. His shirt was buttoned wrong, one side higher than the other. He had the lost clock gear pinched between finger and thumb. He must have brought it with him without knowing.

"Back to bed," Cora said.

"Not till you answer."

"It's schoolhouse paper."

He looked from the pages to the gutted geography book to the red mark rising on Thea's wrist. "You grabbed Miss Bell over schoolhouse paper."

"Dell," Cora said.

Thea said, "I found a letter hidden in that book. Your father wrote it. August is in it too."

Cora swung toward her. "Thea."

"I'm not reading it to him in the kitchen. I'm telling him he heard right."

Dell leaned his shoulder against the frame because standing hurt and pride hurt worse. "How much did he sign?"

"Enough that you're not hearing it on your feet," Cora said.

"That means yes."

She crossed to him and reached for the sling. He flinched away before she could settle it.

"Don't."

"You'll strain the arm."

"Arm's already strained."

His gaze slid over both of them again, slower this time. Their faces. The letter. The wrist. The space between them that was always supposed to look harmless. What he caught was not the whole shape of it. Partial knowledge did worse work.

"This ain't only about him," he said.

The words left a raw gap in the kitchen, and Cora had nothing quick enough to throw over it. "Go lie down."

Thea picked up the letter and slid it back into the hollowed book. "I'm taking this to Hester's."

"Put that down," Cora said.

"No."

Dell looked at the book under Thea's arm, then at Cora, then back to Thea. "You're leaving?"

After a beat too long, Thea said, "I'm sleeping in town tonight."

"At Hester's?"

"Yes."

His mouth moved around some other question and gave it up. He had seen too many people leave Morrow to waste breath on the first fact of departure.

"Fine," he said.

Nothing in the word was fine. He turned awkwardly, one arm useless, shoulder blading sharp under the shirt, and went back to the front room with the bed springs squealing at his weight the moment after.

Cora stood in the kitchen listening to the mattress take him. Then came the small hard sound of something metal thrown against the wall. The clock gear, maybe. Or the whole damn clock.

Thea said, "That's what hearing it through a door does."

"You think I don't know that." Cora kept her voice low because Dell was ten feet away and because if she let it rise it might not stop. "He can't lace his shirt with one hand. He wakes up sore enough to curse the boards. He's still got fever sweat in the bed. I'm not handing him this and watching one more thing break tonight."

"Tonight was already broken."

"Then don't make it worse."

"I won't. I won't help you bury it again."

The bargain showed itself whole then, mean and simple. After dark, in the room with the lamp turned low, she could reach for Thea as if want were its own permission. By daylight the old order climbed back on: Ezra's name, Dell's hurt, the note, the town's stare. Thea went back to boarder, helper, schoolteacher. The woman in the left-behind hours. Cora had called that enough because the rest of life kept arriving first.

"Then take your things," she said. "If you're set on town, take them."

"I am."

She went down the short hall to her room.

Cora followed because she could not stop herself. The room was small and already half stripped by Thea's living in it: one iron bed, one washstand, a nail line where a picture had once hung before the dust ruined it, books stacked on the floor because there had never been a proper shelf. The air held Thea's particular mixture of starch, cold cream, chalk, and the faint medicinal sweetness of the violet water she used so sparingly Cora could count the applications by week. A pair of sensible pumps sat under the bed with the toes neatly aligned. The habit of that pierced Cora almost as badly as the leaving.

Thea pulled the valise from beneath the chair and set it open on the quilt. She moved quickly but not wildly. That made it worse. If she had been weeping or cursing, Cora could have answered it. Order had always been Thea's angriest shape.

"You don't need to make a procession of it," Cora said.

"I'm carrying a bag two miles. That's all."

"At sundown. Every porch on the road will count."

"They have been counting since I moved in."

She folded a dark dress, then another. She added stockings, underthings, her hairbrush with the missing tooth in the handle, the small blue tin of lozenges, the notebook where she kept children's recitations and grocery figures on alternating pages. Cora watched each object vanish and felt the house altering around the loss before the woman herself had gone through the door.

"You'll come back by morning," Cora said. It sounded like an order stripped of authority.

"No."

"You don't know that."

"I know enough."

"Enough to do what?"

Thea stopped packing long enough to look at her. "Enough to know I can't keep living as the piece of your life that gets pulled close after midnight and put away by breakfast."

Cora leaned a shoulder against the doorframe because she did not quite trust her legs. "Don't say it like that."

"Why not. That's how it's been."

"That isn't all it is."

"No. It's also Dell's powders and the chickens and mending and keeping my face straight in town while the woman I love calls me Miss Bell in daylight and asks me to help hold up a lie at night."

Cora's grip slipped on the doorframe. A flake of paint stuck to her damp palm. Thea kept going, quieter now, which hurt worse.

"I stayed after Lincoln because you asked and because I wanted to. I stayed for you. I stayed for Dell with one arm and a fever and for a house that looked ready to split apart. I stayed for the one room in this county where I didn't have to pretend. I didn't stay to help arrange your dead husband into the shape that hurts least."

"He was more than this letter."

"I know he was." Thea pushed a pair of stockings deep into the valise with both hands, as if force could make order hold. "I know what drought and shame and August's desk can do to a man. I know he made ugly arithmetic because he thought winter was coming for his family. But I won't help keep him clean at the price of everybody still breathing."

Cora stepped farther into the room. "You think that's what this is. Clean."

"I think it is what this house has been eating on."

"What this house has been eating on is credit and eggs and whatever story gets us one more week." Cora heard herself getting cruel and could not seem to stop. "If Dell puts the wrong pieces together now, he will cut himself on them. If the town does, August will use it. They will make the auction into punishment. They will name Ezra a thief and me a fool and you something fouler."

Thea's face changed at that, grief and anger crossing too fast to sort. "Then stop letting fear choose every word."

"Easy for you to say. You can leave."

"I can leave because I'm leaving what I wanted." For the first time her voice rose. Not loud, but stripped. "Don't talk as if this is some clean exit I've been saving for a pleasant hour."

Cora said, "You could've gone to Lincoln."

"Yes."

"Shelves and pay and women who don't ask this of you."

"Yes." Thea fastened one clasp on the valise and fought the sprung other one. "I chose you instead. That was my choice. This is too."

Cora had no answer that did not sound like a smaller version of the same bargain.

Thea looked up from the valise. "Tell Dell about the letter when he can hear it. Tell him his father was trapped and wrong both. Tell me to go in daylight if that's what you need. But don't keep asking for my body in the dark and my silence by sunup."

Cora's shoulders jumped before she could stop them.

Thea drew a breath, steadied herself, and cinched the valise strap around the middle. "Hester told me, after the schoolhouse emptied, that if the farm ever got too tight I could have the back room over the bakehouse. The spare key's under the blue crock by the flour bin. I laughed when she said it. I'm not laughing now."

"You already arranged it."

"No. Hester's simply been alive longer than either of us."

The old woman's dry voice almost seemed present in the room at that. Cora could picture the bakery plain as if she stood in it: the yeast smell, the tins of fennel and horehound, Hester coughing into a square of cloth and then apologizing to nobody. Thea in the back room there. The image felt impossible and immediately real.

Cora moved aside because the valise was coming toward the door and she would not have it knocked from her hands like a comic wife in some traveling show. That pride remained, pointless and fierce.

Thea paused when she reached her. Cora's skin went alert with a hope she despised. She thought Thea might touch her after all. Not kiss. Not hold. Just lay a hand on the arm as one human being lays claim to another before a separation. Thea didn't.

"I loved him too, in my way," Thea said. "Enough to know he shouldn't be kept clean at the price of all the living."

Cora's answer came out low and rough. "Get out before I say something worse."

Thea's mouth trembled once, not with fear. With the effort of holding back whatever softer thing wanted saying and had no safe place to land.

"That isn't difficult tonight," she said.

She went past.

Cora heard the front room fall silent when Dell realized Thea was carrying a bag. The screen door rasped once as she opened it, once as it struck shut behind her. Dell said, "Miss Bell," from the bed, but too late for anything except farewell.

Thea answered from the porch, voice muffled by the screen and evening air. "Mind the sling. It slips when you're angry."

"I know."

"I know you do."

Then her steps crossed the yard, firm despite the blistered heel, the valise bumping once against her skirt every third stride. Cora stood in the hall and listened until the sound of her gave itself over to the wind.

When she turned back, Dell was propped on one elbow in the front room, white around the mouth from the pain of it. The broken alarm clock lay in pieces on the floor. One brass wheel had rolled under the stove-side chair. His good hand gripped the blanket hard enough to wrinkle it into ropes.

"Lie back," Cora said.

"No."

She did not have strength for another order, so she went and picked up the clock parts instead. The little spring had sprung clear. It would take him an hour to set it right, if he bothered.

"Was it bad?" he asked.

Cora kept her eyes on the floorboards. "Yes."

"Was it about Pa?"

"Yes."

He breathed in shallow through his nose. "And was there more than that?"

Only then did she look at him. He was watching her too directly now for a child and too wounded for a man and exactly seventeen in the miserable middle.

"What do you mean by that?" she said, because cowardice does not always announce itself grandly. Sometimes it came in the old tired grammar of delay.

Dell laughed once, then stopped because the laugh hurt. He pressed his good hand against the bandage on his ribs and shut his eyes until the pain passed.

"I heard enough," he said at last. "Enough to know it wasn't only him."

Cora stood there with the broken clock in her palms and no lie ready that could cover all the pieces.

Outside, the road to town held Thea somewhere on it, walking toward yeast and coffee and Hester's back room. Inside, Dell waited with his father's blood in him and questions she had spent two years trying to pace out one careful teaspoon at a time. By morning August would have his papers ready. The kitchen behind her still smelled of beans, camphor, lamp oil, and the paper that had not burned.

There was no sound in the house now except Dell's breathing, the kettle ticking itself cool on the stove, and the wind rubbing dust against the windows as if it meant to come in whether invited or not.


# Chapter 15

Saint's Basin showed itself a few yards at a time through the lantern in August's hand, which had smoked its own glass yellow. He had left the motorcar on the section road because the field track was not a road anymore but two chopped ruts and a soft shoulder where the diggers' wagon had cut in after the last little rain. The moon stayed snagged behind a low wool of dust and cloud. Each step off the road tugged at his shoes. Mud caught him by the heel and then let go with a wet sucking sound that traveled too far in the open dark.

Knapp's note kept crackling in his coat pocket whenever he bent. Final papers tomorrow. Need full auction schedule, title corrections, deficiency estimates. The words had been typed hard enough to emboss the back of the sheet. August could feel their ridges through the wool as if the man had reached ahead of himself and put fingers into his chest.

The basin lay quartered open where Hester Blum and her little congregation had cut it. Even in the dark he could make out the spoil ridge, the slumped edge where the wall had given way on Dell Vale, the blacker stripe of damp earth showing through powder-gray crust. Someone had left a broken shovel handle propped against a stake. A twist of bandage, dirty now and stiff as paper, had snagged on a clod near the trench lip and fluttered every time the wind found it. That scrap bothered him more than the hole did. A boy's broken arm could be entered in a report as injury at unauthorized excavation. A strip of linen fouled with clay made the thing vulgar.

He set the lantern down and listened.

The fields south of the Vale place whispered the way dry wheat always did at night, a sound like hands worrying cheap cloth. Somewhere farther off a sheet of tin knocked once, then not again. The seep itself made almost no noise. That was what shamed him most. All that argument, all those men, all the papers and years, and the water answered with a patient trickle no louder than somebody wetting a thumb to turn a page.

He had told himself twice on the walk out that he came only to see what sort of nuisance the hole had become. He had told himself the bank held the note, the county watched the site, and a man in his position had a duty to inspect anything likely to become liability. By the time he stood over the black damp, the wording had lost its usefulness. He knew why he was there. He had spent the evening in his rooms above the bank with the lamp turned down and could not keep his thoughts inside their proper channels. Knapp's note had done what Cora's shouting, Hester's coughing accusations, and the whole damned basin business had not done. It had shown him a future in which all his clean files amounted to a better-labeled corpse.

He crouched with more effort than he cared to feel. His knees gave a complaint. Cold went through the cloth at once. The lantern glazed the seep in a narrow oval. Black water, a film of clay, one pale reflection of flame shivering every time he shifted his weight. He remembered this place when it had not been called a place at all, only the wet hollow south of the Vale acreage, good for ducks, bad for wheels, a waste by every useful measure a man of business had been taught to trust. In spring it used to hold sedge and frogs and a rim of greener grass that showed even in July. The memory embarrassed him. It felt sentimental now, and sentiment was a luxury he had spent twenty years training out of himself.

He took off one glove and laid his bare hand against the damp bank. The mud was colder than the air. Grit worked into the lines of his palm. When he lifted his hand, the lantern glass fogged for an instant from the disturbance of heat and breath, and in that yellowed cloud another night rose up under his eyes.

His father stood where the seep lay now, though in the memory there was more water and the stars were clear. He had his hat pushed back and a willow switch in one hand, stripping the bark off it with his thumbnail while Otto Blum spoke. That was his father's way when another man held the floor: not interrupting, not agreeing, merely reducing some living thing to fibers while he waited to overrule it. Otto had his trousers tucked into muddy boots and one suspender hanging loose, as if he'd dressed in a hurry to keep the basin from being murdered before breakfast. He kept scraping the side of one boot against the other, a neat irritated motion, trying to knock off mud that gathered again at once.

"You cut tile through here," Otto said, and his voice came up from the water and the glass both, "you send spring straight out of the land. It won't stop here. It'll pull from up there too. The grass goes thin first. Then the top goes."

August's father did not look at Otto while he answered. He bent, snapped the willow switch in two, and tossed the pieces into the dark water. "What goes," he said, "is standing waste. I will not leave taxable ground to cattails because you are sentimental about birds."

There had been three men besides them. August knew that even before he saw himself, younger and narrower, with the survey stakes in his hands and his good Sunday coat buttoned wrong at the middle because he had dressed by lamplight. He was not speaking. He was listening, as he had done all his life. Otto turned to him once anyway, perhaps because there was more use pleading with the son than the father, or perhaps because Otto had never understood how completely a boy could be trained by admiration.

"It is not birds," Otto said. "It is what holds when the wind starts."

His father smiled then, though only with the corners of his mouth. It was that small bloodless look that meant he had decided a man was inconvenient rather than dangerous. "If the wind starts," he said, "we will meet that weather when God sends it. Meanwhile people need wheat and credit, not a marsh."

The memory ended before anybody moved. It always did that, these visitations: stopped at the exact point where explanation might begin, as if the world were willing to accuse and unwilling to testify. The lantern glass cleared. August saw only his own bent shape and the pinched gold of flame.

He sat back too fast and his heel slid. Mud took him to the ankle. One hand struck the spoil bank, sank wrist-deep, and came up black. He said, very softly and without dignity, "Damn it."

The smell that rose when the mud opened under his weight was thick and old, not the clean smell of fresh rain but something shut away for years and forced up at last: roots, wet clay, a sourness like long-soaked boards. He wiped his hand on his handkerchief and only spread the black farther. The basin did not care how carefully a man dressed.

He ought to have gone back then. Instead he moved the lantern closer and leaned over the water until his own face blurred in it. The flame doubled, stretched, and then the black surface deepened into polished desk wood. Ezra Vale sat on the client chair in August's office, hat in both hands, folding the brim inward and outward until it had a sharp beak in front. He used to do that whenever he lied, or whenever he meant to ask for something he knew he had no standing to demand. August had noticed the habit long before he understood it. Ezra's coat sleeves were dark at the cuffs where he'd washed at the pump and not dried properly. A thread of red feed-sack stitching clung to one cuff. He had probably leaned against Cora's mending basket on the way out. That domestic bit of color made the whole scene worse.

"You said till threshing," Ezra said. In the memory his voice was not angry yet. It had been worn smooth from repeating itself. "That was the talk in this room."

August heard his own answer before he saw himself. "The bank discussed a contingent extension under additional security. I said no maturity could stand unless the paper would bear examination."

It was a hateful sentence because it was true in the way a gate can be true when it swings shut.

The memory sharpened. August sat behind the desk, cuffs straight, pen clean, the county examiner's circular open beside the blotter like scripture. He had been thinner then and more certain that thinness proved virtue. Ezra leaned forward with his hat crushed between his palms.

"I gave you the water tract," Ezra said. "You wanted that, you got it. I need the rest of the spring. I can't take this home to her the way it stands."

"You should have taken it home to her before you signed anything."

Ezra flinched as if August had reached across and struck him. August remembered the mean satisfaction of that. He had told himself for years that the satisfaction had been incidental, no more than the heat a man feels from the stove while he performs a necessary task. The basin did not permit that version. The basin returned the heat too clearly.

"Don't preach at me," Ezra said. "If you meant to foreclose, you could've said it plain."

"I mean to keep the bank from carrying a visible indulgence it cannot support."

"That's not plain."

"It is plain enough."

Ezra stood up so suddenly the chair legs barked over the floorboards. Dust on the office window leaped in the vibration. He set both hands on August's desk, leaving crescents of damp where his palms touched the polished wood.

"You've got church money in here," he said. "Widows' money. I know it. I know whose names sit in your drawers. But you've got mine too, and I'm asking you for ninety days that won't cost you a thing if the wheat stands."

"If the wheat stands," August said, and heard himself laying the phrase down like a blade between them, "half the county believes itself rescued. That is not a banking standard."

Ezra's face changed then. Hope went out of it first. Pride stayed another second, maybe two, and then that too. He looked suddenly the age he actually was and not the younger, quicker man people remembered from church suppers and harvest crews. "All right," he said. "Then say it plain."

August had said it. He saw his own mouth move in the black water. "Sign the deed. Pay the interest. We will review again after quarter's close."

In the memory Ezra's thumbs dug deeper into the hat brim until the felt beak folded flat.

The surface shivered and the office vanished. For one sick moment he thought the memory was done, but then the lantern flame lengthened and showed him another fragment, later the same night. His office again, empty now except for himself. He stood with the ledger open and the loose-leaf posts unscrewed. His penknife lay beside the inkwell. The original page, the true page with the extension entered under the proper month and his own initials beside it, had already been lifted out and set by his elbow. He remembered thinking not that he was falsifying a record, only that he was reconciling a contradiction between indulgence and survival. His hand moved in the memory with terrible steadiness, sliding the copied page into place, flattening it with the side of his palm, and tightening the posts one by one as neatly as if he were dressing a ledger for inspection.

When the water went black again, August stayed bent over it until his lower back began to tremble. He had not inherited the problem entire. That was the lie he had preferred because it still allowed him to think of himself as a steward standing in a breach made by other men. He had inherited a vocabulary and a habit of arrangement. The copied leaf had gone under the posts by his own hand, and the true page had slept where he put it.

Wind crossed the basin then, small and cold. It touched the wet mud, touched his face, moved on. He thought of Cora in the yard after Ezra's burial, shoulders squared as if posture itself might keep the house from tilting. He thought of Dell, white with pain and fury, letting women carry broth to his bed because the arm left him no choice. He thought, against his will, of Thea Bell's room gone dark at the Vale place and the fact that he had noticed it from the road three nights running. The town was coming apart by inches, private seam after private seam, and he had spent years calling that order.

He stood with effort, nearly left the lantern behind, and turned back toward the road. Mud cooled on his cuff. With each step the ground released him reluctantly, as if the basin had no intention of letting a man off with witness alone.

* * *

He was at the bank before sunup. The stove had gone dead in the night and the office held that special chill of buildings made to look solid while failing at the edges. Dust lay over the counter, the teller cage, the brass rail, even over the green blotter on his desk though he had wiped all of it before locking up. He took the rag from the bottom drawer and wiped again. The motion steadied him until he noticed the dried black crescent on his right cuff and found that no amount of brushing would make it look like anything but mud.

He made coffee on the little plate in back and burned it because he was watching the street instead of the pot. Morrow woke slower now than it used to. There were no children's feet by the school because there was no school to fill, and no line at the bakery. One truck rattled past with its bed half empty of furniture and chicken crates, headed east toward the depot road. August watched it until it cleared the window, then looked away as if he had been caught staring at indecency.

Knapp came just after eight with a leather valise in one hand and his hat in the other. He always brushed the hat before entering any room in Morrow, not with annoyance exactly but with the patient concentration of a man determined not to let western Nebraska into his lining. Today he paused on the threshold to tap the brim against his knee and then took a peppermint from a small round tin before speaking. He let it melt against one cheek while he looked over the office, as if even his breath required mediation in this town.

"Morning," he said. The peppermint made the first word sharper. "You look as if you slept badly."

August did not offer him the easy lie. "I slept little."

Knapp nodded as if that were the expected professional condition of both of them and set the valise on the customer chair instead of the floor. "I hope your stove is better than the hotel stove. They nearly boiled my eggs into bullets."

The detail might have been intended as courtesy. It struck August harder than courtesy should have. Men who meant to ruin a place usually took more trouble to sound formal about it.

"Coffee is ruined too," August said. "If you want some, take it now while it's merely scorched."

"That sounds about right for the morning."

Knapp drank standing up, one hand around the cup, the other still on his hat brim. He had a raw place at the side of his thumb where he kept worrying the felt binding. August had not noticed that before. He had the compactness of a man who rode trains, slept in bad rooms, and called that a career. He was not farm labor and not desk softness either, but something between.

August laid out the auction docket, the corrected title abstract, the note schedule, the tax figures. Paper formed its own kind of barricade between them. He kept aligning the corners without meaning to.

"Here is the Vale file," he said. "The county taxes are rolled in as previously discussed. Sale notice remains set for Saturday. If the weather holds, attendance will be sufficient."

Knapp looked at the stack but did not reach for it. "You still say if the weather holds as if weather were the deciding officer."

"In this county it generally is."

"Not anymore."

The word sat between them. August could hear the front sign creak outside, FARMERS STATE BANK groaning softly on its chains.

"You asked me in July," Knapp said, "whether a clean docket, regularized titles, and a prompt sale season might help Morrow present better at district review. I told you clean files help every place. That was true. What I did not tell you was that your place had already gone past the point where better presentation changed the recommendation."

August folded his hands because they wanted to reach for the papers again. "Recommendation from whom?"

"Lincoln first. Washington after that if they bother looking." Knapp took the peppermint from his mouth and set it on the saucer before continuing. "School's closing. Trade's moving out. The rail spur's under review. Half your acreage west of town is in some stage of delinquency or tax distress. The land-retirement men don't write sermons. They write maps. On their map, Morrow's already mostly a clearance problem."

The office seemed to narrow around August's desk because the facts had finally reached his body. The ceiling suddenly felt too low for him.

"Mostly," he repeated. He heard the pettiness of the word and could not stop himself. "That is a convenient adverb."

Knapp gave him a tired look rather than an offended one. "I use it because there are still people here and I need to speak about them in files built for acreage. I try not to let the file swallow the fact. That's the best I can offer."

"And the bank?"

"The bank may keep its sign awhile. A sign is not a town."

August turned his head toward the window without seeing it. He had been working for months under the miserable consolation that if he kept the ledgers exact enough, if he called notes in the right order, if he gave the county examiner nothing loose to seize, then when the larger men came around Morrow might still look disciplined enough to spare. It did not need to look prosperous. It only needed to look orderly. An orderly town, he had believed, could argue for continued existence.

Knapp seemed to read some portion of that thought from his face. "I'm not saying paperwork is nothing," he said. "I'm saying you can't ledger your way back into rainfall or people."

"No," August said. "But I can keep this office from appearing criminally managed."

That got Knapp to reach for the file at last. He flipped the top sheet, then the next, his fingertips leaving small clean arcs in the dust. "That is closer to the proper question."

August felt the skin between his shoulder blades cool. "Explain."

Knapp set the pages down carefully. "When a settlement contracts, people at the far end don't remember the weather first. They remember signatures. Whose name accelerated. Whose name certified title. Whose name swore the record was regular. If there is anything in these files that would fail under full review, this is the last reasonable morning to tell me."

The clock on the wall clicked once. Outside, a wagon rolled by so slowly the iron rim seemed to scrape the day open.

"You make that sound charitable," August said.

"It isn't charity." Knapp picked up the peppermint, turned it between finger and thumb, and put it back in his mouth. "It's self-interest of a broader sort. I don't enjoy sending avoidable messes upward. They come back down with more people attached."

August almost laughed. The sound did not quite make it out. "You want tidiness."

"I want legibility."

There it was again: that bureaucratic mildness that infuriated more effectively than cruelty. A cruel man could be hated cleanly. Knapp kept speaking as if he were arranging chairs after a funeral.

"If your question is whether an immaculate set of foreclosure files saves your town," Knapp said, "no. If your question is whether a concealed irregularity ruins the little standing you still have with the district and leaves you personally exposed when somebody starts tracing blame, yes. Those are different questions."

August looked down at his own hands. Mud had dried into the cuticle around one thumbnail. He had scrubbed and missed that.

"Suppose," he said slowly, "a man believed at the time that a particular adjustment preserved the bank from wider panic."

"Then he believed that."

"And suppose he was not entirely wrong."

Knapp's mouth bent, not quite into a smile. "That's the sort of sentence men use when they're about to hand me something I won't like."

August rose before he had decided to. His chair legs grated. "Wait here."

Knapp did not ask where he was going. That restraint made the next minutes worse.

* * *

The vault smelled of iron, paste, and old paper that had absorbed twenty years of sweat from men's cuffs. August shut the door behind himself without latching it. In the dim interior the shelves looked like pews in a chapel built for account books instead of mercy. Cancelled notes. Deeds. Tax receipts. Insurance riders. The family names of Morrow flattened into folders thick with delay.

He went to the plat atlas first, then stopped, because that was where he had moved the page after Cora's first visit to the office about Ezra, not where he had hidden it originally. He had relocated it twice in nine days, each move disguised from himself as prudence. In truth he had been handling his guilt like contraband, never bold enough to burn it, never honest enough to file it.

He found the sheet at last inside the back cover of the atlas between Morrow Township and a list of delinquent parcels clipped from last year's county circular. The paper had taken a shallow bend from the hiding place. Blue-black entries marched across it in his own hand. Note number, borrower, interest received, renewed maturity, collateral reference. Ezra Vale's line sat there with the true month and the additional term plainly entered, no larger on paper than any other line on the page. Small things were how men damned each other. They did it with columns instead of opera.

He stood with the page in his hand and remembered the sound the loose-leaf posts had made that night: a faint metal squeak, not dramatic enough for sin. He remembered too, because the basin would not let him keep only one side of the matter, that there had been three other delinquent notes in the drawer and two old women with savings in the bank who would have lost bread money if panic broke hard enough. Both things were true. He had protected a failing institution and he had done it by choosing which man could be broken most quietly.

When he came back out, Knapp had opened the Vale file and was making notes in the margin with a pencil sharpened almost indecently fine. He looked up, saw the page in August's hand, and set the pencil down.

August did not sit. "This is the original ledger leaf for the renewal period covering Vale's note."

Knapp held out his hand. August looked at it for a second too long before giving him the sheet.

Paper made a dry whisper going from one man to the other. That was all: no thunder, no gust, no clock stopping, just the ordinary sound of custody changing.

Knapp read in silence. Once he went back to the top of the page and read the line again. August watched his eyes make the calculation.

"This extends the maturity under the later month," Knapp said.

"Yes."

"And the deed in the file is entered under the earlier month."

"Yes."

Knapp laid the page flat on the blotter and smoothed it with his fingertips, a gesture August recognized with a sick little start as his own. "Did Vale know you reversed the order."

"No."

"Did anyone?"

August could have answered with qualifications. He did not. "No."

Knapp looked up then, full on him for the first time that morning. "Why keep it?"

August had expected anger, perhaps disgust. The plain curiosity was harder to endure. He found that the answer arriving in him was not a respectable one.

"Because destroying a record and displacing it are not the same trespass," he said. "I told myself I had altered sequence for the bank's protection, not abolished fact. Keeping the page allowed me to imagine a line I had not crossed."

Knapp's expression did not change, but something in it settled. "And now."

August looked at the page, not at the other man. "Now there is no use preserving my own distinction if the town is gone anyway."

"That is not confession," Knapp said.

"No." August swallowed coffee bitterness and old mud both. "Confession would include motive in full. I do not have the stomach for that this morning."

He expected Knapp to call him coward. Instead Knapp asked the more damaging question. "Then give me the business version."

August let out a breath. "Ezra Vale signed the deed willingly enough under pressure. The debt was real. The interest delinquency was real. The extension was also real when entered. I later removed its force because the examiner was coming, the bank could not show another soft renewal, and I decided Mrs. Vale's household would bear the cost more quietly than public weakness at the bank."

Knapp held his gaze another beat. "That is at least legible."

The humiliation of gratitude for that word almost turned August's stomach.

"What happens to it now?" he asked.

"It leaves your desk." Knapp folded the page once, carefully, along an existing bend rather than making a new crease. "I attach a memorandum and carry it with the file. District will see it if district asks. The examiner will see it if I judge the title irregularity material to the transfer review."

"You judge."

"I judge first. Others later."

That was mercy of a bureaucratic sort, and August hated himself for recognizing it. He moved back to the desk, pulled a blank form from the drawer, and fed it into the typewriter.

Knapp watched him. "What are you doing?"

"Correcting the part still subject to my hand."

The keys struck hard because his fingers were not steady. Each letter bit through the ribbon with more force than necessary. He rolled the carriage back and began again lower on the page when the first line misaligned. Upon completion of foreclosure sale on the Vale note, Farmers State Bank waives any personal deficiency remaining against Cora Vale, widow, and will pursue no further collection beyond proceeds of sale. The words were ugly. He did not care. They needed only to stand.

As he typed Cora's name, what came to him was not her face in any theatrical sense but her hands at the bank counter two weeks ago, one palm flat on the wood, the other closed around her gloves as if the gloves were the only soft thing left she could afford to crush. He thought of eggs counted for cash, sewing done by lamp, the extra fifty-two dollars a month that had been coming into that house through Thea Bell and was likely gone with the school anyway. A deficiency judgment would not only take the farm. It would reach after the rest of her years with a spoon.

He pulled the sheet free, signed it, sanded the wet ink, and slid it across.

Knapp read faster this time. "Why not postpone the sale instead?"

August had known the question was coming. He had been asking it of himself since the basin. The answer he gave first was the defensible one.

"Because the note remains in default. Because taxes remain due. Because the land will not carry what is owed even if I indulge sentiment for another month."

"That is the bank answer."

August looked past him at the teller cage, at the dust caught in the wire mesh. "The other answer is no prettier."

"Let's hear it."

He was tired enough now that the edges of his diction frayed. "If I stop the sale, I've got to tell the whole town I used one man's desperation to patch a failing office and then rewrote the patch. I've got to put the bank into open disgrace before harvest is even buried. Depositors stampede. Other notes collapse sooner. Maybe the county closes me before winter. Maybe that's justice. It isn't rescue."

Knapp said nothing.

August went on because silence had become less bearable than speech. "The farm is not solvent because I altered a page. It was not solvent before that either. Ezra signed more than one ruin. The basin. The deed. The debt itself. I helped sharpen the blade, yes. I did not forge the whole knife."

Knapp leaned back at last, chair giving a dry complaint. "You know how that sounds."

"Like excuse."

"Like classification," Knapp said. "You still want the damage sorted by proper drawer."

August almost answered no, but that would have been another lie. "Yes."

Knapp put the waiver beside the folded ledger page. "Then this is what you're willing to do: surrender the proof, surrender any claim on her after the sale, and keep the sale."

"Yes."

"You understand she won't thank you."

That, at least, was easy. "She should not."

Knapp studied him with a look August could not read. It was probably fatigue instead, one tired official recognizing another species of tiredness. He opened the valise and placed the folded page inside an inner pocket, then laid the signed waiver on top of it. Leather shut over both with a dull snap.

That sound was worse than the handoff had been. The handoff still contained choice. The closing valise did not.

"I'll need a duplicate of this mailed to Mrs. Vale today," Knapp said, tapping the waiver. "No surprises on deficiency after the sale. If you're doing one clean thing, do it cleanly."

"It will be done."

Knapp rose, took up his hat, brushed the brim once more with his thumb, and paused by the chair. "For what it's worth, Mr. Poeschel, most towns die by weather and price together. Men merely help them along and then quarrel over proportions."

August looked at him. "That is meant kindly."

"No," Knapp said. "Accurately, I hope."

Then he left.

The office settled around the absence almost at once. Wagon noise from outside, the clock, a draft under the back door carrying in the smell of coal smoke from somewhere down the block. August remained standing until his knees reminded him of the basin.

On his desk the Vale file lay open where Knapp had worked through it. The empty space where the ledger page ought to have been was not visible in any literal sense; the page had been hidden elsewhere for days. Still he could feel the vacancy in the room the way a tongue finds a missing tooth.

He made the duplicate notice for Cora. Carbon paper smudged his fingers blue. He addressed the envelope in his best clerk's hand and hated the hand for looking so calm. Then he drew the auction sheet back toward him and checked the date, the hour, the legal description, the improvements to be sold. House. Shed. Chicken run. Equipment worn and partial. Acreage as described. A life translated into lots.

He signed where the bank required his name.

Mud flaked from his cuff and fell onto the margin beside the word SALE. He brushed at it, then stopped. The smear it left looked darker than dust and more honest. Outside, Morrow kept moving through its thin morning with the stubbornness of places that have not yet been informed, in their own hearing, that they are mostly dead already.

August folded the notice, rang for no clerk because there was no clerk to spare, and set it in the out-tray for posting. Then he sat alone at his desk with the open file, the spoiled coffee, the smell of damp earth drying on his sleeve, and the dark smear he had left beside SALE while the notice waited to go out toward the Vale place.


# Chapter 16

Cora's Singer sat on the porch with a sale tag pinned through a strip of muslin. The tag lifted and settled in the breeze, a pale tongue. Beyond it, the Vale yard had been turned into merchandise.

The iron bedstead leaned against the chicken fence with its paint scabbed off in curls. Two kitchen chairs sat upside down on the table so their legs stuck up like a pair of split birds. The blue enamel washbasin, cracked at the rim where Dell had dropped it at twelve and lied about it, had been set out on a feed sack beside the wash pitcher and the chipped coffee pot. Ezra's brace and bits lay in a row on burlap, wiped clean because Cora had not been able to bear the thought of strangers accusing even his tools of slovenliness.

She had spent dawn carrying the house into the yard with Dell trailing her in the cold yellow light, one arm bound up in splints and cloth and the other made clumsy by anger. Whenever he bent to help, the broken side of him told on him. His face would flatten, and the breath would go out of him through his nose like he was trying not to make a sound in church.

"Leave the trunk," she told him when he reached for the cedar chest with his good hand. "I'll get it."

"I can lift a damn box."

"You can drop it."

He stepped back because there was nothing else to do with a mother who was right and in no mood to be thanked for it. He wore Ezra's old work shirt because it was the only thing cut loose enough to go over the splints. The left sleeve hung empty and pinned. Every time Cora saw that sleeve it gave her a fresh, small hatred for the basin, the town, the weather, the whole string of men and paper and bad ground that had led them here. She did not let herself stop at any one hatred long enough to get soft inside it. There was too much to carry.

Milt Renshaw drove in from Hayes Center a little after seven with a wagon bed full of folding trestles and a voice already warmed up. He was a county auctioneer, narrow as a lath, with a nicotine-yellow mustache and one suspender held together by a brass safety pin polished bright from habit. He had taken off his hat as soon as he came through the gate, which Cora respected, and then gone immediately to sorting her life into order, which she did not. He tapped each tag against his front tooth before tying it on, as if numbers had to be tested there first.

"We'll keep the household close to the porch and the implements farther out," he had said, not looking at her much because decent men knew where to put their eyes on a foreclosure morning. "Birds last, if you've still got enough of them to trouble with. Land after dinner unless the sky decides otherwise."

The sky had decided something already. A brownness lay low in the south beyond the section road, not yet a wall, not even a proper threat, only a stain where the horizon ought to have held clean. The air kept lifting grit off the packed yard and laying it back down in a finer sift. Dust worried the cuffs of her dress and found the damp at the corners of her eyes. Everybody in the county knew how to read that kind of day. Even so, people came.

They came in farm trucks with slatted sides, in wagons with the grease singing dry in the wheels, on foot from town with handkerchiefs at their throats and empty feed sacks folded under their arms in case bargains turned bulky. A foreclosure pulled folks the way a revival did. Some came because they needed things cheap. Some came because they liked the sight of another family failing one fraction lower than themselves. Some came because it was a way to spend a Saturday without admitting the wind had beaten every other use out of the week.

Cora knew most of them by boot leather and shoulders before she knew them by face. Mrs. Kroll from two roads north arrived with her hem let down in one uneven hurry and a crochet hook stuck through her hatband like a hatpin because she had walked out mid-mending. Eli Voss, who had lost two boys to California and still talked as if they were due home for threshing, climbed down from his truck rubbing his right knee before it fully straightened. There were town women in last year's hats, boys pretending not to stare at the bed and washstand, and three men from farther east who examined the cultivator teeth as if looking at a dead horse's mouth. Cora did not hate them for coming. Need had put its hands on everybody. But she hated the way they slowed when they reached her gate, as if grief were something to browse.

August Poeschel arrived on foot from the road where his Ford had stopped, maybe because the lane was too rutted, maybe because he thought walking in made him less of an announcement. He wore his dark coat though the morning had already turned close. Dust had got onto the shoulders of it and stayed there, a gray pelt he had not noticed or could not brush away without drawing more eyes. He carried a leather folio under one arm and no hat in his hands. Carefully shaved, heavy through the middle, he still brought town authority with him the way some men brought weather. Heads turned toward him and then away, quick. Cora watched him smooth the top sheet on the clerk's card table before he set down his folio. He smoothed it again after there was nothing left to smooth.

He did not come to her at once. For that she was grateful. He had come last evening with the true ledger page in his folio and his face looking worse than if he had been drunk. He had put the paper on her kitchen table and told her, in that church-bell voice of his, that the bank would waive any personal deficiency after the sale. The auction would proceed. The note stood. The property would pass. But when it passed, it would be done.

He had not asked forgiveness. He had not spoken Ezra's name until Cora forced it out of him. When she said, "You knew what you were doing to him," August had answered, "Yes," and then sat there with both hands on his knees as if waiting for a doctor to cut. She had hated him for the answer because there was no comfort in it. She had hated him more for bringing relief in the same satchel as proof.

Now the ledger page lay folded in the pocket of her apron. She could feel its rectangle against her hip each time she turned.

Dell came to stand beside her on the porch steps before the first lot was called. He kept his eyes in the yard and away from August and away from the road to town. She knew who he was waiting to see and knew better than to mention it.

"You don't have to stand the whole thing," she said.

"I do."

"You'll wear yourself sick."

He gave a little shrug with the good shoulder. "I'm sick already."

He was not fevered now. The heat had gone out of him days ago. What was left was the soreness in the bones and the humiliation of needing help to button a cuff or tie a boot and the new knowledge that had been circling him ever since he had overheard enough in fragments and whispers to know his father had not died clean. She had not spoken straight to him yet. She had kept putting it off one hour at a time, first because of his arm, then because Thea had gone to Hester's, then because the auction notices were being nailed up and every day arrived with a new practical emergency that offered itself as an excuse.

He looked older in stillness than in motion. In motion he was a boy all elbows and rashness. Standing there with his mouth flattened and his eyes fixed, he looked like somebody already learning the bad economy of grown men, how to spend words only after they had failed you.

Milt climbed onto the wagon tongue and rolled his shoulders once. "We'll commence," he said. His voice was bright without being merry, a trade voice, built to cut through wind and pity both.
