"Terms are cash unless prior arrangements are made with Farmers State Bank. Settle with the clerk as you go. We'll start with household goods, then tools and poultry, and we will see what the weather allows us for the remainder."

That was how it began, with terms instead of prayer or apology.

The first thing gone was a bundle of Mason jars, twelve to the string, some clear and some clouded where years of hard water had etched them. Mrs. Kroll bought them for thirty cents and did not meet Cora's eyes as she counted the coins onto the clerk's table. Then the washboard went. Then the kettle with one loose handle, then the rocker Ezra had made too low in the back because he never measured twice if he could help it. A boy from the east road got the lamp with the green glass base for fifteen cents and grinned at his own luck until his mother pinched the inside of his elbow hard enough to sober him.

Cora had thought humiliation might come like a blow, one hard crack that would either take her down or not. It came instead in teaspoons. It came in hearing Milt praise the strong drawers in her kitchen table because one runner still held. It came in watching a stranger turn back the quilt on her bed and thumb the batting to feel whether it had gone lumpy. It came in the way people said "good oak" and "bit worn" and "ought to do yet" about things that had held her marriage, her son's sleep, her own blood in one month or another. Nothing in the yard stayed itself. Every object became a mouth talking about value.

Dell took a step off the porch when Milt held up Ezra's brace and bits.

"Easy," Cora said.

"He's selling Pa's tools to men that couldn't sharpen them."

"And if you limp out there and holler, then what?"

His jaw worked. "Nothing."

"That's right."

He hated her for the rightness of it. She could see the hatred and the plea braided together in him and felt ashamed of being grateful that the crowd kept his eyes off her face. She put her hand on the porch post instead of on him. The post was rough with old paint and held steady. That was better than flesh.

The brace and bits brought less than the Singer. That offended her in a private, foolish place. Ezra had used those bits until the wooden handles shone dark as chestnuts. The man who bought them, a renter with barley straw still clinging to his vest, turned the brace once in his hand and said to nobody in particular, "I'll have to true this chuck." He said it almost tenderly, and Cora's anger broke wrong, not at him but at the fact that she was glad the tool had landed with a man who knew what it was.

The hens went all together in two squalling crates, their combs gray with dust. Their panic made the yard sound uglier than it looked. Children laughed. One hen had worked her beak half through the slat and jerked at the air as if the day itself might be edible. Dell swore under his breath. August glanced toward him and then away.

By ten the porch was nearly bare. The front room had been opened to traffic because people kept asking to inspect what Milt had not yet hauled out, and Cora had finally stopped trying to guard each threshold. Women walked through her house with auction tags in their fingers. Men stooped in her pantry and discussed hinges. A woman smelling of camphor opened the linen cupboard and commented that she had fewer sheets than a family her size ought to own, and that shame bit harder than the sale price on the milk separator, because it carried no usefulness in it, only arithmetic laid against her housekeeping.

She was in the kitchen when she heard Hester's cough at the door and turned too quickly, knocking her hip against the enamel-topped table. Pain ran hot and narrow up her side. Hester stood there broad and stooped in a black dress that had been brushed but not fully rid of flour at the cuffs. She had pinned a sprig of dried sage into her collar, maybe for the smell, maybe because habit still reached for decency on public days. Beside her stood Thea, hatless despite the wind, in a brown skirt that had gone shiny at the seams and a cream blouse mended with thread a shade too dark at one cuff.

They had come together from town. They stood together now.

The sight of it struck Cora lower than anger. For a second she could feel the exact shape of the empty half of the mattress upstairs, the place where Thea's weight had once made its own warm trough. She had wanted Thea there and had not wanted her there and had spent the whole week despising herself for both. Now, with the kitchen full of her own tagged spoons and two women at the door who knew more about her than the crowd ever would, she found she could not move her face into any expression worth having.

Hester saved her. "Wind's turning mean," she said, as if she had come to report on bread rising. "We'll likely be inside before long."

Thea looked at Cora once, directly. There was no softness in it and no cruelty either. There was only attention, which might have been worse. She carried the letter in her handbag. Cora knew that without seeing it.

"You ought to sit some, Dell," Hester said. "You're white as paper."

"I ain't."

"Then paper has improved."

That pulled one short sound out of him, not a laugh but the memory of one. Hester took it and walked with it to the porch as if handling an egg.

Thea stayed in the kitchen doorway. "Do you need anything moved?"

Cora looked past her shoulder into the yard where strangers stood around her stove. "No," she said. Then, because public speech came easiest when it stayed practical, "There's coffee if you want it. It tastes like the pot."

"Everything does."

It would have been possible to leave it there. The old habit was to leave it there, let speech end where it could still pretend not to matter. But Cora had slept badly with the ledger page under the sugar crock and August's voice in her kitchen and the thought of this morning coming no matter what she did. Her anger had cured into something cleaner overnight.

"Did you bring it?" she asked.

Thea touched the handbag once with her fingertips. "Yes."

"Good."

Thea's brows moved a fraction. She had expected resistance. Cora could see that and hated herself again, though less than before. "I didn't bring it to torment you," Thea said quietly.

"I know what you brought it for."

"Do you."

Cora's first impulse was to answer practical, mean, and false. Instead she said, "I know enough."

That did not mend anything. It did, however, keep the break from widening in front of the open pantry and the women fingering her cups. For now it was the most she had.

Outside, Milt called for bids on the dresser mirror. Men shifted to make room. The mirror had been taken off the wall in the front bedroom and propped on a chair, its gilt frame gone black in the corners where hands had never polished it. Cora could not look at it long. Too much had shown in glass that year.

She went out in time to see Mrs. Kroll buy the mirror after no one else wanted it. Mrs. Kroll's mouth pinched as if she already regretted the vanity of the purchase. When she lifted the glass, her own face swam there under the dust and another face tried to come in behind it, quick as a breath on a cold pane. Mrs. Kroll gave a sharp little cry and almost dropped the frame.

For one second the mirror held a damp green light that had no business in Cora's bedroom: water rucked by wind, geese jerking their necks up from it, children sliding on a skin of winter ice while somebody off-frame shouted for them to get clear. Then the image broke apart under the movement of Mrs. Kroll's hands and the grit in the yard blew across it. The crowd made the sound people made in church when a hymn went somewhere they had not expected.

Hester did not flinch. August did. It was little more than a draw-in of breath, but Cora saw it.

"Set it down if you don't want it," Milt said to Mrs. Kroll, trying for ordinary and not finding it. "No charge if it turns ghostly on you."

There was uneasy laughter. Mrs. Kroll held the mirror away from her body and said, "I'll take it. I paid for it." Her voice had the anger of a woman who would rather be thought foolish than frightened.

Milt licked his thumb, then seemed to think better of putting his tongue anywhere near the day. He wiped it on his trouser seam. "All right then. Next lot."

The sale went on. Of course it did. That was the indecency of most public things. A wonder, a death, a confession, a child's arm snapped wrong in a ditch, and still somebody asked what came next and whether the coffee was hot.

The tool chest brought more money than Cora expected and less than Dell deserved. He had built the little partition inside it himself, fitted for wrenches and screws and the pliers with one handle wrapped in friction tape. A filling-station man from the highway took it for four dollars ten and said, "Boy can come around if he wants odd work once he's mended." He had a round moon face blistered red across the nose and a habit of cracking his thumbnail against his teeth when he was thinking.

Dell stared at him hard enough to be rude. "Maybe I won't."

"Dell," Cora said.

The man only shrugged. "Offer stands or it don't. Makes no odds to me."

It made odds to Cora because the offer, clumsy as it was, exposed the thing she had been trying not to place in words: there might be a life for Dell that had not got this yard in it. The thought came mixed with relief and offense. She did not know which part of herself to trust around it.

By eleven, the brownness in the south had lifted. The line of it was clearer now, not a cloud bank but a moving bruise. Dust ran low across the stubble beyond the chicken run. Somebody tied a handkerchief over a child's mouth. Milt began taking the lots faster, his chant tightening.

"Cupboard, who'll start me at a dollar, dollar, who'll say a dollar, seventy-five in the back, now one, one, one and a quarter..."

The words lost meaning and became weather of their own. Numbers struck the yard and passed. Cora stood through the stove, the washtub, the pressure canner with the warped lid, the porch glider Ezra had dragged home secondhand from a farm east of McCook and repaired in three different woods. That sold for almost nothing. The woman who bought it ran one finger over the armrest groove where years of hands had worn the paint smooth. Cora had the stupid urge to say, It squeals if you lean too far left. As if instructions were still hers to give.

August came to the porch once at noon when the crowd broke for sandwiches and coffee and the air tasted more strongly of alkali. He stopped three steps short of her.

"Mrs. Vale."

"What."

He looked toward the road where the sky had gone the color of old pennies. "If you want the land portion moved earlier, we can do it before the worst of the wind."

"Why."

"Because I would prefer to complete the legal business while men can hear it."

"They can hear fine."

"That may change."

She could have let him go with that. Instead she took the folded ledger page out of her apron and held it between two fingers. The paper looked pitifully small for the damage in it.

"You preferred a good many things," she said.

His face altered not at all, which was its own labor. "Yes."

"You gave me this. Are you going to say different in front of them?"

"No."

"Then stand there and don't speak unless I ask you."

If anyone else had said that to August Poeschel in the old days, Cora might have waited to see them crushed flat. He only looked at the paper, then at her, and answered, "Very well."

The quiet of it shook her more than denial would have. There was no satisfaction in commanding a man already standing in the ruins of his own authority. He turned and went back to the clerk's table. She put the page away. Her fingers smelled of paper dust and sweat.

She had not meant, even then, to say anything public. She told herself she had only wanted him pinned to his own proof. She told herself she needed the paper in her pocket because otherwise the town might decide it had all been weather after all, Ezra weak and August stern and the rest of them merely unlucky. But wanting proof ready and choosing speech were not the same thing. That choice came later, with the land.

Milt called everyone to order again a little after noon. The wind had risen enough to make the sale tags chatter against chair rungs and tool handles. Men buttoned their collars. Women held skirts between their knees. The crowd thickened on the porch and in the front room because nobody wanted to stand full in the yard anymore. Somebody shut the kitchen window, but the latch did not catch on the first try and the sash rattled in its frame like bad teeth.

There was not much household left. The last lots came fast: the clock that no longer kept hour, two sacks of chicken feed, the kitchen table itself. Cora stepped back into the doorway when Milt sold the table so the buyer's sons could carry it out. The empty patch of floor it left behind looked indecently white where the linoleum had been protected from years of grit.

Then Milt took off his hat and unfolded the legal notice.

"We will now offer for sale the real property commonly known as the Vale place," he said. His voice lost all its trade brightness on the formal words. "Foreclosure under lien held by Farmers State Bank of Morrow. Improvements include dwelling, shed, chicken run, windmill, and cultivated acreage as described in the notice."

He began to read the section lines and boundaries. Cora heard none of them whole. The words slid by like fence wire under a glove, catching only in spots. South. West. Half. Quarter. Township. Range. It was her land reduced to directions men could write without seeing it.

Outside the front window the brown wall had climbed high enough to blot the lower sky. It gave the room a strange dimness though the hour was early. Dust hissed under the doorsill. A man in a feed-store cap dark with old sweat said from the back, "Lord, here she comes," and Eli Voss answered, rubbing his bad knee as if the weather had reached it first, "Ain't it always weather gets the last say."

That did it.

Cora did not know whether it was the "always" or the relief in the man's voice that split something in her. Maybe it was hearing the lie arrive so easily, welcomed like grace. Maybe it was Dell beside the mantel with his hurt arm tied up and his whole face listening. Maybe it was Thea standing against Hester's shoulder near the kitchen, not looking at Cora because she had stopped asking to be chosen in public. Maybe it was August five feet from the clerk's table with his eyes down, ready to let the sale roll over the truth one final time because procedure knew how to survive shame.

Whatever it was, it took hold below thought.

"Stop," she said.

Milt halted in the middle of a boundary line. The room held itself still.

"Mrs. Vale," August began.

"No." Her own voice surprised her. It was not loud. It was clean. "No, you don't get to hurry this part."

Several heads turned from August back to her. A child near the front door started to whimper and was hushed. Cora stepped out from the wall into the center of the room where the kitchen table had been an hour earlier. She could feel every eye making new use of her.

"You can sell it," she said. "I'm not asking you not to. But you don't get to sell it for one more weather story."

Milt still held the notice in both hands. He looked from her to August the way a man looked at two dogs deciding which one had the legal right to bite. August did not rescue him.

"This isn't the time," August said at last, formal even now. "The sale is under notice and must proceed."

"It will." Cora turned enough to look at him full. "After."

No one laughed. The lack of laughter gave her more room than she had expected. She reached into her apron and unfolded the ledger page once, enough that the changed date showed like a bruise on the paper.

"He brought me the true page last night," she said. "He can tell you if he wants. He won't say I'm lying."

August's face went colorless under the dust. He did not speak.

The silence after that was not belief exactly, but it was appetite. Cora felt it move through the room like a draft.

She could have said Ezra signed thinking the extension was honest. She could have said August altered what came after. She could have held Ezra up half-clean and left the rest buried. That was the old temptation and she knew it by heart. Then her eyes found Dell again. He had gone very still, a thing Dell only did when something was hurting him enough to turn him careful. She thought of him hearing Ezra sob in the barn and never telling her, a secret she did not know from his mouth but knew by the strange vacancy that had opened in him after his father's death and never fully shut. She thought of Thea carrying the letter all week because Cora had not wanted to touch it. She thought of Saint's Basin dark with seep water and all the town's fathers walking around with dust in their lungs and innocence in their stories.

She was tired of innocence. It had cost too much.

"Mrs. Blum," she said.

Hester lifted her head. "I'm here."

"Tell them what that ground was."

The room shifted again. Hester did not move at first. She stood with one broad hand on the back of a chair that had already been sold, and for a second Cora saw how thin the skin had gone over her knuckles, how the cough had hollowed the notch above her collarbone. Then Hester came forward into the empty floor space and put herself there as if she had been expected all along.

"It was a basin," Hester said, and she did not raise her voice. She never wasted breath that way. "You all know that, and if you don't know it, you know your fathers knew it. It held spring water and winter water. Geese came down on it. Cattle stood belly-deep there in dry years and did not die. Otto said leave it be. He said the grass there kept what little mercy this country got."

A woman near the door with a baby pinning her dress flat at one hip muttered, "That was years ago."

Hester turned her head toward the voice without hurrying. "Yes. That's how history works."

A few people looked down.

"They cut tile through it," Hester went on. "They drained it because wheat paid in good years and men got greedy in the same ordinary way they always do, not grand, not wicked enough for a sermon, just greedy and pressed and stupid together. They broke the meadow and plowed it right through. Otto said if you bled that basin and stripped the grass, the south wind would have a cleaner run at us. He said the soil would go. He said one bad cycle would turn hunger into weather. Folks called him old. Folks laughed. Then they took the wheat money when it came."

Her breath hitched. Cora saw her save it and continue.

"Now every time the sky turns brown, people stand around and say the Lord did it or Kansas did it or bad luck did it. We did a share. That town did a share. Those papers did a share. That dead man did a share." She pointed not at August first but at the floorboards under them, the house itself, as if Ezra were still somewhere in the grain. "And this one" - now she lifted her finger at August - "did his share twice over."

The room did not gasp. It took the words in like a blow to the ribs, with a rough collective sound and then a held breath after.

At the front window the dust began to move.

Cora saw it before anyone said anything. Grit had filmed the lower pane all morning, ordinary enough. Now the glass darkened in patches where the room's closeness warmed it and the pressure outside changed. A wash of green-black rose under the dust, then the quick silver flash of water. Men standing shoulder to shoulder in the front room found themselves looking out through Saint's Basin as it had been: not mythic, not pretty, just wet ground furred with sedge and the low backs of geese tipping at the edge. Then the image jerked and changed. A line of men with tile spades. A horse straining in harness. Otto Blum younger and straighter, driving a survey stake into mud and turning hard toward somebody beyond the frame with his mouth open in anger.

Mrs. Kroll crossed herself before she remembered where she was.

The enamel coffee pot on the sold sideboard flashed next, its blue chipped shoulder catching a different memory: children skating in patched coats while somebody laughed from shore; then the same sweep of ground split open with a ditch and wicker baskets of dirt passed hand to hand. Mrs. Kroll glanced down at the mirror under her arm as if it might turn on her again, but it held only her own frightened face. The room had no place left to put its eyes except on the truth moving from surface to surface.

Milt said, very softly, "Well."

August took one step forward. "This is not evidence in any legal sense."

Hester barked a laugh that turned into coughing. "You still talking like paper is the only thing can tell on a man?"

Cora moved before she had time to think better. She went to Hester, braced a hand under her elbow while the cough worked through her, and then faced the room again with Hester's weight against her palm, solid and alive. It was the first person she had touched all day.

"Miss Bell," she said.

Thea's face changed when Cora used the public name. Something in it hardened, then steadied. She opened her handbag and took out the folded sheets Ezra had hidden in the geography book. The paper was softer than the ledger page, worn at the creases, handled too many times in too few days.

August said, "I would advise against this."

Thea looked at him with schoolroom calm so exact it was almost contempt. "I'm not asking advice."

The line ran through the room and set its own order there. Cora felt it. Thea stepped beside Hester and Cora, not touching either of them. The lack of touch was its own kind of charge. Half the town knew enough to feel it and not enough to name it safely.

"Read it," Cora said.

Thea unfolded the first page. Her hand was steady until she found the opening line; then the paper gave one small shake and stilled again.

"This was written by Ezra Vale," she said. "It was found hidden in the schoolhouse with Otto Blum's map copy. I will read it as he wrote it."

She did not look up again for several sentences. Her voice went out measured and clear, built to carry to the back row of desks and now to the back wall of a sold front room.

"Cora,

"If this comes to you, then I failed twice, once in the field and once at the table where I ought to have told you plain. I signed the basin paper because August said the note would carry another year if I did, and I wanted another season more than I wanted to listen to Otto or to what I knew myself. I knew that low ground held water for a reason. I knew the south field never did right after a dry spring unless that basin stayed wet. I said yes anyway because I was tired of standing in the kitchen with nothing to show you but weather."

No one moved. The room had gone so still Cora could hear the dust ticking against the window glass.

Thea turned the page.

"I told myself every man around here had made the same bargain in one shape or another, and maybe that's true, but it's coward's comfort. I wanted wheat where water had been. I wanted the bank off my neck. I wanted Dell to stay in school and you to quit mending the same dress till the cloth gave up. Want isn't excuse. It's only want."

Dell's mouth had opened slightly. He was not breathing through it, only holding it there as if something in his face had forgotten the next instruction.

Thea went on.

"After I signed, August brought the paper back different than I had understood it, dated wrong, tighter than what he had spoken in his office. I should have fought then. I should have brought it home and put it in your hands and let you call me fool to my face. I did not. I kept thinking one more harvest would hide the first bad choice and then the second. By the time I understood there was no hiding any of it, I had laid shame on this house thicker than dust."

Thea stopped before she reached the end, because the next line hurt enough that even she had to take one breath before lifting it into air.

Cora said, "Keep going."

Thea's eyes lifted once, quick, and in them Cora saw fury, pity, and the old appetite she had tried to starve into patience. Then Thea read the last paragraph.

"I don't ask you to forgive me for the basin or the note. I ask only that Dell know I was not cheated clean. I put my hand to it. I did it because I was afraid of failing you and because I couldn't bear being seen failing already. That's smaller than love and uglier than duty, but it's the truth. If there's any mercy in me worth naming, it's only that I know what I have done to the ground and to you.

"Ezra."

Thea lowered the paper.

For a moment the only sound was the storm working itself closer, a long rasp along the clapboards. Cora did not look at August. She did not need to. The letter had not absolved him. It had done something worse. It had made room enough for more than one guilty person in the story.

Dell was staring at the floor. She could not tell if he was seeing the boards or some older night in the barn, Ezra's shoulders bent over on themselves while the horses shifted in the dark and Dell, too young and too ashamed to wake his mother, listened through the plank wall and called it not his business. She had never been inside that moment with him. She was not inside it now. But she could see the shape of it in the way his good hand opened and shut against his thigh, not striking, not reaching, only trying to learn what it was holding.

Milt cleared his throat once. "Well," he said again, but this time there was no trade in it.

From the back of the room, Eli Voss spoke up. "If the bank changed the paper after-"

August cut him off, not sharply, almost wearily. "The bank altered the effective date. Yes."

Every head in the room turned to him.

He stood by the clerk's table with the sale ledger open and both hands flat on it. The pose might once have made him look in command. Now it made him look like a man bracing through a dizziness he had earned.

"The note is still due," he said. "The sale is still lawful under the foreclosure. I have waived any further claim on Mrs. Vale beyond the property itself. None of that changes what I did. None of it changes what Ezra Vale signed first."

There it was, the thing he could not keep procedural any longer. It was no confession broad enough to cleanse him, but it was not a lie either. Cora felt the room mark him down in a different ledger than the one under his hands.

Mrs. Kroll said, half to herself, "God help us."

Hester, done coughing for the moment, answered, "He has had a long while and left most of it to us."

Something like a laugh moved through the room and failed. It was too hard a day for laughter to get purchase.

The dust at the window thinned. Once seen, the memory had gone. The basin vanished back into grit and streaks. The coffee pot dulled. The mirror in Mrs. Kroll's arms held only her own frightened face again. That seemed right to Cora. Truth never stayed glowing long. It had to be carried after.

She felt suddenly emptied, though not relieved; relief belonged to easier people. Some hard knot under her ribs had finally cut loose, and the absence of it left her unsteady.

"Go on then," she said to Milt.

He looked at her as if making sure. She nodded once.

Milt refolded the notice with care he had not shown it before. "Real property," he said, and his auction voice came back altered, lower. "What am I bid on the Vale place as described."

Silence answered him.

That, more than the earlier gawking or the handling of her sheets or the cheap prices on the rocker and the glider, nearly broke Cora. Her farm was not even worth an eager theft. Men looked at their boots. One of the eastern buyers studied the window as if maybe another miracle would save him from participation. Outside, the windmill creaked once and stopped.

Milt named a starting figure. Nothing.

He cut it. Nothing.

August said, "The bank will enter a credit bid."

The words were plain enough, but they landed in Cora's body like the first cold mouthful of creek water after fever, shocking not because she had not expected it, but because expectation had not dulled the touch. The clerk wrote. Milt repeated the amount. He asked for better. No one gave it.

Eli Voss spat into the dust by the door and said, not quite under his breath, "Ain't a man alive can make wheat on that side now."

Hester turned on him. "Not after you fools cut its knees."

No one contradicted her.

Milt waited the proper time anyway. Trade had rules even in shame. "Fair warning," he said. "Last call."

Cora looked at the window where the basin had shown itself and was gone. She looked at Dell. He had raised his head. His face was wet with nothing as simple as tears; the dust had pasted two clean tracks down from his eyes and then dried there.

"Ma," he said.

Only that. But in it there was no accusation. There was no plea to stop the sale or drag Ezra back upright or fix the week and the year and the whole bad country. There was only the raw fact that he was still her son and still standing.

Milt brought his hand down on the doorframe because there was no gavel. "Sold to Farmers State Bank."

The room exhaled. People began at once to move in all the practical ways disaster invited. Men asked where they should load what they had bought before the wind rose worse. The buyer of the stove wanted to know if he could fetch it tomorrow if the storm hit hard. Mrs. Kroll hugged the mirror to her chest and nearly collided with the filling-station man carrying Dell's tool chest. A child started crying for no reason anyone could point to except the room full of adult nerves.

It angered Cora that the world resumed so quickly. It also saved her. If everyone had stood there solemn, if they had given her some formal silence to bear, she might have shattered under it. Instead she was offered labor, and labor was the oldest mercy she knew.

She moved to the porch and began answering questions.

"The stove pipe goes with it."

"No, the crate's yours now."

"Take the hens before they smother."

"Not that pan. That one's cracked through."

Her voice went practical on its own. Dell tried to help and nearly fainted lifting one end of the chicken crate, which sent her into a fury sharp enough to keep her from weeping.

"Set it down, for Christ's sake."

"I'm trying."

"Try less. Breathe first."

He did breathe, angry and obedient at once. Hester took the crate from him with the filling-station man's help and muttered, "You two would start a fistfight in a graveyard if it improved the dead." Dell gave her a bleak sideways look. Hester answered it with one of her own, and the matter passed.

Thea came to Cora on the porch while the room emptied around them. She had folded Ezra's letter back along its old creases. Dust had settled in the lines of her mouth. Up close Cora could see that her collar button had been sewn on in haste that morning and one thread already stood loose.

"Do you want this?" Thea asked.

Cora looked at the pages. They did not look like a revelation anymore. They looked like paper somebody had sweated over with a bad pen and worse courage. "Yes," she said. "Keep it a little longer. I can't take it now."

Thea held her eyes. "All right."

There was too much between them for more. The porch, the buyers, Dell a few feet away, August still at the clerk's table finishing the business of her dispossession. Yet something had altered in the air between Cora and Thea, not mended, not softened, but made less false. Cora had finally brought the truth out where Thea had insisted it belonged. That did not erase the wound of making Thea carry it first. It only meant they were wounded in the same weather now.

August approached when most of the crowd had gone to their wagons. The wind had strengthened enough that he had to hold his coat shut with one hand. He stopped at the foot of the steps and looked not at Cora but at the boards under her shoes.

"You may remain in the house until tomorrow noon," he said. "After that the bank will expect vacancy."

"Generous."

His mouth tightened. "It is the only arrangement I can make before transfer."

"You always have one more arrangement."

That got his eyes up at last. There was no defense in them, which was almost harder to receive than if he had tried for one. "Yes," he said.

Cora could have cursed him then. Could have told him exactly what she hoped the town remembered when it looked at his windows from now on. But the day had moved beyond the satisfactions of cruelty, and she was too tired to waste a good sentence on a man already living under his own.

"Go home, August," she said. "You're no use to me now."

He gave one short nod. Then he turned into the wind and walked down the path to the road with dust lifting around his trouser cuffs.

The yard thinned. Wagons creaked out. The eastern buyers covered their purchases with tarps and tied knots that would not hold if the storm meant business. Mrs. Kroll set the mirror face-down in her truck bed and crossed herself again when she thought nobody watched. Milt came to settle his own papers, his yellow mustache gone grayer with grit.

"I'm sorry for the day of it," he said.

It was not the broad apology of a man asking to be forgiven for his trade. It was narrower and more useful than that.

"You worked fair," Cora said.

He nodded, relieved by the exactness. "I'll leave the lot sheets with the bank and the unsold scraps by the shed. There ain't many."

"There never are."

One corner of his mouth moved, not quite a smile. "No, ma'am."

When he had gone, the place looked worse than if thieves had taken it in the night. A thief at least would leave the shape of a struggle. The yard after auction had a picked-clean order to it. Here and there lay the things no one had wanted: a split scoop shovel, three jars without lids, the glider cushion too mouse-eaten to bring a bid, a child's slate from years back, blank with dust. The porch boards showed pale rectangles where furniture had stood. From upstairs came the new loudness of an emptied room, every sound given back thin.

Cora stepped into the front room and stopped where the kitchen table had been. The floor still held the white ghost of its outline. The house smelled different already. Less of coffee and cloth and their own bodies. More of old wood opened up to strangers' handling.

Dell came in behind her. He moved carefully now, tired enough to stop pretending otherwise. Hester had gone to see whether her wagon could be turned in time on the lane. Thea was at the back door gathering the last of the letter and the two cups she had rinsed because even after a public wreck she could not leave a cup dirty if there was water to spare.

"You ought to sit," Cora said.

"In what."

The answer was so flat it almost made her laugh. She looked around the bare room. "On the floor then."

He did, lowering himself against the wall one inch at a time until the bad arm was safe. Dust showed bright on the knees of his trousers. He stared at the place where the table had been.

"I thought he was one thing or the other," he said after a while. "A man done wrong, or a bastard." He swallowed. "Turns out he was scared."

Cora leaned her shoulder against the doorframe. The wood was cool despite the crowd that had been there minutes before. "Yes."

"I heard him that night." Dell kept his eyes on the floor. "In the barn. Crying. I didn't get you."

There it was at last, plain as a dropped plate.

She let the silence hold a beat, not to punish him, only because anything quick would be wrong. The back door screen rasped once where Thea moved and then went still again.

"You were a boy," Cora said.

"I was near grown."

"You were a boy with a father in pieces and no map for it."

He rubbed the heel of his hand hard against one eye. "I thought if I got you, then it'd make it real."

"It was real whether you fetched me or not."

He nodded, once. The nod went all through him.

"I don't know if I hate him more now or less," he said.

"You don't have to settle that today."

"I know." He drew breath and let it out slow. "But I reckon I can leave him now."

That landed truer than anything else said all day. Cora felt it in the emptied room, in the white mark where the table had stood, in the open future she had not wanted and could no longer keep from coming. Leave him. It did not mean stop loving him, stop grieving him, or pardon him. It meant leaving the fixed dead weight of the story that had pinned Dell to this farm as if a saint lay buried under every furrow.

At the back door Thea turned. She had heard. Of course she had. She did not speak into the moment. Another week, Cora thought, and I might have lost even this kind of mercy from her, the knowledge of when not to step in.

The first hard gust hit then, shaking the house from sill to eave. Dust drove against the windows with a sound like dry grain thrown by a shovel. Hester shouted from outside for them to hurry if they meant to beat the road into town.

Cora pushed off the frame. There was no chair left to brace a hand on, no table to circle, no excuse remaining in the room. She crossed to Dell and held out her hand. He looked at it a second before taking it with his good one.

"Come on," she said. "We'll go to Hester's."

He got to his feet. Thea lifted the bag from the counter and stepped aside for them to pass, not servant, not stranger, simply making a place where there had not been one before. As Cora moved by, the back of her hand brushed Thea's sleeve. The contact was brief, almost nothing. It sent a live line through her all the same.

Outside, the yard had gone the color of dirty wool. The sold things were already becoming other people's burdens under tarps and rope. The house behind them, stripped and lawful and no longer hers, stood with its windows dulled back to glass, with no green water, no geese, no Ezra in a bank office. Truth had done its work and withdrawn, leaving them to the ordinary labor after revelation: loading what little remained, coughing in the wind, walking on.

Hester had the wagon turned crooked at the gate, cursing the left wheel under her breath with the concentration of a woman who reserved profanity for equipment and men who earned it. She wore Dell's bedroll slung over one shoulder and Cora's cedar chest already roped in place. Thea climbed up first to make room. Dell followed slowly, jaw tight with pain. Cora put one hand on the wagon board and looked back once at the porch.

The sale tag still hung from the empty nail where her Singer had stood. It beat itself against the clapboard in the rising wind.

Then she climbed up and let Hester drive them toward the bakery in town while the first real edge of the storm came on.


# Chapter 17

By dusk the bakery windows were gray again. After Hester drove them in ahead of the storm, Cora and Dell had gone back out to settle what could still be settled while she and Thea started supper. The dust had laid itself over the glass in a fine, patient skin, as if the town had not already spent the afternoon pawing through Cora Vale's life and pricing it by the lot. Hester stood at the stove with her apron tied too loose at the waist, stirring onions through bacon grease while a pan of cabbage buns browned in the oven. Every few minutes the spoon stopped. She bent, coughed into a square of linen, folded the linen shut, and went on.

Thea had stopped pretending not to see.

"Let me take that," she said when the coughing fit finally forced Hester's palm flat against the stove ledge.

"And have you stir onions like a woman punishing them for their sins?" Hester straightened. Her eyes watered, but the look she gave Thea was dry as old wood. "No."

She always pocketed the blackest crust for herself, as if bitterness were a private taste she did not care to explain. Tonight the burnt heel of one loaf already sat wrapped in a scrap of wax paper by her elbow, waiting for later. Above her left ear, two hairpins crossed in a slanted X. Thea had never once seen them match.

"You can't keep coughing that way."

"Watch me." Hester gave the onions another turn. "Slice the cabbage finer. Dell chews like he's racing somebody."

The kitchen behind the shop was hot from baking and close with yeast, coffee grounds, flour, and the bitter syrup Hester kept for her chest in a brown bottle by the lamp. A damp towel covered the bowl of risen dough. The shelf above the sink held the sealed dust jars in a crooked row, their glass gone amber in the weak light, each lid ringed with old wax. Thea tried not to look at them for long. Since the auction she had not wanted any surface in Morrow to remember another thing.

She cut the cabbage on the scarred board, working the knife through the pale curls, and listened to the town outside settle into its boarded, emptied evening. A truck changed gears on Main and faded. A screen door rasped somewhere down the block. Then only wind against the alley and the faint rattle of cooling tins.

"Will she come?" Hester asked.

Thea knew which she meant. She kept her eyes on the knife. "She said yes."

"And Del?"

"He was with her."

Hester snorted once, not quite a laugh. "That boy has been with her since he was put in her arms, and half the trouble is that neither of them has ever known where one of them ended and the other started."

Thea slid the chopped cabbage into the waiting bowl. She wanted to ask whether Hester thought that had changed today, with Ezra's letter spoken out loud before half the town, with August Poeschel standing there to hear his own work named for what it was, with Cora asking for the truth even though it cost her the last fiction she had been able to live inside. She wanted to ask whether Hester thought any of them could still be repaired, or whether survival at this point only meant learning how to travel damaged.

But Hester hated questions that wanted blessing disguised as wisdom.

So Thea said, "The dough's ready."

"I can see that."

Hester dusted the table, then stood with both hands braced on the edge before she trusted herself to lift the bowl. The movement was small, but the effort of it showed plain in the pause that followed. Under the kitchen heat her face had gone the flat color of bread dough. When she drew breath, the air seemed to catch somewhere low and have to be hauled the rest of the way up by force.

Thea moved closer anyway. "At least let me fill them."

"You can fill them. If you overstuff them, I'll throw the lot in the alley."

That was permission. Thea took it.

They worked without grace and without needing any. Thea pinched dough into rounds, spooned in cabbage and onions and the stingy shreds of salt pork Hester had saved for the occasion, then folded the edges shut. Hester moved along behind her, turning each seam under with the side of her thumb, swift and exact even now.

"You're nervous," Hester said.

"You can tell from my bread?"

"I can tell from the way you keep listening for the door."

Thea almost denied it. That was still one of the oldest habits in her, to take her own feeling and dress it in grammar until it looked manageable. Instead she set another bun on the tray and said, "I don't know what she'll want from me tonight."

"Probably the truth. Just not all of it at once."

"That's one of your sayings, not advice."

"No." Hester leaned one hip against the table until the wave passing through her chest eased. "My sayings are prettier. Advice is uglier and more useful."

Thea smiled despite herself. It felt strange on her face after the day.

When the first knock came, it was Dell's way of knocking: one hard rap, then two quicker ones after it, as if the first had merely announced that the rest were serious. Hester was already reaching for the oven door. Thea wiped her hands on her skirt and went through the shop.

Cora stood in the back entry with dusk on her shoulders and Dell half behind her, tall enough now that he filled the narrow doorway even slouched. Auction dust had dried into the hem of Cora's skirt and gone pale in the blunt ends of her cut hair. She still held herself like a woman braced against weather, but there was no house left to defend in the set of her body now, only habit. Before she even looked fully at Thea, she bent to push the loose strip of screen back into the frame where it had sprung near the latch. She could not cross a threshold without mending something if there was anything to mend.

Dell had a carpenter's pencil behind his right ear and a folded newspaper timetable sticking out of his back pocket, the edge gone soft from being handled. His left forearm lay bound and awkward against his middle. He had tried to wash up, but dust still sat in the seams beside his nose and in the whorl at the temple where his hair refused every comb it ever met.

"You said six," he told Thea, sniffing past her into the kitchen. "I count better than that."

"I made eight," Hester called. "One for each of us and two for greed."

"Then I came to the right house."

The answer was quick enough to sound easy. Thea heard the strain under it anyway, the way his voice tried at manhood and slid back toward boy if pain caught it wrong.

Cora met Thea's eyes at last. There was flour on Thea's sleeve. There was a crease in Cora's face from the hat ribbon she had worn all afternoon and taken off too late. For one long second Thea knew the exact shape of Cora's mouth and did not know whether she would ever be allowed near it again.

"You can hang your coats there," she said, and hated how schoolmarm the sentence sounded as soon as it left her.

Cora's mouth moved once at one corner, too slight to count as a smile and too warm to be accidental. "We're not staying long enough to need them."

Hester came in from the kitchen with the first tray, and the room altered around her. The light stayed gray, but the smell of butter and scorched crust drew everybody toward the table.

"You'll stay long enough to eat sitting down," she said. "If anybody starts in on plans before I've got soup in bowls, I'll box ears, broken arm or no broken arm."

Dell's gaze went to the spoon rack as if he were judging whether she could still reach one to throw. "I'd like to see you try."

"You would not," Hester said. "You're vain about your face."

That got him. He ducked his head so they would not see the grin come all the way in.

The table in the kitchen had been pulled out from the wall for four and then amended for a fifth with a chair that had once belonged in the front room, its cane seat mended with clothesline. Cora, without thinking, folded a square of old seed catalog and shoved it under the short leg before anyone sat. The wobble vanished. She took the chair nearest the stove, as if warmth were a thing that should go first to the oldest body in the room and could only be trusted to do so if she kept watch on it.

The soup was potato, onion, and milk, thick enough to hold heat. The buns came out scorched on top in spots and shining with a smear of butter Hester must have been hoarding. There was pickled beet relish in a chipped bowl, and a plate of sliced pears preserved in thin syrup that proved Hester had been lying for weeks when she said she had no sugar to spare.

The sight of it hit Thea harder than if Hester had set down a roast goose.

"You extravagant old fraud," she said.

Hester lowered herself into her chair with a care she tried to pass off as annoyance. "It's not extravagance if it clears the shelf. Eat."

For the first minute only spoons sounded. Steam came up against faces still powdered with the day. Dell burned his mouth, swore into his spoon, and looked around as if daring correction.

Hester tore a bun in half. "Profanity's allowed if it's descriptive."

"Then that soup's hot as hell."

"That's better."

Cora had not yet looked directly at Thea again. She ate fast at first, the way people do when they have spent a day being watched and want at least one human act to go unobserved. Then the food slowed her. Some of the drawn steel left her shoulders. She took another bun. Thea watched her notice, without comment, that Hester had left the caraway off half the batch. Hester knew Cora had hated the taste since she was twelve and swallowed a mouthful of the seeds on a church picnic to prove she was not delicate. Thea did not know how many people in Morrow still remembered a thing so small. Maybe only the women who had kept one another alive this long.

"I heard old Pritchard offer ten cents for our washstand like he was doing the wood a favor," Dell said around his third spoonful.

"He'd haggle with his own coffin," Hester said.

"He rattled the drawer first." Dell mimed it with his good hand, sharp and contemptuous. "Three times. As if he thought money might hatch in there if he worried it."

Cora set her spoon down. "Mrs. Pritchard tried all the cupboard latches in the kitchen. Every one."

"Maybe she meant to buy one," Thea said.

Cora looked at her then, fully this time. Weariness had made her eyes clearer, not duller. "She doesn't cook. She only inventories."

Dell laughed once, surprised into it. Hester's answer followed on top of his.

"That's a profession in this town."

The laugh stayed. It moved around the table, thin at first, then truer. Thea felt it in her ribs like something loosening.

Hester used the opening. "Now. Since we're not discussing plans and I refuse to let this meal turn into a funeral with spoons, somebody tell me the worst song ever written."

"All of them," Dell said.

"Coward's answer."

"That one about the red river," he said. "Any fellow with a weak jaw sings it when he's had beer."

"He doesn't need beer," Cora said. "He only needs dusk."

"And somebody to ignore him," Hester added.

Thea said, "There was a boy in normal school who used to sing sentimental hymns at girls by the library steps."

Dell stared. "At them?"

"At them."

"Did it work?"

"Not on any girl with sense."

"Then the song wasn't the trouble," Hester said.

Thea surprised herself by laughing. "No. The trouble was his hair oil. You could smell him before the second verse."

"There you are," Hester said. "No song survives pomade."

Cora broke off a piece of bun and sopped her bowl. "Ezra used to sing work songs under his breath and leave out half the tune. Just the lines about mules or rain barrels. If the part was about a girl, he never remembered it."

It was the first time she had said his name without the whole room tightening around it. Thea saw the change happen, saw Dell hear it too. He looked down into his soup, not hiding from it exactly, only making space.

"Otto couldn't carry a tune if you nailed it to his coat," Hester said. "But he had three dirty verses for every church picnic song ever printed, and he knew precisely when to save them till the children had run off."

"You sang them with him," Thea said.

Hester sniffed. "Only so he wouldn't make a fool of himself alone."

"Merciful woman."

"I wasn't."

Dell pointed his spoon at her. "You're smiling."

"That's windburn."

The pears went around. Hester made everyone take some. Outside, the light thinned to the color of old paper. The lamp on the table gathered them tighter inside its reach. The bakery walls kept out the worst of the evening wind, but not all of it. Thea could hear grit brush the outer brick with the sound of something testing a knife.

"Books, then," Hester said, as if she had written the order of topics in advance. "Miss Bell, since that's your trade. Best one and worst one."

"You make it sound like flour grades."

"Everything is a flour grade if you live long enough."

Thea thought of the schoolhouse shelves, the atlas with Alaska still looking like a rumor, the McGuffey readers with names inked inside from children now in Colorado or dead or merely gone where Morrow no longer got to count them. She thought of the geography book with its cut-out hollow and the letter that had split her life at the seam.

"Worst," she said, "is any reader that tells children the country is orderly."

"That's cheating," Dell said. "That's not one book."

"Then the blue civics text with the eagle on it."

"Good," Hester said. "It always looked smug."

"Best," Thea said, and found herself cautious for no reason she could name. "A railway timetable, if you're truly going somewhere. A cookbook written by a woman who's survived company. A novel with enough weather in it that you trust the people before they speak."

"That's three," Dell said.

"I teach. We multiply."

He considered that. "Best book's a repair manual."

"For what?"

"Anything with bolts." He shrugged with one shoulder. "A machine tells the truth if you can read the marks."

Thea watched him say it. He still pretended, when it suited him, that books and school had slid off him like rain off oilcloth. Yet there was the pencil behind his ear and the timetable soft in his pocket, and he had read Ezra's letter with his face set still as split stone while half the town listened. Seventeen, she thought. Seventeen, and already he had learned to hide whatever in him was tender by disguising it as function.

"Then what's the worst?" she asked.

"Any poem printed in a farm paper."

Hester barked out a laugh so sudden it tipped into coughing before she could close her mouth against it. The cough doubled her over. The linen came up too slow this time. Thea saw the string of spit go gray across Hester's knuckles before it disappeared into cloth. Cora half rose. Hester waved her back down without looking up, the refusal sharp as a slap.

The fit worked through her by degrees, leaving gray spit on the linen and a tremor in her shoulders before it finally let go.

When Hester sat again, she took a sip of water and said, voice rougher, "That answer stays."

Dell's ears had gone red. He hated fright showing on him almost as much as pain. He tore the remaining half of his bun into small hard pieces and arranged them by size beside his plate.

"You're right, though," Thea said to him, because she knew he needed the floor returned without pity. "Farm-paper poems are usually about sunsets written by men who have never looked up from a ledger."

"Or calves," Cora said. "Bad poems love calves."

"Too easy," Hester said.

"I've seen them." Cora's mouth shifted again, closer to a smile now. "Weak little verses about dew on a nose."

"You have become literary, Mrs. Vale," Thea said.

"Don't start."

But there was warmth in it, and it went under Thea's skin like drink.

The talk wandered after that because Hester let it and because none of them could bear, for one hour, to make every sentence earn its keep. Yeast came next, naturally, because the buns were good enough to deserve explanation.

"People kill starter by fussing over it," Hester said. "They want to warm it, feed it, stir it, worry at it every ten minutes. Leave it alone half the time and it behaves better."

"You're talking about people," Dell said.

"I'm talking about dough. If it wounds you, improve yourself."

"Is that why you hide your crocks under towels?" Thea asked.

"That's because dust has no manners."

Cora ran a thumb over the seam of her bowl. "I always thought bread ought to have some meanness in it. Sweet bread doesn't trust the world enough."

"There she is," Hester said softly, almost to herself.

Thea did not ask what she meant. She knew. This was the Cora who had not vanished at auction, only been driven so far inward all afternoon that Thea had feared whatever remained might never turn its face outward again.

Dell leaned back, wincing despite himself when his ribs reminded him of the movement. "If California's all oranges, maybe the bread out there tastes foolish."

"Who told you California's all oranges?" Hester asked.

"Magazine ad. At the depot last spring. A woman in white gloves holding half a fruit like it was a jewel."

"That wasn't for you," Thea said.

"I know that." He rubbed one thumb over the edge of his spoon. "Still looked like it smelled better than here."

Cora lifted her eyes. "Does it?"

The question was aimed at Thea, not because she had been to California, but because of the four of them she had spent the most time pretending maps could answer anything.

"Not all of it," Thea said. "I imagine some of it smells like hot tar and fish and the back side of money. But the depots I've seen in other places smelled different from ours. Cinders. Wool coats damp from weather. Coffee. Once, in Omaha, there was a crate of bananas split open and turning black, and the whole platform smelled like sugar trying to rot politely."

Dell stared in real pleasure. "Bananas."

"I didn't say you could afford one."

"Didn't ask if I could."

Cora said, "What about Lincoln?"

Thea had not been there in years. Long enough that the memory came back with pieces missing and the pieces missing mattered less than the fact of motion itself.

"Coal smoke," she said. "Wet newspaper. Perfume if somebody's trying too hard. The lunch counter grease. Sometimes oranges in winter if the right car comes through. And people hurrying like they mean to get somewhere before the day closes on them."

"Sounds proud," Hester said.

"Mostly it sounds loud."

Cora looked at the lamp chimney, at the flour in the cracks of the table, at nothing Thea could name. "Maybe every place smells different once you know you're allowed to leave it."

No one answered immediately. The sentence had come out of her without warning, plain as weather, and it sat with them.

Dell broke it first, mercifully. "Nebraska smells like singed wheat and axle grease."

"Cow manure," Hester said.

"That too."

"Old coffee grounds," Thea said.

"Wet wool in March," Cora said.

"Hot iron after rain," Dell added.

"You all missed the obvious," Hester said. "It smells like dirt. Which would be a comfort if it ever stayed where it belonged."

They laughed again, quieter this time, because the truth of it had teeth.

By the time the bowls were empty and the last of the pears taken up in syrup with bread, the room had changed shape. The day still stood there at full size. The loss still had its whole weight. But the table had made a place big enough to hold a few things besides grief, and that felt almost indecent in its relief.

Thea stood to gather plates. Cora stood at the same time, their chairs scraping together.

"I'll wash," Cora said.

"You don't have to."

"I know that."

The words were ordinary, but the pressure under them tightened the room.

Dell, as if hearing what ought to be given privacy and refusing to look as if he heard it, scooped up the cutlery one-handed and carried it to the sink. "If I'm sent out of the room, I'm taking a bun with me."

"Take two," Hester said. "And bring me the blue tin from the pantry after."

He paused. "The round one or the square?"

"Round. Don't go inventing."

He went, and Hester leaned back with her hands on her lap, not resting exactly, but borrowing a minute from her own body by force.

At the sink Thea poured water from the kettle into the dishpan and added cold till the enamel stopped ticking. Cora rolled her sleeves once. Her hands were still rough with dust from the sale ring and the house and whatever else the day had asked her to touch. When Thea passed her the first plate, their fingers met in the wet.

Thea almost pulled back. Cora did not.

"I was ugly with you," Cora said, looking down at the plate she was wiping. "At the house. Before."

Thea had thought about that fight in enough versions to have worn grooves in her own mind. In some, she won it cleanly. In some, Cora did. The truth had never let either woman come out whole.

"You wanted him easier than he was," she said.

"I wanted the dead to stay useful." Cora's mouth tightened. "That's uglier than I said it then."

"Yes."

Cora nodded once, accepting the answer as if it were a tool she had asked to borrow. "I asked you to choose me over the truth because I was frightened of what was left if I didn't. That wasn't fair."

The plate in Thea's hands was already clean. She kept washing it anyway. "No."

"I know." Cora set the dried plate aside, lined exactly with the edge of the drainboard. "I'm not asking you to make it smaller."

Thea's throat hurt, but not in any stock way she could name. It was the steam, the lamp heat, the scrape of Cora's thumbnail on old crockery, the fact that all afternoon Thea had stood in public with Ezra's letter shaking only once in her hands and now one plain apology from the woman she loved made the whole room feel less solid.

"I hated you for being right where it cost me," Cora went on. "I don't mean I stopped wanting you. I mean I hated you in the same breath. I figure that's worth saying plain."

Thea let out something that might have become a laugh in another life. "That's unpleasantly honest."

"Well."

"I hated you for asking it," Thea said. "And I understood why you asked. That was worse."

Cora's hand flattened on the rim of the dishpan. The skin over her knuckles was split white from weather and lye soap both. "Can you stand to go on with me if I don't promise pretty?"

Thea turned then. Cora stood close enough for the damp front of Thea's dress to hold her heat like a handprint. They were nowhere near a kiss, but the space between their mouths had already turned dangerous.

"I don't want pretty," Thea said. Her voice came out too formal for a beat, and she heard it. She started again. "I don't want another rescue story either. I want to know if when we leave this town, I leave it alone."

Cora looked at her for a long second. "No," she said, and Thea's heart dropped before the rest arrived. "Not alone."

It would have been enough if Hester had not chosen that moment to cough again, harder, in the other room.

Both women turned. Hester was waving them off already, furious at being the occasion for concern. Dell came back from the pantry with the round blue biscuit tin tucked under his good arm and stopped when he saw her.

"I'm fine," Hester said.

"That's a lie," Dell said.

"Yes," she said, surprising them all by not denying it. "Set the tin down."

The round blue tin had once held Danish butter cookies and now had a dent in the side where Otto, according to Hester, had dropped it off the wagon seat twenty years ago and sworn for a full mile. Dell put it in the middle of the table. Thea dried her hands. Cora rolled her sleeves back down as if the talk at the sink could be folded with the fabric and carried privately a little longer.

Hester laid her palm on the lid. Her hand looked broader than ever with the veins standing at the wrist.

"I didn't ask you here to feed you only," she said. "Though that was part of it, and don't let anybody tell you otherwise. People think a meal is decoration. It's not. It's a way of saying I know you've still got a mouth and a gut and tomorrow will need both."

Dell sat again, slower now, wary. The pencil remained behind his ear.

"Open it," Hester said to Thea.

Thea lifted the lid. Inside, wrapped in flour sacks and old hand towels, lay six jelly jars packed close, each sealed with wax around the lid. Dust sat inside them in shades from pale wheat to darker brown, not much in any one jar, never more than a hand's skim. Tucked along one side was a small paper packet folded from old ledger stock and tied with thread gone soft from age.

Even before Hester touched it, Thea knew what that packet was.

"Otto's seed," Hester said. "Buffalo grass. Saved from before everybody around here decided roots were less important than bragging."

Cora's face changed in a way almost no one but Thea would have seen: recognition first, then fear of the sentiment rising behind it, then the hard work of holding both still.

Hester went on. "It won't make anybody rich. That's one virtue in its favor. It sits low, takes hold deep, and minds its own business. If you give it a patch and a little luck, it keeps the ground from lifting and traveling. Otto kept saying we ought to lay it back around the basin edges where the water used to spread and soak instead of run off. Men with more acreage than sense laughed at him till the whole county started blowing east."

Dell leaned over the tin, intent as if the packet were a carburetor part. "It'll still grow?"

"If the world's not entirely rotten." Hester's mouth bent. "Seed wants to outlive us. That's its main talent."

She tapped one jar with her finger. The glass gave a small bright note.

"These are yours now too. All of them. I've kept them long enough."

Thea looked up sharply. "Hester."

"Don't begin. I know what I said." Hester drew breath carefully, then another. "I kept thinking I'd carry them myself till there was a better moment. My chest has informed me that waiting on better moments is for fools and Methodists."

"You're Lutheran," Dell said automatically.

"Exactly."

That got a quick crooked smile from him, but it vanished almost at once.

"You know the rules," Hester said. Her voice had flattened into its working tone, the one she used for recipe weights and weather warnings. "Each jar holds one true thing. Open it and you've spent it. Don't crack the seal because you're curious. Don't open one to win an argument. Don't open one just to feel worse on purpose. Use them when memory's in danger of being scrubbed clean or turned holy. Use them when one of you starts lying about how it was."

Her gaze moved from Thea to Cora to Dell and did not spare anybody.

"That includes lies you tell out of love," she said.

No one answered.

Thea touched one jar through the towel. It was cool. Lighter than she expected and heavier too, because her hand supplied the rest.

"How many?" Dell asked. "To each, I mean."

"You're already dividing them," Hester said.

"Somebody has to carry them."

"Good. Then carry them with your head on." Hester nudged the packet toward the center. "The seed isn't a relic. Don't make a church object of it. If you've got ground fit for it someday, plant it. If you haven't, then give it to ground that deserves it. Saint's Basin first, if you're wise. After that, anywhere you mean to keep from turning traitor."

Cora put two fingers lightly on the packet, then took them away. "Why us?"

Hester gave her a look so direct it bordered on insult. "Because who else is left with enough sense to know it isn't magic? Because I'm tired. Because memory that sits on my shelf after I'm gone is only another thing for dust to cover. Because Otto didn't save seed for me to die preserving a tin."

The sentence dropped into the room with the force of a pan set hard on a table.

Dell's head came up. "Don't talk like that."

"Like what?"

"Gone."

Hester's face softened, which on her looked almost stern. "Del. I've got no use for pretend. Not from doctors, not from preachers, not from family. I'm here now. That's what counts now. Tomorrow can count itself when it arrives."

Thea saw the boy in him then, not because he looked younger but because he looked betrayed by arithmetic. He stared at the jars, jaw set, and the timetable in his back pocket crackled when he shifted.

Cora spoke before the silence could harden. "All right," she said. Practical first, because that was how she touched pain when she meant to keep moving. "We wrap them in towels and keep them low in the valise. Not the carpetbag. Del, the valise with the shirts. It'll ride steadier."

Dell scrubbed the heel of his hand over his mouth. "Need sawdust or flour sack around the bottoms."

"I've got flour sacks," Hester said.

"And the seed?" Thea asked.

"Inside my handbag," Cora said at once, then checked herself, glancing around the table as if she had spoken too quickly and shown more than she intended. "If that's agreeable."

Safety was part of it. So was the fact that the packet would ride nearest Cora's body.

Hester saw everything and looked away just enough to give them the mercy of not naming it.

"Good," she said. "Now that the dead and the dirt have both had their say, we can come to the living. Where are you three going?"

Hester laid the question between the spoons and the lamp as plainly as another hard thing for the table to hold.

Dell shifted first. "West."

"That's not a place," Hester said.

"It's a direction."

"Only useful if you're a compass."

Thea nearly smiled, but the question had struck too near the spot in her that had been braced all evening. She could feel her own old training rise, the instinct to answer carefully enough that no one could accuse her of wanting too much. She forced herself not to hide inside it.

"I can get us to Lincoln," she said. "At least I think I can. The library may not want me now, and I know that. But I've still got the letter, and I've got enough saved to make the fares that far if we're careful."

Cora's head turned. Pride and shame crossed her face so fast Thea could not have separated them if she tried.

"I don't want you buying me passage like a charity case," Cora said.

"I'm not."

"It would look like it."

"To who?"

"To me."

There was the true objection. Thea felt her own temper stir, not hot, only sharp.

"Then let me say it correctly," she said. "I'm not purchasing you. I'm asking whether we travel the first leg of this road together. If we do, then what I've got is ours for that leg. If we don't, then I'll keep my money and my dignity and you can keep yours, and everybody can die respectable."

Dell stared at her, impressed despite himself. Hester closed her eyes briefly, either from fatigue or approval.

Cora let out a breath through her nose. "You always know how to make a sentence put on stockings."

"You understood it."

"I did."

Thea made herself stay still. This was the beat where she usually over-explained and spoiled whatever honesty had just arrived. She bit back three further clarifications and let the room do some of the work.

Dell spoke into the gap. "I've got money too."

Cora turned on him. "From what?"

"From fixing Pritchard's pump and the Reiners drill in August. And from the threshing belts I sold Mayberry after the south shed came down." He lifted his good shoulder. "Not a king's ransom. Enough that I don't need to sit there while you two talk like I'm freight."

Hester nodded once, as if he had finally said the minimum acceptable thing.

"Good," she said. "You're not freight."

"I know I'm not freight."

"Then stop looking wounded when adults forget it and correct them."

Dell's ears reddened again, but he did not duck his head this time. "Fine. I'm correcting everybody now. I can go to Lincoln. After that I might go farther. I still want California, or the Corps, or anything with engines that aren't half dead already. But I'll go to Lincoln first. I won't slip off a platform and leave a note under a plate if that's what you're worried about."

Cora had been worried about exactly that. It showed.

"I wasn't going to say it that way," she muttered.

"You were thinking it that way."

"Maybe."

The honesty of it pulled the meanness out of the exchange before it could root.

Thea said quietly, "Lincoln first gives us a place to stand and decide from there. It doesn't have to be more promise than that tonight."

Cora looked at the jars, at the packet of seed, at Dell's bent wrist resting beside the tin, at Hester's face already graying with tiredness around the mouth. When she spoke, the words came in the order they always did with her when something mattered too much: practical thing first, feeling after if it could not be avoided.

"We'll need to sort what we can carry. Clothes. Papers. Del's sling wraps. The letter." She touched the edge of the tin. "These."

Then she lifted her eyes to Thea.

"Far as Lincoln," she said. "I can promise that. I can get on that train with you and Del and not turn coward halfway to the depot. Beyond it, I won't tell stories yet. I don't know what I'll be once I'm not on that farm, and I won't lie to keep you near me."

Thea had thought the clean sentence she wanted might sound grander than that. It did not. It sounded smaller and truer and therefore hit deeper.

"All right," she said.

Hester's gaze sharpened. "That isn't enough from you."

Thea almost laughed from nerves alone. "You run a tyrannical supper table."

"Yes."

Thea folded her hands in her lap so no one would see them shake. "All right," she said again, clearer. "Lincoln first. After that, if I want something more or different, I'll say it before it turns to poison. I won't stay silent and call it virtue. And I won't ask either of you for forever while the ash from today is still in your hair."

Dell absorbed that with a seriousness beyond his years and beyond his patience, but he did not sneer at it. That was its own kindness.

"Good," he said. "Then mine is this. Lincoln first. After that, I get to say what I want too. Out loud. Not after I've done it."

Cora swallowed. The muscles in her jaw worked once.

"Yes," she said.

Thea would have trusted almost any other mother to gather him in then, to spoil the hard-won truth with comfort. Cora only reached across the table and set her hand over his for a single second, weathered palm to narrow wrist, then took it back. It was more moving than an embrace would have been because it was exactly all she could bear in front of witnesses.

Hester leaned back. The effort of the conversation had cost her. That was visible now in the bluish hollows at her temples and the way she was measuring each breath as if she meant to make the supply last.

"There," she said. "A plan narrow enough to be honest. Miracles happen."

"You don't believe in miracles," Dell said.

"I believe in bread rising and people occasionally surprising me. That's enough wonder for one life."

She pushed the tin gently toward Thea. Then the seed packet toward Cora. Then, with one blunt finger, she tapped Dell's folded timetable where it still showed from his pocket.

"And you," she said, "if you insist on going west eventually, at least learn the names of what you'll be leaving behind. Fool boys dream better when the map has labels."

He blinked. "I know the labels."

"Then stop hiding them in your pocket like contraband."

For the first time all evening he grinned without flinching, full and young and impossible not to love.

"Yes, ma'am," he said.

Hester clicked her tongue. "Don't 'ma'am' me like a deputy sheriff."

Outside, the wind had dropped. In its place came the strange after-silence that belonged only to dust country, when even a town's emptiness seemed to listen. The lamp flame shivered once in the chimney and steadied.

They packed the jars before anyone could say something that would cheapen what had just been said cleanly. Dell fetched flour sacks and cut strips from one with his pocketknife. Thea wrapped each jar in towel and cloth.

"Not in the tin," Hester said when Dell reached for it. "That's cupboard storage. Del, get the cracker crate from under the bread bench. If those jars ride tomorrow, they ride flat."

He crouched, swore under his breath when his ribs caught, and came up with a shallow wooden crate that still smelled faintly of salt and paper. Hester made him set it on the floor beside her chair. Thea lined it with folded sacks. Dell wedged the wrapped jars tight along the bottom one-handed while Cora tore an old quilt into the right shape of padding by opening seams instead of fighting the cloth. The blue tin went back to the shelf empty. The travel crate filled the way a thing meant to last had to fill: glass low, cloth between, quilt over, no waste motion.

Cora slid the seed packet into her handbag between a comb and the small purse where she kept household cash. Hester watched all of it, correcting the placement once, then again, because of course she did.

When the work was finished, Cora straightened with the handbag strap in one hand and a look on her face Thea knew too well by now. Gratitude trying to disguise itself as departure.

"We'll get out of your way," Cora said.

"You'll do no such idiotic thing." Hester's voice came rough with the cough she was holding back. "You'll sleep here. The boy's arm is broke, your eyes are crossing from tiredness, and I'm not having anybody start for the depot in the dark because pride's got hold of the reins. Leave after first light if you're still determined to be fools. You'll think straighter after sleep."

Dell looked from one woman to the other. "I can make a pallet by the flour sacks."

"Good," Hester said. "Then you've solved one quarter of the problem."

Cora opened her mouth, likely to say she had slept in worse places and did not need charity, and Thea saw Hester decide in advance not to let the sentence live.

"Miss Bell's upstairs room is still Miss Bell's," Hester said. "You get the cot behind the counter. Del gets his pallet. I'll keep my own bed till the Lord drags me out of it by the ankle. Now stop arguing and pack like people who mean to leave."

The order of it steadied the room. Dell rolled his tools into the old canvas wrap he used for field jobs and tied it with twine using his teeth when his left hand failed him. Cora and Thea sorted shirts, stockings, sling wraps, and papers between the two valises Hester kept under the hall bench for flour deliveries. Thea went upstairs once and came back with the books she could not bear to abandon, tied together with clothesline so they would not burst apart in transit. Cora scrubbed the breadboards after, hard enough to leave the wood smelling wet and raw. Dell filled the coffee pot and set it by the stove for morning. The jar crate, wrapped now in the old quilt, went under the worktable with the valises. Dell's tool roll lay beside them. Thea's books rested on top, square and ready.

At one point Cora came to her where she was knotting the clothesline around the books.

"I meant what I said," she said, low enough that Dell would have had to lean to hear.

"So did I."

"Lincoln first."

"Lincoln first."

Cora's hand slid from the rope to Thea's wrist. Flour from the towel marked her thumb white. The touch lasted only a beat. It was enough to make Thea feel, all at once, how starved she had been for any contact not shaped by duty or audience.

"Don't let me make fog again," Cora said.

It sounded stripped to the grain: a promise that love would not be allowed to fog the world again. That was better than romance.

"Then don't ask it," Thea answered.

Cora nodded and went back to shake out the blanket for the cot.

By the time the stove was banked and the back door barred, the bakery had been remade for departure and for night. Cora's handbag hung from the chair by the counter with the seed packet inside it. Dell was already easing himself down onto the pallet he had made by the flour sacks, jaw tight against the pull in his ribs. Hester stood in her doorway in her chemise, one hand braced to the frame, and watched Cora spread the flour sack over the narrow cot as if supervision were the final thing keeping the building upright.

"Enough," she said when Thea reached for one more loose spoon on the drainboard. "Go upstairs. If I die because you insisted on tidying, I'll come back and haunt you incompetently."

"You'd do it competently," Dell said into his blanket.

"Of course I would."

That was the last laugh of the night.

Later, in the room above the shop, Thea lay awake listening to the bakery settle around the cold. No steps went out toward the street. Below her, Dell turned once on the pallet and muttered at the pain of it. The cot behind the counter complained softly under Cora's weight and then went still.
